Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, after the Lee, Schmalz, Keating, and Ha (2020) paragraph.

Suggested text: McDonald et al. (2021) extend this metadata argument by examining six game development artifact collections and interviewing 29 creators, information professionals, and game researchers. Their 123-term taxonomy and best-practice recommendations show that game metadata work must address development, organization-related, and marketing materials; mixed physical and digital formats; vague terms such as "design document" and "prototype"; creator knowledge; rights restrictions; and the need for participatory archival processing between game companies and cultural heritage institutions.

# **Abrams, S. S., & Van Eck, R. N. (2021). Checked out? Videogame programming in U.S. libraries and implications for videogame literacies. *Journal of Adolescent & Adult Literacy, 65*(3), 227-236. https://doi.org/10.1002/jaal.1197**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `67C8TZZX`; Better BibTeX citation key `abramsCheckedOutVideogame2021`; attachment key `HB5XLDSI`.

Abrams and Van Eck investigate the presence, forms, barriers, and literacy implications of videogame programming in U.S. libraries. Their central argument is that videogames fit naturally within libraries because videogame play involves digital, multimodal, social, and participatory literacies. Videogame literacies include understanding how games work, using digital and nondigital resources, navigating social interaction, collaborating, sharing knowledge, and making meaning with players, spectators, screens, controllers, rules, spaces, and game systems. The article therefore treats videogame programming as a possible literacy service, not simply as recreation or teen entertainment.

The study draws from a larger mixed-methods project involving more than 1,000 U.S. librarians from all 50 states. The survey was administered through Qualtrics between August 2017 and May 2018 and included questions about library setting, programming, roles, videogame programming, videogame play, device ownership, and barriers to adoption. A total of 1,016 U.S. librarians responded. Respondents were roughly distributed across urban, suburban, and rural settings; most held library science graduate degrees and had worked in their libraries for about 12 years. The article combines quantitative survey findings with qualitative open-ended responses.

Abrams and Van Eck use the framework of first-order and second-order barriers. First-order barriers are external constraints such as funding, hardware, software, space, furniture, training, technical support, and policies. Second-order barriers are internal constraints such as beliefs, confidence, perceived value, assumptions about youth, comfort with games, and concerns about behavior or noise. This framing is especially useful for GBLS because it shows that game programming depends both on material infrastructure and on professional culture.

The prevalence findings complicate assumptions that gaming is already widespread in libraries. Of 804 librarians who answered the relevant question, 384, or 48%, reported having some form of videogame programming, while 420, or 52%, did not. Among libraries with programming, most programs were recent: 63% had existed for less than five years, and 35% had existed for less than three years. Nearly half of libraries with programming relied only on scheduled access, about one-fifth relied only on open play, about one-fifth offered both, and a small group reported other models such as games or consoles for checkout. The authors argue that programming structure matters for literacy. Scheduled play fits familiar library event models but may restrict the iterative, ongoing, peer-supported nature of videogame literacies; open play enables access but may miss opportunities for tournaments, group play, or structured reflection.

First-order barriers were substantial. Librarians tended to agree that the difficulty of supporting videogames was a significant barrier. They reported that general funding for new programming could be difficult, and many thought current library structures would not support videogame programs because of limited funds or lack of institutional value. Training was especially uneven. Informal training was the most common source of preparation, including independent searching, informal conversations, and feedback from other librarians. Library science degrees accounted for less than 12% of training, and formal professional development accounted for 22%. The authors therefore call for library science programs, teacher education, professional development, and accreditation standards to address videogame literacies more explicitly.

Second-order barriers were also clear. Many librarians owned gaming devices and expressed positive attitudes toward games, but they often lacked confidence in their ability to play games or support game play. Some comments showed enthusiasm for teen bonding, cooperative play, Japanese language/culture programs, intergenerational game play, problem solving, and library spaces where teens saw the library as welcoming. Other comments revealed concerns about management, setup, noise, hygiene, behavior, adult patron complaints, staff acceptance, space use, and whether games had value. The authors note that some comments were gendered or stereotypical, which matters for GBLS because adult assumptions about teens and gaming can shape which literacies libraries recognize as legitimate.

For the GBLS project, this source gives a national-scale account of videogame programming as literacy infrastructure. It supports the argument that games in libraries can foster participatory, multimodal, social, and layered literacies, but that those benefits depend on programming design, staff preparation, institutional support, and youth involvement. It also provides a practical assessment lens: asking whether a GBLS program is limited by resources, training, space, and equipment, or by beliefs, confidence, professional identity, and assumptions about patrons. The authors' recommendation to start small, such as with a videogame discussion club, is useful for libraries that cannot immediately support hardware-heavy programming.

The study has limitations. It relies on survey responses and self-reported perceptions, and it does not directly observe youth learning or measure outcomes of videogame literacy. Differences across library types and local inequities are not fully analyzed in this article. Still, the source is highly valuable because it names the real conditions under which videogame programming is adopted, constrained, and interpreted by librarians. It also helps the GBLS review connect videogame play to adolescent and adult literacy research.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Abrams and Van Eck (2021) frame videogame programming as a site of videogame literacies: multimodal, social, participatory meaning-making involving games, players, spectators, spaces, rules, and digital and nondigital resources. Their national survey of 1,016 U.S. librarians found that fewer than half reported videogame programming, most existing programs were recent, and adoption was shaped by first-order barriers such as funding, training, hardware, and space as well as second-order barriers such as confidence, beliefs about games, and assumptions about teen behavior.

# **Baker, C. J. (2014). *Video games: Their effect on society and how we must modernize our pedagogy for students of the digital age* [Master's thesis, Virginia Commonwealth University]. VCU Scholars Compass.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `2QU6TCDA`; Better BibTeX citation key `bakerVideoGamesTheir2014`; attachment key `AHX2N6VF`.

Baker's thesis is a broad argument for recognizing video games as culturally significant, socially prevalent, and pedagogically useful. The thesis is organized around four main areas: the prevalence of video games in U.S. society, the role and benefits of games across fields such as medicine, rehabilitation, psychology, economics, art, social-change games, and theatre, the need for a modern pedagogy suited to students of the digital age, and controversies around violent games and gaming addiction. The author draws heavily on popular and scholarly discussions of games, including industry demographic data, flow theory, game-based learning principles, and debates about whether games cause violence or addiction.

The most relevant chapter for the GBLS project is Chapter 3, where Baker argues that traditional education often leaves students passive and disengaged, while good games model interest-driven learning through clear goals, accessible rules, immediate feedback, exploration, unconventional thinking, linear and nonlinear paths, peer guidance, and goal sharing. The thesis connects these design features to the idea that students who have grown up with games may already be accustomed to learning through systems, challenge, feedback, and iterative problem solving. It also treats video games as participatory media that differ from film and television because they require active player decision-making.

Despite those overlaps with game-based learning theory, the source is not a strong fit for direct inclusion in the GBLS literature review. It is not about library services, library collections, library programming, library instruction, or library staff practice. It also does not present original empirical data about libraries or game-based services. Much of its discussion is a general synthesis of game culture, pedagogy, medical uses, theatre, and public controversies, and stronger GBLS sources already cover adjacent points with direct library relevance. For example, Abrams and Van Eck (2021) address videogame literacies in U.S. libraries, Lee et al. (2017) address game advisory and recommendation, and Lee et al. (2020, 2021) address library metadata and preservation infrastructure.

For the larger GBLS project, Baker may remain useful as broad background on game-based pedagogy and the cultural normalization of games, especially if the introduction needs a non-library example of arguments circulating around games and education in the 2010s. However, it should not be added to `review.md` at this stage because it would dilute the library-service focus of the review and duplicate stronger sources.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No suggested review placement. Recommended Zotero filing: `Explicitly Excluded Literature`.

# **Buchanan, K., & Vanden Elzen, A. M. (2012). Beyond a fad: Why video games should be part of 21st century libraries. *Education Libraries, 35*(1-2), 15-33.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `5RC5BETF`; Better BibTeX citation key `buchananFadWhyVideo2012`; attachment key `CHFVAEF7`.

Buchanan and Vanden Elzen offer an early, library-facing argument for treating video games as part of 21st-century library collections and programming. The article is not an empirical study, but it is valuable as a practitioner framework. The authors argue that librarians do not need to be avid gamers to understand why video games matter; rather, they need enough media literacy, advisory confidence, and service imagination to recognize games as visual, interactive simulations that communicate ideas, motivate patrons, and invite critical conversation.

The article begins with two examples, *September 12th* and *Beyond Good & Evil*, to show that games can communicate complex political, ethical, and civic ideas. *September 12th* models the consequences of missile strikes in a fictional Middle Eastern city, making player action part of the argument about terrorism and collateral damage. *Beyond Good & Evil* places the player in the role of Jade, a photojournalist investigating propaganda, corruption, occupation, and resistance. These examples support the authors' claim that games deserve First Amendment seriousness because, like books, plays, and films, they communicate ideas through both familiar narrative devices and medium-specific features.

The authors frame the library mission around three services: access, motivation, and guidance. Access means making media and technology available, especially to patrons who might otherwise be excluded. Motivation means encouraging discovery, enjoyment, learning, and library use through appealing materials. Guidance means helping patrons connect interests, needs, questions, and formats without controlling or censoring their choices. This framework is useful for GBLS because it translates game services into familiar library values: collect, invite, advise, contextualize, and support.

The article's media-literacy discussion is also important. Buchanan and Vanden Elzen describe video games as visual, interactive, and simulation-based. Because games use icons, visual design, player choices, feedback, modeled systems, and authored constraints, they require both traditional and new literacies. Players need to read images, understand rules, interpret choices and consequences, recognize author assumptions, and think critically about what a simulation simplifies or exaggerates. The authors connect this to critical literacy: players should learn to ask what values, biases, and assumptions are built into a game's systems.

The authors identify three broad reasons people play games: immersion, challenge, and connection. Immersion lets players inhabit alternate contexts and identities. Challenge gives players "hard fun," feedback, flow, trial and error, and opportunities to develop portable skills. Connection includes self-discovery, shared play, discussion, community, and broader civic conversation. These categories anticipate later library-advisory work on appeals while remaining practical for librarians who need basic vocabulary for explaining why games draw patrons in.

The article offers concrete suggestions for collection development and programming. Libraries should decide whether games belong in general collections, review or curriculum collections, gaming labs, staff programming collections, or circulating equipment collections. They should consider age ratings, platforms, single-player and multiplayer formats, genre variety, critically praised games, patron expertise, and local needs. The authors recommend using ESRB ratings, game-review sources, and experienced gamers as advisors. They also discuss educators and game scholars as special patron populations, especially in education libraries, and suggest serious games, games for curriculum exploration, and titles that provoke scholarly or civic discussion.

For access, the article emphasizes catalog visibility, display, checkout ease, outreach, social media, and the possibility of circulating consoles or setting up kiosks. For programming, it suggests integrating games into existing groups and events for children, teens, adults, seniors, English-language learners, guilds, and academic communities. It also encourages libraries to connect games to other materials: books, graphic novels, films, fan fiction, game design, music, podcasts, lectures, author/designer talks, and other cultural media. This cross-media orientation makes the source especially relevant to GBLS collection and advisory work.

For the GBLS project, Buchanan and Vanden Elzen help establish an early service rationale for games in libraries. Their framework of access, motivation, and guidance is still useful for explaining why game services are not a fad or a distraction from core library work. The article also helps connect collections, programming, media literacy, critical literacy, advisory, and cultural programming. Its limits are that it predates much of the later empirical literature, offers broad recommendations rather than assessment data, and uses examples from a 2012 platform and media environment. Even so, it remains a strong practitioner foundation for why libraries should engage games as media, technology, and culture.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Collections, Circulation, and Access`, after the Lee, Clarke, Cho, and Windleharth (2017) paragraph.

Suggested text: Buchanan and Vanden Elzen (2012) offer an early practitioner framework for adding video games to library collections and programs through the familiar service values of access, motivation, and guidance. Their argument treats games as visual, interactive simulations that require critical literacy and can support discovery, advisory, civic discussion, cross-media connections, circulating collections, game labs, kiosks, curriculum collections, and programming for patrons ranging from teens to educators and game scholars.

# **Copeland, T., Henderson, B., Mayer, B., & Nicholson, S. (2013). Three different paths for tabletop gaming in school libraries. *Library Trends, 61*(4), 825-835. https://doi.org/10.1353/lib.2013.0018**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `FFP8YPTK`; Better BibTeX citation key `copelandThreeDifferentPaths2013`; attachment key `PIXJWQWM`.

Copeland, Henderson, Mayer, and Nicholson present three practitioner accounts of tabletop gaming in school libraries. The article begins from the observation that school-library gaming has two primary goals: education and recreation. Tabletop games, if chosen well, can serve both goals because they are inexpensive, portable, durable, adaptable, social, and easier to integrate into classrooms than many digital games. The authors focus on nondigital games: board games, card games, and role-playing games played around tables rather than on screens.

The first account, from Teresa Copeland at Tesseract School in Arizona, describes a school where games are integrated across grades and curricular areas. Younger students use games for vocabulary, dictionary skills, online safety, word recognition, physics, logic, pattern matching, and mathematics. Older students participate in role-playing games tied to history and literature, including a Reacting to the Past-style "Trial of Socrates" and a teacher-written Salem Witch Trials game. These role-playing activities require students to research time periods, read primary and literary sources, write speeches in character, avoid anachronisms, negotiate with peers, and reflect after play. The account is especially useful for GBLS because it shows how role-playing games can turn historical and civic questions into participatory inquiry.

Copeland's examples also emphasize the importance of flexible facilitation and debriefing. In tabletop role-play, teachers and librarians can adjust events on the fly, reward student research, respond to creative suggestions, and let outcomes vary across iterations. Socrates may be acquitted, imprisoned, or commit suicide; accused witches may escape, be convicted, or accuse others. The games are memorable because students take control while adults facilitate from the edges. Post-game discussions about free speech, treason, democracy, accusation, and group deception turn game experience into reflective learning.

The second account, from Brenda Henderson at Trinity High School Learning Resources Centre in the United Kingdom, describes an after-school board game club called "Unplugged." The club attracted students who were not drawn to other after-school activities and gave them a safe, sociable library space for play. Games had to fit a one-hour time limit, and the library selected accessible, engaging modern board games such as *Zombies*, *Marrakech*, *Ticket to Ride*, and *Forbidden Island*. Henderson emphasizes that board game clubs support reading rules, mental arithmetic, turn-taking, graceful winning and losing, consensus-building, cross-year friendships, and social connection. The club also filled an access gap for students who lacked people to play with, could not afford designer board games, or mostly played single-player video games at home.

The third account, from Brian Mayer at the Genesee Valley Educational Partnership in rural western New York, describes a shared school-library game collection of more than 200 modern board and card games aligned to state and national learning standards. Mayer uses four selection criteria: the game should provide an engaging play experience; connect to curriculum through mechanics, theme, or strategy; fit reasonable time constraints; and offer a good return on investment in relation to student learning. The account is valuable because it insists that educational games must first be good games. Students respond when they realize they are playing something fun and challenging rather than a thinly disguised worksheet.

Mayer also describes two expansions: life-skills work and tabletop game design. For life skills, librarians and special education teachers used games to support gross and fine motor skills, social skills, cooperative play, team play, and problem solving. Language-independent games helped students participate without reading ability becoming a barrier. For game design, students studied modern tabletop games as models and then built their own games to represent curricular concepts. Because tabletop prototypes can be made with paper, cards, poster board, and index cards, students can focus on representing systems, rules, and concepts rather than learning complex digital creation tools. The design process also lets students with different strengths collaborate.

For the GBLS project, this article is a strong service-model source. It shows that tabletop gaming in libraries can be curricular, recreational, social, inclusive, standards-aligned, and design-oriented. It connects several review themes: clubs and recurring play groups, TTRPG and role-play programs, instruction and information literacy, game design workshops, facilitation, reflection, and access. It also helps explain why tabletop games remain important even in a digital-games literature review: they are affordable, adaptable, socially rich, and transparent enough for players to understand and modify game systems.

The article is practitioner-based rather than a formal assessment study, so its evidence is anecdotal and local. Still, the three cases give concrete implementation models and vocabulary for school-library GBLS work. They also show that the library role can range from collection provision to club facilitation, curriculum collaboration, game mastering, professional development for teachers, and design coaching.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Clubs and Recurring Play Groups`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Copeland et al. (2013) show that tabletop gaming in school libraries can follow multiple service paths: curriculum-integrated role-play, after-school board game clubs, shared standards-aligned game collections, life-skills programming, and tabletop game design workshops. Their practitioner cases emphasize affordability, portability, rule reading, social interaction, cross-age friendship, curricular alignment, flexible facilitation, and post-game reflection, making tabletop games a practical bridge between recreation and instruction.

# **Elkins, A. J., & Hollister, J. M. (2020). Power up: Games and gaming in Library and Information Science curricula in the United States. *Journal of Education for Library and Information Science, 61*(2), 229-252. https://doi.org/10.3138/jelis.2019-0038.r1**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `9IPKA2CE`; Better BibTeX citation key `elkinsPowerGamesGaming2020`; attachment key `IZ4VQZWN`.

Elkins and Hollister examine whether U.S. Library and Information Science education prepares future librarians to support games and gaming in libraries. The article starts from a tension that is central to the GBLS project: games are widespread in American society and already present in public, school, and academic libraries, but LIS curricula rarely offer sustained preparation for game collections, programming, literacies, or services. The authors connect games to digital literacy, problem solving, communication, critical thinking, information literacy, media literacy, social learning, and access to digital media, arguing that games belong in professional education because library workers increasingly encounter them in practice.

The study follows an earlier curriculum scan by the authors that found only a small number of ALA-accredited U.S. programs offering games- or gaming-related courses. In this article, they survey LIS educators who teach or have taught graduate-level courses in U.S. LIS programs. The survey asked about demographics, professional background, personal and professional experience with games, whether respondents used or discussed games in their teaching, barriers to inclusion, and resources that might help them integrate game-related content. The survey was distributed through professional listservs, social media, ALISE channels, ALA GameRT spaces, and related networks between February and June 2018.

The completed sample was small: 24 valid responses. That limitation matters, and the authors explicitly state that the findings are not generalizable. Even so, the results are useful because they name the kinds of professional education barriers that appear downstream in library practice. Of the respondents, 41.7% said they neither used nor discussed gaming in their courses, while 58.3% did. Among gaming-exclusive educators, half said they had no interest in adding gaming, half said students had not expressed interest, and half were unsure how gaming related to their coursework. Other barriers included not knowing how to integrate gaming, not feeling knowledgeable enough, seeing gaming as unsuitable for their courses, difficulty changing course content, and difficulty setting up special topics courses.

For educators who did include gaming, personal experience mattered most. A majority of gaming-inclusive respondents identified personal experience with gaming as an influence, and some also cited conference sessions, professional development, textbooks, or best-practices guidebooks. The authors find that gaming-inclusive educators tended to have more teaching experience, position titles more often associated with tenure, more experience or identity connection with gaming, and higher preferences across a wider range of digital and analog game genres. Gaming-exclusive educators were not necessarily nonplayers; some reported frequent play and enjoyment, but their preferences clustered more narrowly around casual forms such as matching, card, puzzle, and word games. The authors interpret broader game exposure as one possible reason some educators see more curricular applications.

The discussion is especially valuable for GBLS because it links professional preparation to institutional conditions. Many respondents, both gaming-inclusive and gaming-exclusive, reported that they had not encountered game-related content in their own MLS education. Most with prior library or information-professional experience also had not used games professionally. This lack of exposure may limit educators' sense of what games can do in library contexts. The authors also suggest that tenure status, curricular flexibility, and administrative support may shape whether educators feel able to add game content or develop special topics courses. In other words, the absence of games from LIS curricula is not only a matter of individual preference; it may reflect structural constraints in professional education.

The article's practical recommendations map directly onto a GBLS research and training agenda. Educators who do not currently include games but are open to doing so named relevant course modules, best-practices guidebooks, conference sessions, assignment materials, textbooks, and professional development as supports that might encourage integration. Elkins and Hollister therefore call for evidence-based professional development materials and experiences that clearly convey the relevance of games to library practice and build on adult learners' existing knowledge. They also point toward future interviews with gaming-inclusive LIS educators to identify best practices.

For the GBLS project, this source supports the professional-practice side of the literature review. It helps explain why many libraries may be interested in games but unevenly prepared to design, assess, catalog, facilitate, or defend game-based services. It also gives the project a concrete training mandate: GBLS work should not only describe successful programs, but also create reusable modules, assignments, examples, and professional development resources for librarians and LIS educators. The study is small and survey-based, so it should be used as exploratory evidence rather than a national estimate. Its value lies in naming a preparation gap and identifying the supports that could make game-based library services more teachable and sustainable.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda > Professional Practice and Training Gaps`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Elkins and Hollister (2020) identify a professional-preparation gap behind GBLS implementation: in a survey of 24 U.S. LIS educators, 41.7% neither used nor discussed gaming in their courses, and many gaming-exclusive respondents cited uncertainty about relevance, integration, knowledge, course-change constraints, or lack of student interest. Their findings suggest that reusable course modules, assignments, best-practices guides, conference workshops, and administrative support are part of the infrastructure needed to make game-based library services teachable in LIS programs.

# **Forsythe, C. (2021). Game on! Reflections of a board game librarian: An examination and application of non-traditional collections and recreational play in the academic library. *Emerging Library & Information Perspectives, 4*, 143-156. https://doi.org/10.5206/elip.v4i1.13442**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `3LSZ4R4E`; Better BibTeX citation key `forsytheGameRecreationalPlay2021`; attachment key `NRGIQN3X`.

Forsythe offers a practitioner reflection on creating and managing a board game collection and program in a community college academic library. The article is framed through the broader rise of non-traditional library collections: items such as tools, seed libraries, craft kits, technology kits, musical instruments, puzzles, and board games. Forsythe argues that these collections can attract new patrons, support library goals, promote social inclusivity and equity, increase comfort in the library, support programming, and create opportunities for community partnerships. Board games fit this logic because they provide shared, low-cost access to materials that can be expensive to own and difficult to store individually.

The article's local case comes from an academic library that already lent or supported several non-traditional resources, including video games, consoles, calculators, media equipment, 3D printing, and laser cutting. The library had also become a learning commons after merging with a learning centre, making collaborative learning, social exchange, and out-of-classroom learning central to its identity. Forsythe, working as a part-time reference specialist and unofficial "board game librarian," applied prior research on tabletop role-playing game collections to a board game collection of more than 25 games managed over nearly two years.

Forsythe organizes the case around practical collection and program decisions. The needs assessment began with alignment to institutional values: improving students' social and technical skills, providing a friendly and safe environment, attracting non-library users, and supporting collaborative learning. The library also considered its student demographics, especially a large international student population that was more likely to engage with programming than domestic students. This shaped selection because language-independent games such as *Tsuro* could reduce linguistic barriers and support inclusive participation.

Collection development depended on budget, selection criteria, and community knowledge. Games were purchased incrementally, three or four at a time, and recommendations were drawn from ALA GameRT, BoardGameGeek, and the League of Librarian Gamers. Forsythe used BoardGameGeek data to guide selection, especially number of players, user ratings, playing time, and weight or complexity. The local goals favored games for two or more players, games playable in 45 minutes or less, and lighter-weight games that students could learn during programming. The collection was then refined through observation and a short feedback survey about students' favorite games, enjoyment, willingness to return, and suggestions.

The circulation and preservation details are particularly useful for GBLS implementation. The collection was non-circulating, stored in a staff-only area, and used in-library by request or during programming. Drawing on Robson et al. (2018), Forsythe used clear book tape to protect boxes and rulebooks, component inventories under box lids, labeled bags for pieces, and RFID tags to keep games from leaving the library. Students could sign out games for in-library use with a manual tracking form and student ID. These details show how even a small tabletop collection needs policies, storage, preservation routines, inventories, and use tracking.

Programming made the collection visible and socially meaningful. The original plan was a monthly two-hour board game night, but student feedback pushed the program to three hours twice per month. Forsythe supplemented the library collection with personal games to fill gaps, test mechanics, and learn what students liked before purchasing. Marketing included posters, flyers, social media, word-of-mouth, and partnerships with the international centre and student association. Reported benefits included reducing library anxiety, turning attendees into library regulars, providing no-cost access to board games, and supporting language practice, global citizenship, problem solving, critical thinking, and social intelligence. Challenges included the need for volunteers, limited finances and marketing capacity, uneven attendance, and the interruption of in-person programming by COVID-19.

The article also addresses pandemic adaptation. Forsythe notes that library closures and restrictions reduced access to physical spaces and pushed libraries toward electronic resources and online programming, creating both access opportunities and barriers for patrons without reliable technology or broadband. The article highlights virtual board game programming at Valdosta State University's Odum Library, including Discord-based community spaces, trivia, virtual escape rooms, Jackbox Games, and online board game platforms. Forsythe compares Board Game Arena and Tabletopia, noting differences in game access, synchronous/asynchronous play, rule support, learning curve, and cost.

For the GBLS project, Forsythe is valuable as a concrete academic-library service model rather than an outcome study. It connects non-traditional collections, tabletop game selection, in-library use, recurring programming, student belonging, international student engagement, collection maintenance, feedback loops, and virtual adaptation. It also reinforces that a "game collection" is not self-executing: staff need selection tools, preservation practices, storage decisions, policies, partners, marketing, volunteers, and facilitation capacity. The evidence is reflective and local, but the implementation vocabulary is directly useful for libraries building or assessing tabletop game services.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Circulating and In-Library Collections`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Forsythe (2021) gives a practical academic-library model for board games as a non-traditional collection and recurring program. Her community-college case links needs assessment, language-independent selection for international students, BoardGameGeek-informed criteria, box and component preservation, in-library tracking, student feedback, partnerships, and pandemic-era virtual options, showing that tabletop collections require the same policy, maintenance, marketing, and facilitation infrastructure as other library services.

# **Hill, C. (2016). Play on: The use of games in libraries. *The Christian Librarian, 59*(1), 34-42. https://doi.org/10.55221/2572-7478.1157**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `48FGJ9GY`; Better BibTeX citation key `hillPlayUseGames2016`; attachment key `Y3FGY9X9`.

Hill provides a concise literature review of games in libraries, written as a general professional article for librarians considering whether gaming belongs in library collections and programs. The article begins by acknowledging a common objection: gaming may appear irrelevant to the library's mission or a waste of limited resources. Hill responds that games can serve library goals by increasing exposure to new information technologies, supporting community involvement, attracting patrons who do not otherwise use the library, and helping libraries plan collections and programs around emerging literacies and recreational media.

The article situates contemporary gaming work within a longer history of play in libraries. Drawing especially on Nicholson's history of games, toys, and puzzles in North American libraries, Hill notes that libraries have supported games for more than a century, including chess clubs, tournaments, toy lending during the Great Depression, puzzle tournaments, and children's social activities. This historical framing is useful for GBLS because it counters the idea that games are merely a recent fad or a departure from library work. Instead, games are presented as part of libraries' longstanding role as social, educational, recreational, and community spaces.

Hill organizes the rationale for library gaming around several themes. Games can support active, contextualized learning; after-school game programs can foster social skills through cross-age play and peer teaching; games can shape behavior; and digital games can help patrons develop twenty-first-century literacies such as critical thinking, problem solving, collaboration, information literacy, and media literacy. The access argument is central: if expensive devices, systems, or games are unevenly distributed, libraries can act as social equalizers by offering access and media mentorship much as they have offered internet access.

The article also names implementation challenges that recur across GBLS sources. Librarians may worry about spending resources on games, and administrators may require evidence from other successful programs. Game programs can be noisy and may need careful scheduling or space planning. Collections can face theft, replacement costs, parental challenges over content, and budget limits. Hill also warns that libraries should avoid educational games that overemphasize curriculum while underemphasizing game quality, a point echoed in later sources that insist games must first work as games.

One of Hill's most useful contributions is its overview of game categories and service settings. Digital games, video games, role-playing games, and tabletop games are treated as distinct but overlapping library possibilities. Digital games are linked to active, goal-oriented, contextualized, adaptive, feedback-oriented learning. Video games are discussed as circulating materials, access stations, events, and potential academic resources. Role-playing games are described through collaborative storytelling and community building, while tabletop games are presented as portable, affordable, durable, adaptable, and transparent enough for players to understand mechanics.

Hill then surveys public, school, and academic library uses. Public libraries can use gaming programs, summer reading game elements, senior programming, teen clubs, and tournaments to build community and invite patrons into other services. School libraries can integrate games into language learning, history role-play, after-school clubs, and classroom collaboration. Academic libraries can offer game nights, circulating games, gaming stations, curriculum-support collections, new-student orientation games, and information-literacy games. The article also highlights community involvement and collaboration, including student organizations, campus departments, local game stores, retailers, and board game clubs.

The final sections address gamification and game creation. Hill distinguishes reward or points systems applied to existing practices from the creation and playing of games to teach library concepts. Gamification is tied to orientation, information literacy, resource use, reading programs, and motivation. Game creation is framed as a way for patrons to learn design, art, writing, planning, testing, local history, and collaboration by making games rather than only playing them.

For the GBLS project, Hill is best used as a synthetic professional overview. It does not provide original assessment data or a detailed case study, and much of its evidence comes through secondary discussion of other sources. Still, it helps map the range of game forms and library settings that GBLS must account for: public, school, and academic libraries; digital, video, role-playing, tabletop, gamified, and user-created games; collections, programs, access, literacy, community, partnerships, and advocacy. It is useful for introductory framing and for showing how mid-2010s library writing consolidated earlier games-in-libraries arguments.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Game Modalities in Library Contexts`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Hill (2016) offers a synthetic professional map of library gaming modalities, grouping digital games, video games, role-playing games, tabletop games, gamification, and game creation across public, school, and academic libraries. Because it is a literature review rather than original assessment, its main value is definitional: it shows how mid-2010s library discourse connected games to access, new literacies, community building, collections, programming, partnerships, orientation, information literacy, and advocacy.

# **Ponto, K., Tredinnick, R., Verbeke, M., Kopp, K., Swanson, L., & Gagnon, D. (2023). Waddle: Using virtual penguin embodiment as a vehicle for empathy and informal learning. In *Proceedings of the 29th ACM Symposium on Virtual Reality Software and Technology (VRST 2023)*. ACM. https://doi.org/10.1145/3611659.3617211**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `W445KKUU`; Better BibTeX citation key `pontoWaddleUsingVirtual2023`; attachment key `CU8SVMS4`.

Ponto, Tredinnick, Verbeke, Kopp, Swanson, and Gagnon present *Waddle*, a 20-minute virtual reality experience designed to support informal learning and empathy by embodying the user as an Adélie penguin. The paper is a short ACM extended abstract rather than a full-length library article, and it does not study a library program. Its relevance to GBLS is therefore transferable: it offers evidence about how an immersive, game-like, narrative VR experience can be designed and assessed for informal learning outcomes that libraries might care about when they host VR, STEM, ecology, or experiential learning programs.

The design centers on virtual embodiment. The authors situate the work in research on embodied learning, empathy, perspective-taking, and animal embodiment in VR. They note that earlier animal embodiment experiences have sometimes struggled because the learner's movements do not align well with the animal body. *Waddle* responds by emphasizing gesture congruence and visuomotor synchrony. The learner enters a snowy Antarctic scene from a low penguin-height perspective, sees a beak where their nose would be, sees a penguin body and flippers when looking down, and can view their penguin avatar in an icy mirror. Quest 2 hand tracking is used so users can act without controllers.

The experience takes users through major events in an Adélie penguin's life. They build a nest by picking up rocks with their beak, find a mate, perform a mating dance, and defend an egg from a Skua attack. The experience was developed with educational game designers, VR researchers, and research scientists. This collaborative design process matters for GBLS because it shows how playful informal learning experiences can combine scientific content, interaction design, narrative structure, and embodied mechanics rather than relying on passive display or novelty alone.

The study involved 43 students in grades 3 through 8, ages 8 through 13, recruited in groups from informal learning organizations during a science festival on a major university campus. The authors measured empathy with the Dispositional Empathy with Nature scale, embodiment with a variation of the Avatar Embodiment Questionnaire, and informal learning with open-ended questions about life events and challenges for Adélie penguins. Scores for empathy and embodiment were averaged on a scale from -4 to 4, pre/post differences were tested with paired t-tests, and relationships were examined with Pearson correlations.

Results suggest that *Waddle* supported learning, embodiment, and empathy. The article reports learning gains, an overall positive embodiment score, and a statistically significant increase in empathy from pre-experience to post-experience. It also reports a significant positive correlation between embodiment and post-experience empathy. The authors conclude that direct embodiment of a penguin may be possible in VR and that non-human virtual embodiment may be a useful tool for education and appreciation of other creatures.

For the GBLS project, the source is not evidence about library implementation, staffing, collection policy, or program assessment. It should not be used to claim that libraries have successfully run *Waddle* or that VR programs automatically produce empathy. Its contribution is narrower: it provides a design-and-assessment example for immersive, playful informal learning that could inform library VR programming. It reinforces lessons from library VR sources that content quality, embodiment, interaction, audience, accessibility, and learning goals matter more than putting patrons into a headset for novelty's sake.

The limitations are substantial but clear. The paper is brief, the sample is small, the setting is a science festival rather than a library, and the article provides only compressed detail about the learning measures. Still, for a GBLS review that includes VR, AR, and immersive technology, *Waddle* is a useful adjacent example of how a game-like VR experience can be tied to empathy, informal STEM learning, ecology, and embodied perspective-taking.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, after the Lee, King, Dahya, and Lee (2020) paragraph.

Suggested text: Ponto et al. (2023) are not studying a library program, but *Waddle* offers adjacent evidence for how immersive, game-like VR can support informal learning and empathy when embodiment, narrative, gesture congruence, and assessment are designed together. In a 43-student science-festival study, the penguin embodiment experience produced learning gains, a significant increase in empathy, and a correlation between embodiment and post-experience empathy, making it a useful design reference for libraries considering VR beyond novelty demonstrations.

# **Post, C., Henry, T., Nunnally, K., & Lanham, C. (2023). A colossal catalog adventure: Representing indie video games and game creators in library catalogs. *Cataloging & Classification Quarterly, 61*(7-8), 792-814. https://doi.org/10.1080/01639374.2023.2280207**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `WB2GDKPF`; Better BibTeX citation key `postColossalCatalogAdventure2023`; attachment key `BDSYUC55`.

Post, Henry, Nunnally, and Lanham examine how libraries can represent digitally distributed indie narrative games and their creators in library catalogs. The article begins from a shift that matters deeply for GBLS collections: video games are increasingly digital, platform-mediated, frequently updated, and produced by small studios or individual creators rather than only by large publishers releasing boxed games on physical media. The authors argue that library game collections should include indie games and alternative forms of play, not just mainstream commercial titles, but that doing so requires catalogers to adapt descriptive cataloging, subject cataloging, and name authority practices.

The study uses action research grounded in an applied cataloging project. The authors created 17 original MARC records and substantially enhanced one existing record for 18 digitally acquired games from Choice of Games, a publisher of independently made, text-based narrative games. They also created 33 original Library of Congress Name Authority File records for individuals involved in producing those games and edited four existing records to include game-design work. The reflective analysis compares this work with cataloging mainstream video games, other electronic resources, and narrative media such as books.

A central finding is that digitally distributed games resemble other electronic resources in some ways, but games still require game-specific description. OLAC guidelines supported much of the descriptive cataloging, including online-resource description, technical requirements, platform as edition, and notes about interactivity. For Choice of Games titles, the authors described the works as computer programs and games, but also used "text" as a content type because these are interactive text-based works rather than moving-image games. They recorded Steam as a platform while also using Choice of Games' own bibliographic information, which more closely resembles a book copyright page than the sparse credits often available for mainstream games.

The article is especially useful for understanding the instability of digital game manifestations. Minor patches and bug fixes may not require new records, but game records require the same vigilance as other online resources because URLs, platforms, access conditions, updates, downloadable content, and online functions can change. The authors note that many physical games now also depend on patches, downloadable content, or online play, so the line between physical and digital game description is blurring. Indie games distributed through Steam, itch.io, Kickstarter, studio websites, and episodic release models may require more precise version and relationship description when libraries collect them for game history or design research.

Subject cataloging raises a different problem. Narrative games occupy a hybrid position between literature and games: they have story, characters, setting, and themes, but they are also interactive computer games with mechanics and player choice. The authors found LCSH useful for narrative subject matter and OLAC video game genre terms useful for gameplay, but existing terms such as "Plot-your-own stories" and "Interactive book video games" did not match the language of narrative game communities. They therefore supplemented controlled vocabularies with terms such as interactive fiction, text-based games, and interactive narrative games, arguing that player and maker terminology can improve discovery.

Name authority work is the article's strongest contribution to GBLS infrastructure. Mainstream game records often rely on corporate creators because individual credits are hard to identify or too numerous. Indie narrative games, by contrast, often foreground authors, editors, artists, programmers, publishers, and small creative teams. By creating name authority records for people involved in Choice of Games titles, the authors attempted to represent how these games are actually made and to integrate indie game creators into the bibliographic universe. They argue that authority records help future libraries share work, support discovery, and recognize game creators as cultural producers alongside authors, filmmakers, and musicians.

For the GBLS project, this source strengthens the cataloging, metadata, discovery, and cultural-collection portions of the review. It pairs well with Lee, Schmalz, Keating, and Ha (2020) and McDonald et al. (2021): those sources focus on development artifacts, preservation, and taxonomy, while Post et al. show how everyday bibliographic cataloging must change when libraries collect digital indie games. It also supports equity and representation arguments, because indie games often document experiences, communities, creators, and play forms marginalized by mainstream definitions of "real games." The article is not about programming or patron outcomes, but it is directly about whether libraries can make diverse game materials findable, citable, and connected.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, after the McDonald et al. (2021) paragraph.

Suggested text: Post et al. (2023) extend the metadata discussion from game artifacts to catalog records for digitally distributed indie narrative games. Their action-research project created or enhanced MARC records for 18 Choice of Games titles and added 33 LC name authority records, showing that catalogers need to describe platforms, access conditions, version stability, interactivity, narrative subject matter, gameplay mechanics, community terminology such as "interactive fiction," and the individual creators whose work is often invisible in mainstream game cataloging.

# **Robson, D., Phillips, J., & Guerrero, S. (2018). Don't just roll the dice: Simple solutions for circulating tabletop game collections effectively in your library. *Library Resources & Technical Services, 62*(2), 80-90. https://doi.org/10.5860/lrts.62n2.80**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `PNVXZLYB`; Better BibTeX citation key `robsonDontJustRoll2018`; attachment key `T3MQW6LE`.

Robson, Phillips, and Guerrero provide one of the most concrete operational studies of tabletop game circulation in libraries. The article reports on a University of North Texas Media Library project designed to determine how much preservation and processing work is actually necessary for circulating tabletop games. The problem is practical and important: as board game collections grow, libraries need procedures for preserving boxes and components, preventing loss, checking games in and out, and keeping staff labor reasonable. The UNT collection had grown from a closed-stacks, campus-use collection to a larger circulating collection, eventually reaching hundreds of games, and the authors wanted evidence rather than anecdotes from gaming forums or local habit.

The project brought together a media librarian, preservation librarian, cataloger, circulation desk manager, and student assistants. The team purchased four new copies each of *Ticket to Ride* and *Betrayal at House on the Hill* because the two games included varied boards, cards, tokens, movers, room tiles, and other components. Each copy received a different level of preservation and processing, ranging from almost none to very extensive intervention. The authors define preservation as protective treatment for physical pieces, such as sleeves, contact paper, tape, cloth corners, spray, wax, or binding, while processing refers to barcoding, labeling, bagging, and inventory routines designed to support circulation and reduce loss.

The study tested both circulation use and simulated damage. The eight game copies circulated over an eight-month period, with staff rotating copies so each would circulate at least 15 times. After check-in, staff assessed damage and loss. The project also used pop-up messages in the circulation system and staff guidance to manage the research process. After the circulation phase, the authors ran controlled stress tests on components by exposing cards, tiles, boards, instructions, and boxes to hazards such as water, soda, salsa, cheese dip, stacking, and table-edge stress. Damage was rated on a scale from no damage to unusable.

The findings are useful because they resist overengineering. The fully preserved and fully processed copies had no damage or loss, but the differences between minimal and extensive processing were not large enough to justify the staff time and cost in most cases. The authors conclude that tabletop games can circulate like other library items without expanding preservation and processing procedures far beyond normal practice. Games do require more management time than books, but not so much that libraries should avoid collecting them. The acceptable level of damage will vary locally, but the UNT study suggests that libraries can make evidence-based tradeoffs between durability, cost, and staff burden.

The best-practice recommendations are concrete. UNT decided to sleeve cards, especially for high-use or hard-to-replace games, because cards quickly absorb liquid, oils, and contaminants and marked cards can affect gameplay. The library also reinforced game boxes with cloth corners, using in-house binding capacity and leftover cloth tape to reduce cost. Game pieces were bagged because bagging was inexpensive and helped minimize loss. The authors chose minimal processing for circulation: each game is cataloged, a single barcode is added to the box lid, and a single item record is used for circulation. Call numbers appear on lids and sides to support stacks management.

For the GBLS project, this source is important because it turns "board game collections" into an operational service model. It shows that access to tabletop games depends on mundane infrastructure: cataloging, barcodes, labels, inventories, bags, component sleeves, box reinforcement, circulation workflows, staff training, check-in expectations, replacement tolerance, and local preservation capacity. It also pairs well with Forsythe (2021), which cites Robson et al. for practical preservation techniques; together, the two sources show both how a small academic board game program can be built and how a larger circulating collection can be maintained.

The article is not an assessment of learning, belonging, or patron outcomes. Its evidence comes from two selected games and one university media library, so local conditions such as in-house binding capacity, staffing, circulation volume, and replacement budgets matter. Still, its narrowness is a strength for GBLS implementation: it gives libraries a tested starting point for circulating tabletop games without assuming that every piece needs intensive preservation or every check-in needs exhaustive staff counting.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Circulating and In-Library Collections`, after the Forsythe (2021) paragraph.

Suggested text: Robson, Phillips, and Guerrero (2018) provide the strongest operational evidence for circulating tabletop collections. In a University of North Texas study of differently preserved and processed copies of *Ticket to Ride* and *Betrayal at House on the Hill*, they found that fully preserved copies reduced loss and damage but usually did not justify the labor cost; their resulting practice favors card sleeves for vulnerable or high-use games, cloth-reinforced box corners, bagged components, catalog records, a single barcode/item record, and minimal processing that keeps games circulating without overwhelming staff.

# **Seay, J. A. (2018). Considering games and gamification in libraries & associated entities: In praise of real people. *Against the Grain, 30*(5), 86-87.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `DCF8ZU93`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesGamification2018d`; attachment key `YFYTYZ5Z`.

Seay's column is a short professional essay rather than a research study, but it captures a useful argument within library gaming discourse: analog, immersive, co-present games can support learning in ways that many individual screen-based educational games do not. The piece appears in a column on games and gamification in libraries and associated organizations, and it continues Seay's interest in analog games, escape rooms, and educational gaming. Its tone is informal, but the argument is clear enough to be useful for the GBLS review as a conceptual and practitioner-facing source.

The column begins with a learning problem: people forget a large proportion of newly presented information, and attention in lecture settings is limited. Seay invokes the forgetting curve and classroom attention-span discussions to argue that conventional information delivery struggles to produce durable learning. He then lists characteristics of effective learning experiences: they are impactful, relevant, engaging, motivating, inspiring, and playful. Games are attractive because they can combine these qualities, but Seay cautions that simply calling something an educational game is not enough.

The main distinction in the piece is between digital learning games and analog, socially immersive games. Seay acknowledges the power of digital technologies, including virtual reality, and notes that digital games dominate much of the educational games market. At the same time, he argues that many routine digital educational games are used for practice, rote learning, dexterity, or memorization, and that they often create one-person-one-screen experiences even when learners occupy the same room. For the GBLS project, this critique is useful because it helps explain why library game services should not automatically privilege digital formats as the most innovative or educationally meaningful.

Seay proposes a category he calls "collaborative multi-player immersive games." By this he means analog activities that put groups of players into a problem, issue, or scenario and give them mechanics for cooperative or contested interaction. The core features are real-time, group, social interaction in an immersive environment. Escape rooms are his most familiar example, described as a form of problem-based learning in a room. He also briefly introduces educational live action role-playing games and megagames as related forms that use physical presence, role-play, factional goals, research, writing, and large-scale social dynamics.

The column's discussion of Edu-LARP is especially relevant to library instruction and outreach. Seay describes educational live action role-playing as experiential learning that engages cognitive, affective, and behavioral dimensions. He points to historical and civic scenarios and to Barnard College's Reacting to the Past model, where students supplement role-play with research and writing. This overlaps with other GBLS sources on TTRPGs, LARPs, and library escape rooms because it highlights role assumption, scenario design, embodied interaction, and debriefable experience rather than only puzzle completion.

The piece also ties analog play to the "high tech high touch" idea: as technology becomes more pervasive, people may value slower, chatty, face-to-face, co-present interaction. Seay argues that analog game experiences make verbal and nonverbal communication central. This helps GBLS articulate why board games, escape rooms, LARPs, and megagames are not merely lower-tech substitutes for digital games. They can be deliberately chosen when the service goal is social interaction, collaborative problem solving, peer communication, motivation, or embodied engagement.

For the GBLS project, this source should be used cautiously. It is not empirical evidence, does not report a library case study, and uses broad claims about attention, retention, and learning. Still, it offers useful practitioner language for distinguishing game forms by affordance: digital games may excel at simulation, visualization, and individual practice, while analog immersive games may excel at co-presence, group dynamics, communication, and social learning. It therefore supports review sections that ask libraries to match game form to service goal and to treat facilitation and social design as central rather than incidental.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Analog, Digital, and Hybrid Forms`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Seay (2018) offers a practitioner argument for analog, co-present game forms as distinct from many screen-based educational games. Although not an empirical study, the column usefully frames escape rooms, Edu-LARPs, and megagames as collaborative, multiplayer, immersive activities whose value lies in real-time social interaction, role assumption, communication, and problem solving, reinforcing the GBLS principle that libraries should match game form to learning and community goals rather than treating digital novelty as inherently superior.

# **Seay, J. A. (2018). Considering games and gamification in libraries & associated entities: Surely, you can't be serious: When library folk go game. *Against the Grain, 30*(2), 58-59. https://doi.org/10.7771/2380-176X.8063**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `E3J2XAST`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesGamification2018a`; attachment key `E4CFA9IE`.

Seay's column is an informal professional essay introducing why games, serious games, escape rooms, and gamification matter for libraries. It is not an empirical study or a systematic literature review, but it is useful as evidence of how library practitioners were framing game-based services in the late 2010s. The column argues that libraries have become "transformed by games" and that games are no longer marginal novelties restricted to children's programming or occasional recreation.

The opening example is the commercial escape room: a timed, multiplayer environment in which players solve puzzles, interpret clues, search the room, and use strategy to reach a goal. Seay immediately connects these practices to library work by noting that escape rooms depend on knowledge, skill with knowledge, information retrieval, clue-finding, and problem solving. He presents library escape rooms as a natural fit for outreach, promotion, and instruction, especially information literacy. He points to contemporary library coverage of BreakoutEDU, research-library escape rooms, youth programming, and library escape-room manuals to show that the form had already become a visible professional trend.

The column then widens from escape rooms to games in libraries more generally. Seay describes public-library game nights, circulating board game collections, board game outreach, ALA tabletop gaming networks, video game events, and board game play around titles such as *Settlers of Catan*, *Ticket to Ride*, and *Pandemic*. The key point for GBLS is that games are framed as part of libraries' community-space function. Seay argues that group video game participation and the board game renaissance helped fuel the idea of libraries as places for gathering, play, and community interaction.

Seay also introduces "serious games," defined broadly as games designed for a purpose beyond entertainment. Serious games let educators and trainers simulate risk and reality without exposing learners to actual risk. This gives GBLS language for connecting library game services to education, training, professional development, socialization, activism, and public engagement without reducing play to frivolity. The column cites the MIT Game Lab's broad claim that play now supports domains ranging from entertainment to education, art, activism, science, and socialization.

The gamification section is especially relevant to the review's conceptual terrain. Seay defines gamification as adding games or game-like elements to a task to encourage participation, but he cautions that it is more than simply making unpleasant tasks palatable. Engagement and participation are the critical terms. He describes gamification as a system of elements and principles that encourage or compel users, then reframes behavior manipulation as user-centered instructional engagement. This ambivalence is useful for GBLS because it keeps gamification distinct from games while also raising questions about design ethics, motivation, and agency.

For academic and school libraries, Seay emphasizes games and gamification as tools for learner engagement. He notes that academic libraries use problem-solving games, badges, incentives, and gamified instruction to introduce research and information literacy skills that might otherwise be taught through bibliographic instruction lectures. The column therefore overlaps with later empirical work on escape rooms and misinformation games, but it functions more as a snapshot of the profession's enthusiasm and vocabulary than as evidence of outcomes.

For the GBLS project, this source supports broad framing. It helps explain why a review must distinguish among games, game-based learning, serious games, gamification, escape rooms, board game collections, and game nights. It also shows how practitioners connected games to outreach, instruction, library promotion, community space, and user engagement. Its limitations are significant: the article is playful, anecdotal, and promotional, with no assessment data. It should therefore be used as contextual practitioner discourse rather than as proof that game services work.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Games, Gamification, and Play`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Seay (2018) captures the practitioner-facing vocabulary that often bundles games, serious games, escape rooms, and gamification together in library discourse. The column is anecdotal rather than evaluative, but it is useful for distinguishing related service logics: escape rooms as information-seeking and problem-solving instruction, serious games as purposeful simulation, gamification as user-engagement design, and game nights or board game collections as expressions of the library as community space.

# **Seay, J. A. (2018). Considering games and gamification in libraries & associated entities: The "G" word. *Against the Grain, 30*(4), 68-69. https://doi.org/10.7771/2380-176X.8311**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `ME9ZJJK8`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesGamification2018`; attachment key `C4E5W3E6`.

Seay's "The G Word" is a short professional column aimed at readers new to gamification. It is useful for the GBLS review because it explicitly distinguishes games from gamification, a distinction that is often blurred in practitioner discourse. The column begins with Ralph Koster's definition of a game as a rule system that creates a simplified model of some aspect of reality. Seay contrasts this with gamification, which does not necessarily involve playing a complete game. Instead, gamification applies game thinking, game dynamics, or game-like elements to a non-game context in order to engage people and solve problems.

The column draws heavily on Bohyun Kim's ACRL writing about library gamification. Seay emphasizes that gamification is not the same thing as library game night, board game collections, or simply letting patrons play. Its central function is motivation. Game mechanics such as points, progress tracking, levels, badges, leaderboards, quests, rewards, and competition can make ordinary activities feel more purposeful, visible, or compelling. Seay uses everyday examples such as fitness apps and Habitica to show how gamification turns routine activities into tracked, goal-oriented systems.

For libraries, the column argues that gamification fits a long-standing service problem: how to engage users with resources, programs, services, orientations, instruction, and professional development. Seay notes that academic libraries have applied gamification to student orientation and information literacy instruction. He also cites Kyle Felker's discussion of point scoring and the Lemon Tree game at the University of Huddersfield, where resource usage could be point-scored by department or discipline. These examples show how existing library data, systems, and user behaviors can become inputs for game-like engagement systems.

The article also stresses that gamification does not have to require expensive technology. Seay repeats Kim's point that a successful library instruction game can be run with pencil and paper, and he names summer reading badges as a simple example. This matters for GBLS because it separates gamification from both high-cost digital platforms and full game development. A library can gamify an activity through visible progress, goals, rewards, roles, and feedback even when no digital game is present.

One of the more distinctive parts of the column concerns gamification for librarian professional development and promotion. Drawing on Lindsay Cronk's LITA Blog writing, Seay imagines promotion as a mission with quests, battles, rewards, collaborators, research outputs, publications, and "power-ups." Although playful, this example widens the scope of GBLS-adjacent gamification beyond patron instruction. It suggests that library organizations can use gameful structures internally to frame professional learning, collaboration, and career advancement.

The column is also useful because it contains an implicit caution. Seay acknowledges that gamification can sound like behavior manipulation and describes the larger social trend in which apps, marketers, educators, and institutions incentivize everyday behavior. He prefers to frame library gamification as user-centered instructional engagement, but the ambivalence remains. For the GBLS review, that tension is important: gamification can motivate participation, but it also raises questions about whether points and badges support meaningful learning or merely nudge compliance.

For the GBLS project, this source should be treated as practitioner framing rather than evidence. It does not assess outcomes or give a detailed implementation case. Its value is conceptual vocabulary: games are bounded rule systems, while gamification is the application of game dynamics to non-game contexts. It helps the review avoid treating all game-related library work as equivalent. A board game night, a circulating game collection, an escape room, a gamified orientation, and a badge-based reading program may all belong under GBLS, but they operate through different mechanics, labor demands, and service goals.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Games, Gamification, and Play`, after the Seay (2018) practitioner-vocabulary paragraph.

Suggested text: Seay's companion column on "The G Word" sharpens this distinction by defining games as bounded rule systems and gamification as applying game thinking and game dynamics to non-game contexts. For GBLS, this distinction matters because gamified orientations, badge systems, resource-use competitions, summer reading rewards, or professional-development quests operate through motivation, feedback, progress, and status rather than through the fuller play structures of board games, videogames, TTRPGs, or escape rooms.

# **Seay, J. A. (2019). Considering games in libraries and such: The ultimate library instruction. *Against the Grain, 31*(6), 65.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `YFLBM9RD`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesLibraries2019`; attachment key `TC7HN4A3`.

Seay's "The Ultimate Library Instruction" is a short professional column written as a narrative reenactment of a gamified library instruction scenario. It is not an empirical report, and it does not provide implementation data, assessment results, or a formal lesson plan. Its value for the GBLS project is as a practitioner design vignette: it dramatizes how a database-searching session could be reframed as a team-based, time-limited, immersive challenge with story, props, progress tracking, competition, and escalating stakes.

The scenario places students in a library classroom transformed into a nuclear-plant emergency. The lights are low, screens display a "Nuclear Core," warning symbols pulse, a temperature gauge tracks danger, and a set of glowing tube props represents unstable fuel rods. The librarian enters in a lab coat, announces that the core is approaching meltdown, and divides students into teams. Each team receives instructions that lead them into an application requiring them to solve problems using Web of Science search results.

The column's instructional design is gameful rather than merely decorative. Students must interpret sparse instructions, work through levels, answer questions, solve database-related problems, use clues from printed articles, open a locked box, decode information, and combine their data to shut down the fuel rods. Public progress indicators on the classroom screen show each team's movement toward the core, turning the activity into a visible race. Alarms, lights, countdown pressure, animated doors, physical props, and team coordination all support the scenario.

For GBLS, the column demonstrates how gamification and escape-room-like design can be applied to a familiar library instruction problem: students often experience database demonstrations as abstract, passive, or boring. Seay imagines instruction as an embodied problem space in which search tasks have immediate narrative consequences. The activity makes database use purposeful inside the fiction of the game, while also introducing collaboration, competition, tactile puzzles, and print/digital source use.

The most useful lesson is not that every library should build a nuclear-core classroom game. Rather, the source illustrates several reusable design principles: create a scenario with a clear goal, divide learners into teams, make progress visible, connect each puzzle to a real information task, use time pressure carefully, combine digital and analog clues, and let students discover procedures through play. It also shows how librarian facilitation can maintain the fiction without overexplaining the learning objective.

The limitations are substantial. Because the column is a narrative sketch, the reader cannot know whether the described game was fully implemented, how students performed, what learning outcomes were measured, whether the activity improved search skills, or how much staff time and equipment were required. Its place in the literature review should therefore be illustrative rather than evidentiary. It belongs alongside stronger studies of library escape rooms and game-based information literacy as a practitioner example of what a gamified database lesson might look and feel like.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**
