Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Abrams and Van Eck (2021) paragraph.

Suggested text: Seay (2019) offers an illustrative, fictionalized design vignette for gamified database instruction: students race in teams to prevent a nuclear-core meltdown by solving Web of Science search problems, interpreting article clues, opening a locked box, and combining codes through a visible progress system. The column is not assessment evidence, but it usefully shows how library instruction can be designed as a scenario with stakes, props, feedback, collaboration, and information tasks embedded in play.

# **Seay, J. A. (2019). Considering games in libraries and such: Space guardians: New Year's musings of how libraries of old (including but not necessarily old librarians) have returned just in time to save us from digital anxiety. *Against the Grain, 31*(1), 51.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `APGAWNSY`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesLibraries2021a`; attachment key `CB5N9ENZ`.

Seay's "Space Guardians" column is a reflective practitioner essay about libraries, analog media, board games, and digital anxiety. The Zotero metadata dates the item to 2021, but the attached PDF itself appears in the February 2019 issue of *Against the Grain*. The piece is not a research article or program report; it is a short philosophical column. Its usefulness for the GBLS project lies in the way it articulates one rationale for analog game services: board games and other tactile, co-present practices can function as a response to digital overload and a renewed need for shared physical spaces.

The column begins with Seay describing the experience of handling print reference books while weeding a reference collection. This becomes a meditation on what libraries gain and lose when reference use moves online. Online sources are faster and more searchable, but Seay emphasizes the sensory and contemplative qualities of browsing, touching books, turning pages, and moving through physical collections. That nostalgia is not the central GBLS point, but it prepares the larger argument: libraries remain important because they are not only access systems but also places where bodies, materials, attention, and people meet.

The gaming thread enters through Seay's holiday listening to a board game review podcast. He describes the idea of cross-table, face-to-face interaction, wooden blocks, dice, and tactile play as soothing during a break from digital media. The column connects this feeling to Seay's recurring argument that the board game resurgence reflects a broader longing for analog interaction. Board games are presented not simply as entertainment objects, but as media that restore slower, social, tactile, and embodied forms of attention.

Seay then draws on Douglas Rushkoff's *Present Shock* and the idea of "digiphrenia," or digitally provoked mental chaos. In Seay's reading, the resurgence of board games, vinyl records, analog practices, and physical library gathering spaces responds to information overload, constant digital immediacy, and the dissonance between digital selves and analog bodies. For GBLS, this is useful as practitioner language for why analog games have value even in technologically sophisticated library environments.

The column's library argument is that libraries have a long history as safe, contemplative, public, physical spaces. Seay suggests that coffee shops and board game cafes may be responding to social longing for analog activity, but libraries have a much deeper institutional history of offering gathering places for attention, reading, conversation, and shared cultural materials. Board game collections and makerspaces become part of this renewed analog mission. In a playful phrase, Seay imagines librarians as "guardians of analog."

For the GBLS review, this source supports the conceptual terrain rather than a program model. It helps explain why analog game services should not be treated as technologically backward. A board game collection, tabletop club, or game night may meet needs for attention, social connection, tactile engagement, and relief from digital saturation. It also strengthens the community-space argument: games can help libraries function as places where patrons gather around shared materials and interact face-to-face.

The limitations are clear. The piece is anecdotal, nostalgic, and speculative. It does not assess a board game program, survey patrons, or analyze library outcomes. It should therefore be used as a light practitioner framing source, not as evidence that analog games reduce anxiety or improve well-being. Its value is rhetorical and conceptual: it connects analog play, library space, and community presence in a way that complements more concrete sources on board game collections, recurring play groups, and library-as-commons services.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Analog, Digital, and Hybrid Forms`, after the Seay (2018) analog/co-present paragraph.

Suggested text: Seay's later "Space Guardians" column adds a complementary practitioner rationale for analog game services: board games, books, makerspaces, and physical library spaces can answer a longing for tactile, face-to-face, slower social interaction amid digital overload. The essay is speculative rather than evidentiary, but it helps explain why analog GBLS formats may be valuable precisely because they gather bodies, materials, attention, and conversation in shared public space.

# **Seay, J. A. (2018). Considering games and gamification in libraries & associated entities: How the longing for tabletops has revitalized games. *Against the Grain, 30*(3), 64-67.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `WVJ69C9H`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesGamification2018c`; attachment key `IUT5BRF7`.

Seay's "How the Longing for Tabletops has Revitalized Games" is a practitioner essay about the resurgence of tabletop and analog games in a highly digital culture. The article is not research evidence, but it gives the GBLS review useful language for why board games, card games, escape rooms, LARP, and megagames should be treated as significant library formats rather than nostalgic curiosities. Seay frames the board game renaissance as a response to high-tech life and as a source of face-to-face community.

The column begins with an anecdote from a library classroom in which Seay discovered that many students had never played a board game. That moment becomes a contrast with the later resurgence of tabletop gaming: people gather in pubs, public libraries, board game cafes, hobby shops, and International Tabletop Day events to play games with boards, cards, pieces, and other tangible materials. Seay highlights board game cafes as social spaces where patrons value community and a chance to put down smartphones.

The main conceptual frame is John Naisbitt's "high tech high touch" idea. Seay argues that as communication becomes increasingly mediated by screens, email, chat, text, and social media, people may long for real human contact, tactile materials, synchronous conversation, and nonverbal cues. Tabletop games meet that longing because they gather people around a table in real time and make interaction visible, embodied, and social. The point for GBLS is not anti-technology; rather, analog play can answer needs that digital systems often intensify.

Seay also connects tabletop games to broader market and cultural shifts. He notes the growth of the hobby game market, the rise of board game versions of video games, and the reverse trend of digital versions of board games. This matters because it suggests that analog and digital game cultures are intertwined rather than separate. Libraries that collect or program tabletop games are engaging a live, evolving cultural field, not simply preserving an older form.

The column's library implications are broad. If board game cafes thrive by offering community, tables, game access, and social play, libraries are already well-positioned to support similar needs through public space, collections, programming, and facilitation. Seay also argues that libraries' interest in escape rooms may be only the beginning of a wider turn toward immersive group learning formats such as LARP and megagames. These formats extend the high-touch logic of tabletop games into larger interactive learning environments.

For the GBLS project, this source helps articulate why analog games remain central even in a literature review that includes digital games, VR, and gamification. Analog games can support social presence, communication, community-building, low-tech access, tactile engagement, and collaborative learning. The column pairs well with more concrete sources such as Copeland et al. (2013), Forsythe (2021), Robson et al. (2018), and Seay's later analog-space columns.

Its limitations are important. The article is playful, anecdotal, and speculative, with no systematic data about library patrons or program outcomes. It should therefore be used as practitioner discourse and conceptual framing rather than evidence that tabletop programs produce particular learning or social outcomes. Its best use is to support the review's claim that libraries should match game format to purpose: analog tabletop formats are especially strong where co-presence, social connection, and material interaction are desired.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Analog, Digital, and Hybrid Forms`, after the `Space Guardians` paragraph.

Suggested text: Seay's tabletop-resurgence column similarly frames board and card games as "high touch" responses to high-tech life. As a practitioner essay, it is not outcome evidence, but it helps name the service affordances of tabletop GBLS: face-to-face communication, nonverbal cues, tactile pieces, shared tables, low-tech access, and social community, with escape rooms, LARP, and megagames appearing as extensions of the same analog/immersive impulse.

# **Seay, J. A. (2019). Considering games in libraries and such: On using a game to actually teach something. Part I: The accidental learner. *Against the Grain, 31*(5), 72-80.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `2JMNMMRA`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesLibraries2021`; attachment key `43AWQDDF`.

Seay's "The Accidental Learner" is a reflective professional column about designing an escape-room-like learning game. The Zotero metadata dates the item to 2021, but the PDF appears in the November 2019 issue of *Against the Grain*. The article is not a formal study, but it is useful for GBLS because it addresses a practical question that often sits underneath game-based library services: does using a game to teach actually work, and how does a designer turn a traditional lesson into a playable learning experience?

Seay frames the column through his own uncertainty after receiving a small teaching grant to create escape rooms for teaching. He describes the moment when game-based pedagogy becomes real implementation: ideas that sound persuasive in theory must be turned into practical mechanics, themes, content, puzzles, and experiences. This makes the column useful as practitioner reflection on design labor. Game-based instruction is not just "make it fun"; it requires decisions about whether theme or content leads, how mechanics support learning, and how fun and instruction can be integrated.

The column's main example is an escape game created for a science summer camp with the theme "Mars and Stars." Because the activity was framed as a fun elective, Seay felt less pressure to prove learning outcomes, but he still designed it as more than mindless fun. The scenario became a crash-landing-on-Mars survival game. Participants were science campers aged roughly 11 to 17. The design used theater, theme, role-play, puzzles, cartography skills, "deep thinking," colorful lights, and the survival premise to create engagement.

The strongest design insight is Seay's distinction between content and experience. He argues that content may be central, but experience is what hooks participants and stays with them. Before designers can reach learners' minds, they often have to capture attention and emotion. In the Mars game, participants reportedly had fun and learned about the science, necessities, danger, and adventure involved in planning and executing a Mars mission. They also practiced cooperation and problem solving under pressure. For the GBLS project, this language is useful because it connects affect, memory, collaboration, and problem solving to game-based learning design.

The column also introduces the idea of accidental learning. Seay suggests that the game did not make learning fun by turning every fact into entertainment; rather, participants learned while having fun inside the larger experience. This distinction matters for library instruction. A good GBLS design may embed catalog searching, database use, source evaluation, or space navigation inside a meaningful scenario so that the learning is necessary to progress, rather than treating the learning content as a quiz pasted onto a theme.

For the GBLS review, this source pairs with Seay's "Ultimate Library Instruction" vignette and with stronger empirical studies of escape rooms. It provides a practitioner account of the transition from game enthusiasm to design practice and helps explain why reflection and transfer must be designed into game-based instruction. It also supports the idea that libraries may need prototypes, playtesting, and cross-domain examples before they can build effective library-specific games.

The limitations are substantial. The column does not include assessment data, sample size details beyond a broad age range, learning measures, or a replicable lesson plan. The Mars game was not a library program, though Seay explicitly frames it as preparation for library-skills instruction. The source should therefore be used as design reflection rather than evidence of effectiveness. Its main contribution is vocabulary for the craft problem of making learning playable without reducing either the learning or the play to decoration.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Escape Rooms and Breakout Boxes`, after the Wedlake et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Seay (2019) adds practitioner design reflection to the escape-room literature by asking how a game can actually teach something rather than merely decorate instruction. His Mars survival game is not a library assessment study, but it usefully distinguishes content from experience: participants may learn science, cooperation, and problem solving because those tasks are embedded in role-play, puzzles, pressure, and narrative stakes, suggesting that library escape rooms need designed transfer rather than theme alone.

# **Seay, J. A. (2019). Considering games in libraries and such: A liminal response to watching paint dry. *Against the Grain, 31*(2), 37.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `LHF32QZC`; Better BibTeX citation key `seayConsideringGamesLibraries2019a`; attachment key `CKAFVP7D`.

Seay's "A Liminal Response to Watching Paint Dry" is a short practitioner essay about library instruction, student disengagement, anxiety, and the potential of immersive game environments. The column begins with a familiar library instruction problem: students arrive expecting a dry database or library-resources session, and the librarian faces blank expressions, low energy, and little visible feedback. Seay describes this not as student incapacity but as a problem of engagement, perceived relevance, and anxiety.

The article's most useful move is to connect library instruction to Mark Carnes's work on Reacting to the Past role-immersion games. Seay summarizes Carnes's reflection on a failed seminar and the discovery that students' "sophisticated disinterest" could mask fear of appearing ignorant in front of an instructor or peers. This is directly relevant to library instruction because students may enter a one-shot session with similar insecurity: they need the knowledge, but they may also feel exposed by not knowing how to search databases, navigate resources, or ask questions.

Seay uses Carnes's response to frame game-based learning as more than entertainment. If students are anxious about displaying ignorance under ordinary classroom rules, role-immersion games can move them into an alternative identity and a different social space. Instead of simply being told knowledge, students are put into a world where they need to use the subject matter in a meaningful context. This is the familiar idea of learning by doing, but Seay emphasizes that it is engaged doing within a designed environment.

The key term is "liminal." Seay describes Carnes's game environments as threshold spaces where ordinary rules are suspended or subverted, status and social hierarchies can be inverted, uncertainty and emotional intensity rise, and imaginative expression becomes possible. For GBLS, this is a useful conceptual frame for why role-play, escape rooms, and scenario-based instruction can feel different from ordinary library teaching. The game creates a boundary-crossing space where students can act, test, collaborate, make mistakes, and engage with difficult material under new rules.

Seay then applies this logic back to library instruction. If Reacting to the Past can transform students' relationship to history texts and seminar participation, escape-room-like or role-immersive library instruction might transform students' relationship to databases, catalogs, sources, and research processes. The column ends with a playful imagined "Get out of Library Hell" escape room, but the underlying point is serious: even a one-shot instruction class can be redesigned as a more engaging learning environment.

For the GBLS review, this source supports a theoretical and practitioner bridge between TTRPG/LARP programming, escape rooms, and information literacy instruction. It helps explain why games may be useful not only because they are fun, but because they change the social and emotional conditions of learning. Students can engage under a role, within a scenario, and around a shared challenge rather than as isolated novices being evaluated by experts.

The limitations are again clear. The article does not present a library implementation or assessment data, and its claims rely on Carnes's role-immersion work plus Seay's practitioner reflection. It should not be used as proof of learning outcomes. Its contribution is a design concept: game-based library instruction can create liminal learning spaces that reduce the stiffness of ordinary instruction and make research tasks part of purposeful action.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Seay (2019) nuclear-core vignette paragraph.

Suggested text: Seay's "A Liminal Response to Watching Paint Dry" gives a related explanation for why game-based instruction may help with disengagement: role-immersion and escape-room formats can create liminal spaces where ordinary classroom hierarchies and anxieties are temporarily suspended. Drawing on Mark Carnes's Reacting to the Past work, Seay argues that learners may participate more fully when they enter a scenario, assume roles, and use knowledge inside a meaningful problem space rather than being asked to display competence in a conventional lecture setting.

# **Downie, S., & Proulx, S. (2022). Investigating the role of gamification in public libraries' literacy-centered youth programming. *International Journal of Play, 11*(4), 382-404. https://doi.org/10.1080/21594937.2022.2136637**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `AARZCMQI`; Better BibTeX citation key `downieInvestigatingRoleGamification2022`; attachment key `EMV6ERFX`.

Downie and Proulx investigate how gamification appears in public libraries' literacy-centered programming for children. The article begins from the observation that public libraries have long supported early literacy through storytime, summer reading, outreach, and children's spaces, but that the professional literature has paid relatively little attention to how gameful design may already be shaping these services. Their central question is not whether libraries are explicitly naming their work "gamification." Instead, they ask whether the public presentation of youth literacy initiatives shows design patterns associated with gamification and with self-determination theory.

The theoretical frame is useful for GBLS because it keeps gamification distinct from simply playing games. Downie and Proulx describe gamification as the application of game elements to non-game contexts in order to increase motivation, engagement, experimentation, exploration, or social participation. They emphasize that gamification works through mechanics, dynamics, emotional responses, feedback, goals, recognition, rewards, and users' sense of meaningful progress. They pair this with self-determination theory, especially autonomy, competence, and social relatedness. That pairing lets the article discuss points, badges, rewards, physical spaces, and playful storytimes without reducing motivation to external prizes.

The method is a public-facing online survey of 16 prominent U.S. public libraries. Because there is no official list of the "best" children's libraries, the authors combined Library Journal Star Library rankings with popular online lists and blog-style recommendations of notable children's library spaces. They then examined websites, social media posts, and other online materials during the COVID-19 closure period to identify literacy-related in-person and virtual offerings. The study therefore analyzes how libraries presented their youth literacy services to children, guardians, and the public; it does not observe programs directly, interview librarians, or measure children's literacy outcomes.

The survey found several common strategies. Every library offered some form of storytime and summer reading challenge, and 81 percent offered virtual storytimes. Half used Beanstack for summer reading, 81 percent designated prizes based on completed reading minutes, and half offered activities that went beyond basic logs to include community exploration or STEAM activities. Most libraries provided children's website sections, and many had substantial children's physical spaces with specialized rooms, materials, technology, or programs. Book kits, workshops, outreach, tutor-oriented programs, reading buddies, and recommended third-party digital resources also appeared.

The discussion is organized around summer reading programs, storytime, and physical spaces. In summer reading, Beanstack is treated as a clear example of gameful infrastructure: it provides progress displays, checklists, activity categories, badges, points, immediate audio/visual feedback, and final rewards. Downie and Proulx read these features ambivalently. Badges and rewards can support goal-setting, feedback, self-efficacy, and habits of reflection when they are meaningful and aligned with users' values, but they can also undermine motivation if perceived as controlling. They note that the Beanstack examples were relatively strong on autonomy and competence, but comparatively weak on social relatedness because they lacked peer comparison, sharing, or discussion features.

Storytime is analyzed as gamified play rather than as passive listening. Modern library storytimes, in their account, are dialogic, embodied, playful, scaffolded, and socially shared. Librarians pause for questions, encourage children to imagine or extend stories, integrate rhymes, music, dance, puppets, pantomime, finger art, yoga, therapy dogs, sensory-aware formats, and multilingual or age-specific sessions. The authors connect these features to autonomy, competence, social belonging, early literacy, identity experimentation, and children's ability to communicate meaning through movement and creative performance before they can fully articulate ideas in speech or writing.

The analysis of physical spaces extends gamification beyond screens and badges. Children's library spaces such as learning gardens, technology rooms, oversized books, rainforest domes, playful book returns, mascots, and hands-on installations are read through autonomy, play, affinity, and space. Downie and Proulx argue that well-designed children's spaces invite exploration, self-directed interaction, and imaginative meaning-making while still providing enough guidance to focus attention. Laramie County's "learning spots," for example, use mascot-marked plaques to explain activities, name their benefits, pose reflection questions, and recommend related books. The point is not that the library becomes a videogame, but that spatial design can create goals, feedback, exploration, and meaningful choice.

For the GBLS project, this article is a strong bridge between conceptual gamification and everyday public-library youth service. It shows that game-based library service does not always announce itself as a game night, videogame tournament, escape room, or TTRPG club. It can also appear in summer reading platforms, storytime facilitation, children's room design, book kits, outreach, and literacy challenges. The article also offers a useful caution for assessment: because it studies public websites and social media, it provides evidence of design patterns and professional framing rather than evidence of literacy gains or patron experience. That limitation makes it valuable for the review's distinction between service design, professional discourse, and demonstrated outcomes.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Games, Gamification, and Play`, after the Seay (2018) "G Word" gamification paragraph.

Suggested text: Downie and Proulx (2022) provide a stronger empirical and analytic grounding for gamification in youth library services by examining public-facing websites and social media from 16 prominent U.S. public libraries. They find that summer reading clubs, storytimes, and children's spaces often use points, badges, progress tracking, authentic rewards, dialogic play, movement, sensory and imaginative activity, mascots, learning spots, and exploratory environments to support autonomy, competence, and social relatedness; because the study analyzes public messaging rather than patron outcomes, it is best read as evidence of design patterns and professional framing rather than proof of literacy gains.

# **Moid, A., Raza, M. M., Javed, M., & Jahan, K. (2023). Role of digital humanities in libraries: Mapping gamification gimmicks. *DESIDOC Journal of Library & Information Technology, 43*(4), 249-257. https://doi.org/10.14429/djlit.43.4.19233**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `X84679ZZ`; Better BibTeX citation key `moidRoleDigitalHumanities2023`; attachment key `U8PD24GD`.

Moid, Raza, Javed, and Jahan provide a bibliometric overview of gamification research, with a smaller discussion of its relevance to libraries and digital humanities. The article defines gamification in familiar terms as the incorporation of game mechanics, game thinking, feedback, goals, badges, point systems, leaderboards, and user levels into non-game activities. Its library claim is broad: gamification can attract users' attention, engage them in game-like library activities, influence user behavior, and make learning more engaging. The article also links gamification to digital humanities tools and to gamified e-library services, though the digital humanities connection remains more contextual than deeply analyzed.

The study uses Web of Science data from 2013 through February 25, 2023. The authors searched the topic field for "Gamification*" and, after applying an English-language filter, analyzed 4,778 records with R, RStudio, Bibliometrix, and Biblioshiny. Their indicators include relevant sources, authors, globally cited documents, affiliations, country scientific production, three-field plots, word growth, keyword plus, word clouds, and co-occurrence networks. In other words, the article maps the research field around gamification rather than studying a specific library program, user group, or learning outcome.

The broad bibliometric findings show a rapidly expanded research area. The dataset includes 1,564 sources, 13,861 authors, and more than 136,000 references. The highest annual record count was in 2021, followed closely by 2022, while 2013 had the smallest count. The most productive sources are outside librarianship, with journals such as *Sustainability*, *Computers in Human Behavior*, *JMIR Serious Games*, and *Frontiers in Psychology* prominent in the results. Hamari appears as the most productive author, and the most globally cited documents include work by Seaborn, Dominguez, Hanus, and Koivisto. The United States, Spain, and China lead in country production, while the University of Pennsylvania, University of Granada, and other large research institutions appear among the most relevant affiliations.

For the LIS slice, Moid et al. report approximately 200 documents related to library and information science within the broader gamification dataset. They identify an increasing publication pattern since 2013, with a peak around 2017 in their LIS subset, and note sources such as the *International Journal of Information Management*, *Information Technology & People*, *European Journal of Information Systems*, and *Information & Management*. The article concludes that gamification became especially visible during the pandemic period and that tools such as interactive quizzes, digital badges, and virtual escape rooms can help attract patrons and popularize information products.

The source's usefulness for GBLS is mostly contextual. It supports the claim that gamification is a large interdisciplinary research area rather than a niche library fad, and it shows that LIS scholarship has engaged the term, though on a much smaller scale than education, psychology, business, health, and information systems. It also names common library-facing examples: orientation, websites, databases, education, virtual escape rooms, coding clubs, badges, quizzes, and interactive storytelling. These examples align with other sources in the review that treat gamification as a design logic for engagement, progress, motivation, and instruction.

At the same time, the article should be used cautiously. It sometimes treats gamification, game-based learning, digital humanities, and gamified e-library services as overlapping concepts without fully separating them. Its statements about libraries are largely drawn from prior literature and broad claims rather than from new empirical evidence about library patrons or programs. The bibliometric search is also broad enough that most of the analyzed records are not library-specific. For GBLS, then, Moid et al. are best used as a field-mapping source: evidence that gamification discourse has grown, that LIS is participating in that discourse, and that library-oriented gamification needs more precise definitions and stronger program-level assessment.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Games, Gamification, and Play`, after the Downie and Proulx (2022) paragraph.

Suggested text: Moid et al. (2023) add a bibliometric backdrop for this definitional problem: their Web of Science analysis of 4,778 English-language gamification records from 2013 to early 2023 found rapid growth across many fields and roughly 200 records in library and information science. The article is too broad to serve as evidence of GBLS outcomes, and it sometimes blurs gamification, game-based learning, digital humanities, and e-library services, but it usefully shows that library gamification sits inside a much larger interdisciplinary discourse where precise definitions and local assessment are still needed.

# **Helbing, R. R., Lapka, S., Richdale, K., & Hatfield, C. L. (2022). In-person and online escape rooms for individual and team-based learning in health professions library instruction. *Journal of the Medical Library Association, 110*(4), 507-512. https://doi.org/10.5195/jmla.2022.1463**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `NT3EMT7Q`; Better BibTeX citation key `helbingInpersonOnlineEscape`; attachment key `H6FLIHEI`.

Helbing, Lapka, Richdale, and Hatfield present a case report from the University of Houston Health Sciences Libraries on adapting escape rooms for health professions library instruction. The article begins from a familiar instructional problem: health professions students need to locate, evaluate, and use scholarly literature, but library instruction is often delivered in short orientations or one-shot lectures that leave little time for application. The authors frame game-based learning as a way to let students practice library skills in a low-risk, active learning environment where problem solving, planning, teamwork, critical thinking, and attention to detail are required for progress.

The case report describes escape rooms developed for optometry, pharmacy, and medicine students between 2019 and 2021. Across these iterations, the library collaborated with faculty to tailor activities to course and program needs. Each scenario was framed as a research proposal rather than as a purely decorative puzzle theme. For example, optometry students were asked to investigate the feasibility of a new vision screening program for underserved children in Houston and had to find evidence through library resources rather than relying on Google. This framing made library skill development feel connected to professional practice, grant support, patient care, and evidence-based decision making.

The in-person team-based optometry version took place after a preparatory lecture. Teams of five or six students had 45 minutes, one computer, and no phones or tablets. They used the library website, PubMed, Journal Citation Reports, paper clues, Google Forms, Google Docs, and the physical library space to solve sequential tasks. Objectives included locating an optometry resources guide, identifying empirical articles, describing peer review, finding a journal impact factor, applying PICO to a database search, obtaining full text through PubMed, setting up ILLiad, and knowing whom to ask for help. Facilitators included librarians and faculty, hints carried a small time penalty, and successful teams unlocked a physical lock box.

The pharmacy and medical iterations show how the same game structure can be adapted across settings. Pharmacy students completed an on-site virtual individual escape room in a lecture hall, using laptops, Google Forms, Google Docs, PubMed, the MeSH Database, and PubMed Search Builder. Medicine students completed a fully remote individual escape room during online orientation, with a brief lecture and demonstration followed by independent online puzzle work, direct-message hints, a virtual lock box, and a short debriefing. A later optometry version also moved fully online. The formats therefore ranged from in-person/team to hybrid/individual to fully online/individual.

For GBLS, the most useful contribution is the implementation detail. Helbing et al. identify several design decisions libraries must make: whether the activity should be team-based or individual, how much time and money can be spent, whether the setting should be physical, hybrid, or online, and whether grades or points should be attached. Team-based formats support communication and collaboration, while individual formats better demonstrate individual proficiency. Initial planning took roughly 12 hours, but later adaptations required about three to five hours. Online tools such as forms, documents, and QR codes can keep costs low, while physical rooms may feel more active and help students learn the library space.

The article is also helpful on grading and accessibility. Points can signal that the activity matters and encourage full participation, but they can shift attention toward fairness, cheating, hint penalties, or rank rather than toward skill development. Completion-based grading created a lower-pressure environment in the pharmacy version. The authors also caution that escape-room clues should avoid unnecessary barriers for learners for whom English is not a first language; spelling, grammar, idioms, puns, and expressions can create friction unrelated to the learning objectives. Team-based formats may help distribute those burdens.

The evidence is modest. The authors report that embedded formative assessments showed students completed the tasks within the allotted time, and informal observation and anecdotal feedback suggested enjoyment and engagement. They do not provide systematic survey data, comparison groups, long-term retention measures, or formal assessment of information literacy gains. Still, the case report is valuable for GBLS because it demonstrates how escape-room design can be integrated into real health sciences library instruction, adapted across modalities, and aligned with concrete research and resource-use objectives rather than simply added as a novelty.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Escape Rooms and Breakout Boxes`, after the Seay (2019) Mars survival/design-reflection paragraph.

Suggested text: Helbing et al. (2022) add a concrete health sciences library implementation model, describing optometry, pharmacy, and medicine escape rooms delivered in in-person team, hybrid individual, fully online individual, and later online optometry formats. Their case report ties puzzles to PubMed, PICO, MeSH, full-text access, resource guides, ILLiad, journal impact factors, and help-seeking, and its main contribution is practical design guidance: begin with learning objectives, work backward into clues, test for ambiguity, plan for early finishers, consider team versus individual formats, keep costs low with common tools, and decide carefully whether grading will support or distort learning.

# **Farkas, B., Shang, Y., & Alhourani, F. (2022). Teaching tip: Teaching business process concepts in an introductory information systems class: A multi-level game-based learning approach. *Journal of Information Systems Education, 33*(4), 306-323.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `MR2KV4NF`; Better BibTeX citation key `farkasTeachingTipTeaching2022`; attachment key `D7WITCAN`.

Farkas, Shang, and Alhourani describe a multi-level game-based learning approach for teaching business process concepts in an introductory information systems course. The article is grounded in information systems and business education rather than library service. Its learning goals are for students to define business process concepts, explain a business process to stakeholders, and describe the role of information systems, especially ERP systems, in business processes. The authors argue that conventional lecture, textbook, quiz, and LMS simulation approaches are too abstract and too isolated from the organizational contexts in which information systems operate.

The teaching method uses a sequence of three increasingly complex game-based exercises. Students first inspect bags of M&M candies to learn basic business process modeling concepts such as start, end, activity, and gateway. They then complete a role-based card-sorting and product-manufacturing activity to learn pools, lanes, roles, responsibility, extensibility, reuse, and cross-role dependencies. Finally, student teams operate a simulated company using an SAP ERP simulation, then diagram the underlying business process and the role of the information system in that process. The pedagogy is intentionally scaffolded: early games use familiar everyday activities and stronger instructor guidance, while later exercises increase complexity and reduce guidance.

The article provides some evidence of perceived effectiveness. Across two classes totaling 86 students, 63 students responded to an eleven-question seven-point Likert survey. The authors also discuss end-of-semester comments and their own observations. Students reported that the activities were fun, collaborative, hands-on, and helpful for understanding business process diagrams. The authors found moderate to strong correlations among several survey items about learning and studying, but they also acknowledge class-level differences, self-selection bias, possible delivery differences, pandemic-related disruption, and the absence of controls or comparison with other pedagogical methods.

For GBLS, this source is best treated as excluded rather than incorporated into the review. It does use game-based learning, scaffolding, role play, simulation, collaboration, and reflection, all of which are relevant to educational game design in a broad sense. However, the article is not about libraries, library instruction, library users, library collections, cultural heritage, game collections, information literacy, public programming, school libraries, public libraries, or academic library services. Its core domain is business process modeling in a business-school information systems course.

Because the GBLS review already includes library-specific and near-library escape room, instruction, VR, TTRPG, metadata, collection, and programming sources, adding Farkas et al. would dilute the scope without adding necessary library evidence. The article may remain useful as a background teaching-design reference if the project later needs a broad appendix on game-based learning pedagogy, but it does not warrant placement in the main GBLS literature review.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No suggested addition. Explicitly exclude from the GBLS literature review because the article studies game-based learning in a business information systems course, not game-based library services or library-adjacent cultural/information work.

# **Lan, Y.-J., Botha, A., Shang, J., & Jong, M. S.-Y. (2018). Guest editorial: Technology enhanced contextual game-based language learning. *Educational Technology & Society, 21*(3), 86-89.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `ZR7WBBYF`; Better BibTeX citation key `lanGuestEditorialTechnology2018`; attachment key `WR73956X`.

Lan, Botha, Shang, and Jong introduce a special issue on technology-enhanced contextual game-based language learning. The editorial argues that games can support second-language learning when they require language input and output across linguistic form, semantic meaning, and pragmatic use. It situates game-based language learning within contextual, experiential, and sociocultural learning traditions, emphasizing authentic environments, meaningful social interaction, scaffolding, and low-stress opportunities to practice a second language.

The editorial's main function is to frame the special issue rather than report an original study. It describes how technology can extend language-learning contexts beyond conventional classrooms through computer-mediated communication, mobile devices, augmented reality, and 3D virtual environments. It then summarizes ten accepted papers on mobile language games, gamified interactive response systems, flipped contextual game-based learning, augmented paper interfaces for Chinese character learning, competitive vocabulary games, Kinect-supported English learning, MMORPG-based English role play, a contextual game-based system called PlanetAdventure, game translation as fandom literacy, and 3D virtual environments for special education students with language delay.

The source is relevant to game-based learning broadly, especially where a later project might need background on digital games, virtual worlds, contextual learning, language learning, or transfer between formal learning and real-life application. It also shows how game-based learning research often examines motivation, anxiety, self-efficacy, learning performance, learner behavior, and design principles across specific educational domains.

For the GBLS literature review, however, the source should be explicitly excluded. It does not discuss libraries, librarians, library instruction, information literacy, library collections, public programming, cultural heritage, access to games, game advisory, metadata, or any library service context. Although language learning and literacy can matter to libraries, this editorial is centered on classroom and educational technology research rather than game-based library services. Including it would broaden the review from GBLS into general educational game-based learning without adding library-specific evidence.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No suggested addition. Explicitly exclude from the GBLS literature review because the editorial concerns technology-enhanced contextual game-based language learning, not library services or library-adjacent information/cultural work.

# **Welhouse, Z., Lee, J. H., & Bancroft, J. (2015). "What am I fighting for?": Creating a controlled vocabulary for video game plot metadata. *Cataloging & Classification Quarterly, 53*(2), 157-189. https://doi.org/10.1080/01639374.2014.963776**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `DTE3PY5T`; Better BibTeX citation key `welhouseWhatAmFighting2015`; attachment key `XBQB9RQU`.

Welhouse, Lee, and Bancroft develop a controlled vocabulary for video game plot metadata as part of the University of Washington and Seattle Interactive Media Museum video game metadata schema work. The article begins from a cataloging problem that is directly relevant to libraries: video games are cultural and research objects, but existing organizational systems often describe them through platform, title, publisher, price, and broad genre while giving little reliable access to what a game is "really about." Commercial sites, fan summaries, Steam tags, WorldCat summaries, and Library of Congress Subject Headings may supply useful fragments, but the authors argue that none provide consistent, standardized, plot-level access for discovery, browsing, collocation, advisory, or scholarship.

The article is grounded in user warrant. Earlier GAMER/SIMM work identified personas including game players, parents, collectors, scholars, developers, librarians/curators, teachers, and industry professionals, and this article uses that broader research to justify plot/narrative as a metadata field. In interviews with 56 users, 21 mentioned story, plot, or narrative as important, often as one of their top factors, while also noting that there was no effective way to search games by it. A later survey of 1,257 participants ranked "Plot/Narrative" as the seventh most useful metadata element, with positive responses from 71 percent of participants. For libraries, this user evidence matters because plot access could support readers' advisory-like game advisory, parent mediation, curricular matching, thematic displays, exhibits, and research into game stories over time.

The method combines domain analysis and card sorting. The authors collected candidate terms from RPGs, instruction books, in-game text, package descriptions, fan-created descriptions, game websites, wikis, encyclopedias, and other game-related sources. They then reduced and organized a large term list using criteria of accuracy, understandability, and comprehensiveness. The article also reviews literary plot classification systems, including appeal elements, folktale motifs, dramatic situations, and "basic plots," but finds that these systems are often too broad, too fine-grained, too literary, or too difficult for catalogers to apply consistently to games.

Their main design contribution is a pair structure inspired by a faceted approach. Each plot statement combines a controlled VERB and OBJECT, with an implied SUBJECT of the player-character or characters. Examples include seeking treasure, escaping a dystopia, restoring order, developing friendship, confronting self, preventing disaster, discovering intrigue, and saving the world. Limiting records to three plot pairs discourages cataloging every side quest while pushing catalogers beyond a single generic statement such as "characters save world." In the sample records, the structure allows games within the same genre, especially RPGs, to be collocated by plot elements that would otherwise be hidden by broad genre labels.

For GBLS, the article strengthens the review's cataloging and discovery thread. It shows that game collections need metadata that supports how patrons actually seek, compare, and interpret games, not only how vendors package them. Plot metadata can help a librarian connect a game to novels, films, nonfiction, class topics, or patron interests; it can help a teacher pair a game with literary themes; and it can help researchers ask cultural questions about recurring stories in games. In this sense, game-based library service includes not only events and play programs but also the infrastructure that makes games findable, recommendable, and intellectually legible.

The limitations are also important for the GBLS review. Plot metadata introduces hard cataloging questions about granularity, optional side quests, nonlinear narratives, moral choices, games with thin or no plot, and the amount of game knowledge required from catalogers. The article also names practical concerns around spoilers, the need for free-text summary fields, and possible crowdsourced description to supplement professional cataloging. These problems are useful because they show that game metadata is not a simple matter of adding more tags; it requires local policy, interface choices, user testing, domain expertise, and perhaps collaboration with game communities.

Overall, Welhouse et al. are best used as foundational evidence for video game cataloging, metadata, and advisory rather than as a program outcome study. The article does not evaluate a library service intervention, but it gives the GBLS project a concrete vocabulary for why discovery systems matter: richer game metadata can turn video games from loosely described entertainment objects into searchable cultural materials that support access, interpretation, curriculum, preservation, and library-mediated recommendation.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the section heading and before the Lee, Schmalz, Keating, and Ha (2020) paragraph.

Suggested text: Welhouse, Lee, and Bancroft (2015) offer an earlier game-metadata foundation by treating video game plot/narrative as a user-warranted access point rather than a decorative summary. Building from personas, interviews with 56 users, a 1,257-participant survey, domain analysis, and card sorting, they argue that existing catalogs, genre labels, commercial descriptions, and fan summaries do not reliably describe what games are really about. Their VERB-OBJECT controlled vocabulary supports collocation across plot elements and can complement genre, theme, setting, mood, and visual style; for GBLS, this matters for game advisory, thematic displays, curricular matching, and culturally serious discovery, while also surfacing practical metadata problems around granularity, spoilers, cataloger expertise, free-text summaries, and crowdsourced description.

# **Ninaus, M., Kiili, K., Cortez, R., Hazin, I., Wortha, S. M., Klein, E., Weiss, E. M., & Moeller, K. (2023). The added value of game elements: Better training performance but comparable learning gains. *Educational Technology Research and Development*. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11423-023-10263-8**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `A95N9V7K`; Better BibTeX citation key `ninausAddedValueGame2023`; attachment key `KTIE5AFQ`.

Ninaus and colleagues test whether intrinsically integrated game elements improve learning in a digital fraction magnitude training task. The study is not library-based, but it is useful for GBLS because it gives empirical support for a caution that recurs across library gamification: game elements can change engagement and in-task behavior without necessarily improving durable learning outcomes. The authors situate their work between two competing claims. On one hand, game-based learning may increase cognitive engagement, effort, motivation, generative processing, and persistence. On the other hand, added game elements can become extraneous cognitive load or "seductive details" if they distract from the instructional goal.

The study uses a pre-post randomized training design with 85 adult participants. Participants trained fraction magnitude understanding over five consecutive days, about 15-20 minutes per day, using digital number-line estimation tasks. One group used a game-based version with narrative, visual game aesthetics, an avatar, virtual incentives, feedback, and level structure. The comparison group used a matched non-game version stripped of those game elements but preserving the core number-line estimation task. Learning was measured with paper-based number-line estimation before and after training.

The key finding is deliberately mixed. Both the game-based and non-game-based conditions improved from pretest to posttest, confirming that the number-line training itself supported fraction magnitude learning. However, the two groups did not differ significantly in learning gains. During training, participants in the game-based condition responded more accurately than those in the non-game-based condition, but they also responded more slowly. The authors interpret this pattern as possible evidence that game elements increased cognitive engagement and shifted participants' priorities toward accuracy over speed during the training process.

For GBLS, this distinction between training performance and learning outcome is important. A library escape room, gamified orientation, reading challenge, or badge system may produce better in-session behavior, more careful task completion, more time on task, or stronger visible engagement without automatically producing stronger transfer, retention, information literacy, reading, or wellness outcomes. Ninaus et al. therefore support a review argument that game-based library services need assessment designs that look beyond attendance, completion, enjoyment, and in-game success.

The article is also useful for design vocabulary. The authors emphasize "intrinsic integration," meaning that game mechanics and learning mechanics should be tightly connected rather than pasted together. They also argue that researchers need well-matched control conditions when testing the specific contribution of game elements. Without such controls, positive outcomes may be due to novelty, real-time instruction, the base task, collaboration, or extra time rather than to the game elements themselves.

The limitation for GBLS is scope. This is an adult math-learning experiment, not a study of libraries, librarians, patrons, collections, library instruction, or library programming. It should not be used as evidence that library game services work. Its proper role is adjacent methodological and conceptual support: it helps explain why GBLS assessment should separate engagement, performance during play, transfer, and longer-term outcomes, and why gamification should be evaluated as a specific design intervention rather than assumed to add value by default.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Games, Gamification, and Play`, after the Moid et al. (2023) bibliometric paragraph.

Suggested text: Adjacent experimental work also cautions against assuming that game elements automatically produce learning gains. In a randomized five-day fraction-training study, Ninaus et al. (2023) found that intrinsically integrated game elements improved training accuracy and increased response time, suggesting greater cognitive engagement or a shift toward accuracy over speed, but did not produce significantly larger pre-post learning gains than a matched non-game condition. For GBLS, the lesson is methodological: gamified library services should distinguish engagement and in-session performance from transfer, retention, and other longer-term outcomes.

# **Wang, D.-C., & Huang, Y.-M. (2023). Exploring the influence of competition and collaboration on learning performance in digital game-based learning. *Educational Technology Research and Development, 71*, 1547-1565. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11423-023-10247-8**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `AAVP3UL7`; Better BibTeX citation key `wangExploringInfluenceCompetition2023`; attachment key `S8V9BXLX`.

Wang and Huang examine how competition and collaboration interact in digital game-based learning. The article is not a library study, but it is relevant to GBLS as adjacent design evidence because many library game formats, including tournaments, escape rooms, team challenges, reading competitions, and gamified orientations, combine competitive and collaborative structures. The authors begin from the premise that competition can be a "double-edged sword": it may motivate engagement and improve performance, but it can also create stress, frustration, anxiety, or a focus on winning rather than learning. Collaboration may buffer those negative effects by providing social support, shared goals, discussion, negotiation, and mutual assistance.

The study developed an intergroup competition with intragroup collaboration game for question-bank practice. Students competed in two-person teams while also depending on teammates to complete different parts of the task. The authors built a research model linking perceived competition, perceived collaboration, perceived enjoyment, perceived usefulness, attitude toward using, behavioral intention, and self-reported learning performance. Data came from 108 students in one department in Taiwan, and the analysis used partial least squares structural equation modeling.

The findings emphasize the motivational power of competition within this particular design. Perceived competition had a stronger total effect on learning performance than perceived collaboration. Competition influenced behavioral intention directly and indirectly through perceived enjoyment, while collaboration influenced behavioral intention indirectly through perceived usefulness. Perceived competition also directly affected perceived collaboration, suggesting that intergroup competition can stimulate intragroup collaboration when team members share a common goal and have interdependent roles.

For GBLS, the article's most useful contribution is its design caution. Competition should not be treated as inherently good or bad. It can prompt excitement, acceptance, persistence, and team coordination, but it can also distort attention if the competition mechanism is too complex or if winning is disconnected from the learning or service goal. Wang and Huang argue that game designers should keep competition mechanisms simple and clear, link victory conditions to learning performance, divide roles fairly, and make the educational usefulness of the game explicit rather than relying only on entertainment value.

The limitations matter. Learning performance was self-reported rather than measured with an exam or performance artifact, the sample was drawn from one department in Taiwan, the game was a specific question-bank/Tetris-style system, and the authors explicitly caution that competition may not be more important than collaboration in all digital game-based learning contexts. The study should not be used as evidence that competitive library programs produce learning outcomes. Its proper use in GBLS is conceptual and methodological: it helps libraries think carefully about when competition supports a service goal, when collaboration should be built into the structure, and how assessment should distinguish enjoyment, intention to participate, collaboration, and actual learning or transfer.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Key Game Studies Debates Relevant to Libraries`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Adjacent work on competition and collaboration also matters for GBLS design. Wang and Huang (2023) found that, in a digital question-bank game using intergroup competition and intragroup collaboration, perceived competition was more strongly associated with self-reported learning performance than perceived collaboration, and competition also stimulated collaboration by giving teammates a shared goal. Because the study is not library-based and uses self-reported performance, its value is cautionary rather than evidentiary: library tournaments, escape rooms, reading challenges, and gamified orientations should keep competition mechanisms simple, link winning conditions to the service or learning goal, divide team roles fairly, and assess outcomes beyond enjoyment or intention to participate.

# **Maranchak, M., & Maranchak, N. (2023). Gamification mechanics usage for UGC promotion in online library marketing. *Ukrainian Journal on Library and Information Science*, (11), 21-33. https://doi.org/10.31866/2616-7654.11.2023.282652**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `FDZ94PLN`; Better BibTeX citation key `maranchakGamificationMechanicsUsage2023`; attachment key `TLENEZCZ`.

Maranchak and Maranchak examine how gamified mechanics can stimulate user-generated content (UGC) in the internet marketing of libraries. The article is directly relevant to GBLS because it extends gamification beyond instruction, escape rooms, orientations, and reading challenges into the everyday social-media work of library visibility, trust-building, and community interaction. Its premise is that libraries now compete for user attention in Web 2.0 environments and can benefit when users voluntarily create, tag, share, review, photograph, film, remix, or otherwise circulate content about library spaces, materials, services, and events.

The authors define UGC broadly as content created and distributed by users rather than by marketers: posts, comments, reviews, photos, videos, podcasts, ratings, comparisons, wiki-style entries, creative designs, and other social-media formats. They argue that UGC matters because it functions as a form of social proof and word-of-mouth marketing. When patrons post about a library, its spaces, its books, or its services, other users may perceive the institution as more trustworthy than they would if they encountered only direct advertising from the library itself.

Methodologically, the article uses descriptive, analytical, comparative, bibliographic, selective observation, grouping, and content-analysis methods. It analyzes the work of internet marketers for Ukrainian libraries and other brands, relevant scholarly and professional literature, and examples of social media mechanics. It is therefore not an outcomes study and does not measure user behavior before and after a library intervention. Its contribution is practical and conceptual: it identifies UGC formats, gamified activation strategies, and design components that library marketers can adapt.

The article's practical recommendations are useful for GBLS service design. Maranchak and Maranchak recommend that libraries begin by identifying what motivates their target audience and what UGC formats are comfortable for that audience on the relevant platform. Possible library-facing prompts include asking users to choose a book character for a Story, make a short Reels or TikTok video with a favorite book in the library, post a short review with a book photograph, tag the library account, use a proposed hashtag, or create visual content around a library space. The authors also discuss library-relevant examples such as user photographs of Kyiv libraries and tagged book-related Instagram content.

The authors propose several design components for gamified UGC activation: an idea aligned with the audience's interests, an accessible content format, a carefully chosen reward, a call to action, and a platform suited to the desired content. Rewards do not have to be material; users may value being reposted by the library account, appearing in Stories, gaining organic reach through collaboration features, or receiving emotional satisfaction from interaction with a favored institution. The authors also stress moderation and selection: libraries should choose content that fits relevance, reputation, legality, and minimum quality criteria, and if winners are selected, the process must be transparent to avoid reputational damage.

For the GBLS review, this source broadens the scope of gamification in libraries. A gamified service need not be a full game or a scheduled program; it can be a lightweight social-media challenge, creative prompt, visual installation, hashtag campaign, poll, quiz, status reward, AR filter, sticker, or participatory content mechanic. The article also connects online and offline design: art installations, photo zones, decorated interiors, readable signs, lighting, platform affordances, and social-media prompts can work together to invite patrons into voluntary creative participation.

The source's limitations are important. It relies on conceptual analysis, examples from marketing, and selective observation rather than rigorous library assessment. It also frames users partly through marketing language, which may sit uneasily with library values if implemented without care. Still, its strongest GBLS contribution is to show that gamification can support library-public relationships, user voice, trust, and participatory promotion when libraries design clear, voluntary, platform-appropriate activities and evaluate them through engagement, reactions, subscriptions, leads, number of participants, and quality of created content.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Maranchak and Maranchak (2023) extend library gamification into Web 2.0 marketing and user-generated content, arguing that comments, reviews, photos, videos, Reels, TikToks, podcasts, ratings, creative designs, hashtags, tags, and other patron-created content can function as social proof for library trust and visibility. Their article is practical and conceptual rather than evaluative, but it usefully identifies lightweight GBLS mechanics outside formal programs: social-media challenges, polls, quizzes, AR filters, stickers, status rewards, creative prompts, reposts, collaborative posts, photo zones, art installations, and platform-specific calls to action that invite patrons to create content about library spaces, books, services, and events.

# **De Long, K., Garrison, J., & Ryan, M. (2017). All in: A public services philosophy for the twenty-first century academic library. *portal: Libraries and the Academy, 17*(2), 201-204.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `2M3AP2GL`; Better BibTeX citation key `longAllPublicServices2017`; attachment key `DCF7HISC`.

De Long, Garrison, and Ryan introduce a special issue of *portal: Libraries and the Academy* on public services in twenty-first-century academic libraries. The editorial argues that academic libraries are no longer merely support units but institutional partners in teaching and learning, faculty scholarship, student success, informed citizenship, global awareness, and knowledge creation. The authors describe public services as a broad philosophy of user engagement rather than a single department or desk-based function.

The editorial's central claim is that contemporary academic libraries need an organization-wide public services philosophy. Access services, cataloging, facilities, information technology, liaison work, scholarly communication, instruction, collections, assessment, and learning spaces should all be oriented toward users' needs. The article frames this as "the library in the life of the user," emphasizing outward-facing relationships, user-centered language, flexible learning spaces, innovative technologies, collections as service, space as service, assessment, and engagement across the organization.

For the GBLS project, the article has only indirect value. Its user-centered service philosophy is compatible with game-based library services, especially where GBLS work involves collections, spaces, technologies, outreach, liaison relationships, and assessment. The editorial also briefly mentions another article in the special issue about lending video game consoles to support a game-design curriculum. That referenced article may be directly relevant if it appears elsewhere in the Zotero database.

This editorial itself, however, should be explicitly excluded from the main GBLS literature review. It does not analyze games, game-based learning, gamification, game collections, TTRPGs, escape rooms, esports, videogame preservation, or game-based programming. Its contribution is broad public-services framing rather than game-specific evidence or practice. Including it in the review would dilute the GBLS scope when more directly relevant public-service and game-collection sources are available.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No suggested addition. Explicitly exclude from the GBLS literature review because this editorial is a broad academic-library public-services framing piece rather than a study or substantive discussion of game-based library services.

# **Swiatek, C., & Gorsse, M. (2016). Playing games at the library: Seriously? *LIBER Quarterly, 26*(2), 83-101. https://doi.org/10.18352/lq.10161**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `Q9CUHJCI`; Better BibTeX citation key `swiatekPlayingGamesLibrary2016`; attachment key `UUVU9JL3`.

Swiatek and Gorsse provide a substantial overview of gaming initiatives and management issues in French academic libraries. The article begins from the observation that academic libraries had historically treated games as minor, youthful, or more appropriate to public libraries, but that French academic libraries increasingly use games for documentary research training, student welcome activities, public engagement, cultural programming, and organizational innovation. The authors focus on non-virtual and public-oriented games already organized in and by libraries, including board games, mystery games, alternate reality games, live-action role playing, training games, and playful research challenges.

The article's examples are especially useful for GBLS. The University Pierre et Marie Curie library received an innovation award for murder-party events during first-year welcome week. INSA Lyon ran a two-month alternate reality game that began virally and virtually and ended in a real library event. The University of Haute-Alsace ran a Cluedo-like rally that became part of students' curriculum. Other examples include Zombibli, a motion-recognition game about library rules; BnF/Gallica's weekly document-discovery puzzle; Montpellier's OSNI Hunter, which invited users to identify unusual scientific objects both online and in the library; and law-library training built around transforming a new student avatar into a fully equipped first-year law student through documentary research.

The authors argue that gaming activities need more than enthusiasm. In many academic libraries, gaming initiatives rely on individual staff skill and personal involvement while lacking explicit service definition, budget, external communication, publicity, knowledge management, and a formal place in organizational charts or job descriptions. They recommend that libraries establish a gaming strategy and calendar, connect games to institutional goals, gather user needs, learn from colleagues and gamers, collaborate with publics where appropriate, and decide whether games are a means, an end, or both.

One of the article's strongest GBLS contributions is its management vocabulary. Swiatek and Gorsse distinguish a strategic game manager from an operational game designer. The game manager does not need to be an experienced gamer; that role advocates, translates, coordinates, builds policy, manages communication, supports knowledge management, and connects collaborators and partners. The game designer or game expert creates, facilitates, adapts, and evaluates the activity, and may come from outside librarianship. This distinction helps libraries avoid assuming that every librarian must become a game designer while also showing that games require real professional labor.

The article also treats staff training and internal communication as core infrastructure. Staff resistance can be stronger than administrative resistance when workers cannot identify with the activity, so the authors recommend formal support from leadership, internal communication, staff awareness, and training through play. Examples include staff team-building puzzles, classification-based treasure hunts, decision-making games, and game-like design competitions for training creators. The point is not simply morale; staff need to understand how games connect to library missions, user mediation, and professional skills.

For GBLS, the article is valuable because it frames games as services that require organizational change. The authors emphasize knowledge management, reports, reusable documentation, web publication, conference presentations, posters, professional networking, and feedback loops. Visibility can help secure budgets, stabilize games as services, and justify job-description changes. They also warn that students and users may resist poorly designed or poorly mediated games, especially when games feel disconnected from disciplinary or practical goals. This reinforces the review's broader argument that game-based library services need purpose, fit, facilitation, assessment, and sustainability planning.

The limitations are those of an overview and professional essay rather than a formal empirical study. The article does not provide systematic evaluation data across the French examples. Still, it is one of the stronger operational sources for GBLS because it moves beyond whether libraries can host games toward how games become legitimate, managed, communicated, staffed, and sustained parts of academic-library service.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Plan for Operations and Sustainability`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Swiatek and Gorsse (2016) make operations and sustainability central to academic-library gaming. Their French overview shows that murder parties, alternate reality games, curriculum-linked rallies, weekly document puzzles, object-identification challenges, motion-recognition games, and research-training scenarios can attract users and teach documentary research, but only become durable services when libraries formalize strategy, budgets, communication plans, staff training, knowledge management, job descriptions, and public gaming policies. Their distinction between a strategic game manager and an operational game designer is especially useful for GBLS staffing: libraries need advocates and coordinators as well as people with game-design and facilitation expertise.

# **Knapp, N. F. (2019). Using technology to foster "real reading" in the school library and beyond. *Knowledge Quest, 48*(1), 54-59.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `WEAAMT6U`; Better BibTeX citation key `knappUsingTechnologyFoster2019`; attachment key `CDJGR2MW`.

Knapp argues that school librarians' literacy and technology roles should not be treated as competing responsibilities. The article is not primarily about game-based library services, but it is useful for GBLS because it shows how digital tools, including game-based reading programs and game-related online texts, can support school-library literacy work. Knapp begins from the concern that technology use may distract from reading practice or from school librarians' mission of helping students become lifelong readers. She responds that technology can support traditional literacy development when used deliberately.

The article identifies five ways technology can foster "real reading." First, technology can make basic literacy practice more effective and more engaging. Knapp explicitly names game-based programs for early readers, including commercial and free reading games, as useful add-ons for vocabulary, decoding, word recognition, and repetitive practice. She cautions that such programs should not become the primary means of teaching reading, but argues that they can motivate novice readers to practice skills that flash cards and worksheets often make disengaging.

Second, technology increases the number and variety of texts available to readers. Knapp discusses e-books, free online book repositories, student-created books, and online informational texts. For GBLS, the most relevant point is her argument that school librarians can capitalize on students' fascination with a videogame or movie by guiding them toward related websites and fan fiction written in favorite imaginary worlds. She names fan fiction sites, teen writing platforms, sports sites, craft sites, and other online spaces as legitimate routes into reading when librarians help students judge quality, appropriateness, and credibility.

Third, technology can scaffold texts for struggling readers and writers through text-to-speech, translation, read-aloud, speech-to-text, e-book supports, and accessibility tools. Fourth, it can personalize and differentiate instruction by offering texts at different reading levels, multilingual supports, simplified reference articles, tutorials, and homework-help resources. Fifth, it can make reading social through student-written reviews, QR codes linking to reviews, private reading groups, likes/dislikes, book talks, book trailers, YouTube, Amazon, Goodreads, and other literacy-focused platforms.

For the GBLS project, Knapp is best used as a supporting literacy source rather than as evidence about game programs. It helps explain why games, game-related stories, fan communities, and digital reading practices can belong in a school-library literacy ecology. A student who reads fan fiction connected to a videogame, plays a reading-skills game, records a book trailer, or reviews a book on a social platform is participating in a broader network of technology-supported reading practices that school librarians can guide.

The limitations are clear. The article is a practitioner-facing overview, not an empirical study, and games are only one part of its broader argument about technology and reading. It should not be used to claim that game-based reading tools improve literacy outcomes on their own. Its value is in the service logic: school librarians can connect technology, games, fandom, accessibility, differentiated texts, and social sharing to support reading motivation and access.
