## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Seay (2019) liminal instruction paragraph.

Suggested text: Knapp (2019) adds a school-library literacy perspective to this instructional thread. Although her article is broader than GBLS, she notes that game-based reading programs can make basic vocabulary, decoding, and word-recognition practice more engaging as supplemental tools, and that librarians can use students' interest in videogames, movies, fan fiction, online worlds, reviews, book trailers, and social reading platforms as pathways into "real reading." The source should be used cautiously, but it reinforces the idea that games and game-adjacent media can support literacy when librarians connect them to access, scaffolding, reader choice, and social sharing rather than treating them as replacements for reading instruction.

# **Cross, L., Piovesan, A., Sousa, M., Wright, P., & Atherton, G. (2023). Your move: An open access dataset of over 1500 board gamer's demographics, preferences and motivations. *Simulation & Gaming.* https://doi.org/10.1177/10468781231189493**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `A28988GV`; Better BibTeX citation key `crossYourMoveOpen2023`; attachment key `W98L946P`.

Cross, Piovesan, Sousa, Wright, and Atherton report and release an open dataset on board gamer demographics, preferences, and motivations. The article is not a library study, but it is useful adjacent evidence for GBLS because many library tabletop services must make practical choices about which games to collect, circulate, teach, or use in programs for different audiences. The authors argue that modern board games have grown commercially and culturally, and that games are increasingly used for serious purposes in education, therapy, mental health, social interaction, reading, numeracy, and other interventions. Their core claim is that people selecting or designing board games need better information about who board gamers are and which mechanics, themes, formats, and motivations different groups prefer.

The study used an English-language Qualtrics survey of 1,603 board gamers recruited through board gaming social media groups, forums, subreddits, Facebook groups, and a large game distributor. The sample was deliberately aimed at active hobby participants rather than the general public. Respondents reported demographics, diagnoses of mental health or neurodevelopmental conditions, gaming experience, hours played per month, preferred platform, preferred player count, game length, components, style, classification, type, mechanics, themes, choice criteria, motivations, social importance, and sense of community. The survey categories were developed through consultation with players, designers, distributors, academics, clinicians, and BoardGameGeek terminology.

The broad demographic picture confirmed some existing assumptions while complicating them. The sample was mostly men, mostly white, highly educated, and concentrated in the 18-45 age range, with a mean age just over 32. More than half had an undergraduate degree and about one-fifth had a postgraduate degree. At the same time, the dataset included a large Chinese subgroup, a substantial number of women, and more than one-quarter of respondents who reported at least one mental health or neurodevelopmental condition. The most common reported conditions were depression, anxiety, autism, dyslexia, and ADHD.

The preference findings are especially useful for service design. Most respondents identified as casual or mid-core/core players, played 5-29 hours per month, and preferred in-person play over online play. They most preferred games for 3-4 players and 30-60 minute sessions, with cards and boards rated above dice or app/web hybrids. Competitive all-vs-all and fully cooperative styles were both rated highly, while one-vs-all was lowest. European and hybrid games were preferred over American-style games. In mechanics, action point allowance, deck/bag/pool building, worker placement, and engine building were strong, while player elimination and memory were weak. Fantasy, science fiction, and adventure themes rated highest, while war, horror, and transport themes rated lower overall. Gameplay and mechanics mattered most when choosing games; components mattered least. Social interaction and strategising were the strongest motivations, while competition was lowest.

For GBLS, the article helps libraries think beyond simply adding "popular board games." Its findings suggest that group size, session length, social motivation, player elimination, theme, complexity, and accessibility matter when designing programs or circulating collections. Libraries planning a one-shot game night, a recurring board game group, a school or youth program, a wellness-oriented program, or an intergenerational event can use this article as a reminder to match game choices to audience, goals, and local constraints. The dataset also supports inclusion work: different age groups, genders, and neurodivergent or mental-health subgroups may prefer different mechanics, themes, lengths, and motivations, so a collection or program built around one imagined "board gamer" can quietly exclude many potential participants.

The article should be used carefully. Because participants were recruited from hobby board game spaces and received a Steam board game key, the sample is not representative of library patrons, casual families, non-English-speaking communities, or people who might try board games only because the library lowers access barriers. The dataset can inform selection and design, but it should not substitute for local needs assessment, patron feedback, or inclusive facilitation. In the GBLS review, Cross et al. are best treated as adjacent audience and design evidence for tabletop services rather than direct evidence about library program outcomes.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Curate for Inclusion, Access, and Advisory`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Cross et al. (2023) provide adjacent audience data for tabletop GBLS through a survey and open dataset of 1,603 board gamers' demographics, preferences, and motivations. The sample was mostly men, white, highly educated, and active in the hobby, but it also included substantial variation and more than one-quarter of respondents reporting a mental health or neurodevelopmental condition; respondents generally preferred in-person 3-4 player games lasting 30-60 minutes, cards and boards, competitive all-vs-all or cooperative styles, Euro/hybrid designs, and were motivated most by social interaction and strategising rather than competition. Because the study samples hobby board gamers rather than library patrons, it should not define library demand, but it can help libraries choose tabletop formats with attention to inclusion, accessibility, group size, session length, social goals, and population-specific preferences.

# **Kroski, E. (2020). Why create digital breakouts? Opportunities for libraries. *Library Technology Reports, 56*(3), 8-9.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `2PINRDBM`; Better BibTeX citation key `kroskiWhyCreateDigital2020`; attachment key `PV66LSEE`.

Kroski's chapter is a short practitioner rationale for digital breakouts in libraries, published as chapter 2 of the *Library Technology Reports* issue *How to Create Free Digital Breakouts for Libraries*. The larger issue defines digital breakouts as online, escape-room-like experiences built with free web tools, lock forms, puzzles, clues, narratives, learning objectives, and virtual settings. Chapter 2 focuses specifically on why libraries might create them and where they fit within library service.

The central argument is that digital breakouts offer libraries a flexible way to inform, instruct, and engage patrons while still providing a game-like pastime. Kroski frames them as related to live escape room adventures, but with important differences: they are not bound to a physical room, they can often be played individually, they can be distributed widely online, and they can be built around a single subject, skill set, grade level, or learning objective. This makes them attractive for libraries that want the engagement of escape rooms without the space, staffing, props, and throughput constraints of live events.

The chapter names several library applications. For information literacy and bibliographic instruction, librarians can design puzzles that require players to locate catalog items, explore databases, identify fake news, find peer-reviewed articles, cite sources, and practice using library resources. For curriculum support, a breakout can be tailored to a class, discipline, school subject, or university course and can help librarians build faculty partnerships. For staff training, digital breakouts can replace or supplement handouts and presentations with an interactive, gamified training experience.

Kroski also emphasizes critical thinking and problem solving. Like escape rooms, digital breakouts require players to reason, make inferences, deduce patterns, try, fail, adjust, and try again. In the library context, this matters because the puzzles can make research and resource-use skills feel purposeful rather than procedural. The chapter further identifies outreach and marketing as service opportunities: because digital breakouts are portable and web-distributable, they can introduce patrons to library resources, services, events, and programs at little or no cost. Kroski also points to STEM learning, including coding and math puzzles, as a natural fit for inquiry-based online breakout design.

For the GBLS project, this source is useful because it extends the escape-room literature into a digital and low-cost format. It gives practical language for libraries that want to use game structures for instruction, outreach, curriculum support, youth programming, or staff learning but cannot host a physical escape room. It also connects well with implementation-oriented sources: a digital breakout still needs a narrative, a clear learning objective, appropriately difficult puzzles, testing, and assessment, but the format changes the operational constraints.

The limitations are straightforward. The chapter is not an empirical study, does not report participant outcomes, and makes some claims about retention and engagement without formal evidence. It should therefore be cited as a practitioner design rationale and service-model source rather than proof that digital breakouts improve information literacy or staff training. Its best use in the review is to show the range of breakout formats and the reasons libraries adopt them, while leaving effectiveness claims to studies with assessment data.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Escape Rooms and Breakout Boxes`, after the Helbing et al. (2022) paragraph.

Suggested text: Kroski (2020) extends the escape-room discussion to digital breakouts, framing them as low-cost online escape-room-like games that libraries can use for information literacy, bibliographic instruction, curriculum support, youth programming, outreach, STEM learning, and staff training. The chapter is a practitioner rationale rather than assessment evidence, but it usefully identifies why the digital format matters: breakouts can be single-player, distributed widely online, built with free web tools, tailored to one subject or skill set, and used to teach catalog searching, database use, fake-news identification, peer-reviewed source recognition, citation, and library services without requiring physical rooms or equipment.

# **Smith, A.-L., & Baker, L. (2011). Getting a clue: Creating student detectives and dragon slayers in your library. *Reference Services Review, 39*(4), 628-642. https://doi.org/10.1108/00907321111186659**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `6ETYG8AH`; Better BibTeX citation key `smithGettingClueCreating2011`; attachment key `5RE7XB87`.

Smith and Baker describe two self-paced academic-library orientation games created at Utah Valley University Library after focus groups showed that students were intimidated by the size of the new 190,000-square-foot library and lacked knowledge of library services. The article is a case study rather than a controlled experiment, but it is especially useful for GBLS because it reports concrete design choices, assessment data, and implementation lessons from a library that needed to reach large numbers of new students without adding a heavy staff burden.

The first game, *Get a Clue*, was a physical mystery-tour game launched at the beginning of the Fall 2009 semester. The library's promotions committee adapted the familiar premise of *Clue*: students moved through library spaces, read clues, eliminated suspects, and learned about services, collections, floor locations, and circulation processes. The game was designed to take about 30 minutes, long enough to introduce the building but short enough to hold attention. Staff used floor maps, clue posters, a short video introduction, prize drawings, and faculty promotion to encourage participation. The first version drew 311 entries, but only 55 percent correctly identified the villain, leading the committee to simplify the second version by removing some elimination mechanics, dropping the registration requirement, and giving students a stamped game piece for proof of completion.

The second game, *LibraryCraft*, was an online medieval quest modeled loosely on popular computer-game conventions such as avatars, chapters, points, inventory, and fantasy artwork. Instead of moving students through the physical building, *LibraryCraft* moved them through the library website. Students looked up books and articles, answered questions about services such as interlibrary loan and media checkout, and unlocked new chapters after correct answers. The design used HTML and JavaScript, pop-up help, task hints, and a reference-desk cheat sheet so that students could keep moving even when they were confused. The committee also gathered feedback from student workers on artwork, story, tasks, and wording, trying to make the experience entertaining without letting the game drift away from library-learning objectives.

The assessment findings are modest but directly relevant to GBLS. In the Fall 2010 version of *Get a Clue*, 338 students completed a post-game survey. Most participated for a class assignment or extra credit, and 63 percent had visited the library fewer than five times, meaning the game reached its intended audience. More than 91 percent rated the game easy or very easy, nearly 90 percent reported being more comfortable navigating the library, 92 percent described it as a good use of time, and 92.3 percent would recommend it to other students. For *LibraryCraft*, 52 students responded to the first survey question, and 84.5 percent agreed or strongly agreed that the game taught them a new library skill; 87 percent agreed or strongly agreed that they would recommend it. Comments suggest that students valued the story, graphics, and the way the game required them to search rather than simply receive answers.

The article's implementation lessons are as important as its outcomes. Both games were revised because complexity interfered with learning. The authors note that a game can fail when students do not understand the rules, when vocabulary is too difficult, when tasks require too many unrelated steps, or when digital clues depend on unstable URLs and e-book records. They argue that orientation games need a level of difficulty that is "doable" but not boring, and that designers must accommodate varied reading levels and varied familiarity with library resources. They also emphasize faculty buy-in, advertising, prizes, proof of completion, launch timing, and student feedback as practical infrastructure. These details align strongly with the GBLS principle that facilitation and operations are part of the service, not afterthoughts.

For the larger GBLS project, Smith and Baker provide one of the clearest examples of games as orientation infrastructure. The case shows how a library can turn wayfinding, service awareness, catalog/database use, and research support into playable tasks, and how asynchronous games can scale orientation when staff cannot provide tours or workshops for every incoming student. The article also illustrates the difference between superficial gamification and purposeful game-based service design: the games worked best when story, challenge, feedback, and progression were aligned with concrete library behaviors. The evidence is limited to short post-game surveys and self-reported comfort or skill gains, with heavy dependence on course credit, but the case remains strong practitioner evidence for game-based orientation, especially in academic libraries.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Knapp (2019) literacy-technology paragraph.

Suggested text: Smith and Baker (2011) provide a more concrete academic-library orientation case through Utah Valley University's *Get a Clue* and *LibraryCraft*. Their self-paced physical mystery tour and online quest helped students learn building navigation, library services, website resources, and basic search tasks with limited staff burden; post-game surveys reported increased comfort navigating the library and self-reported library-skill learning, while the authors' revisions show how complexity, unclear rules, unstable digital clues, advertising, proof of completion, and faculty buy-in shape whether orientation games actually work.

# **Brown, R. T., & Kasper, T. (2013). The fusion of literacy and games: A case study in assessing the goals of a library video game program. *Library Trends, 61*(4), 755-778.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `Q44D8QIG`; Better BibTeX citation key `brownFusionLiteracyGames2013`; attachment key `XBE8KRJC`.

Brown and Kasper offer an assessment-focused case study of a public-library video game club in South Carolina. The article begins from the argument that games and gaming programs are already part of library practice, but that evaluation of those programs remains underdeveloped. Their central questions are practical GBLS questions: what do participants in a library video game program learn, how do library gaming programs assess participants, and what steps can improve assessment? The study is especially useful because it treats a game club not only as an attendance draw or entertainment service, but as an informal learning environment whose goals, rules, staff labor, and assessment practices can be examined.

The program under study had four broad goals: increasing literacy, encouraging positive behaviors, fostering the development of new skills, and promoting critical thinking. Each goal had associated outcomes. To demonstrate literacy, game club members were required to get a library card and check out at least four books each month. To demonstrate positive behavior, they had to follow dress, speech, behavior, and sportsmanship rules. Skill development and critical thinking were supported through workshops, tournaments, special events, technology projects, and other opportunities. Members had access to console games, computer games, board games, and role-playing games, though console games were most preferred during the study.

Methodologically, the study used participant observation rather than surveys alone. Between June and October 2010, the researcher conducted ninety hours of observation at two branches in the pseudonymous Elizabeth County library system. One branch had a dedicated game room and dedicated game club staff; the other had a smaller, more visible gaming area near the entrance and staff who supervised gaming while also managing other library work. Field notes were coded through a grounded theory framework. The resulting descriptive model organized findings around three areas: staff, game club members, and assessment.

The staff findings matter because they show that game clubs depend heavily on adult facilitation. Staff acted as role models, rule enforcers, and innovators. They learned new games, played alongside youth, provided individualized attention, modeled technology use and information seeking, used teachable moments, and connected games to reading, writing, digital media, research, career exploration, and library work. Rules were not simply restrictions; they were used to reinforce program goals around behavior, academic standing, access, and respect for shared resources. Staff also adapted to youth interests by starting clubs, supporting digital arts and local-history projects, encouraging primary-source use, and turning game-room ownership into creative work.

The game club member findings center on community, motivation, and emotion. The club functioned as a close-knit, family-like space where some youth preferred playing at the library even when they owned games at home because the library allowed them to play with others. Members protected game-club resources, socialized across peer relationships, and experienced the library as a safe, shared place. Motivation was shaped by the incentive structure: youth could earn game time by checking out books, reading, assisting staff, participating in activities, or meeting academic and behavior expectations. The study also shows that game programs require emotional facilitation. Competition, frustration, noise, and exclusion had to be managed, and staff needed to know when a participant was reaching a breaking point.

The assessment findings are the article's strongest GBLS contribution. Brown and Kasper show that the program already tracked some metrics: library cards, monthly book checkouts, report-card eligibility, attendance, event participation, behavior, and event feedback. But their observations suggest that richer assessment should also examine academic skills, life skills, game skills, social behavior, motivation, emotion, technology literacy, reading, and information literacy. They recommend moving from punishment-only rules toward reward-based point systems, tracking good behavior, connecting academic performance to graded feedback, building hobby or skill systems with points or badges, integrating books and reference resources into the gaming area, and using intake and follow-up measures such as reading motivation or library-anxiety instruments.

For the larger GBLS project, this article is important because it pushes the review beyond the question of whether games attract youth. It shows that video game clubs can become community centers, mentoring spaces, informal literacy environments, and platforms for library familiarity, but only if libraries design the surrounding service infrastructure. Staff presence, rules, incentives, relevant materials, emotional support, and assessment practices determine whether gaming remains leisure access or becomes a broader learning service. The study also highlights the assessment gap that recurs across GBLS literature: attendance and enjoyment are not enough. Libraries need measures that can capture learning, literacy, social behavior, confidence, library anxiety, technology use, reading motivation, and transfer into school or everyday life.

The limitations are clear. This is an exploratory qualitative case study of one county library system, with pseudonymous sites and no experimental comparison or long-term outcome tracking. The authors are appropriately cautious about the difficulty of linking literacy or learning directly to game play. Even so, the article provides one of the most useful early frameworks for evaluating GBLS because it names the goals, observable behaviors, and possible assessment tools that libraries can use when they want to justify and improve game clubs.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Brown and Kasper (2013) make the assessment problem explicit through ninety hours of participant observation in a public-library video game club. Their case shows that attendance and enjoyment miss much of what game clubs may do: staff modeled information seeking, enforced rules, mentored youth, and connected play to reading, technology, local history, behavior, academic standing, library cards, book checkouts, and social belonging. They recommend richer measures of reading motivation, library anxiety, information literacy, behavior, skill development, and reward-based progress so libraries can evaluate whether gaming programs advance learning and community goals rather than merely drawing participants.

# **Slobuski, T., Robson, D., & Bentley, P. J. (2017). Arranging the pieces: A survey of library practices related to a tabletop game collection. *Evidence Based Library and Information Practice, 12*(1), 2-17. https://doi.org/10.18438/B8JG7H**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `Q5BJVXJY`; Better BibTeX citation key `slobuskiArrangingPiecesSurvey2017`; attachment key `8GIATGC3`.

Slobuski, Robson, and Bentley provide a baseline survey of how libraries acquire, describe, process, circulate, and program around tabletop game collections. The article defines tabletop games broadly as games played in real-world social settings, including board games, dice games, card games, collectible card games, and role-playing games. Its core contribution to GBLS is operational: it shows that tabletop games are increasingly present in libraries, but that library practices remain fragmented because standards, professional guidance, and shared workflows lag behind patron interest and market growth.

The study begins by situating tabletop collections in a long library history of play. Libraries have supported chess clubs, toy libraries, circulating games, gaming programs, International Games Day events, and other play services for well over a century. Yet the authors argue that research and guidance for tabletop game collections lag behind work on video game collections. For librarians, the question is often no longer whether tabletop games belong in libraries, but how to select, catalog, process, lend, preserve, and make them discoverable. The article frames games as similar to other realia and special collections: if they are not cataloged, described, visible, and integrated into workflows, they become hidden and access remains staff-dependent.

Methodologically, the authors developed an online survey with input from academic, public, and school librarians and tested it with members of ALA's Games and Gaming Round Table. The survey was distributed in June and July 2015 through snowball sampling via library discussion lists and electronic outlets. One hundred nineteen libraries responded. Public libraries represented 66 percent of respondents, academic libraries 28 percent, and the rest special, curriculum-resource, school, or other libraries. Eighty-one percent of respondents reported having a tabletop game collection.

The collection-development findings show that libraries build these collections through mixed, often local channels. Among libraries that acquire games, 84 percent purchased games and 66 percent accepted donations. Most purchasing went through general online vendors such as Amazon or Barnes & Noble, but many libraries also used physical game stores, physical chain stores, online game vendors, library vendors, thrift stores, garage sales, and eBay. Budgets were generally small: 46 percent of responding libraries had up to $249 for tabletop games, 30 percent had no purchasing budget, 12 percent had $250-$500, and 12 percent had more than $500. Donation practices varied from accepting only complete, good-condition games that met collection needs to accepting nearly anything offered.

The cataloging results are especially important for GBLS access and discovery. Of 94 libraries answering the cataloging question, 31 percent created bibliographic catalog records for tabletop games, 51 percent did not, and 18 percent did so sometimes. Board games were the most frequently cataloged format, followed by card sets and role-playing guides. Only 22 libraries reported using OCLC to catalog games, and none said that more than 75 percent of their cataloged games already had OCLC records. Subject and genre access also remained inadequate: only 22 percent of 36 respondents found Library of Congress subject headings sufficient, while many used local headings drawn from BoardGameGeek, Father Geek, publisher descriptions, reviews, curricular areas, grade levels, awards, and game mechanisms. The authors conclude that perceived complexity and the absence of standards keep tabletop collections more hidden than other library materials.

Processing and circulation practices were similarly varied. Of 79 respondents, 46 percent created item records with barcodes for tabletop games, 46 percent did not, and 9 percent did so sometimes. Most libraries left games in original containers; 54 percent separated pieces into bags, 22 percent reinforced boxes, and only 6 percent sleeved cards. Circulation also depended on local definitions and workflows. Forty-four percent of 78 responding libraries reported circulating tabletop games, but when in-library and in-school use were counted alongside off-site borrowing, 65 percent allowed in-house use, 1 percent in-school use only, and 34 percent off-premises lending. Loan periods varied, but among circulating libraries 70 percent allowed holds and 76 percent allowed renewals.

One of the article's most useful findings is that feared loss and damage were less severe than many librarians expected. Forty-eight percent of 80 respondents counted pieces at each return, but 28 percent never counted pieces; only 34 percent purchased replacements when pieces were lost, and most did not charge replacement fees. Respondent comments repeatedly suggested that lost pieces and damage were rare, manageable, or not always fatal to play. Some libraries bought duplicate thrift-store copies for replacement parts, contacted publishers, made case-by-case judgments, or used creative methods such as weighing components to check completeness. The authors argue that fear of loss should not prevent circulation.

Programming also appears as a major part of tabletop collection use. Of 78 libraries answering the programming question, 71 percent offered programming around tabletop games. Programs included teen gaming events, family board game events, adult gaming events, board game design events, game jams, tournaments, lectures, club meetings, and other game days. Libraries often drew on staff and faculty, teen or student clubs, local board game meetups, game retailers, professional agencies, volunteers, local gamers, and community game stores. This matters for GBLS because tabletop collections are not isolated objects; they often become program infrastructure, partnership opportunities, and community connection points.

For the larger GBLS project, Slobuski et al. help translate tabletop enthusiasm into library operations. The article gives concrete evidence that tabletop games can be collected, cataloged, circulated, renewed, held, inventoried, displayed, programmed, and replaced, even without perfect standards. It also shows where infrastructure gaps remain: local metadata, subject and genre vocabularies, processing standards, component inventories, circulation policies, replacement practices, and professional comfort. The authors' conclusion is usefully pragmatic: libraries should not wait for codified standards before piloting discoverable, preserved, borrowable tabletop collections, but researchers and professional organizations should develop best practices that reduce local reinvention.

The limitations are typical of a snowball-sampled professional survey. The sample overrepresents libraries already connected to gaming conversations and cannot estimate the overall percentage of libraries with tabletop collections. Some questions also reveal inconsistent definitions of circulation. Still, the article is a key GBLS source because it provides one of the clearest field snapshots of tabletop collection practice before later operational studies such as Robson, Phillips, and Guerrero (2018).

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Circulating and In-Library Collections`, immediately before the Robson, Phillips, and Guerrero (2018) paragraph.

Suggested text: Slobuski, Robson, and Bentley (2017) provide the baseline survey context for tabletop collections. In responses from 119 libraries, they found that tabletop games were being purchased, donated, cataloged, barcoded, bagged, circulated, used in-house, renewed, placed on hold, inventoried, and programmed in highly varied ways, with many libraries still lacking catalog records, subject/genre standards, processing guidance, and shared circulation practices. Their findings also reduce a common barrier: loss and damage were generally less severe than expected, suggesting that libraries can pilot discoverable and borrowable tabletop collections while professional standards continue to mature.

# **de Groat, G. (2015). A history of video game cataloging in U.S. libraries. *Cataloging & Classification Quarterly, 53*(2), 135-156. https://doi.org/10.1080/01639374.2014.954297**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `87E2V4JF`; Better BibTeX citation key `degroatHistoryVideoGame2015`; attachment key `YDPD2AYT`.

de Groat provides a technical history of video game cataloging in U.S. libraries, tracing how descriptive rules, MARC coding, subject/genre practice, and title authority work struggled to accommodate games from the late 1970s through the transition from AACR2 to RDA. The article is not about public-facing gaming programs, but it is central to GBLS because collections, advisory, access, preservation, and scholarship all depend on whether games can be described, collocated, searched, limited, and distinguished in library catalogs.

The article begins from a blunt premise: video games have always been an awkward fit in regular cataloging workflows. There have never been specific descriptive cataloging rules for video games, so they were usually treated under broader and constantly changing rules for machine-readable data files, computer files, electronic resources, computer programs, interactive multimedia, moving images, or realia. de Groat explains the institutional structure behind this problem: descriptive cataloging rules, Library of Congress practices, subject and genre vocabularies, name/title authority files, and MARC encoding all developed through systems built primarily for books and later adapted to nonbook materials.

The descriptive-cataloging history shows repeated lag between technology and standards. Early AACR2 rules for "machine-readable data files" focused on data and programs rather than interactive consumer software. Early guidance even suggested that cartridge-based video games might be treated as realia, while later ALA guidelines placed them under computer-file rules. Across AACR2 revisions, terminology shifted from "machine-readable data file" to "computer file" to "electronic resource," and physical descriptions shifted from program-file statements to computer cartridges, computer disks, optical discs, CD-ROMs, DVDs, and conventional terminology. These changes made it difficult to collocate like resources or reliably identify particular carriers and platforms.

The article is especially useful on MARC workarounds. MARC created fields for computer files, type of computer file, physical description fixed fields, file characteristics, and system details, but the values and fields were not designed around the complexity of video games. Platform information was often trapped in a system requirements note, a 538 field, or even improvised into an edition statement. The 500 general note became a place where catalogers put useful information that had no better structured field. RDA and later MARC 21 fields added more granular content, media, carrier, sound, video, and digital-file elements, but de Groat argues that these fields still describe simpler electronic resources better than interactive, multi-component games.

The subject and genre discussion is the article's strongest GBLS contribution. de Groat shows that Library of Congress Subject Headings and genre practices were built around books and works about games, not the cataloging of games themselves. Game-genre headings existed as topical headings for books about games rather than as genre/form headings for games. The distinction between "computer games," "video games," "electronic games," "simulation games," "software," and "interactive multimedia" remained unstable. Some headings could be used through workarounds; others could not. Platform or brand headings such as Sega Dreamcast video games were sometimes used even though platform is not a genre. de Groat concludes that the entire field of game genre headings and subdivisions needs overhaul.

Title authority practice creates another layer of difficulty. Library catalogs need titles not only to describe an item in hand but also to collocate versions, translations, adaptations, franchises, and works about games. de Groat explains that computer program titles moved awkwardly between subject and name authority files, while rules for preferred titles under RDA did not give specific guidance for video games. Catalogers had to check authority files and bibliographic files for conflicts, decide whether a game title conflicted with a film, television program, subject heading, or other game, and choose qualifiers such as "Computer game," "Video game," or "Game" without clear rules. This is especially important for games tied to franchises, ports, editions, sequels, and cross-media works.

For the larger GBLS project, de Groat provides historical grounding for the claim that game collections require infrastructure, not merely enthusiasm. If catalogs cannot reliably express platform, carrier, interactivity, genre, subject, franchise, preferred title, version, or relationship to other media, patrons and staff cannot easily discover, compare, recommend, preserve, or study games. This matters for public access, collection development, circulation, curriculum support, game studies scholarship, archives, and future digital preservation. The article pairs well with Welhouse et al. (2015) on narrative/plot metadata and with later artifact-metadata work by Lee et al. and McDonald et al.

The limitations are those of a standards-history article. de Groat does not evaluate patron search behavior, local catalog interfaces, or the effect of catalog records on circulation or use. It is also necessarily technical. Still, it is one of the most important infrastructure sources for GBLS because it shows that the metadata problem is not incidental; it is rooted in decades of rule changes, MARC constraints, Library of Congress policy gaps, and the mismatch between book-centered cataloging traditions and complex interactive media.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: de Groat (2015) provides the historical foundation for game cataloging as a GBLS infrastructure problem. Her account shows that U.S. video game cataloging moved through shifting AACR2, MARC, and RDA treatments of machine-readable data files, computer files, electronic resources, moving images, carriers, system requirements, and platform notes, while subject, genre, and preferred-title practices remained tied to book-centered Library of Congress policies that never fully accounted for games as complex interactive works.

Section and exact location: `Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda > Infrastructure and Metadata Gaps`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: de Groat (2015) also clarifies why metadata gaps persist: platform information is often trapped in notes or improvised fields, game genre headings are inconsistent or topical rather than true genre/form access points, "computer games" and "video games" are not cleanly distinguished, franchise and title authority work lacks clear guidance, and the Library of Congress no longer catalogs games within the workflow that generates the policies most U.S. libraries follow. These gaps limit discovery, advisory, preservation, and scholarly use of game collections.

# **Wood, K., & Carter, D. S. (2018). Art and technology: Archiving video games for humanities research in university libraries. *Art Libraries Journal, 43*(4), 185-195. https://doi.org/10.1017/alj.2018.29**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `PF6CY459`; Better BibTeX citation key `woodArtTechnologyArchiving2018`; attachment key `LEI83BZ7`.

Wood and Carter argue that video games have become significant cultural, technological, and artistic objects that require collection, curation, preservation, and access in university libraries. The article combines a literature review on academic video game collections with a case study of the University of Michigan's Computer & Video Game Archive (CVGA), one of the largest academic game archives of its kind. For GBLS, its value lies in showing how game collections can support humanities research, pedagogy, visual analysis, art history, film/media studies, game studies, and interdisciplinary scholarship, not only recreation.

The article opens historically by linking early computer program libraries and the circulation of *SPACEWAR* in 1962. The point is that games and library-like systems for storing, testing, cataloging, and sharing software co-evolved from the beginning of computing culture. Wood and Carter then situate contemporary video games as transmedial objects: games can move across boards, cartridges, disks, consoles, computers, handheld devices, servers, downloads, phones, tablets, and virtual reality environments. This transmedial state makes game collection and cataloging difficult because the "same" game may depend on specific hardware, software, interface, network, carrier, and control conditions.

The literature review emphasizes that university libraries collect games for several overlapping reasons: faculty research, classroom instruction, student curricular needs, entertainment, game design programs, historical preservation, and access to culturally important media. The authors cite selection criteria that include technical merit, aesthetic quality, gameplay, educational value, maintenance, ease of use, cost, language, teaching relevance, real-world skills, historical value, and cultural value. They also note persistent issues such as violent or sexual content, censorship concerns, digital rights management, online games, company partnerships, and the need for collaboration across libraries when access barriers exceed one institution's capacity.

The University of Michigan CVGA case gives the article its concrete service model. Opened in 2008, the CVGA housed nearly 8,000 games from more than seventy systems by 2018, along with board and card games, tabletop role-playing games, books, player guides, game novelizations, graphic novels, gaming magazines, secondary sources, and ephemera. In 2017 it welcomed nearly 5,000 visitors and recorded use of more than 16,000 collection items. Users can browse games on shelves and play them at gaming stations, including space for motion and VR gaming and tables for board games. Because the CVGA maintains both software and hardware, the collection is limited to in-house use and is staffed during open hours.

The preservation discussion is especially useful for GBLS infrastructure. A representative game archive must manage cartridges, cassette tapes, magnetic disks, optical discs, digital downloads, consoles, handhelds, computers, controllers, and other access equipment. Some items are difficult to acquire in good condition, especially microcomputer games from the 1970s and early 1980s that circulated through flea markets, plastic bags, magazines, or hand-typed code. Some media are more fragile than others: cartridges may be robust, while each cassette load may risk failure. The CVGA chose not to take a "white glove" approach; instead, it deliberately allows games to be played and experienced by users, accepting wear as the cost of access. Mitigation strategies include archival sleeves, basic repair skills, and caches of replacement systems and controllers.

The mission and use cases show why university game archives matter for research. The CVGA supports teaching, class-directed research, independent research, and open recreational use. Courses and projects draw from art, music, history, literature, education, psychology, computer science, engineering, language instruction, and game design. Examples include studies of race and ethnicity in the *FIFA* series, race options in character generation across role-playing games, time in *The Legend of Zelda*, games about historical trauma and the American Civil War, video game music for non-music majors, samurai representation in games, programming inspiration for game-creation courses, engineering research using racing games, and board games for instructional English practice.

Wood and Carter also make a humanities argument for game libraries. They position game studies as a multidisciplinary field shaped by questions from anthropology, history, textual studies, computer science, sociology, media studies, cinema studies, design studies, visual and material culture, and art history. The article connects library collections to debates over ludology and narratology, race and representation, aesthetics, politics, avant-garde games, contemporary art, museum exhibitions, and game images as research objects. They argue that libraries can shape the field by providing subject guides, co-housing games with exhibition catalogs and game-studies journals, and partnering with visual resource centers to include still and video captures from games in image databases with consistent metadata.

For the larger GBLS project, Wood and Carter strengthen the review's claim that games are cultural media and research infrastructure. A game archive is not only a storage room or recreation lab; it is a space where hardware, software, staff expertise, cataloging, repair, preservation, visual resources, related scholarship, classroom support, and public access converge. The article also clarifies the tension at the center of game preservation: preserving playable experience requires allowing use, but use creates wear. Libraries must therefore balance access and preservation while keeping enough contextual and technical infrastructure to let future researchers understand games as aesthetic, technological, historical, and social artifacts.

The article is a case study and conceptual essay rather than an assessment study. It does not provide patron survey data or long-term preservation metrics. Its strongest evidence comes from the University of Michigan CVGA description and the broader literature on academic game collections. Even so, it is a strong GBLS source because it explains why academic libraries are essential to game studies and why game labs and archives should be considered service models for research, scholarship, and creative production.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Research, Scholarship, and Creative Production`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Wood and Carter (2018) make research and creative production a concrete GBLS service area through their case study of the University of Michigan Computer & Video Game Archive, which housed nearly 8,000 games across more than seventy systems alongside secondary sources, tabletop games, ephemera, hardware, staffed access, and in-house play. Their account shows that university game collections support game studies, art history, film and media studies, music, history, computer science, engineering, language instruction, and creative game design, while also requiring preservation choices about playable access, fragile media, controllers, repair, replacement hardware, cataloging, and visual-resource metadata.

# **Littman, D. M., Milligan, T., Berry, R., Holloway, B., & Scott, M. L. (2024). What do recently housed young people imagine for the future of third places? Using game-based inquiry to (re)imagine affirming, youth-centered third places. *American Journal of Community Psychology, 74*(1-2), 127-141. https://doi.org/10.1002/ajcp.12750**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `PPQP65Q2`; Better BibTeX citation key `littmanWhatRecentlyHoused2024`; attachment key `2RB84RRJ`.

Littman, Milligan, Berry, Holloway, and Scott use game-based inquiry to ask recently housed young people with experiences of chronic housing instability how they would (re)imagine future third places. The article is not a library program evaluation in a narrow sense, but it is highly relevant to GBLS because libraries are explicitly named as third places, and because the method treats play as a way to surface design knowledge from young people who have often been excluded, surveilled, or displaced in public and community settings. The authors frame play as both a human right and a portal into imagining new ways of being when ordinary social hierarchies can be suspended.

The study took place with residents at Laurel House Apartments, a permanent supportive housing program for young adults in Grand Junction, Colorado. The sample included 21 residents across six game sessions. Rather than using a survey or interview alone, the researchers designed the *Future of Third Places Game*, an original board game in which players entered a future world, filled out worldbuilding and personal expertise cards, responded to community request cards, and built physical or social design elements from craft materials. The request cards were grounded in literature and prior conversations with residents about barriers in present-day third places, such as being stared at while eating, lacking safe places to rest, or needing places to gather without spending money. Sessions lasted roughly an hour to an hour and a half, were recorded and transcribed, and were analyzed through thematic analysis using Oldenburg and Brissett's third-place framework as an initial lens.

The central findings revise conventional third-place theory for young people who have experienced housing instability. Participants argued, through their designs and explanations, that affirming third places must offer agency and individualization, meet everyday needs, and be explicitly inclusive. These were not abstract values. Participants imagined places that meet many needs and do many things; portable and adaptable physical spaces; freedom to choose how to play; choice for privacy or connection; practices that attend to and subvert oppressive social hierarchies; the assurance that people will be around; and free amenities. Designs included mobile community centers, sleeping pods, multipurpose community-center rooms, under-21 social spaces, adult parks, hang pods that could be open or closed, drop-in centers where adults support youth without being in charge, always-open places, free food, barter systems, indoor games, craft areas, and sober community spaces.

For libraries, the article's strongest contribution is its insistence that inclusion cannot be presumed from openness or neutrality. Participants challenged the idea that third places automatically flatten hierarchy simply because they are public. They wanted spaces where adult power, classism, ageism, ableism, hostile architecture, anti-loitering norms, and cost barriers are actively addressed. They also complicated the assumption that third places are primarily social. For young people whose first and second places have failed to provide safety, shelter, food, rest, or belonging, third places may need to meet everyday physical and relational needs as well as offer play.

The methodological contribution is equally important for GBLS. The game was not used merely to make research fun; it created an inquiry structure in which participants could work from fictionalized community requests, make artifacts, narrate design decisions, collaborate or play solo, and keep one imagined design they wished existed in the present world. This suggests a practical use for libraries: game-based inquiry can become a community planning and co-design tool for teen services, youth spaces, makerspaces, game rooms, VR labs, outreach programs, or any service that claims to be youth-centered. A library could adapt the game to invite patrons to design third-place features, then translate imagined elements into policies, furniture choices, staffing models, hours, amenities, privacy options, and program formats.

The article should be used carefully. It is based on a small qualitative sample in one Western Colorado supportive-housing site, and the game cards were intentionally shaped by prior knowledge of participants' experiences, which may have elevated some needs over others. The researchers also chose not to collect individualized demographic information for ethical and relational reasons, limiting intersectional analysis. Even with those limits, the source is valuable because it gives GBLS a design-justice vocabulary for thinking about youth-centered library play spaces: agency, everyday needs, explicit inclusion, adaptable space, free access, privacy, connection, and anti-oppressive facilitation.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Littman et al. (2024) extend the third-place argument through game-based inquiry with recently housed young people who had experienced chronic housing instability. In the *Future of Third Places Game*, participants used worldbuilding cards, community request cards, and craft-based design to imagine spaces that offered agency and individualization, met everyday needs, and were explicitly inclusive. For GBLS, the study shows that library-as-third-place programs cannot rely on nominal openness or neutrality; youth-centered game spaces should actively address privacy and connection, free amenities, food and rest, under-21 social life, portable or adaptable space, supportive adults who are not simply "in charge," and practices that subvert rather than reproduce hostile or exclusionary public-space norms.

# **Brennan, R. (2023). Wellbeing in the school library. *School Librarian, 71*(2), 10-11.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `QKJ77I42`; Better BibTeX citation key `brennanWellbeingSchoolLibrary2023`; attachment key `Q7RFJJHM`.

Brennan's short practitioner feature describes a school library redevelopment at Stonyhurst College with wellbeing, purpose, and community as its organizing terms. The article is written from the perspective of a newly appointed college librarian who arrived after the library had gone several years without a dedicated librarian. The More Library was architecturally impressive, but its main role had narrowed to silent study. Brennan treats that quiet function as valuable, especially in a boarding-school setting where many pupils need calm space away from busy houses and playrooms, but argues that the library could also become a more welcoming and inclusive third place for students' social and emotional needs.

The intervention began with environmental and access-oriented changes rather than a named game program. Brennan streamlined cluttered areas, updated displays and signage, added plants, motivational posters, and artwork, and worked to make the library more visible and inviting from the corridor. The emphasis is on small, cumulative changes that shift the affective identity of the library: students should experience it not only as a place to complete coursework before deadlines, but as a student-centered space where wellbeing is visible and where pupils feel permitted to spend informal time.

The Zotero abstract and article metadata identify several later additions that connect the piece to GBLS. Brennan encouraged students to visit during breaks and introduced chess, computer games, and film screenings to foster friendships and conversations. These activities are not described in evaluative detail, but they matter because they frame games as part of a wellbeing ecology rather than as standalone entertainment. Chess and computer games appear alongside displays, social space, films, Mental Health First Aid training, and a Reading Well collection. The service logic is that playful and social activities can help students enter the library, connect with peers, talk more easily, and experience the library as a supportive third place.

For the larger GBLS project, Brennan is a modest but useful source for the emerging wellness, mental health, and care section. It shows how games can be folded into ordinary school-library wellbeing work without requiring a large program, new technology suite, or formal curriculum. The article also pairs well with third-place sources: the library is explicitly positioned between home and school, which is especially significant in a boarding context where those categories blur. Games, in this account, help widen the library's repertoire from silent study to community, conversation, relaxation, and belonging.

The limits are substantial. This is a reflective practitioner article, not a research study; it provides no participant data, outcome measures, or detailed description of the chess and computer-game activities. The attached PDF appears to include only the first page of the article, while the citation and abstract indicate pages 10-11. The source should therefore be cited cautiously and used as illustrative practice evidence rather than proof of effectiveness. Its value is in naming a service pattern: low-barrier game and media activities can support a wellbeing-oriented school library when embedded in broader changes to space, staffing, mental-health awareness, reading support, and student-centered culture.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Emerging Area: Wellness, Mental Health, and Care`, immediately after the Lee et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Brennan (2023) offers a small school-library practitioner example of games as part of a wellbeing ecology rather than a standalone program. In redeveloping Stonyhurst College's library from a primarily silent study space into a more welcoming third place, Brennan introduced break-time activities including chess, computer games, and film screenings alongside environmental changes, Mental Health First Aid training, and a Reading Well collection. The article is illustrative rather than evaluative, but it shows how low-barrier play can support friendship, conversation, relaxation, and belonging within a broader student-centered mental-health service model.

# **Broussard, M. J. S. (2014). Using games to make formative assessment fun in the academic library. *The Journal of Academic Librarianship, 40*(1), 35-42. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.acalib.2012.12.001**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `5DNWG3IV`; Better BibTeX citation key `broussardUsingGamesMake2014`; attachment key `EY7TZQJI`.

Broussard argues that academic library instruction needs more formative, in-the-moment assessment and that educational games can make that assessment less intimidating for both students and librarians. The article begins from a familiar pressure in higher education: libraries are expected to show evidence of learning, but many common assessment tools are summative, large-scale, expensive, and too late to improve learning during the session itself. For instruction librarians, especially those working in "one shot" settings, Broussard argues that formative assessment is particularly valuable because it lets librarians and students exchange feedback while there is still time to adjust instruction.

The article's conceptual move is to connect formative assessment and game-based learning. Broussard defines formative assessment as frequent, informal feedback used by the instructor to modify instruction while learning is still happening. Educational games, in turn, are treated as environments where players try out skills, receive feedback, revise strategies, and move toward harder tasks. Drawing on game studies and education literature, Broussard argues that games and formative assessment share eight elements: active learning, feedback, motivation, moratoriums, scaffolding, critical thinking and self-assessment, social learning, and stealth assessment. This makes games promising not because they add novelty to instruction, but because they can turn evidence of learning into part of the activity itself.

Broussard grounds the argument in two information literacy games developed at Lycoming College's Snowden Library. *Secret Agents in the Library* was a one-hour freshman composition game that mixed online activities with physical exploration of the library. Students worked in teams of "rookie agents" to investigate an intruder in the library's "information mainframe," using reference materials, books, databases, and MLA citation style along the way. The game ended with a clicker-based debrief, allowing the librarian to identify confusion and provide mini-instruction when needed. *Goblin Threat* was an online plagiarism game in which players cleared rooms by finding hidden plagiarism goblins and answering questions about citation and academic honesty. Incorrect answers produced mini-tutorials and repeat opportunities; correct answers advanced the player and gave progress feedback.

The article is especially useful because it treats feedback design as instructional design. Games can provide specific, frequent, immediate, and adaptive feedback through scores, sounds, mini-tutorials, progress reports, access to new spaces, and facilitator coaching. In Broussard's examples, wrong answers do not simply mark failure; they create chances to revise understanding. The concept of moratoriums is also important: game spaces can let students practice imperfectly with reduced consequences, which is especially appropriate in library sessions where the goal is preparation for later research work rather than graded performance during the library visit.

Broussard also emphasizes that games alone are rarely enough. Debriefing, clicker review, discussion, journaling, and other wraparound assessments help players transfer game experience into real-world information practices. This point matters because several of the library games discussed in the article are technically simple; Broussard argues that good learning design is more important than technological polish. Carefully written questions, purposeful scaffolding, social play, humor, role play, and post-game reflection can all make simple games instructionally meaningful. At the same time, games are not appropriate for every learning objective, and library games require faculty buy-in, attention to design flaws, and early alignment between objectives and game activities.

For the GBLS project, Broussard provides one of the clearest bridges between game-based library instruction and assessment. The article supports a view of GBLS in which games are not only outreach tools or engagement devices but also environments for observing learning, correcting misconceptions, encouraging self-assessment, and making formative feedback feel like help rather than evaluation. It also strengthens the review's recurring claim that facilitation and reflection are core infrastructure. The evidence base is not experimental; the article is primarily conceptual and illustrative, using prior literature and two local game examples rather than a controlled outcome study. Still, it gives GBLS a vocabulary for designing information literacy games around active learning, feedback, scaffolding, safe failure, debriefing, and assessment beyond attendance.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Smith and Baker (2011) paragraph.

Suggested text: Broussard (2014) links academic-library instruction games directly to formative assessment. Using *Secret Agents in the Library* and *Goblin Threat* as examples, she argues that games can make one-shot information literacy instruction more responsive by embedding feedback, scaffolding, safe failure, social learning, self-assessment, and debriefing into play, so evidence of learning appears while librarians can still correct misconceptions.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Brown and Kasper (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Broussard (2014) also reframes assessment itself as a design problem: games should connect learning objectives to activities early, provide specific and immediate feedback, and use wraparound reflection such as clicker review or discussion to help students transfer game experience into later research behavior. This supports GBLS assessment approaches that look for formative evidence during play, not only post-program satisfaction.

# **Bell, L., & Trueman, R. B. (Eds.). (2008). *Virtual worlds, real libraries: Librarians and educators in Second Life and other multi-user virtual environments*. Information Today.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `UU8SVMJT`; Better BibTeX citation key `bellVirtualWorldsReal2008`; attachment key `DK8XX22Y`.

Bell and Trueman's edited volume is an early snapshot of library and education projects in Second Life and other multi-user virtual environments (MUVEs). The available Zotero PDF is a two-page published book review, not the full 272-page volume, and the Zotero notes provide the table of contents. This entry therefore treats the source as a map of an important early professional conversation rather than as chapter-level evidence. Even in that bounded form, the book is directly relevant to GBLS because it documents librarians experimenting with services inside game-like networked environments where avatars, place, presence, simulation, and community shaped what library work could look like.

The review frames the volume as the first text aimed at helping librarians participate in Second Life and other MUVEs. Its audience included school, public, academic, research-library, and library-school contexts. The book was organized into three broad sections: virtual worlds and libraries; the Alliance Virtual Library Project; and virtual worlds and education. The table of contents points to chapters on library, education, and museum applications for children, tweens, and teens; massively multiplayer online worlds; edutainment before Second Life; the Alliance Virtual Library Project; management in virtual environments; reference services in a virtual world; collections in virtual environments; virtual branch libraries; Teen Second Life; HealthInfo Island; speculative fiction and mystery programming; historical environments such as Renaissance Island and Land of Lincoln; and instructional uses of Second Life for distance learning, research help, and MLIS teaching.

For GBLS, the strongest contribution is the way the volume broadens "game-based library service" beyond discrete games or programs. The review describes virtual-world library projects as service environments: librarians created branch libraries, reference desks, collections, author programs, book discussions, displays, health-information islands, historical simulations, teen spaces, and classroom extensions inside virtual worlds. This matters because the game-like world is not merely a wrapper around a lesson. It becomes the service site itself, with spatial design, avatar etiquette, in-world objects, collaboration, and a felt sense of presence shaping how patrons encounter librarians, collections, and programs.

The review also surfaces operational tensions that remain relevant for immersive GBLS. Virtual worlds can reduce some barriers of traditional virtual reference, such as depersonalization and channel loss, by giving users embodied presence and shared space. At the same time, they introduce social challenges, including the perception that the environment is "only a game," institutional challenges such as funding and staffing, and personal or behavioral challenges such as etiquette and disruption. The reviewer also notes hardware and server limitations for youth programs, the steep learning curve for novice librarians, and the difficulty of documenting fast-changing virtual platforms in a static book.

Several service areas named in the review connect directly to the current lit review. HealthInfo Island shows virtual worlds as a platform for consumer-health outreach and partnership with medical librarians and health professionals. Active Worlds and Appalachian State University's Information Gardens show virtual worlds as distance-learning library infrastructure. Teen Second Life and library, education, and museum projects show how youth services can experiment with virtual worlds, while also needing attention to platform suitability, technical requirements, and safety. The book's education chapters suggest immersive learning scenarios, such as replicas of historic sites, where three-dimensional virtual presence may support instruction in ways a flat webpage cannot.

The source should be cited carefully. Because the attached PDF is a review by Elisheba Muturi rather than the full edited book, it does not provide enough evidence to evaluate individual chapters or outcomes. Its best use in the GBLS review is to mark an early professional moment when librarians treated Second Life and related MUVEs as real service venues, not just recreational curiosities. It also helps connect later VR, AR, metaverse, and virtual-platform sources to older library experiments with avatar-based reference, in-world collections, virtual branches, health outreach, youth environments, and distance-learning support.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Bell and Trueman's edited volume (2008), represented here through its table of contents and a contemporary book review, marks an early library moment in Second Life and other multi-user virtual environments. The volume maps virtual branch libraries, avatar-based reference, in-world collections, Teen Second Life, HealthInfo Island, historical simulations, speculative-fiction and mystery programming, distance-learning support, and MLIS instruction, showing that game-like virtual worlds were treated as service sites rather than only promotional novelties. Its limits are also instructive for later GBLS: virtual platforms required staff learning, institutional support, etiquette management, hardware capacity, and arguments against the perception that work inside a virtual world was "only a game."

# **Nicholson, S. (2013). Playing in the past: A history of games, toys, and puzzles in North American libraries. *The Library Quarterly, 83*(4), 341-361. https://doi.org/10.1086/671913**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `TRYWDQ2Z`; Better BibTeX citation key `nicholsonPlayingHistoryGames2013`; attachment key `MDH7RASX`.

Nicholson provides a historical synthesis of games, toys, puzzles, and play in North American libraries from the nineteenth century through the early 2010s. The article directly challenges the idea that gaming is a new or anomalous library service. Instead, Nicholson argues that libraries have supported games for more than 150 years through programs, collections, recreation, education, community engagement, school support, research collections, digital literacy, and outreach to underserved patrons. The article is not a comprehensive census of every gaming program, but a documentary history drawn from research articles, library magazines, newspaper notices, event announcements, professional books, and historical examples.

The article begins by defining games broadly as forms of play with goals and structure, including board games, card games, computer games, role-playing games, console games, and other forms. Nicholson distinguishes between collections of games that patrons may borrow or use and gaming programs in which players gather at a specific time for facilitated play. This definitional move is useful for GBLS because it prevents the review from reducing "games" to videogames or treating game-based service as only event programming. In Nicholson's account, games can be materials, services, social occasions, instructional tools, research objects, and collection-development problems.

The historical arc starts with rational recreation and moral betterment in the nineteenth century. Libraries and related social-reform institutions used controlled recreational spaces, game rooms, billiards, chess, and morally themed board games as alternatives to public houses and gambling. The Mechanics' Institute Library in San Francisco, founded in 1854, is identified as home to the oldest still-existing chess club in the United States. Nicholson uses chess to show both continuity and resistance: libraries have long hosted strategic play, but some have also treated games as inappropriate disruptions, as in a New Rochelle case where a patron was arrested after playing chess at a reference table.

During the Great Depression, Nicholson shows, libraries responded to economic stress by supporting shared recreational resources. Toy libraries emerged when children needed access to toys and games, and some early models explicitly linked borrowing to responsibility and character formation. Puzzle contests also strained and activated libraries: patrons used dictionaries and reference works to solve prize puzzles, and libraries responded either by limiting access, protecting materials, teaching research strategies, or turning puzzle-solving into a reference display. These examples matter because they show that game-related services have long involved the same tensions that contemporary GBLS faces: resource allocation, staff time, noise, damage, patron demand, public criticism, and opportunities for instruction.

The article then traces games through public, school, academic, and special collections contexts. Public libraries used games to reach boys' clubs, young people, seniors, and other patrons not already engaged by ordinary services. Children and teens created their own games in library programs; summer reading programs used game structures, puzzles, mysteries, points, and prizes; and intergenerational programs used older adults' game knowledge as a community resource. School libraries collected and facilitated games for instruction, including treasure hunts, Dewey Decimal games, Jeopardy-style quizzes, board-game research projects, classroom game collections, and standards-aligned recreational games. Academic libraries built game collections for research, curriculum, and leisure, from playing-card archives and gambling collections to undergraduate videogame collections supporting game studies and design programs.

Nicholson's digital history is especially helpful because it places videogames inside a longer library-service continuum rather than treating them as a rupture. Libraries used computer games to introduce children and adults to computers, created circulating Atari 2600 collections in the early 1980s, hosted Dungeons & Dragons, Scrabble, video-game trading days, CD-ROM collections, virtual-world interests, Wii bowling for seniors, and large videogame collections. By the late 2000s, ALA initiatives, National Gaming Day, practitioner books, and the Games and Gaming Round Table helped normalize gaming as "just another library service."

For the GBLS project, Nicholson is a cornerstone historical source. It supports the review's argument that game-based library services are not a fad but a recurring adaptation of library missions to changing cultural forms, leisure practices, technologies, and patron needs. It also supplies a unifying service logic: games and supporting materials are information containers that can be selected, described, circulated, facilitated, assessed, and connected to other library resources. Nicholson's conclusion is also a useful caution. Gaming should not be justified only as a lure or reward, because extrinsic reward structures can weaken intrinsic motivation; instead, gaming services should be aligned with library goals and evaluated like any other service. The article's limitations are those of a historical survey: it compiles illustrative evidence rather than measuring outcomes. Even so, it gives GBLS the long view needed to frame contemporary videogame, tabletop, escape-room, VR, maker, and gamification work as part of a durable pattern of libraries supporting play, learning, exploration, and community.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Introduction: Why Game-Based Library Services Matter > The role of Libraries is everchanging`, after the Burek Pierce (2016) paragraph.

Suggested text: Nicholson (2013) broadens this historical frame by showing that games, toys, puzzles, and play have appeared in North American library collections and services for more than 150 years, from nineteenth-century chess rooms and rational recreation to Depression-era toy lending and puzzle contests, school-library games, summer reading challenges, academic game collections, videogame circulation, Wii bowling, National Gaming Day, and game creation. This history reframes GBLS as a recurring library response to culture, recreation, learning, and community need rather than as a recent digital novelty.

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Game Modalities in Library Contexts`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Nicholson (2013) also helps define the GBLS service field by distinguishing game collections from gaming programs and by treating games broadly as structured forms of play across board, card, computer, role-playing, console, puzzle, toy, and hybrid formats. His historical survey shows that games can function as materials, programs, research objects, social infrastructure, digital-literacy tools, outreach mechanisms, and collection-development challenges.

# **Guo, S.-C., & Lan, Y.-J. (2023). Virtual world-supported contextualized multimodal EFL learning at a library. *Language Learning & Technology, 27*(2), 176-198. https://hdl.handle.net/10125/73508**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `43HBDL97`; Better BibTeX citation key `guoVirtualWorldsupportedContextualized2023`; attachment key `RSXJGFW3`.

Guo and Lan report an action-research study of young English as a foreign language learners in a public-library setting in northern Taiwan.
