The article begins from the premise that libraries are informal learning spaces with reading resources, language programs, and emerging digital technologies, but that relatively little research has examined library courses that combine story reading with virtual construction for EFL reading. The study is therefore directly relevant to GBLS because it tests a library-based learning activity that combines picture storybooks, word games, story creation, and a 3D virtual world authoring tool.

The study compared two one-day library reading projects, each lasting six hours and each involving 19 learners from grades 4-6, ages 10-12. The first project used picture storybook reading, reading questions and answers, and English games such as memory games and charades. After the first project improved reading scores but did not produce satisfactory motivation and anxiety results, the instructors revised the design. The second project kept picture storybook reading but added Omni-immersion Vision (OIV), a desktop 3D authoring tool that lets learners navigate virtual sites, place interactive objects, create contexts, and communicate through text or oral modes. Learners first planned stories on posters and then worked in small teams to create 3D virtual environments connected to those stories.

The quantitative results show a useful but cautious pattern. Both groups improved on English reading tests: the picture-book group improved by 21.58 points on average, and the picture-book-plus-VR group improved by 26.84 points, though the difference in improvement between groups was not statistically significant. The affective measures were more distinctive. The PB+VR group showed higher intrinsic and extrinsic motivation, lower learning anxiety, and more positive attitudes toward technology than the PB group; the significant extrinsic-motivation gain suggests that creation in VR can help motivate young learners during informal library learning. The authors argue that VR reduced anxiety partly because learners focused on the environment, avatars, visual effects, and construction task rather than on foreign-language performance alone.

The qualitative analysis is especially useful for GBLS design. The researchers analyzed how learners moved from written notes to drawings to VR construction through semiotic and multimodal meaning-making. In VR, students used fewer written text elements and many more creatures, buildings, natural items, furniture, objects, vehicles, and interactive questions. The virtual environment gave them a larger canvas and new semiotic resources, but also required spatial skills and careful planning. Teams used bilingual text, bulletin boards, directions, and question-posing features to guide visitors through their story worlds. The study therefore treats the virtual world not merely as visual decoration but as a medium for transmodal redesign: students moved meanings across writing, drawing, 3D objects, spatial arrangement, interaction, and audience navigation.

For the larger GBLS project, Guo and Lan strengthen the argument that library game and immersive-technology programs can support literacy when they are embedded in a purposeful learning sequence. The article connects traditional library strengths, such as story reading and language programs, with playful activities and maker-like digital creation. It also offers a useful program pattern: begin with accessible texts, use low-stakes games and read-aloud work, introduce the virtual tool separately from the language task, have learners plan collaboratively on paper, then let them build and share virtual worlds that carry story meaning. This pattern is valuable for libraries considering VR, virtual worlds, Minecraft-like tools, or 3D creation as literacy supports rather than technology demonstrations.

The limitations are important. The two projects were held two months apart rather than as a randomized simultaneous comparison, each lasted only one day, and the sample was small. The PB+VR group began with higher pre-test scores and motivation, which complicates causal interpretation. The study also used one virtual construction platform, and some learner difficulties may have resulted from limited practice with 3D spatial positioning rather than the learning design itself. Still, the article provides one of the clearer empirical examples of a library-based virtual-world program with pre/post reading tests, motivation and anxiety measures, and analysis of student-created artifacts.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the Bell and Trueman (2008) paragraph.

Suggested text: Guo and Lan (2023) provide a more empirical virtual-platform example through two public-library EFL reading projects with 38 children in Taiwan. After a picture-storybook, Q&A, and word-game project improved reading scores but left motivation and anxiety concerns, the revised project added OIV, a desktop 3D virtual-world authoring tool, so learners could plan stories on posters and then build multimodal story worlds with text, objects, spatial cues, and interactive questions. The PB+VR group improved reading, showed higher motivation and lower anxiety, and produced richer multimodal artifacts, suggesting that library virtual-world programs work best when they connect immersive creation to literacy goals, planning, collaboration, feedback, and artifact sharing rather than treating VR as novelty.

# **Buller, R. (2017). Lending video game consoles in an academic library. *portal: Libraries and the Academy, 17*(2), 337-346. https://doi.org/10.1353/pla.2017.0020**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `EYN2H24R`; Better BibTeX citation key `bullerLendingVideoGame2017`; attachment key `K922BR7Z`.

Buller describes how the University of Denver's University Libraries created a video game console lending service to support an emerging interdisciplinary video game design curriculum in the Emergent Digital Practices program. The article is a practical academic-library case study rather than an outcomes study, but it is valuable because it treats consoles as curricular access infrastructure. The library did not frame the service primarily as outreach, entertainment, or a way to attract a demographic group. Instead, the rationale was that students and faculty needed hardware in order to study, compare, critique, and create video games across platforms.

The project began when a faculty grant supported development of a video game collection for courses involving programming, design, writing, and music. After meeting with the grant recipient, library faculty and staff decided that their contribution would be to acquire and circulate the hardware needed to use the game collection. Buller situates this decision in a broader shift in academic libraries: as game design programs grow, libraries may need to support not only books and journals about games but also the games, platforms, peripherals, and use conditions that make game study possible. This is similar to libraries providing microfilm readers, record players, VHS equipment, laptops, or tablets when access to content depends on nonbook hardware.

The article's central decision point was whether to build a dedicated library gaming space or circulate consoles. A dedicated space would have offered more control over equipment care and would have helped students without televisions. But the University of Denver library occupied a newly renovated academic commons where group study rooms were heavily used and space was scarce. A dedicated room would have supported only one console at a time and reduced access to other popular services. Circulating consoles, by contrast, gave patrons more time with hardware in the setting where consoles were designed to be used and better supported detailed study of platform affordances. The library chose circulation as a pilot because the service goal was curricular support rather than drawing patrons into the building.

The hardware choices followed the curriculum. The library purchased the current and prior generations of the three major console families at the time: Sony PlayStation 3 and 4, Microsoft Xbox 360 and Xbox One, and Nintendo Wii and Wii U. This enabled students to compare games across platforms and generations. To reduce barriers for students without televisions and to protect the equipment, the library purchased GAEMS Personal Gaming Environments for most consoles, providing portable screens, speakers, and durable hard cases. Additional carrying cases held controllers, cords, and peripherals.

Buller's circulation details are especially useful for GBLS operations. The consoles circulated for one week, matching the loan period for the video games purchased with the grant, and allowed two renewals unless another patron placed a hold. Holds were important because the library did not know how popular the service would be and wanted to prevent one borrower from monopolizing equipment. The library also created extensive check-in procedures. Staff used forms with pictures of every component, maintained serial numbers for console parts, verified hardware after each circulation, and checked software accounts before recirculation. Because users could log in with personal gamer profiles and store payment information, staff had to make sure accounts were logged out to protect patron privacy and prevent fraudulent charges.

The article also documents internal resistance. Some staff questioned whether console lending was an appropriate library service and worried about start-up costs and movement away from traditional materials. Library leaders responded by tying the purchases to demonstrated curricular need, likely circulation, and the library's mission to support teaching, research, scholarship, and creative work. This internal discussion became an opportunity to revisit the changing role of the academic library and to build staff understanding of nontraditional collections.

For the GBLS project, Buller adds a concrete console-lending model to the literature on circulating and in-library collections. It shows that access to games may require access to hardware, screens, controllers, accounts, security practices, and staff workflows. It also links game collections to academic programs and creative production, not only recreation. The service was reported as successful: it met circulation expectations without formal advertising, relied on word of mouth from faculty, students, and library staff, kept consoles in good condition through double-checking procedures, expanded to additional controllers based on student feedback, and helped secure further collection support. The limitations are also clear: there is no formal patron survey, learning assessment, cost-benefit analysis, or long-term circulation data in the article. Still, it is a highly transferable operational case for academic libraries deciding whether console hardware belongs in the collection and how to lend it responsibly.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Circulating and In-Library Collections`, after the Robson, Phillips, and Guerrero (2018) paragraph.

Suggested text: Buller (2017) extends circulating game collections from software and tabletop materials to console hardware. At the University of Denver, console lending supported an interdisciplinary game design curriculum by giving students access to multiple platforms and generations, portable screens, controllers, holds, renewals, component checklists, serial-number verification, profile logout procedures, and privacy protections; the case shows that game collections often require lending the access infrastructure around games, not only the games themselves.

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Research, Scholarship, and Creative Production`, after the Wood and Carter (2018) paragraph.

Suggested text: Buller (2017) also links game hardware lending to academic creative production. Because students in game design courses needed to compare how games used different console affordances, the library treated PlayStation, Xbox, and Nintendo systems as mission-aligned curricular resources analogous to other hardware needed to access specialized media.

# **Kirsch, B. (2019). Virtual reality: The next big thing for libraries to consider. *Information Technology and Libraries, 38*(4), 4-5. https://doi.org/10.6017/ital.v38i4.11847**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `L9HTX4RM`; Better BibTeX citation key `kirschVirtualRealityNext2019`; attachment key `X43TQ6IU`.

Kirsch's short *Information Technology and Libraries* editorial is a practitioner reflection on virtual reality after the 2019 EDUCAUSE Annual Conference. It is useful for the GBLS review because it captures a moment when higher education libraries were beginning to treat VR as a service area akin to makerspaces, device lending, laptops, tablets, 3D printers, and other emerging technologies. The article is not an assessment study, but it names practical service choices and risks that recur in later VR library literature.

The piece begins by observing that EDUCAUSE had more than 20 programs and sessions about virtual reality, alongside broader technology trends such as esports in high schools and higher education. Kirsch notes that some institutions were creating VR content while others were using pre-created content. Content creation was especially visible in medical and health sciences contexts, where VR can let students practice procedures they may encounter infrequently in workplaces, and in STEM contexts, where VR laboratories may simulate expensive equipment or lab experiences.

Kirsch gives particular attention to 360-degree content as a lower-barrier entry point. Creating full VR content in tools such as Unity can be difficult and time-consuming, but educators can use 360-degree cameras, Matterport scans, or even smartphone tools such as Google Street View to build virtual settings. Kirsch reports creating a free, basic 360-degree library tour with an iPhone and Google Street View, noting that it was not high quality enough for headset VR but was a useful budget-conscious starting point. This distinction matters for GBLS because it separates scalable virtual orientation and tour work from more intensive immersive content development.

The article then turns to library service models. Libraries might create 360-degree tours, develop higher-quality VR experiences, teach information literacy or search strategies in VR environments, offer VR labs, circulate standalone headsets such as Oculus Go or Oculus Quest, or support local VR content creation with cameras and software. Kirsch explicitly compares the opportunity to makerspaces: libraries are well situated to support VR in education if they pair access with trained support.

The most concrete GBLS contribution is the risk and operations checklist from Kirsch's own library, which circulated an Oculus Go. She names health risks such as motion sickness, dizziness, and epileptic seizures; the need to tell students to stop use if they have adverse reactions; cleaning wipes and sanitation for shared equipment; app updates and paid-app controls through associated tablets or phones; passcodes to prevent unauthorized purchases; privacy concerns with social apps; the emotional realism of scary apps; and the need for trained staff or student workers who can teach others to use the headset. These concerns align closely with later implementation studies and help make VR a managed library service rather than a novelty purchase.

For the larger GBLS project, Kirsch should be used as practical framing. It supports a conservative implementation argument: libraries can start small with a 360-degree tour or a circulating headset, but they still need policy, training, content selection, privacy decisions, sanitation, safety warnings, and staff capacity. Its limitations are clear: it is brief, anecdotal, and does not report patron outcomes or usage data. Still, it is a useful bridge between enthusiasm for VR and the mundane infrastructure that determines whether patrons can actually use it responsibly.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, immediately after the Lee, King, Dahya, and Lee (2020) paragraph.

Suggested text: Kirsch (2019) adds a concise practitioner checklist for VR services in academic libraries. Drawing on EDUCAUSE sessions and an Oculus Go circulation example, she distinguishes expensive immersive content creation from lower-barrier 360-degree tours, and identifies operational issues that libraries must settle before offering headsets: motion sickness and seizure warnings, sanitation, app updates, passcodes and purchase controls, social-app privacy, the realism of frightening content, and staff or student-worker training. The piece is anecdotal, but it helps translate VR enthusiasm into service policy and support requirements.

# **Dahya, N., Lee, J. H., Lee, K. J., King, W. E., Goel, M., & Yassin, H. (2019). *Virtual reality in public libraries*. University of Washington Information School. https://ischool.uw.edu/vrinlibraries**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `PX3RT98L`; Better BibTeX citation key `negindahyaVirtualRealityPublic`; attachment key `NHCXCGQC`.

Dahya, Lee, Lee, King, Goel, and Yassin's report presents the broader Washington State public-library VR project that later informs Lee, King, Dahya, and Lee (2020). It is worth summarizing separately because the report includes patron survey and interview findings, site observations, demographics, and practice recommendations that are only partly visible in the later article. The project was funded by Washington State Libraries, the Office of the Secretary of State, and Oculus, and studied VR programming at seven public-library sites in Washington, including urban, regional, and rural locations.

The study asked what happens when public libraries offer access to VR as a tool for informal learning and community engagement. Each library designed its own programming, while Oculus provided a suggested list of freely available educational games and experiences. The research team interviewed 23 librarians at the start and end of the project, producing 36 librarian interviews; observed library programs from March through June 2018; collected 185 patron surveys; and interviewed 39 patrons. Approximately 400 patrons experienced VR during the study period, with some returning up to five times.

The report distinguishes between expectations about VR and actual library experiences. Patrons and librarians imagined VR as a tool for learning, empathy, engagement, accessibility, schoolwork, therapy, job training, and safe practice. Yet many of these aspirations were not directly tested through the short drop-in VR experiences actually available in the libraries. Patrons reported intense immersion, interactivity, curiosity, and excitement, but the report cautions that patrons often learned more about VR technology itself than about the content of a particular VR experience. If content learning is the goal, the authors argue, librarians need clear learning outcomes, structured teaching processes, and more deliberate curriculum or pedagogy.

The patron findings complicate simple access narratives. Survey respondents overwhelmingly enjoyed VR, with 99% reporting enjoyment and 84% saying they really enjoyed it; 69% were first-time users; 97% reported learning about VR technology; and 78% reported learning about the experience's content. However, 81.6% of survey respondents reported playing video games, and boys, men, and patrons already comfortable with games were often more likely to try VR. Girls who tried VR enjoyed it, but fewer expressed initial interest. Most patrons using VR were White, though the Tukwila site showed strong participation from its non-White regular patron base. The report therefore treats equitable access as a design problem, not an automatic consequence of putting expensive hardware in a library.

The implementation findings are highly practical. Librarians needed significant lead time to learn the hardware, explore software, plan programming, update equipment, staff events, and manage waivers, especially for youth. The Oculus age restriction disappointed tweens and required alternate activities while siblings or friends used VR. Space mattered: public setups could recruit curious patrons because people saw others using VR, while private rooms reduced self-consciousness and allowed wraparound activities such as Ozobots, Perler beads, slime, 3D pens, and coloring. Promotion also mattered. Posters and web announcements were less effective than themed events, fairs, personal invitations, and clear explanations of why VR might matter locally.

For the larger GBLS project, the report is a strong reminder that VR is not just a technology-access service. It is also a social, spatial, pedagogical, equity, and facilitation problem. Even single-user VR became social when spectators watched the mirrored screen, coached the participant, reacted together, or began conversations with librarians. At the same time, some patrons felt watched or isolated while wearing the headset, and some experienced dizziness, nausea, fear, or disorientation. The report's strongest advice is to connect VR to broader library programs and local interests, build wraparound activities, offer a mix of educational and entertaining content, and move toward creation in VR rather than only consumption.

The report's limitations are useful to state. The study did not show strong evidence that short VR drop-ins produced empathy, deep content learning, or broad social-equity outcomes, despite participants' hopes. Its evidence is strongest for early access, enjoyment, social interaction around the headset, operational lessons, and questions for future programming. That makes it a foundational GBLS source for VR implementation because it resists both hype and dismissal: public libraries can make VR available in meaningful ways, but only with intentional design, outreach, staffing, space, accessibility support, and definitions of value beyond novelty.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, immediately after the Lee, King, Dahya, and Lee (2020) paragraph.

Suggested text: Dahya et al. (2019) provide the broader report behind that Washington public-library VR project, adding patron-side and equity evidence from 185 surveys, 39 patron interviews, site observations, and approximately 400 VR users. The report shows that patrons overwhelmingly enjoyed VR and often learned how to use the technology, but it also complicates claims about empathy, content learning, and equity: participation skewed toward gamers, boys, men, and White patrons except where local patron demographics differed, and meaningful learning required clearer outcomes and wraparound pedagogy. Its implementation guidance emphasizes lead time, staff practice, waivers and age restrictions, public versus private setup, safety, themed outreach, adjacent activities, and movement from VR consumption toward creation.

# **Giles, K., Shuyler, K. S., Evans, A., & Reed, J. (2019). Creating a library orientation card game to reach new transfer students. *Public Services Quarterly, 15*(3), 236-245. https://doi.org/10.1080/15228959.2018.1488643**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `G4QURU9H`; Better BibTeX citation key `gilesCreatingLibraryOrientation2019`; attachment key `BQBCJAUX`.

Giles, Shuyler, Evans, and Reed describe the creation, implementation, and assessment of a library orientation card game for new transfer students at James Madison University. The article begins from a specific service problem: transfer students have distinct academic and social transition needs, but transfer orientation at JMU offered only short, concurrent, optional sessions with uncertain attendance, little setup time, limited staff availability, and no guarantee that students would arrive at the library with much time to spare. The library wanted to replace a short lecture about resources and services with something more interactive, welcoming, and social.

The transfer-student focus is important. The authors note that transfer students may face increased academic demands, uneven information literacy preparation, difficulty finding community, work-life-school pressures, and a need to learn a new institution quickly. Some transfer students miss first-year library instruction normally embedded in introductory or core courses, yet are still expected to navigate upper-level research and campus systems. The library orientation therefore needed to introduce spaces, services, policies, subject librarians, remote database access, and basic library resources while also helping students feel welcome.

The chosen format was a traditional tabletop card game modeled after *Apples to Apples*. JMU librarians already had experience with games, including online citation and database-search games, an Information Literacy Game adaptation, an alternate reality game for first-year engineering majors, and game-based training for student employees. An ARG was rejected for transfer orientation because the session was too short and the group too large; a videogame was impractical because the available study area lacked public computers. A familiar card game could be played at tables, reset quickly, scale to variable attendance, and work without much staff facilitation.

The game itself adapted a student-employee training card game. It used question cards about JMU Libraries and answer cards containing a mix of factual library information, campus references, and humorous or popular-culture content. The project team revised cards to remove internal staff procedures and add patron-facing transfer-student content, such as subject specialist librarians and off-campus database access. During testing, the team discovered a design problem: in the original training game, moderators could explain correct answers, but orientation did not have enough staff for a moderator at every table. The solution was to print the correct answer on the back of each question card so the round judge could reveal it after choosing the winning answer.

The revised game included 27 question cards and 144 answer cards and debuted at transfer orientation in 2015, with about 100 total attendees across two sessions. Facilitators gave a brief greeting and rules explanation, then circulated to answer questions. The cards prompted conversation and laughter; some students asked facilitators about library services and resources, and at least one table exchanged contact information after the session. The library also distributed a handout of the game questions with correct answers, bookmarks, and branded water glasses. Over the next year, the game was reused as a freshman-level icebreaker, at student-athlete orientation, and during new faculty orientation.

The 2016 assessment was modest but useful. Ninety-nine students attended the transfer orientation sessions, and 85 completed an IRB-approved survey. Responses were strongly positive: 93% said the game was enjoyable, 92% said it helped them learn about the library, 86% said it helped them feel more comfortable in the library, and 89% recommended using it in future orientations. Comments were few but generally favorable, describing the game as informative, entertaining, and preferable to reading a pamphlet. Some students wanted more humorous answer cards, while others preferred a more informative session, suggesting a tension between orientation as affective welcome and orientation as information delivery.

For the GBLS project, this article is useful because it shows how game form can be matched to local constraints and audience needs. The *Apples to Apples* structure worked because many students already knew the rules, the game could stop at any point without needing a special endgame, groups could play at different speeds, and students with uneven prior library knowledge could still participate. In fact, the authors argue that a game in which knowing the correct answer was not required to win was well suited to reducing library anxiety and welcoming transfer students. The game also served as a structured social icebreaker, which matters because transfer students may need peer connection and a sense of belonging as much as library facts.

The limitations are clear. The study relies on immediate self-report, facilitator observation, and a simple survey rather than testing learning retention, later library use, or transfer-student social outcomes. It also reports a local, low-cost implementation rather than a generalizable intervention. Still, it offers a transferable design model: adapt a familiar game, build correct answers into the materials when facilitation capacity is limited, use humor and social play to reduce anxiety, provide take-away information after play, and revise cards annually as services, spaces, and cultural references change.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Broussard (2014) paragraph.

Suggested text: Giles, Shuyler, Evans, and Reed (2019) add a low-cost transfer-student orientation model through a JMU Libraries card game based on *Apples to Apples*. Designed for short sessions, uncertain attendance, limited staff, and no computers, the game embedded correct answers on the back of question cards, let groups play at different speeds, and used humor and familiar rules to introduce spaces, services, policies, subject librarians, and remote database access.

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Littman et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Giles et al. (2019) also show how orientation games can address belonging, not only information transfer. For transfer students who may arrive with uneven library preparation and weak social ties at the new institution, the card game created structured conversation, laughter, peer contact, and a less anxious first encounter with the library; 86% of survey respondents said it helped them feel more comfortable in the library.

# **Horban, Y., & Skachenko, O. (2019). A library in the game: Teaching information literacy and preventing plagiarism. *Bibliotechnyi Visnyk, 5*(253), 26-33. https://doi.org/10.15407/bv2019.05.026**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `JTZKAHE4`; Better BibTeX citation key `horbanLibraryGameTeaching2019`; attachment key `UBJHVFGK`.

Horban and Skachenko provide a Ukrainian-language survey of how foreign university libraries use games and gamification to teach information literacy, citation practice, academic integrity, and plagiarism prevention. The article is written from the perspective of the Scientific Library of Kyiv National University of Culture and Arts and explicitly notes that, in Ukrainian library practice, gamification of library activity, information literacy formation, and anti-plagiarism instruction through games had not yet received much specialized study. The authors therefore set out to describe international examples and their potential relevance for Ukrainian academic libraries.

The article uses content analysis, analytical-synthetic processing, and generalization rather than a local implementation study. It reviews library and education literature on gamification, games for learning, and information literacy. James Paul Gee, Maura Smale, Mary Broussard, Jessica Urick Oberlin, Karen Markey, Jennifer McCabe, Steven Wise, and other authors appear as key points of reference. The article also cites the 2018 Horizon Report and game-industry data to argue that games are increasingly important educational technologies and that game-based approaches are especially familiar to students who grew up in digital environments.

The most useful part of the article is its annotated account of digital games and tutorials used by academic libraries in the United States, Canada, and Portugal. Horban and Skachenko discuss the University of Florida's *Gaming Against Plagiarism* project, including its three mini-games: *Cheats and Geeks*, *Frenetic Filing*, and *Murky Misconduct*. In their description, these games ask students to recognize plagiarism, falsification, fabrication, poor citation, insufficient paraphrase, self-plagiarism, and other research-misconduct problems through board-game, sorting, timed, and detective mechanics. The authors also summarize *Gamàgio*, a Portuguese platform game for plagiarism prevention; Lycoming College's *Goblin Threat Plagiarism Game*; Rutgers-Camden's *The Cite is Right!* anti-plagiarism game show; Indiana University's plagiarism tutorials and tests; James Madison University's *Tic-Tac-Toe Citation Game* and *Magnetic Keyword*; the University of Washington's APA and MLA Citation Game; and Acadia University's *You Quote It, You Note It!* citation tutorial.

Across these examples, the authors identify recurring affordances of game-based library activity: challenge, curiosity, risk, competition, and feedback. Many of the games use multiple-choice questions, hidden or timed tasks, points, levels, certificates, progress tracking, avatars, drag-and-drop citation ordering, immediate feedback, repeat attempts, and increasingly difficult questions. The authors argue that these mechanics can motivate students to improve information literacy, choose correct citation types, use reliable research tools, understand academic integrity, and avoid plagiarism in research writing.

For the GBLS project, the article is useful in three ways. First, it shows that game-based information literacy and anti-plagiarism instruction had become visible enough internationally by 2019 to be synthesized for Ukrainian academic librarians, extending the review's geographic frame beyond North American and Anglophone implementation reports. Second, it brings together a set of anti-plagiarism and citation games that can be cross-referenced with more detailed primary sources already represented in the review, especially Broussard and Oberlin, McCabe and Wise, Smale, and Markey et al. Third, it reinforces a practical design point: library games for information literacy tend to work through feedback, repetition, clear goals, short duration, and alignment between game actions and instructional purpose.

The source should be used cautiously. It is a narrative review and descriptive scan, not an empirical assessment of patron learning, and many examples are drawn from public web resources rather than direct observation. The English abstract also summarizes the claims more clearly than the extracted Ukrainian text permits for some details. Still, the article belongs in the GBLS review because it shows how anti-plagiarism and citation games circulate internationally as a recognizable academic-library service model and because it explicitly frames gamified information-literacy instruction as a response to academic dishonesty in higher education.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Giles, Shuyler, Evans, and Reed (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Horban and Skachenko (2019) add an international synthesis of games for information literacy, citation, and plagiarism prevention. Writing for Ukrainian academic librarians, they describe 12 digital games and tutorials from university libraries in the United States, Canada, and Portugal, including *Gaming Against Plagiarism*, *Goblin Threat*, *The Cite is Right!*, *Tic-Tac-Toe Citation Game*, *Magnetic Keyword*, and APA/MLA citation games; the article is descriptive rather than evaluative, but it shows that anti-plagiarism game services had become a recognizable international GBLS pattern.

# **Valenti, S., Lund, B., & Wang, T. (2020). Virtual reality as a tool for student orientation in distance education programs: A study of new library and information science students. *Information Technology and Libraries, 39*(2), 1-12. https://doi.org/10.6017/ital.v39i2.11937**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `FDBYMG7J`; Better BibTeX citation key `valentiVirtualRealityTool2020`; attachment key `6WKCIG8Z`.

Valenti, Lund, and Wang study whether virtual reality can support new-student orientation for distance library and information science students. The article is especially relevant to GBLS because it connects VR, game engagement theory, library-school education, orientation, and distance learning in an experimental design. The authors frame VR as a technology associated with gaming and learning and ask whether its immersive qualities can help students learn about a program, a profession, faculty, library services, and academic spaces when they cannot regularly access the physical campus.

The setting was Emporia State University's School of Library and Information Management, a hybrid MLIS program with cohorts across multiple distance locations in the western United States. New students normally attend a one-day, in-person orientation, but distance learners may still miss the ambient information and sense of presence that campus-based students get from physical spaces, faculty contact, and institutional familiarity. An initial survey of new MLS students showed that 61% had elevated anxiety about their first course, 55% believed that knowing more about faculty, course structure, and program purpose would reduce anxiety, and students wanted information about curriculum, faculty teaching areas, information access, librarianship, library services, and program spaces.

The experimental phase used a pre-test/post-test design with 29 participating students from South Dakota, Idaho, Nevada, and Oregon. Students were assigned by state to either an experimental group or a control group. The experimental group received a cardboard VR headset and instructions for viewing a researcher-created VR orientation. The control group received links containing the same information in a static, non-immersive format. The VR experience was built in Veer VR using 360-degree images captured with a Samsung Gear 360 camera, plus interactive text boxes, video, audio, and transitions. In both conditions, students completed pre-test and post-test measures on orientation knowledge, program anxiety, and perceptions of VR.

The results are mixed but useful. Nearly 60% of the VR group spent more than 25 minutes in the virtual orientation, and only 10% of the experimental group declined to use the headset. Participants in the VR group generally rated the experience as more fun, helpful, engaging, and informative than the text-and-links group, and they felt somewhat more connected to the school. Both groups improved from pre-test to post-test. The VR group had a larger gain because it began with lower pre-test scores, but the difference between groups was not statistically significant. The VR group also reported less post-experiment anxiety than the control group, but that difference was not significant at the conventional .05 level.

The discussion ties the findings to game engagement theory. VR may increase interest, control, immersion, and perceived purpose because users can move through an environment, choose what to inspect, and experience the orientation more like presence than sequential reading. For GBLS design, this is a useful reminder that immersive technology may help with engagement and orientation even when measurable learning gains are modest. The article also cautions that information overload can create information anxiety, especially in immersive environments; VR orientations may work best when they introduce broad concepts, offer freedom and flexibility, and avoid cramming too much new information into one experience.

For the larger GBLS project, Valenti et al. add an experimental orientation model that complements physical library games such as mystery tours and card games. It suggests that VR can be used not only for public technology access but also for institution-specific orientation, remote connection, and professional identity formation. The same design logic could apply to library orientation for online students, branch campuses, adult learners, remote patrons, or anyone who needs to understand library spaces and services before or without visiting in person.

The limitations are substantial. The sample was small, state assignment was not random at the individual level, some headsets arrived late, troubleshooting was difficult for distance students, and several participants dropped out when technology access became frustrating. The study does not show long-term learning, later library use, or sustained anxiety reduction. Still, it provides a rare comparative study in the GBLS-adjacent VR literature and gives libraries a concrete model for asking whether immersive orientation adds value beyond ordinary text-and-link modules.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, immediately after the Broussard (2014) paragraph.

Suggested text: Valenti, Lund, and Wang (2020) extend orientation games into immersive distance education through an experiment with 29 new LIS students. Compared with students who received the same orientation content as static links, the VR group spent substantial time in a 360-degree orientation, reported somewhat more favorable engagement and connection, showed larger but non-significant knowledge gains, and reported a small anxiety decrease. The study is limited by sample size and technical access issues, but it suggests that VR orientation can emulate some forms of physical presence for remote students when designers avoid information overload and connect immersion to clear orientation goals.

# **Jerrett, A., Bothma, T. J. D., & de Beer, K. (2017). Exercising library and information literacies through alternate reality gaming. *Aslib Journal of Information Management, 69*(2), 230-254. https://doi.org/10.1108/AJIM-11-2016-0185**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `RGMIIFPW`; Better BibTeX citation key `jerrettExercisingLibraryInformation2017`; attachment key `8MDG5JY6`.

Jerrett, Bothma, and de Beer report the design and case-study assessment of *Nomad*, an alternate reality game (ARG) created at the University of Pretoria to teach and exercise library, information, and twenty-first-century literacies. The article begins from a familiar instructional challenge: information literacy is required for academic success, but students may resist compulsory information literacy modules when they do not yet understand their relevance. The authors argue that games, and ARGs in particular, can turn abstract literacies into practical, situated activity.

The theoretical frame combines constructivist learning, game-based learning, and ARG theory. Because ARGs unfold across real-world spaces, multiple media, fragmented narratives, collaborative puzzle solving, and player-created meaning, the authors argue that the genre is especially well suited to information literacy. They draw on the Unified Metaliteracies Framework to describe ARG play as involving gathering, making sense, managing, solving, creating, respecting, and collaborating. In this view, an ARG does not merely deliver library facts; its form asks players to search, evaluate, organize, communicate, and solve problems together.

The target audience included about 300 students across two first-year modules and one third-year module at the University of Pretoria. The modules were selected because they already involved relevant skills: HTML, introductory information science and literacies, and game design/analysis. The game registered about 90 accounts, about 20 of which were active on forums. Eleven players participated in focus groups and questionnaires, divided by degree of activity. The authors are explicit that this was a qualitative case study, not a generalizable experiment. Data came from non-participant observation, document analysis, log-file analysis, questionnaires, focus groups, web analytics, and constant comparative analysis.

The narrative of *Nomad* centers on a mysterious time traveler and a missing character, with players helping through clues that interrupt classes, appear online, and lead to the university library. Major puzzles mapped game progress to concrete literacy tasks. The first puzzle required players to locate physical books through the library portal, collection numbers, QR codes, and notes hidden in books. The second required players to match ISBNs to books, borrow books, and decode ciphers embedded in texts. A riddle puzzle required critical thinking, search query construction, information dissemination, and collaboration. A later puzzle asked players to search academic journal databases and create references for the Nomad's research using the university's Harvard style.

The findings are mixed and therefore useful. Players completed the first book-location puzzle quickly, suggesting that they already had a strong baseline in using the library and library portal. The cipher puzzle slowed play and was eventually abandoned, although it did prompt students to borrow books and work with library materials. The riddle puzzle worked better, while the reference puzzle stalled because players found it too obvious, confusing, or weakly integrated into the story. The six-week game ended prematurely with about 65% of designed content completed, and the authors wrote an alternate ending to provide narrative closure.

Even with those problems, players stayed engaged. They self-organized meetings in the library, used forums and instant messaging, created private communication spaces, shared findings in a cloud folder, and reported that the game made traditional library tasks more meaningful. Players did not always consciously connect *Nomad* to information literacy modules, but they recognized the educational purpose when they noticed the repeated library setting and book-finding tasks. Some saw the hidden educational content as a strength: the story made library-skills practice feel like play rather than work.

The article's Table II is especially valuable for GBLS because it maps game tasks to literacies: finding books, decoding ciphers, academic referencing, constructing internet queries, searching the internet, using academic journal databases, using the library portal, moving across mobile and desktop platforms, communicating through social media, using blogs and forums, group problem solving, evaluating information relevance, rearranging information for context, and exploratory or tangential learning. This gives the review a concrete example of how ARG design can make information and library literacies operational.

For the GBLS project, Jerrett et al. contribute both promise and caution. The promise is that ARGs can make library space, catalog searching, source evaluation, collaboration, and referencing feel like meaningful actions inside a story, and can turn the library into a meeting point for peer learning. The caution is that educational ARGs are fragile. Designers should diversify locations and tasks, avoid excessive repetition, host web content reliably, make timelines and instructions transparent, keep the experience relatively simple and linear, support community recruitment without requiring a large player base, consider action-research designs with stronger outcome measures, and match running time to content. The study's limits include voluntary participation, a small active-player group, self-reported learning, lack of objective post-testing, incomplete gameplay, and local specificity. Still, it is one of the stronger GBLS sources on ARGs because it provides actual design details, literacy mappings, implementation problems, and player response.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Horban and Skachenko (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Jerrett, Bothma, and de Beer (2017) provide a detailed academic-library ARG case through *Nomad*, a University of Pretoria game that mapped story progress to book finding, library portal use, QR codes, book borrowing, cipher decoding, riddle solving, search query construction, academic database use, and Harvard-style referencing. The case is small and qualitative, but players self-organized in the library, used social and collaborative tools, and reported that the narrative made library and information literacy tasks more engaging.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Design for Reflection and Transfer`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Jerrett et al. (2017) also offer design cautions for ARGs and other complex GBLS formats: vary locations and tasks, keep online assets fast and reliable, make timelines and instructions transparent, limit simultaneous puzzles, support but do not require large player communities, and align running time with content. Their unfinished *Nomad* implementation shows that immersion and mystery should not come at the expense of clarity when the service goal is learning.

# **Patino, D. M., Summerlin, E., Benn, S., Roldan, W., Joya, A., Dahya, N., Luke, J., Lee, J. H., & Yip, J. (2021). *Virtual Reality Art & Design Curriculum*. University of Washington Information School Digital Youth Lab. https://ischool.uw.edu/echo-glen-curriculum**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `DZ5X7TJM`; Better BibTeX citation key `dahyaVirtualRealityArt`; attachment key `ESTUU6PX`.

Patino, Summerlin, Benn, Roldan, Joya, Dahya, Luke, Lee, and Yip present a six-module virtual reality curriculum developed through the University of Washington Information School Digital Youth Lab for incarcerated young people living and studying in a juvenile rehabilitation center in Washington State. The curriculum was implemented in 2018-2019 in the school library at Echo Glen and was created with support from IMLS, Washington State Library, King County Library System, and Oculus. Although the item is a curriculum rather than a conventional research article, it is highly relevant to game-based library services because it documents a library-based, youth-centered model for moving VR from one-time technology exposure toward art making, world-building, reflection, and public exhibition.

The curriculum's stated goal is to teach young people about VR technology and digital art making while inviting them to explore art creation across 2D and 3D media. The six instructional days move from introduction to VR, to world-making, future building, VR and music, art, and VR exploration. The program then adds two modules focused on program and exhibit development, in which youth-created art and reflections were prepared for display in a local public library. That exhibit component matters for GBLS because it turns immersive play and creation into a public-facing cultural production process: youth participants were not only users of equipment but makers whose work could enter a civic library space.

The setting shaped the design. The authors explain that the curriculum was created for a highly restrictive environment where students' personal histories, crimes, and trauma could not be solicited or made central. Instead of framing the program around personal storytelling or empowerment, the team framed it around VR concept art design, creative world-making, and future building. The report repeatedly emphasizes an anti-oppressive framework: accepting the reality of structural power, centering intersectionality, asking who benefits from the work, seeking community feedback, reflecting on researcher and educator positionality, and revisiting ethical questions at every stage. These principles are translated into concrete facilitation choices, such as trauma-aware selection of VR experiences, purposeful outreach to girls and young women, attention to racial and gender dynamics, and a room layout that avoided placing headset users with their backs to peers.

The school library setup is one of the curriculum's most transferable pieces. Four VR systems were arranged so the participant in the headset faced out toward the room while the computer screen displayed the experience for spectators. Small groups sat at activity tables where peers could watch, discuss, draw, plan, and prepare for turns in VR. The curriculum therefore treats VR as social even when only one person wears a headset. Spectating, informal conversation, free play, table-based art making, and near-peer mentorship are part of the program design rather than accidental side effects. The modules also foreground rotation, short activities, safety, consent for touch, and the need for facilitators to model headset use before helping participants.

The curriculum's content is especially useful for libraries because it combines commercial VR applications with guided creative and reflective activities. Participants used or observed experiences such as Oculus First Contact, Henry, Google Cardboard stories, Tiny Town VR, Tilt Brush, Sculpt VR, Fantasynth, Beat Saber, Job Simulator, and Fantastic Contraption. Yet the point was not simply access to new technology. Each VR experience was paired with discussion, drawing, world-making, design prompts, or reflection. Future-building activities asked students to imagine libraries in the year 3000; art modules asked them to compare 2D, 3D, and VR creation; music modules invited reflection on how audio shapes game experience; and open exploration days gave participants choice while still connecting work back to the final exhibit.

The public-library exhibit, *Here But Not Here*, extends the curriculum into co-curation. Museology graduate students prepared with readings on virtual reality as an interpretive tool, programming with incarcerated populations, and DIY community-based exhibit development. Onsite sessions asked youth to answer questions such as what VR meant to them and why VR mattered to them. Activities included choosing words for feelings about VR, creating collages, customizing cardboard VR goggles, making Tilt Brush art, and telling stories about their relationship to VR. The final installation at Snoqualmie Library used youth words and artifacts, invited public conversation, and centered the theme that VR is real because students experienced unreal actions in ways that felt real.

For the larger GBLS project, this curriculum is strongest as a program-design and equity source. It does not provide a formal outcome study, and the authors note that research data collection in the juvenile rehabilitation setting was limited by consent barriers and other constraints. Still, it gives unusually rich detail about how a library-linked VR program can be built around anti-oppressive design, trauma-aware facilitation, youth agency, art making, social-emotional and cultural learning, and public cultural participation. It also connects several GBLS concerns that are often separated: technology access, game-like VR experiences, creative production, exhibition, school-library space, public-library partnership, and service to marginalized youth.

This source should be used carefully because it is a curriculum document and report, not peer-reviewed outcome evidence. Its value is in its specificity. For libraries considering VR, it shows that meaningful immersive programming requires more than hardware, software, and safety waivers. The design must answer who the program is for, what kinds of participation are safe and meaningful, how spectators and non-VR activities are included, how youth choice is protected, how artifacts are gathered and interpreted, and how public-library display can amplify participant voices without exploiting them.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, immediately after the Dahya et al. (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Patino et al. (2021) turn those VR implementation lessons into a curriculum model for a highly constrained library-adjacent setting: incarcerated youth in a juvenile rehabilitation school library created 2D/3D art and VR worlds through six modules and then helped shape a public-library exhibit. For GBLS, the curriculum shows how immersive technology can be framed as world-making, art/design, social-emotional learning, and co-curation rather than novelty access; it also foregrounds anti-oppressive planning, trauma-aware spatial setup, consent and safety, choice, spectating, wraparound table activities, and exhibit pathways that preserve youth agency.

# **Hollister, J. M. (2024). Virtual libraries in the new metaverse: An exploratory study on community libraries in Meta Horizon Worlds. *International Journal of Knowledge Content Development & Technology, 14*(4), 113-133. https://doi.org/10.5865/IJKCT.2024.14.4.113**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `KRDN93EF`; Better BibTeX citation key `hollisterVirtualLibrariesNew2024`; attachment key `7GIFL7EU`.

Hollister explores the presence and status of libraries in the emerging metaverse by studying public library-themed worlds in Meta Horizon Worlds, a social virtual reality platform owned by Meta. The study begins from the observation that libraries have long experimented with virtual worlds, especially Second Life, where librarians created digital collections, programming, reference services, and LIS education activities. The newer metaverse landscape raises a related but updated question: if library work moved into earlier virtual worlds, what does library presence look like in a contemporary VR platform built around socializing, games, exploration, and user-created worlds?

The project is framed as a pilot case study guided by two research questions: to what extent libraries are present in Meta Horizon Worlds and what characterizes those libraries. Hollister collected data from June to October 2024 through unobtrusive observation of public worlds that included the word "library" in their title or description. All in-platform visits were conducted in VR with a Meta Quest 2 headset. The author also collected descriptive data from world listings, took screenshots from a first-person perspective, and recorded field notes after each visit. To avoid involving human subjects directly, the author joined only publicly listed worlds and avoided interaction with other users by entering empty worlds or using closed/private sessions when needed.

The sample included 44 worlds after removing worlds that were empty, inaccessible, or created by Meta employees. The worlds were categorized by their creators in varied ways, most often as hangouts or art spaces, with others marked as puzzle, narrative, adventure, exploration, party game, or uncategorized. All were designed for VR use with Meta Quest headsets, although some Meta Horizon access is possible through other devices. The worlds varied in age, update frequency, capacity, comfort rating, age rating, and number of editors. Hollister treats these descriptive details as evidence that library-world development in Meta Horizon Worlds is relatively new, uneven, and labor-intensive.

The qualitative content analysis identified four recurring categories: art and asset libraries, community libraries, personal libraries, and library aesthetics used for games and social spaces. Art and asset libraries were often not libraries in a professional institutional sense; they provided virtual objects, shapes, lighting fixtures, mechanics, portals, and other assets that world builders could reuse. These worlds are still important for GBLS because they use library language to organize access, attribution, and reuse within a creator economy. In a platform built around world-making, a library can become an infrastructure for sharing components rather than a repository of texts alone.

The community library category is the closest to conventional library service. Hollister found a small number of worlds designed to function as virtual community libraries by providing resources, exhibitions, social space, events, curated portals, educational materials, or cultural programming. Only two worlds appeared to have connections to real-world libraries: the Geisel Library Dr. Seuss Matrix and AANHPI Heritage World. Other worlds simulated library spaces around African and African American culture and history, banned books, reading and computer clubs, anti-bullying themes, performances, portals, and community events. These spaces were not generally equivalent to mature Second Life library services, but they show how library functions can be remixed in a VR social platform.

Personal libraries and library-themed game/social worlds show another important pattern. Many users built cozy reading rooms, personal library spaces, recreations of famous media libraries, bars, trivia games, escape-room-style spaces, hidden-key challenges, and social venues with books, shelves, fireplaces, stages, portals, or other library aesthetics. Hollister argues that the library aesthetic has enduring appeal as a symbol of meeting, reading, culture, welcome, and sociality, even when the world does not provide formal library services. For GBLS, that finding matters because it shows that the idea of the library circulates inside game-like worlds as both place and play material.

The discussion is cautious about institutional opportunity. Most virtual libraries in Meta Horizon Worlds were community-created rather than affiliated with real-world libraries, and few provided the level of digital resources or virtual reference found in earlier Second Life library projects. At the same time, the worlds suggest possible future directions for libraries that want to meet users where they are in social VR. The study flags several barriers: platform instability, the shutdown of other VR platforms such as Altspace and Mozilla Hubs, limited interactive book functionality, content-development labor, headset cost, accessibility concerns, privacy concerns, motion sickness, limited high-quality content, and the need for stakeholder partnerships, training, and continuous assessment.

For the GBLS project, Hollister's article extends the virtual-world strand beyond Second Life into the newer commercial metaverse. It is not an assessment of a library-run program and does not show that patrons benefit from institutional VR libraries. Its value is descriptive and strategic: it maps what "library" currently means inside one game-like social VR platform and shows that virtual libraries can appear as asset repositories, community resource spaces, personal identity spaces, social venues, puzzle games, or aesthetic environments. GBLS should therefore treat virtual libraries not only as digital branches but also as worldbuilding, curation, access, community, and play spaces shaped by platform affordances and constraints.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the Bell and Trueman (2008) paragraph.

Suggested text: Hollister (2024) updates this virtual-world strand for Meta Horizon Worlds. Through unobtrusive VR-based observation of 44 public worlds with "library" in the title or description, he found art and asset libraries, community libraries, personal libraries, and library-themed game and social spaces; only two appeared connected to real-world libraries, and few provided full digital resources or virtual reference. For GBLS, the study treats virtual libraries as possible service sites and worldbuilding artifacts while flagging platform instability, world-development labor, accessibility, cost, motion-sickness barriers, limited interactive content, and the need for stakeholder partnerships and ongoing assessment.

# **Walton, J. L. (Ed.). (2024). *Utopia on the tabletop*. Ping Press. https://drive.google.com/file/d/142T45GkcP4KAKxF_6s91klH2ulWDwSKd/view**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `UZAMI5IB`; Better BibTeX citation key `waltonUtopiaTabletop2024`; attachment key `MF5ELQ3U`.

Walton's edited volume brings together utopian studies and tabletop roleplaying game (TTRPG) studies. The collection includes essays, dialogues, two playable games, and an afterword. Its central premise is that the contemporary tabletop roleplaying renaissance is resonant with utopian themes: tabletop games can stage solarpunk imaginaries, ecological harmony, dystopian collapse, postcapitalist speculation, queer and neurodiverse futures, labor organizing, decolonial design, collaborative worldbuilding, and the question of whether serious play can touch real-world issues.

The introduction frames TTRPGs as cultural forms that train players to think from within rules. Walton treats tabletop games as a medium where little systems can make players feel, test, or question big social systems. That argument runs across the volume. Several chapters analyze specific games or actual-play texts, including *The Book of Cairn*, *Arium: Create*, *Paranoia*, *Dialect*, *Masks*, *Dimension 20's The Unsleeping City*, *Dungeons & Dragons*, and *Dungeons & Dragons, Ancestry & Culture*. Other contributions focus on larp, irrigation gaming, art practice, fictioning, labor organizing, and original game design. The through-line is not a single theory of play but a recurring interest in utopia as method, critique, social dreaming, blueprint, failure, repair, and world-making.

The middle of the book is especially relevant to TTRPG studies because it examines how fantasy roleplaying systems carry political assumptions. Chapters on *Dungeons & Dragons* and related homebrew discourse ask how ancestry, culture, race, commodified neutrality, diversity, and actual-play communities shape what players imagine as possible. Other chapters move away from mainstream fantasy and toward indie or critical games, where mechanics may foreground care, social reproduction, language loss, community maintenance, organizing, or alternative institutional forms. In that sense, the volume gives a broad view of tabletop play as more than entertainment: it can be a site for procedural rhetoric, speculative sociology, collective design, and critical reflection.

For the GBLS project, the book is intellectually adjacent but not directly evidentiary. It can help explain why TTRPG programs might matter beyond recreation, especially when libraries use roleplaying to support imagination, collaborative storytelling, identity exploration, civic reflection, or community-building. The volume also offers vocabulary for thinking about design tensions that matter in library programs: whether a game invites repair or critique, whether its worldbuilding is genuinely collaborative, whether inclusion is only surface-level, and whether play helps participants imagine alternatives to the status quo.

However, the book does not substantially address libraries, librarianship, library programs, library collections, school libraries, public libraries, academic libraries, cataloging, access, facilitation in library settings, or library outcomes. Searches of the text show only incidental references to archives, libraries, and related memory institutions, not a sustained treatment of library service. Its primary audiences are scholars and designers in utopian studies, TTRPG studies, speculative fiction, game design, cultural studies, and activist or artistic play communities. Because the GBLS review is trying to build evidence about game-based library services, this volume would require too much inferential work to justify a direct review insertion.

The source should therefore remain in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` while being marked `GPT Summary Complete`. It is not irrelevant to games, TTRPGs, serious play, or the politics of imagination; it is simply outside the current review's service-focused scope. If the project later expands into a theoretical framework for why roleplaying can support civic imagination or social dreaming, Walton (2024) could be reconsidered as background theory. For the present literature review, library-specific TTRPG sources such as Watson et al. (2024), Kaylor (2017), Torres-Roman and Snow (2014), and Sanders (1982) are stronger fits.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No suggested addition. Retain this item in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` because it is a broad TTRPG/utopian studies volume rather than evidence about game-based library services.

# **Henriksen-Bulmer, J., Rosenorn-Lanng, E., Corbin-Clarke, S., Ware, S., Melacca, D., & Fenge, L.-A. (2024). Using game-based learning to teach young people about privacy and online safety. *Interactive Learning Environments, 32*(10), 6430-6450. https://doi.org/10.1080/10494820.2023.2265424**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `B72IBLMX`; Better BibTeX citation key `henriksen-bulmerUsingGamebasedLearning2024`; attachment key `ZG7ACUW7`.

Henriksen-Bulmer, Rosenorn-Lanng, Corbin-Clarke, Ware, Melacca, and Fenge present a case-study account of designing and evaluating a bespoke board game about digital privacy and online safety for young people aged 16-25. The article is not a library study, but it is relevant to the GBLS review as adjacent evidence for game-based digital-literacy instruction. The authors begin from the premise that young people use the internet and social media to communicate, experiment with identity, and build relationships, but that they also face online risks such as oversharing, cyberbullying, trolling, scams, privacy breaches, grooming, harmful content, reputational damage, and commercial data collection. They argue that software controls and parental monitoring are insufficient on their own; young people need digital literacy, privacy awareness, and resilience.

The project sought to create a privacy game that would passively teach players how to stay safe online. The design fused expertise from computing, gaming, health, and social care. The resulting physical prototype was a cross between *Trivial Pursuit* and *Snakes and Ladders*: players moved around a board, answered questions from different privacy categories, collected category tokens, used shield tokens, and used portals to move more easily between areas. The game was deliberately physical rather than digital because the team's survey work found strong youth preference for a physical board game, even though educators and adults were nearly evenly split between physical and digital formats.

The article's design process is useful because it makes the learning architecture visible. The authors reviewed privacy and online-safety frameworks, including Solove's taxonomy of privacy harms, Finn et al.'s privacy categories, Kaspersky's list of online dangers, the UK Council for Internet Safety framework, Ofcom's harm groupings, and Willoughby's risk categories. They then consolidated these into five game categories: managing your information; self-identity and well-being; cyber-bullying and trolling; your relationships and reputation; and privacy and security. The game questions were informed by the literature and by a Prolific survey of 90 adults, parents, guardians, and educators, plus 91 young people aged 18-25. The survey showed both overlap and mismatch between youth and adult concerns: young people were especially concerned with privacy and security, phishing and scams, and location tracking, while adults were more concerned about cyberbullying, sexual predators, privacy, and protection of personal information.

The prototype was evaluated through four focus groups with 27 undergraduate students aged 18-25. Participants completed pre-game and post-game questionnaires about confidence across the five categories, along with usability and play-experience questions. The authors report that all players felt more confident across all five categories after playing. Wilcoxon tests showed improvement across all categories, with the most significant gains in managing your information and your relationships and reputation. Cyber-bullying and trolling and self-identity and well-being also showed significant positive shifts. Participants generally found the game easy to play and not too complex, and the authors observed that most groups discussed questions and possible answers, even when the current question was not their own.

For GBLS, the strongest lesson is that a low-tech board game can make online-safety instruction conversational, social, and non-threatening. The game did not simply quiz individual players; it created a shared space where questions, wrong answers, explanations, and peer talk could reinforce learning for everyone at the table. This matters for libraries because privacy, misinformation, cyberbullying, scams, passwords, account safety, and digital footprints are common public-library and school-library digital-literacy concerns. The article suggests that libraries do not need sophisticated digital games to teach these topics. A well-designed physical game can support discussion, immediate feedback, safe failure, and confidence-building while avoiding some barriers attached to computers, accounts, or specialized software.

The application to GBLS should be cautious. The study did not take place in a library, and the evaluation involved a small, convenient sample of university students rather than the full intended 16-25 age range. The outcome measures were immediate self-reports of confidence rather than long-term behavioral change or objective privacy skills. The authors also note that COVID-19 limited access to younger participants and delayed development of a digital version. Still, the study offers a useful transferable model for information-literacy and digital-safety programming: define risk categories from current frameworks, generate questions from both research and local participant concerns, test the game with users, keep the rules simple, and build for discussion rather than content delivery alone.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, immediately after the Horban and Skachenko (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Henriksen-Bulmer et al. (2024) are not studying libraries, but their privacy board-game case is adjacent evidence for library digital-literacy programs. The team created a physical *Snakes and Ladders*/*Trivial Pursuit*-style game for 16-25-year-olds on managing information, self-identity and well-being, cyberbullying and trolling, relationships and reputation, and privacy and security. In focus groups with 27 young adults, players reported improved confidence across all five categories, with significant pre/post self-rated gains especially in managing information and relationships/reputation. For GBLS, the study suggests that low-tech board games can make online-safety instruction conversational and non-threatening, though transfer to libraries requires caution because the sample was small, university-based, and measured immediate self-report.

# **Burgess, J., & Wallace, A. (2021). Using investigative video games to teach clinical intuition and metacognition in reference transactions. *Journal of Education for Library and Information Science, 62*(4), 477-491. https://doi.org/10.3138/jelis.62-4-2020-0099**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `L7HTHH63`; Better BibTeX citation key `burgessUsingInvestigativeVideo2021`; attachment key `QDQ5QKMZ`.

Burgess and Wallace argue that investigative video games can supplement reference and user-services education by giving MLIS students practice with clinical intuition, metacognition, and reasoning skills adjacent to reference work. The article begins from a problem in online LIS education: as MLIS instruction becomes more widespread online, especially asynchronously, reference instructors may have fewer opportunities to model live reference transactions, provide immediate feedback, and let students practice the real-time uncertainty of reference work. The authors treat the reference transaction as a form of information literacy work, but one that differs from planned instruction because the goals, patron need, resources, and search path may not be clear at the beginning of the encounter.

The conceptual core of the article is clinical intuition. Drawing on nursing and clinical judgment literature, Burgess and Wallace define intuition not as an unexamined gut feeling but as a hypothesis or premise deliberately sought in situations where a practitioner must choose an action. In reference work, clinical intuition develops as workers form and test ad hoc rules about reference interviews, resource selection, and search-string construction. That intuition is useful only if reference workers can reflect on it and explain it. The authors therefore pair clinical intuition with metacognition: the ability to think about one's own thinking, manage tasks strategically, recognize what resources and approaches are available, and refine practice based on outcomes.

The article uses concept analysis to clarify this phenomenon for LIS pedagogy. Burgess and Wallace argue that good reference work is not only a set of procedural skills. It is a complex practice that combines cognitive competencies, behavioral competencies, and social competencies. Reference workers must know how information systems work, use metadata and search tools, select sources, ask open and closed questions, listen actively, avoid jargon, notice sensitive topics, share screens or demonstrate tools, check patron satisfaction, and adapt as new evidence emerges. The problem for online teaching is that some of the most important pieces of this work depend on rule testing and timely feedback, which are harder to reproduce when students only complete readings, watch videos, or search from prewritten questions.

To make the teachable pieces more concrete, the authors reduce reference reasoning to three major types: deductive, inductive, and abductive reasoning. Deductive reasoning applies fixed principles to infer what kind of source or search strategy is likely to fit a patron's need. Inductive reasoning evaluates accumulated evidence, such as search results, and revises assumptions based on what is observed. Abductive reasoning forms and tests hypotheses, such as the likely tacit question behind a patron's initial request. The authors connect these reasoning types to experiential learning theory, especially action/reflection and experience/abstraction cycles, arguing that students need opportunities to act, receive feedback, articulate what happened, and refine their rules.

Investigative video games enter the argument as commercially available practice environments. Burgess and Wallace define investigative games as games whose mechanics let players solve a mystery by finding and evaluating clues. Such games can require players to discover rules, test hypotheses, revise assumptions, persist through uncertainty, and use immediate feedback from the game world.
