The study uses an explanatory sequential mixed-method design. Sixty first-year students were recruited and randomly assigned to two groups of thirty. The control group completed an e-learning orientation, while the experimental group used CMU Journey; both activities lasted three hours and used content created by main-library staff. Students completed a pre-test, an immediate post-test, a one-month delayed post-test, and a modified short Intrinsic Motivation Inventory covering perceived competence, interest, and effort. The researchers also collected open-ended feedback from both groups.

The quantitative results are usefully mixed. Both groups improved from pre-test to post-test. The e-learning group moved from a mean of 3.73 to 12.20, and the mobile-gamification group moved from 3.66 to 14.40. The immediate between-group comparison had p = .036, which the authors treat as not significant at the stricter .01 level; therefore, the game did not clearly outperform e-learning for immediate knowledge acquisition. The delayed one-month scores were more distinctive: the e-learning group averaged 10.60 and the mobile-gamification group averaged 13.76, with p < .001. The authors conclude that the mobile orientation supported longer knowledge retention better than e-learning.

Motivation results were similarly specific rather than sweeping. The control group showed no significant change in perceived competence, interest, or effort. The experimental group showed a significant gain only for Interest, rising from 3.00 to 4.30 with p < .001; perceived competence and effort did not significantly improve. Qualitative feedback helps explain the result. Students often described the mobile orientation as fun, innovative, and a good introduction to the library, and one respondent said it made them want to explore the main library even though they had not initially understood why they needed to visit it.

For the larger GBLS project, this is a strong orientation source because it evaluates a library-service game against an alternative orientation method and includes delayed retention. It supports a careful claim: gamified mobile orientation may not automatically improve immediate learning beyond e-learning, but it can improve retention and interest when it asks students to move through real library space, answer service questions, and connect rewards to exploration. The article also reinforces the importance of comparative assessment and of measuring outcomes beyond satisfaction or attendance.

The limitations are practical and important. The sample was small, came from one university in Thailand, and included only sixty participants. The authors note Android hardware and OS problems, GPS accuracy issues, battery drain, requests for charging access, and student suggestions for richer mechanics, multiplayer features, deeper questions, and improved library spaces. These limits make the article useful for GBLS implementation planning: mobile orientation games require not only game design, but also device support, charging infrastructure, reliable geolocation, accessible alternatives, content tuning, and library-space responsiveness.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Valenti, Lund, and Wang (2020) paragraph. Proposed text: "Intawong, Puritat, Leenaraj, and Arayaphan (2023) provide stronger comparative evidence for mobile orientation through CMU Journey, a Pokémon Go-style geolocation app in which first-year students explored Chiang Mai University's main library, captured monsters, answered library-service questions, and earned points, inventory items, and leaderboard status. Compared with e-learning, the game did not clearly outperform on immediate knowledge acquisition at the authors' stricter threshold, but it produced significantly better one-month retention and significantly higher Interest on the Intrinsic Motivation Inventory."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Miller (2019) paragraph. Proposed text: "Intawong et al. (2023) show why GBLS assessment should separate immediate learning, delayed retention, motivation, and user feedback. CMU Journey's strongest evidence appeared one month later and in the Interest subscale, while qualitative comments surfaced design, content, device, GPS, battery, charging, and library-space issues that a post-event headcount would miss."

# **Webber, S., & Nahl, D. (2011). Sustaining learning for LIS through use of a virtual world. *IFLA Journal, 37*(1), 5-15. https://doi.org/10.1177/0340035210397137**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `53X4P8WS`; Better BibTeX citation key `webberSustainingLearningLIS2011`; attachment key `3AQSCDGC`.

Webber and Nahl argue that Second Life and other virtual worlds offered library and information science a sustainable environment for teaching, continuing professional development, outreach, research, curriculum development, and professional collaboration. The article is grounded in the authors' own practice: Webber taught LIS and information-literacy students through the University of Sheffield's Infolit iSchool island, while Nahl taught University of Hawaii MLIS courses, mentored students in Second Life, and helped develop the LIS Student Union and Community Virtual Library activities. Their claim is not that Second Life is a conventional game, but that its avatar-based, persistent, three-dimensional, user-created environment became a new LIS learning laboratory.

The article first identifies Second Life affordances that made it attractive for LIS education. These include avatars and social presence, text and voice communication, object building and scripting, interactive content, live web pages, simulated environments, community events, volunteer reference services, and geographically dispersed collaboration. Webber and Nahl connect these affordances to online LIS education problems: distance students may need flexible access and community, librarians may need professional-development opportunities without travel, and future information professionals may need to understand virtual worlds as information grounds used by patrons, educators, and professional communities.

The authors also map librarian activity in Second Life. Librarians supported academic staff and students, taught virtual classes, ran book groups and author readings, recreated historical or fictional settings, built interactive learning objects, provided inquiry and reference services, mocked up buildings or services, offered virtual study spaces, organized CPD, and participated in the broader in-world economy and community. They describe the Community Virtual Library as a volunteer-maintained service and note that virtual-world librarianship could generate new roles, including paid virtual-library work in health-information settings.

Webber's teaching examples are especially relevant to information literacy and research training. Undergraduate students interviewed Second Life residents about their information behavior, analyzed transcripts, and reflected on interviewing in a virtual environment. Students also displayed swine-flu information-literacy presentations on three-dimensional models of the SCONUL Seven Pillars and guided visitors through the exhibits during Information Literacy Week in Second Life. Master's students studied Second Life as a learning environment, visited educational sites, attended a Virtual Worlds Best Practices in Education conference session, created posters, and presented them inside a virtual village. Webber emphasizes access to international interviewees and educators, concrete exploration of abstract learning concepts through 3D models, and a stronger sense of place and institutional culture than conventional two-dimensional online tools often provide.

Nahl's University of Hawaii examples make virtual-world librarianship into professional practice. Students in MLIS courses completed reference-shadowing assignments at the Info Island reference desk, planned and produced professional events on Info Islands and ALA Island, worked with librarian mentors, created subject guides and interactive exhibits, practiced reference, communication, marketing, participant observation, and building skills, and integrated Google Groups, Google Docs, Flickr, YouTube, sloog, machinima, and web archives into their virtual-world projects. Student-produced events included an LIS career fair, Banned Books Week programming, an ACRL Information Literacy and Web 2.0 panel, a Hawaiian statehood exhibit, and an LIS student poster conference judged by librarian adjudicators.

The CPD section shows why Second Life mattered as a professional learning platform. Webber and Nahl describe large virtual conferences, tours of specialized environments, themed events such as Information Literacy Week, recurring discussion series, professional meetings, training workshops, sandbox-based skill classes, and interactive exhibits. The benefits they identify include access to international speakers and audiences, no travel cost, flexible attendance across time zones, stronger social presence than many web tools, and opportunities for librarians from different sectors to meet, collaborate, present, and learn. They also acknowledge barriers: participants need reliable broadband, adequate graphics and processing power, time to learn how to function in a virtual world, and willingness to contribute if they want full value from the professional community.

For GBLS, the article extends the virtual-world strand from public-facing service into LIS education, staff development, and professional infrastructure. It shows that game-like virtual platforms can support library work by becoming places where librarians learn, teach, prototype, mentor, exhibit, host reference interactions, and build professional networks. It also complicates later VR and metaverse discussions by reminding the review that many current questions about immersive library services have older roots in Second Life: access barriers, staff legitimacy, training demands, platform labor, international collaboration, virtual reference, in-world collections, and the need to design activities that use spatial and social affordances rather than merely reproducing a website in 3D.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the Bell and Trueman (2008) paragraph. Proposed text: "Webber and Nahl (2011) show how the same Second Life moment also became professional infrastructure for LIS education and CPD. Their Sheffield and Hawaii cases used avatar interviews, information-literacy exhibits, virtual-world librarianship courses, reference shadowing, student-produced professional events, librarian mentors, poster conferences, and in-world CPD to make virtual worlds a laboratory for information behavior, reference, teaching, collaboration, and professional identity; the benefits were international access, social presence, persistent learning objects, and reduced travel, while barriers included broadband, hardware, onboarding time, and the need for active contribution."

# **Brackenbury, T., & Kopf, L. (2022). Serious games and gamification: Game-based learning in communication sciences and disorders. *Perspectives of the ASHA Special Interest Groups, 7*(2), 482-498. https://doi.org/10.1044/2021_PERSP-21-00284**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `8GEHLKNA`; Better BibTeX citation key `brackenburySeriousGamesGamification2022`; attachment key `5NBVBA4K`.

This Zotero record should be treated with caution because the attached PDF does not appear to match the parent item. The parent metadata identifies Brackenbury and Kopf's *Perspectives of the ASHA Special Interest Groups* tutorial on serious games and gamification in communication sciences and disorders (CSD), but the attached PDF extracts as Claudia Schrader's handbook chapter, "Serious Games and Game-Based Learning," from the *Handbook of Open, Distance and Digital Education*. Because the Brackenbury and Kopf article text is not actually present in the Zotero attachment, the record cannot be used as full article evidence for the GBLS review.

Based on the Zotero metadata and external abstract-level indexing, the intended Brackenbury and Kopf article is a CSD education tutorial. It defines serious games as activities that directly incorporate educational content into games and gamification as the integration of game characteristics into nongame activities. Its stated purpose is to help readers understand and use serious games and gamification in classroom and clinical CSD settings. It includes models of motivation and engagement, research on serious games and gamification, in-depth examples from two CSD courses, and implementation suggestions.

The article's abstract-level conclusions emphasize that serious games and gamification can improve motivation, engagement, and learning, and that self-determination theory is a useful model because it distinguishes learners' needs and multiple levels of intrinsic and extrinsic motivation. The recommendations named in the record are to design activities that balance motivation and learning, target specific forms of motivation in meaningful ways, and reconsider the assumption that intrinsic motivation should always be the ultimate goal of learning.

The mismatched attached PDF, while not the Brackenbury and Kopf article, reinforces similar broad educational design themes. It distinguishes serious games from game-based learning, reviews motivational, affective, sociocultural, and cognitive foundations for learning through games, and summarizes mixed evidence from meta-analyses: games can support motivation, self-efficacy, retention, cognitive skills, and learning outcomes, but effects vary by game type, instructional design, learner characteristics, and learning activities. It also emphasizes that designers must balance motivational features with cognitive load, and attend to collaboration, competition, learner control, aesthetic and narrative design, feedback, and adaptive support.

For the larger GBLS project, both the intended article and the attached-but-mismatched PDF are adjacent rather than directly applicable. They may be useful background for thinking about why motivation, engagement, autonomy, competence, relatedness, feedback, challenge, and support matter in serious games and gamified learning. However, neither the available attachment nor the intended article provides evidence about libraries, librarians, patrons, collections, library instruction, public programs, game collections, TTRPG services, virtual library spaces, or library-specific assessment.

Because the review already includes stronger and more directly relevant sources on library gamification, game-based instruction, serious games, and broad educational evidence, this record should not be added to `review.md`. It is best retained as explicitly excluded literature with a note that the Zotero attachment should be corrected if the Brackenbury and Kopf article is needed for a future health, clinical education, or CSD-specific sidebar.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because the item is a broad CSD education tutorial rather than library literature, and because the attached Zotero PDF does not match the cited parent record.

# **Stark, R. K., Opuda, E., McElfresh, J., & Kauffroath, K. (2021). Scavenging for evidence: A systematic review of scavenger hunts in academic libraries. *The Journal of Academic Librarianship, 47*(3), Article 102345. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.acalib.2021.102345**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `FXB6Y5QG`; Better BibTeX citation key `starkScavengingEvidenceSystematic2021`; attachment key `HUC2C6JB`.

Stark, Opuda, McElfresh, and Kauffroath systematically review academic-library scavenger hunts as outreach and instruction tools. Their starting point is that academic libraries often use active learning, play-based exercises, and gamification to make orientation and information-literacy encounters more engaging, especially for students first encountering the library. Scavenger hunts are treated as activities in which participants physically move through a defined library space to encounter information relevant to later use of the library. The authors focus on physical or hybrid scavenger hunts, excluding virtual-only activities and studies without any form of assessment.

The study is framed as evidence-based practice. The authors initially hoped to determine whether librarian-led scavenger hunts effectively oriented students to academic libraries and whether they served as effective information-literacy instruction. Because the available literature was too thin to answer that question directly, they broadened the review to explore examples, methodological quality, and effectiveness claims. Their protocol used PRISMA-P guidance, Cochrane recommendations, SPICE and PICO frameworks, searches across databases, grey literature, conference proceedings, Google Scholar, citation chasing, and a pre-submission search rerun.

Thirteen case studies met the final inclusion criteria. All used some form of formal or informal assessment, but the reporting was uneven. Only four articles explicitly stated the number of participants in the assessment, eight described the participant population, and only one clearly stated the length of the scavenger hunt. The authors found that desired outcomes included fun or engagement, orientation to library spaces, information-literacy learning, technology use, orientation to materials and resources, library policies, and campus partners. The most common learning objectives were space orientation, materials and resources orientation, and use of library technologies; fun and engagement were also common, while information literacy, policies, and campus partners appeared less often.

The included scavenger hunts varied widely in design. Twelve used technology in some form, ten included a theme, eleven used teams, just over half used a time element or race structure, and more than half revised the hunt during the reported project. Only a few assessment tools were reviewed or tested before use. Findings reported by the case studies often included fun or engagement, space familiarity, technology familiarity, familiarity with materials, resources, services, and policies, confidence or comfort using the library, and improvement of scavenger-hunt methods. However, the authors emphasize that these findings often did not align cleanly with stated objectives and were frequently based on informal observation, informal feedback, worksheets, or local surveys.

The review's strongest contribution is its critique of the evidence base. Stark et al. conclude that the published literature does not provide enough evidence to make an evidence-based decision about whether scavenger hunts are effective for academic-library orientation or instruction. The problems include poor research design, unclear reporting, inconsistent data collection, missing or incomplete participant counts, lack of demographic data beyond class year, lack of validated instruments, mixed pilot and non-pilot data, unreported study timeframes, and studies that describe creation and implementation more than outcomes. They also note operational barriers: scavenger hunts can require significant planning, staff coordination, communication across library workers, technology support, scalable design, and partnerships with student affairs or other campus units.

For the larger GBLS project, Stark et al. are directly relevant because scavenger hunts, library races, clue games, and orientation quests are common game-based library service formats. The article validates why these formats are appealing: they can introduce students to library spaces, resources, services, technologies, and partners while setting a welcoming tone. At the same time, it prevents the review from overclaiming. A scavenger hunt that feels fun, draws participants, or helps students move through a building is not automatically evidence of information-literacy learning, durable library confidence, transfer, or later use of resources. GBLS programs need objectives, populations, intervention designs, measures, timeframes, and findings to line up.

The article also gives concrete research guidance for future GBLS assessment. Future studies should collect and report meaningful demographics, participant counts for each iteration, replicable and methodologically sound designs, stable measures across the data-collection period, outcome measures tied to research questions, separately reported data for each intervention, and both short-term and long-term benefit assessment. Stark et al. acknowledge that control groups, blinding, validated measures, and richer demographics may be difficult in ordinary library settings, but they treat those constraints as reasons to improve reporting rather than reasons to rely indefinitely on informal case description.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, immediately after the Yap and Peñaflor (2020) paragraph. Proposed text: "Stark, Opuda, McElfresh, and Kauffroath (2021) synthesize this scavenger-hunt and race-style orientation literature through a systematic review of 13 assessed academic-library cases. The review shows why the format is popular: hunts can introduce spaces, resources, services, technologies, policies, partners, and a welcoming tone through themed, team-based, often technology-supported movement through the library; but it also shows that most published cases emphasize fun, engagement, and implementation more than tested information-literacy or orientation outcomes."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Intawong et al. (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Stark et al. (2021) make this assessment problem field-wide for scavenger hunts: only four of 13 included cases reported assessment population counts, only one reported a clear timeframe, no study combined a stated sample size with a stated study length, and no included article used a validated measure. Their recommendations give GBLS a basic reporting agenda: collect demographics, participant counts, stable measures, intervention-specific data, aligned outcomes, and short- and long-term evidence rather than relying on local enthusiasm, fun, or familiarity claims alone."

# **Nelson, S. B., & Matthews, A. (2025). Roll for initiative: Balancing player and party in library D&D games. *Public Library Quarterly*, 1-22. https://doi.org/10.1080/01616846.2025.2522620**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `EXWIYDKK`; Better BibTeX citation key `nelsonRollInitiativeBalancing2025`; attachment key `AEBCGJRR`.

Nelson and Matthews study public-library Dungeons & Dragons programs through the paired concepts of individuality and community. Their central questions are how library D&D games foster individuality and how they foster community. The article begins from the history and structure of D&D as a collaborative tabletop role-playing game, then connects play to motivation, interest development, belonging, group membership, democratic individuality, and the hybrid individual/relational self. The authors note that much library D&D literature is practical advice for librarians, so their observations and interviews add empirical evidence about what happens in library-hosted games.

The study examined D&D games hosted by two Midwestern public libraries, identified with pseudonyms. Ulfgar Public Library ran online D&D games every two weeks through Zoom breakout rooms, with four games occurring simultaneously; the researchers observed two Ulfgar sessions and interviewed nine participants. Quarion Public Library hosted in-person adult and all-ages games every two weeks, usually with two or three all-ages tables as needed; the researchers observed one adult game, three all-ages sessions, and interviewed five participants. Interviewees included players, DMs, a library director, a young adult librarian, a D&D coordinator, library staff, and one teen participant with parental consent. The authors analyzed fieldnotes and interview transcripts through qualitative coding.

The findings on individuality show that players use D&D to experiment with action, self-expression, heroism, moral choice, and character attachment. Young players sometimes acted in deliberately silly or exploratory ways, while the young adult librarian/DM worked to support expression and bring scattered players back toward collective play. Other players described or enacted spotlight moments: solving a crisis, taking a risk, achieving something dramatic, or being recognized by the party. The authors emphasize that good DMs help each player have time in the spotlight, encourage shy players, allow character subplots, and sometimes use the rule of cool to permit a memorable action even when it bends strict rules.

The findings on party and community show DMs and players treating the game as collaborative storytelling rather than as the DM's fixed plot. Interviewees described the DM as narrator, supporting cast, and facilitator, and they criticized overly rigid story control. Observations showed players helping one another in combat, assisting newer players with leveling up, voting on major decisions, consulting quieter members, and bonding not only with player characters but also with non-player characters. The authors frame this as creative teamwork: players should have fun, speak up, try new things, leave room for others, and work together to make the story.

The library setting matters in several concrete ways. First, the library provides a reliable time and place, physical or virtual, which helps campaigns continue when private friend groups might fall apart. Second, libraries provide DMs or ensure that DMs are available, which solves one of the hardest logistical problems in D&D play. Third, libraries provide resources: D&D books, Roll20 access, 3D printers for figures, staff support, gaming spaces, and related materials. Fourth, the library can function as a safer and more welcoming alternative to game stores or private groups. Participants, especially women and newer players, described library games as friendly to beginners, patient, and welcoming to women and LGBTQ+ players; library staff and spaces supplied a stable, neutral, intentional environment where players could ask for help or raise concerns.

Nelson and Matthews' strongest GBLS contribution is their account of library D&D as democratic community practice. Players want both individuality and party belonging: they want to experiment with characters, have heroic moments, make friends, and feel part of a group. The library deepens this balance by drawing participants from a broader community than family or friend networks, making games more diverse than many private or store-based D&D spaces, and sustaining interest through advertising, stable scheduling, resources, and staff facilitation. The article argues that public libraries communicate that they are spaces for gaming and spaces for everyone, keeping the figurative tavern door open for people who might otherwise be excluded or intimidated.

The article's limitations are clear: it is a small qualitative study of two public libraries, with 14 interview participants, and findings are not necessarily generalizable. Still, it gives public libraries practical implications: D&D can strengthen the library's identity as a third place and community space; it can promote print and digital game-related collections; it can connect with makerspace tools; and it can encourage libraries to learn about staff hobbies and community interests that might translate into programs beyond D&D. The authors call for future research on library-run D&D, safe spaces for all players, marginalized communities, character creation that lets patrons explore identities beyond their own, and other games or events that bring the public together.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## TTRPG and LARP Programs`, after the Sanders (1982) paragraph. Proposed text: "Nelson and Matthews (2025) provide a contemporary empirical public-library D&D account through observations and interviews at two Midwestern libraries. Their study shows that library TTRPG programs do more than provide a table: libraries stabilize campaigns through regular schedules, supply or cultivate DMs, provide books, Roll20 access, 3D printing, and staff support, and create a beginner-friendly alternative to game stores where women, LGBTQ+ players, and inexperienced players may feel more welcome."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Monnier and Winters (2022) paragraph. Proposed text: "Nelson and Matthews (2025) also frame public-library D&D as a democratic third-place practice. Players balance individual self-expression, hero moments, moral choices, and character attachment with party decision-making, mutual aid, shared storytelling, and respect for the DM as facilitator; because the library draws from a broader public than private friend groups or game stores, it can keep the 'tavern door' open for a more diverse community of players."

# **Resources for understanding D&D's new OGL. (2023). *Google Docs*. https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/1afPW1kl9ygogfvnqDrfEC3LoTdVhr2vu5GdIsrH9yhU/edit?usp=drive_web&ouid=104872546336741826329&usp=embed_facebook**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `WRHKEUUV`; Better BibTeX citation key `ResourcesUnderstandingDDs2023`; attachment key `T6639VHU`.

This item is a one-page resource document about the controversy surrounding Wizards of the Coast's proposed changes to the Dungeons & Dragons Open Gaming License. The PDF describes itself as "a resource doc compiling a variety of resources to understand what's going on with Dungeon & Dragons Open Gaming License." Its content is organized as a list of external materials rather than as an essay, research article, program report, or practitioner guide. It includes headings for articles, forum discussions, news reports, podcasts, videos, and miscellaneous resources, with examples such as the Electronic Frontier Foundation, Gizmodo, the original WotC OGL, WotC's response to the leaked update, a Medium article by Noah Downs, a Reddit AMA by a third-party creator, Dicebreaker, Ars Technica, a Foundry Virtual Tabletop Reddit thread, videos on Ryan Dancey and the OGL, and the #OpenDND petition.

The document is relevant to the broader tabletop role-playing game ecosystem because the OGL debate shaped creator, publisher, and player anxieties about D&D's legal and economic infrastructure. For libraries that collect, program around, or teach with TTRPGs, the issue could matter indirectly: public libraries may need to understand why patrons, local creators, or game masters talk about open licensing, third-party publishing, digital platforms, and corporate control of D&D. It may also have indirect relevance for collection development if libraries support independent RPG publishing, local creator communities, zine culture, or tabletop game design workshops.

However, this PDF does not provide library-specific evidence. It does not discuss libraries, librarians, public-library programming, school or academic library services, D&D clubs, TTRPG facilitation, game collections, acquisitions, cataloging, intellectual freedom policies, assessment, patron outcomes, or community impact. It does not offer an argument about how the OGL controversy should affect library collections or services, nor does it document a library response to the controversy. Its value is as a current-awareness link roundup for people trying to understand a specific moment in D&D licensing history.

For the GBLS literature review, this item is therefore best retained as explicitly excluded literature. The review already has stronger sources for library TTRPG programming, including public-library D&D case studies, teen RPG guidance, LARP programming, and RPG collection/cataloging sources. If the project later develops a specialized subsection on intellectual property, licensing, open gaming, creator communities, or RPG collection policy, this resource list might serve as a pointer to primary and journalistic sources. In the present review, though, citing the resource list itself would add little evidence and could blur the distinction between library-service literature and general hobby-industry current awareness.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because this is a brief D&D Open Gaming License resource roundup, not a library-focused study, program report, collection analysis, or service model.

# **Lee, J. H., Clarke, R. I., Sacchi, S., & Jett, J. (2014). Relationships among video games: Existing standards and new definitions. *Proceedings of the American Society for Information Science and Technology, 51*(1), 1-11. https://doi.org/10.1002/meet.2014.14505101035**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `R9XZK748`; Better BibTeX citation key `leeRelationshipsVideoGames2014`; attachment key `B629YLP4`.

Lee, Clarke, Sacchi, and Jett address a specific metadata problem for video games and interactive media: existing bibliographic and cultural-heritage standards can express some relationships between resources, but they do not capture the ways game players, collectors, scholars, and curators understand relationships among games. The article is part of the University of Washington GAMER Group's larger work with the Seattle Interactive Media Museum to develop a standardized metadata schema, controlled vocabularies, and a user-centered conceptual data model for video games.

The article begins from the premise that video games have become significant cultural objects and that more libraries, archives, and museums are collecting them. Unlike books, music, or films, games are shaped by fast-moving platforms, backward-compatibility problems, ports, remakes, re-releases, downloadable content, achievements, online modes, alternate endings, user-created modifications, and platform-specific versions. These relationships affect whether users can identify the game they want, understand what version they have, distinguish similar or related titles, or reconstruct the cultural and technical history of a game.

The authors review how several established standards and models handle relationships: Dublin Core, FRBR, RDA, CIDOC CRM, and Cataloging Cultural Objects. They find that each can express some relevant relationships, such as whole/part, version, derivative, accompanying, sequential, collection, series, influence, or related-work links. Yet the mappings are often too broad, too tied to physical artifacts or events, or too book- and museum-centered to describe games at the right level of abstraction. For instance, broad relationships such as "relation" or "related works" can technically link games, but they lose the distinction between a port, remake, reboot, prequel, sequel, side story, spinoff, crossover, spiritual successor, inspiration, and expansion.

The method combines three approaches: reviewing and harvesting relationship classes from metadata standards and game-related resources; consulting video game experts and enthusiasts from SIMM and local game companies; and examining sample game sets selected for varied genres, platforms, creation dates, and distribution methods. The sample sets included complicated families such as Shin Megami Tensei: Persona, Disgaea, Final Fantasy, Kingdom Hearts, Bejeweled, Civilization V, Pokemon, and Super Mario Bros., plus their related games and additional content.

The article's core contribution is a set of four grouping entities and 11 relationship types. The grouping entities are Series, Franchise, Universe, and Collection. A Series groups related games through numbering, continuing narrative, gameplay, themes, characters, settings, or timelines. A Franchise names intellectual property, related data, and shared content across cultural objects. A Universe is a broader intellectual or creative domain made from recurring ideas, themes, settings, archetypes, and related works, sometimes spanning beyond formal franchise boundaries. A Collection is an anthology of local releases grouped and published together.

The 11 relationship types are isPortOf, isRemakeOf, isRebootOf, isPrequelOf, isSequelOf, isSidestoryOf, isSpinoffOf, isCrossoverOf, isSpiritualSuccessorOf, isInspiredBy, and complements. These definitions matter because they separate relationships that may look similar in ordinary language but have different discovery, access, and preservation implications. A port converts a game to another platform; a remake re-releases an original game with enhanced graphics, stories, or gameplay; a reboot restarts a series or franchise while disregarding an earlier storyline; a prequel or sequel depends on narrative sequence; a side story gives an orthogonal or parallel perspective; a spinoff uses characters or archetypes; a crossover brings together entities from multiple games, series, franchises, or universes; a spiritual successor resembles a previous game as a separate intellectual property; inspiration captures broader artistic or commercial influence; and complements describes additional content that modifies, replaces, or adds gameplay features for a local release.

For the larger GBLS project, this article is important infrastructure literature rather than a program evaluation. It helps explain why library game collections need richer discovery systems than title, platform, publisher, and broad genre alone. Patrons may ask for the right version of a game, games connected to a universe, a sequel order, a game inspired by another game, a remake rather than a port, or additional content needed for play. Researchers and preservationists may need to reconstruct how game works, editions, local releases, platforms, expansions, and related media fit together. The article therefore supports GBLS claims about games as cultural media that require user-centered cataloging and relationship metadata.

The article's limitations are also clear. It is conceptual and metadata-focused, not a study of library users in a circulating collection, and it does not evaluate implementation in a public, school, or academic catalog. Still, its value for the review is strong because it gives specific terms and relationship distinctions that can inform cataloging, advisory, preservation, and cross-collection discovery for library game services.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the Lee, Cho, Fox, and Perti (2013) paragraph. Proposed text: "Lee, Clarke, Sacchi, and Jett (2014) extend the GAMER Group's user-centered metadata work by defining relationship metadata for games and interactive media. Their four grouping entities, Series, Franchise, Universe, and Collection, and 11 relationship types, including port, remake, reboot, prequel, sequel, side story, spinoff, crossover, spiritual successor, inspiration, and complements, show why library game discovery cannot rely on broad 'related work' links alone; patrons, researchers, and preservationists need to distinguish versions, platforms, narrative order, intellectual-property families, additional content, and influence relationships."

# **Pinedo, R., Garcia-Martin, N., Rascon, D., Caballero-San Jose, C., & Canas, M. (2022). Reasoning and learning with board game-based learning: A case study. *Current Psychology, 41*(3), 1603-1617. https://doi.org/10.1007/s12144-021-01744-1**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `SPI4S64W`; Better BibTeX citation key `pinedoReasoningLearningBoard2022`; attachment key `E2GMLBUR`.

Pinedo, Garcia-Martin, Rascon, Caballero-San Jose, and Canas present a qualitative case study of board game-based learning in a public school in Castilla y Leon, Spain. Their purpose is to understand what game-based learning means for a school's educational community and how it affects the teaching-learning process. The article defines GBL as the use of games to support learning in didactic environments or as educational tools, then connects play to twenty-first-century competencies, motivation, executive functions, reasoning, metacognition, cooperation, participation, and educational inclusion.

The study's setting is a public primary school that had introduced a GBL project beginning in the 2016-17 academic year. Teachers received training from board-game experts, introduced the methodology over two school years, and reserved weekly time and space for 30-to-60-minute board-game sessions. The school held 45 board games and changed the games each trimester. The design also included family participation: a family volunteer oversaw and encouraged each group of students while the form tutor moved among groups, supported play, and observed what was happening.

Data came from document analysis, observation, a teacher focus group, and interviews. The observed students were 45 fifth- and sixth-grade students, including students with special educational needs. The researchers conducted 24 observation sessions with groups of four to five players. They also held a focus group with five tutors who had at least two years of GBL experience, interviewed three students, one family volunteer, and one fifth-grade teacher, and analyzed school documents such as the Education Project, Curricular Project, and Annual General Program. The analysis used inductive coding in ATLAS.ti, triangulation across sources, and inter-rater reliability checks.

The findings are organized around three broad themes: educational characteristics of GBL, quality learning, and participation/inclusion. Under educational characteristics, the educational community described games as motivating, emotionally positive, and flexible. Games created challenge, pleasure, confidence, curiosity, and a safer sense of failure than ordinary academic tasks. Teachers and students also described games as adaptable because rules and difficulty could be adjusted to different knowledge levels, learning styles, and special educational needs.

Under quality learning, the study argues that board games can support curricular content, key competencies, cognitive abilities, and executive functions. Participants connected games to oral and written expression, math, estimation, social competence, learning to learn, planning, working memory, emotional regulation, cognitive flexibility, inhibition, initiative, reasoning, creativity, and problem solving. The article is careful about assessment difficulty: teachers believed games improved strategies, memory, attention, and curricular competence, but they also struggled to measure those effects inside conventional subject and evaluation structures.

Under participation and inclusion, the article emphasizes that games made it easier for students with and without special educational needs to stay in the same classroom, interact with peers, cooperate, take turns, manage conflict, and practice social behaviors in a familiar and low-risk environment. Family participation also mattered: volunteers helped sessions run smoothly, improved the family-school connection, gave teachers more room to observe students, and helped families understand classroom methods that could continue at home.

For the larger GBLS project, Pinedo et al. provide useful adjacent evidence about why analog games can support motivation, inclusion, social interaction, and executive-function practice when adults intentionally structure play. Their findings can help librarians think about why board-game programs may need facilitation, family or volunteer support, adaptable rules, inclusive grouping, and reflection on learning goals. The case is especially suggestive for school-library or youth-services programming that partners with teachers or families.

However, the article should not be added to `review.md` because it is not library literature. It studies classroom pedagogy in a Spanish public school, not a library collection, library program, librarian-led instruction, game advisory service, circulating game collection, public-library family program, or school-library collaboration. It does not discuss libraries, librarians, library spaces, cataloging, collections, access, outreach, assessment of library services, or patron outcomes. Because the GBLS review already includes direct library sources on tabletop collections, school-library tabletop gaming, public-library board-game programs, and assessment, this article is best retained as explicitly excluded background rather than cited in the main review.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because the article is a school-classroom board game-based learning case study, not evidence about game-based library services.

# **Porter, M., Sewell, A., Bergstrom, G., & Costello, D. (2024). RTRT: International Games Month. *Google Docs*. https://docs.google.com/document/d/1cC7xc5nZrYRHbFUBOKRetIaRZBtpVP_fAbv4p6Q1Zjs/edit?usp=sharing&usp=embed_facebook**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `ZG9NXQB5`; Better BibTeX citation key `RTRTInternationalGames2024`; attachment key `5P5GQ55S`.

This item is a one-page Round Table Round Table resource sheet for International Games Month, associated with Molly Porter, Amber Sewell, George Bergstrom, and Danielle Costello. The PDF is not an article, case study, assessment report, or practitioner essay. Instead, it gathers links for librarians and GameRT participants who want to learn about International Games Month and related ALA GameRT activity.

It also includes basic conduct rules for GameRT Twitch activity: kindness and respect, no hate speech, moderator removal authority, reporting harassment to a GameRT Twitch moderator, and the option to send a direct message for private concerns. The sheet closes with GameRT blog, Facebook, resource, website, and email links.

For the larger GBLS project, this resource is relevant as evidence of the professional infrastructure around library gaming. It shows that GameRT treats International Games Month as an ongoing program format with shared web infrastructure, examples, public-resource lists, and moderation policies for online gaming spaces. It also points toward practical topics that matter for library game services: event examples, public-facing guides, virtual programming, community conduct norms, and professional peer support.

However, the PDF itself should not be cited in `review.md` as substantive literature. It does not describe a specific library program in enough detail to analyze goals, staffing, participants, game formats, access barriers, outcomes, or assessment. It does not synthesize International Games Month practice, evaluate GameRT resources, or make an argument about game-based library services. The useful evidence likely lives in the linked IGM website, GameRT articles, and local library event pages, not in this brief link sheet.

This item is therefore best retained as explicitly excluded literature/resource material. It may be useful later as a starting point for locating examples of International Games Month programming or GameRT policy language, but adding it to the review would add a citation without adding much analysis. The current GBLS review already has stronger sources for gaming event history, library gaming programs, public-library TTRPG services, tabletop collections, virtual gaming, and professional rationales.

# **Lanter, J., Norton, S., Wright, A., Porter, M., & Costello, D. (2023). RTRT: Addressing Censorship. *Google Docs*. https://docs.google.com/document/u/1/d/13OhgA3ODQFRkl6MrG2miUghXfHjRKMqm5NJEgwKZgOc/edit?usp=drive_web&ouid=104872546336741826329&usp=embed_facebook**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `EKQ3AVH9`; Better BibTeX citation key `RTRTAddressingCensorship2023`; attachment key `CP4N5QCW`.

This item is a four-page Round Table Round Table resource document on addressing censorship, associated with Julia Lanter, Shawn Norton, Amie Wright, Molly Porter, and Danielle Costello. It appears to support a professional conversation involving the Graphic Novels & Comics Round Table and GameRT rather than serve as a research article or program report. The document is structured as a resource list with sections for toolkits, articles, webinars and courses, podcasts, LibGuides, books, websites, videos, additional notes, GameRT Twitch policy, and round table contact information.

The censorship resources are relevant to game-based library services because games, comics, graphic novels, role-playing games, and other media formats often share challenge and moral-panic contexts. The sheet points to the GNCRT Addressing Challenges Toolkit, the Urban Libraries Unite Drag Story Hour Support workbook, ALA and Booklist resources about comic-book bans and challenges, articles on Dungeons & Dragons and the 1980s Satanic Panic, articles on video games and violence claims, a National Coalition Against Censorship timeline of video-game controversies, and webinars on what to do before and during a challenge. It also lists books such as Joseph Laycock's *Dangerous Games*, Jon Chad's *Pinball*, and David Hajdu's *The Ten-Cent Plague*, along with broader banned-books and anti-censorship resources.

The most directly practical part of the document is the additional-notes section. It emphasizes having a collection development plan, treating community outreach and public support as important resources, working on legislative alternatives, lobbying state and local officials, and using the GNCRT Addressing Challenges Toolkit for collection development plans and challenge policies. The GameRT Twitch policy also names conduct expectations for online spaces: kindness and respect, no hate speech, moderator authority, reporting harassment to moderators, and private messaging for sensitive concerns.

For the larger GBLS project, the resource is useful as implementation scaffolding. It reminds librarians that game collections and programs are not only logistical or recreational services; they may require intellectual-freedom preparation, collection-development policy language, challenge procedures, community allies, public communication, online moderation norms, and awareness of older controversies around comics, D&D, video games, and violent media. This context matters for libraries offering TTRPGs, video-game collections, gaming streams, graphic-novel-adjacent programs, cosplay or fandom events, or teen media programming.

However, this PDF should not be added to `review.md` as substantive literature. It does not present original research, a library case study, a challenge-response analysis, or an evaluated GBLS program. It does not explain how a specific library handled a challenge to games or game programming, nor does it offer enough sustained argument to cite as evidence for outcomes, service models, or policy effectiveness. The review already includes stronger direct sources for policy and challenge issues, such as Sanders (1982) on local controversy around a public-library D&D club and Cross, Mould, and Smith (2015) on challenged-materials policies within an academic library game collection. This item is better kept as a resource-trail note for future implementation work.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because this is a censorship-resource link sheet and discussion handout, not a substantive study, program report, or practitioner article about game-based library services.

# **Queertacular Resource List. (2024). *Google Docs*. https://docs.google.com/document/d/1TZ8Ub6hqYjbFaD0LO6LBwxxZvHcsJRuxMcJk0WA5in4/edit?usp=sharing&usp=embed_facebook**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `4UDVWW6X`; Better BibTeX citation key `QueertacularResourceList2024`; attachment key `B45P2R39`.

This item is a nine-page crowd-sourced Queertacular resource list recommended by members of several ALA round tables: the Film & Media Round Table, Games & Gaming Round Table, Graphic Novels & Comics Round Table, and Map and Geospatial Information Round Table. It accompanies a Twitch-broadcast event and includes a survey link, recording link, GameRT Twitch policy, schedule of events, queer media recommendations, and round table contact information.

The event schedule includes a round table discussion about queer representation and creators across media, a dramatic comics reading, a film screening and director chat, a queer geographies segment with an interactive Worldle-style game, and a GameRT-led liveplay of *Yazeba's Bed & Breakfast*. The resource list then organizes recommendations across books, comics, film and television, games, maps and geospatial information, actual plays, archives, zines, magazines, awards, newsletters, and gaming/geek-culture publications.

The games section is directly relevant as a recommendation trail for queer game collections and programming. It lists tabletop, digital, and role-playing games such as *Artisans of Splendent Vale*, *Celeste*, *Coffee Talk*, *Dream Askew*, *Legacy of Dragonholt*, *Life is Strange: True Colors*, *Night in the Woods*, *Our Mundane Supernatural Life*, *The Queer Agenda*, *Romantic*, *Sapphic Space Pirates*, *Stonewall Uprising*, *Thirsty Sword Lesbians*, *Undertale*, *Unpacking*, and *Visigoths vs Mall Goths*. The other-content section adds actual play streams and podcasts, the LGBTQ Video Game Archive, queer zines, the Nonbinary Tabletop Award, Gayming Magazine, and newsletters highlighting games by gender-marginalized creators.

For the larger GBLS project, this item is useful as a professional resource trail for inclusive collection development, advisory, displays, liveplay programming, cross-round-table collaboration, and queer representation across games, comics, film, books, maps, archives, and zines. It also shows GameRT's recurring moderation infrastructure for online events: kindness and respect, no hate speech, moderator authority, harassment reporting, and private communication options.

However, it should not be added to `review.md` as literature evidence. The document does not study library gaming programs, analyze patron outcomes, document a library collection policy, evaluate inclusive game advisory, or describe a specific Queertacular program in enough detail to assess design, staffing, audience, or impact. It is a recommendation list and event handout. If the GBLS project later needs an appendix of inclusive games or examples of queer media programming, this document could be useful, but the main review should rely on sources that make claims about game-based library services and their effects.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

No addition recommended. Exclude from the GBLS literature review because this is a crowd-sourced queer media recommendation and event-resource sheet, not a substantive study, program report, or practitioner article about game-based library services.

# **Virello, M. (2019). Putting the "Extra" in Extraordinary: Creating a Library Comic Con. *Children & Libraries: The Journal of the Association for Library Service to Children, 17*(1), 31-34. https://doi.org/10.5860/cal.17.1.31**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `XF4MXTVF`; Better BibTeX citation key `virelloPuttingExtraExtraordinary2019`; attachment key `KTACWW96`.

Virello offers a practitioner account of developing Southington Library ComiCon from an inherited Free Comic Book Day program into a large-scale public-library fandom event. The article begins from an access argument: commercial comic cons can be expensive, and libraries close access gaps not only for materials but also for experiences. A library comic con can therefore give children and families a low-cost way to encounter characters, stories, performances, activities, and community enthusiasm that they might otherwise miss.

The program began modestly in 2013 as a two-hour passive Free Comic Book Day event in one lower-level room, with a craft, coloring sheets, photos with a cardboard standee, and a scavenger hunt. About thirty people attended. In the following year, Virello added live-action characters through friends who cosplayed as Wolverine, Robin, Spider-Man, and Wonder Woman, and replaced passive activities with hands-on stations such as Kryptonite Disposal, Professor X's Guess, and Spidey's Web. A local comic shop donated comics, families and staff were encouraged to dress up, and attendance rose to seventy.

The article then traces how community participation, staff enthusiasm, and local connections turned the program into a full Southington Library ComiCon. Virello attended commercial comic conventions, learned from panels, exhibits, cosplayers, and performers, volunteered at a superhero day, and built relationships that later produced free publicity and program contacts. By 2015, the Free Comic Book Day program drew more than two hundred attendees and moved from the lower level to the main floor for visibility and space.

The 2016 full ComiCon required a year of planning, budgeting, organizing, brainstorming, and safety preparation. The event used an All Things Pokemon theme in response to patron interest and the rise of Pokemon Go, while also drawing on broader pop culture and library collections. Publicity included local newspapers, a Facebook event page, paid Facebook advertising, a large exterior sign, online event listings, email blasts, Facebook teasers, desk signage, superhero storytimes, and displays of superhero materials that also boosted circulation.

The article is especially useful because it makes event operations visible. Staff brainstormed around in-house strengths, built Pinterest boards, selected affordable crafts and activities, borrowed or partnered for exhibits, booked a face painter, princess sing-along, and balloon twister, solicited donations from comic shops, worked with the police department on traffic and safety, blocked off parking lots, monitored building capacity, and adapted food access with a free water tent and a local food truck. Policy mattered too: staff enforced food and dress-code rules, limited exposed skin, restricted replica weapons and projectile props, avoided overly scary costumed characters, and borrowed safety norms from other comic cons rather than inventing them from scratch.

For GBLS, the program matters because it blends game-based service with fandom, collection promotion, and community event design. Virello lists all-day activities such as scavenger hunts, coloring, video game tournaments, tabletop gaming, storytimes, crafts, Superhero Training Camp, rotating half-hour or hourly activities, costumed character interactions, displays, giveaways, and themed performances. The program gave patrons active, embodied, social experiences rather than only passive media consumption. It also shows how games can sit inside a broader pop-culture festival: video game tournaments and tabletop gaming are part of a larger constellation of comics, cosplay, character play, crafts, storytimes, performances, and collection discovery.

The outcomes are anecdotal but concrete. Attendance grew from roughly thirty people in the passive FCBD version to seventy after live characters and hands-on stations, more than two hundred after broader con-inspired redesign, and nearly nine hundred at the first official Southington Library ComiCon. Virello does not provide survey data or learning assessment, so the article should be read as an implementation case rather than impact evidence. Its value for the review is practical: large-scale fandom events require a long planning horizon, community partnerships, publicity, internal staff creativity, hands-on activity design, policy and safety planning, rotating schedules, visibility, and a willingness to connect staff passions with patron interests.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models`, immediately after the section heading and before `## Circulating and In-Library Collections`, as a new subsection. Proposed text: "## Fandom Festivals and Pop-Culture Events\nVirello (2019) shows how a public library can scale a fandom event from a passive Free Comic Book Day program into a full library comic con. Southington Library ComiCon combined comic giveaways, cosplay, storytimes, crafts, scavenger hunts, video game tournaments, tabletop gaming, themed displays, performers, community partners, traffic planning, costume and prop rules, food logistics, and rotating activities; attendance grew from about thirty people in the initial FCBD program to nearly nine hundred at the first full ComiCon, making the case useful for GBLS as an access-oriented pop-culture festival model rather than a stand-alone game night."

# **Public Library Association. (2023). *Public Library Services for Strong Communities Report: Results from the 2022 PLA Annual Survey*. Public Library Association.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `W8ZIDSLW`; Better BibTeX citation key `publiclibraryassociationPublicLibraryServices2022`; attachment key `4UQ8I3MQ`.

The Public Library Association's *Public Library Services for Strong Communities Report* is a national public-library survey report rather than a game-focused article, but it contains one of the strongest field-scale data points for GBLS: games and gaming programs are already common teen services in U.S. public libraries. PLA fielded the Public Library Services for Strong Communities Survey in fall 2022 as part of a rotating annual survey set. The survey was designed to gather better data about programs, services, partnerships, and facilities that help libraries meet community needs and sustain resilient communities.

The sampling frame included all U.S. public libraries at the administrative-entity level, as defined by IMLS. In total, 1,167 libraries completed the survey, a 12.6% response rate. PLA weighted the results to account for differences between responding libraries and all U.S. public libraries. The report organizes findings by community needs, services, programs, partnerships, facilities, evaluation, detailed results, and survey methodology.

For the GBLS review, the most important result appears in the teen-program section. The survey asked whether libraries offered specific program types for teens, defined as ages 12 to 18. Overall, 65.6% of libraries reported offering games and gaming programs for teens. That made games and gaming the second most common teen program category, behind summer reading at 86.1% and ahead of STEAM programs at 59.1%. The pattern also held across locale types, although with large capacity differences: 92.3% of city libraries, 83.3% of suburban libraries, and 58.3% of town/rural libraries reported teen games and gaming programs.

This finding is valuable because much of the GBLS literature consists of individual case reports, practitioner articles, local program descriptions, and small studies. PLA's survey shows that teen gaming programs are not marginal curiosities; they are a widely reported public-library program type. The result also helps locate GBLS inside broader youth services. Teen gaming sits beside summer reading, STEAM, teen advisory boards, book clubs, health and wellness programs, media/news literacy, financial literacy, college preparation, and after-school or homework programs.

The report also provides infrastructure context for GBLS implementation. Libraries reported an average of 15.8 program types across children's, teen, and adult/all-ages categories, with city libraries averaging 23.7 types, suburban libraries 20, and town/rural libraries 14. When asked about challenges limiting program and service offerings, 73.4% of responding libraries named staffing, 40.7% funding, 25.4% space, and 14.3% low attendance. These constraints help explain why gaming services vary across libraries and why case reports often emphasize staff interest, partnerships, equipment, room acoustics, policy, and administrative support.

Facilities data also matters for GBLS. The report found that since 2011, libraries had often added or renovated programming and meeting spaces, including public meeting rooms, age-specific programming spaces, and large community event spaces. Current spaces that can accommodate programs or events included dedicated children's spaces in 86.5% of libraries, dedicated teen spaces in 55.7%, large meeting rooms or auditoriums in 57.4%, mid-size meeting rooms in 72.7%, outdoor programming spaces in 57.1%, fixed makerspaces in 20.4%, and technology training spaces in 32.2%. Overall, 17.5% of libraries reported some form of mobile programming facility. For GBLS, these numbers clarify that games are tied to space: recurring game clubs, tournaments, VR programs, tabletop collections, and large fandom events all depend on rooms, age-specific areas, outdoor or mobile capacity, staff, and equipment.

The report's limits are important. It does not define games and gaming programs, separate video games from tabletop games or TTRPGs, report attendance for gaming programs, identify age subgroups within teen services, describe program design, or measure patron outcomes. It also does not ask about adult or all-ages games and gaming as a named program category. Therefore, it cannot tell the review what kinds of gaming programs libraries offer or whether they achieve learning, belonging, literacy, wellness, or access goals. Its best use is as national context showing that teen gaming programs are common enough to deserve systematic study and shared professional knowledge.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Introduction: Why Game-Based Library Services Matter` > `### Work is underway, would benefit from intelligence sharing`, immediately after the sentence beginning "Libraries are developing Game Based Library Programming". Proposed text: "Public Library Association (2023) gives national scale to this claim: in a weighted survey of 1,167 U.S. public library administrative entities, games and gaming programs were reported by 65.6% of libraries for teens, making them the second most common teen program type after summer reading (86.1%) and ahead of teen STEAM programs (59.1%). The same report also shows why local examples need infrastructure context: staffing, funding, space, and attendance limit programming, while teen spaces, meeting rooms, outdoor programming spaces, makerspaces, technology training spaces, and mobile facilities shape which GBLS formats libraries can offer."

# **Goddard, J. (2020). Public libraries respond to the COVID-19 pandemic, creating a new service model. *Information Technology and Libraries, 39*(4), 1-4. https://doi.org/10.6017/ital.v39i4.12847**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `LXYNXFMH`; Better BibTeX citation key `goddardPublicLibrariesRespond2020`; attachment key `YQXNSV2P`.

Goddard's short editorial-board essay describes how public libraries responded to the COVID-19 pandemic by re-writing service rules and creating what the author calls a new service model. The article is not a study of games, play, or game-based programming. Instead, it surveys public-library adaptations during shutdown and reopening: electronic resources, virtual community engagement, real-time reference, contactless curbside service, broadband support, online learning resources, and advocacy for emergency library funding.

The opening claim is that public libraries demonstrated community value during the pandemic by helping patrons resume ordinary life, learn, grow, and stay entertained through resources available by library card. Goddard emphasizes that librarians maintained this level of service by improvising under crisis conditions. Once buildings closed, libraries promoted ebooks and other digital platforms. OverDrive usage rose sharply, new users increased, young adult and juvenile ebook readership grew, and services such as Brainfuse, Miss Humblebee's Academy, Gale Courses, and Gale Presents: Udemy saw increased use as children, families, and adults sought school support and skill development.

The article's infrastructure section matters most for general library-service context. Goddard notes that public libraries supported patrons without sufficient broadband by leaving Wi-Fi available outside buildings, strengthening wireless signals, hosting drive-up Wi-Fi hotspot locations, partnering with schools, and lending hotspots. This digital-equity frame is relevant to GBLS only indirectly: many virtual, hybrid, streaming, Discord-based, or online-game services depend on the same connectivity, device access, and staff-support conditions. However, the article does not discuss game access, game collections, online game communities, or game-program design.

The community-engagement section describes a rapid shift to virtual programming through Facebook Live and Stories, Discord, Instagram, YouTube, Zoom, and GoToMeeting. San Jose Public Library, for example, moved story time and reading programs online, created Spring Into Reading, provided a weekly recorded phone story time, and hosted more than 2,000 virtual events after the March 2020 lockdown. Oceanside Library in New York moved quickly from closure to multiple daily online programs, including story times, book chats, and book clubs, and later opened programs to patrons from outside the state through library partnerships. Goddard writes that patrons were exercising, listening to concerts, taking virtual vacations, learning skills, cooking, playing games, and reducing stress through lockdown programming.

For the larger GBLS project, this source is useful as a reminder that library play and game services are embedded in broader service-model shifts, especially the movement toward hybrid, virtual, and contactless delivery. The mention of Discord and patrons "playing games" points toward possible pandemic-era game programming, but Goddard does not develop that point. There are no program descriptions, patron data, design recommendations, game examples, collection practices, facilitation methods, learning outcomes, or analysis of game-based service. The article is therefore too general to support a substantive GBLS claim.

The concluding section argues that the pandemic-era service model should be preserved as part of the "new normal." Goddard recommends that community programs remain both physical and virtual, physical media and books be delivered both at the desk and through contactless service, and reference continue both in person and through chat. For GBLS, this is a broad service-design backdrop rather than direct literature: future game-based library services may likewise need hybrid access models, digital-equity planning, and online facilitation capacity, but those implications must be drawn from other, more game-specific sources.

Suggested review placement: No direct addition to `review.md`. This source should be filed in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` because it is about public-library pandemic service adaptation generally and mentions games only in passing as one activity among many virtual programs. Proposed review text: None.

# Wyatt, D., & Leorke, D. (2024). Playing in the "third place": How games and play are transforming public libraries. *Space and Culture, 27*(2), 254-273. https://doi.org/10.1177/12063312231223118

Unique identifier: `wyattPlayingThirdPlace2024`; Zotero item key `EU8B37X2`; attachment key `DUYSLXMW`.

Wyatt and Leorke (2024) examine games and play as part of the spatial, temporal, and civic transformation of public libraries. Drawing on observations of library spaces and interviews with 27 library staff and managers in Australia, Finland, and Singapore, with additional examples from the United States and elsewhere, the article argues that play is not merely an added program or collection format. Games and play are reshaping library architecture, furnishings, daily rhythms, atmospheres, acoustics, boundaries, and relations to urban policy.

The article begins by challenging a simple "third place" account of libraries. Library studies often describe the public library as a place between home and work, a voluntary civic space for informal gathering, learning, and sociality. Wyatt and Leorke argue that this language remains useful but also obscures contemporary changes. Libraries are now connected to digital urbanism, creative-city policy, smart-city agendas, entrepreneurial citizenship, co-working, maker culture, digital literacy, and redevelopment projects. In that context, play can support humanistic public life, but it can also be enlisted for instrumental goals such as attracting users, producing flexible workers, supporting creative industries, and demonstrating civic innovation.

The historical section is especially useful for GBLS because it links contemporary gaming to older library debates. Games, toys, puzzles, billiards, children's rooms, and playful materials have appeared in libraries since the nineteenth century, often justified as ways to attract new users, educate youth, provide moral guidance, and offer controlled alternatives to more suspect public leisure. The authors show that contemporary objections to gaming, noise, disruption, frivolity, violence, and replacement of books are not new. They recur whenever libraries expand beyond quiet reading and print-centered seriousness.

The Finnish cases make the argument concrete. Helsinki's Oodi Central Library includes a large digital and analog game collection, a PC Gaming Room, console rooms, VR games, retro/accessible arcade spaces, tabletop rooms, role-playing clubs, and game-literacy sessions. These are not peripheral add-ons: they change how visitors move through the building, where spectators gather, how gameplay becomes visible, how rooms are scheduled, and when the library is busy. Other examples show gaming zones near shelves and makerspaces, dedicated Minecraft rooms, after-school gaming peaks, evening board-game programs, and extended opening hours tied to play.

Wyatt and Leorke also discuss children and teen zones, playful architecture, and library-city boundaries. Singaporean libraries include immersive children's rooms, maker spaces, toys, augmented-reality books, costumes, and interactive walls; Melbourne and Singapore examples include castles, tree houses, animal seating, storytelling areas, and playful learning environments. New library buildings use colorful interiors, reading nooks, responsive installations, playful facades, outdoor play areas, and transparent connections to plazas, parks, playgrounds, waterfronts, and cultural precincts. In this sense, play spills beyond designated programs and becomes part of how libraries perform publicness.

For the larger GBLS project, the article provides a needed conceptual correction. It helps move the review beyond program lists by showing that game-based library services are spatial and institutional. A game night, a gaming room, a VR station, a Minecraft club, a makerspace, or a children's play area changes sound, flow, surveillance, privacy, dwell time, staff explanation, public expectations, and the boundary between the library and the city. GBLS planning therefore has to consider architecture, acoustics, scheduling, furniture, spectatorship, children and teen zones, safety, public criticism, and policy narratives about innovation and creativity.

The article's limits are also clear. It is qualitative, interpretive, and focused largely on well-resourced public-library examples, especially in Finland, Australia, and Singapore. It does not measure program outcomes, attendance, learning, belonging, or patron satisfaction. Its value is not causal evidence but analytic framing: it shows why libraries' increasing investment in play is socially consequential and why the public library as "third place" is both strengthened and unsettled by games.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service` > `## Games, Gamification, and Play`, after the Downie and Proulx (2022) paragraph. Proposed text: "Wyatt and Leorke (2024) widen the review's concept of play by arguing that public-library games are not only artifacts, events, or gamified mechanics. In observations and 27 staff interviews across Australia, Finland, and Singapore, they show that play also appears in architecture, furnishings, acoustics, daily rhythms, children's zones, gaming rooms, makerspaces, outdoor connections, and urban-development narratives, making the library itself more playful while complicating the familiar 'third place' frame."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Khademi Zare, Varnaseri, and Bayati (2024) paragraph. Proposed text: "Wyatt and Leorke (2024) make the public-library commons spatial: gaming rooms, console rooms, VR play, tabletop spaces, Minecraft areas, children's play zones, playful interiors, and porous links to plazas and parks change how patrons gather, watch, move, make noise, dwell, and understand who the library is for. Their cases show that GBLS can transform the atmosphere and civic meaning of the library, not just its program calendar."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Plan for Operations and Sustainability`, after the Swiatek and Gorsse (2016) paragraph. Proposed text: "Wyatt and Leorke (2024) show that operational planning for GBLS includes spatial and atmospheric governance. Libraries that add gaming zones, transparent console rooms, VR, Minecraft, evening board games, playful children's areas, or outdoor play links must manage noise, visibility, privacy, spectatorship, school-hour temptation, dwell time, public criticism, and the tension between welcoming play and preserving quiet, safe, multipurpose civic space."

# **Mon, L. M. (2012). Professional avatars: Librarians and educators in virtual worlds. *Journal of Documentation, 68*(3), 318-329. https://doi.org/10.1108/00220411211225566**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `TTGSZMWW`; Better BibTeX citation key `monProfessionalAvatarsLibrarians2012`; attachment key `ERZP5GNB`.

Mon examines librarianship and education in virtual world settings, focusing on how librarians and educators establish professional identity and trust while teaching and providing information services as avatars in Second Life. The study treats virtual worlds as highly graphical, immersive, and "game-like" settings where the professional self is not only textual or institutional but visibly embodied. For GBLS, this article helps explain why library work inside game worlds or game-adjacent virtual platforms requires more than simply opening a virtual branch: staff must manage avatar appearance, spatial cues, credibility, patron expectations, and platform culture.

The study uses a qualitative, ethnographic approach.
