Mon spent more than a year and a half as a participant observer in Second Life librarian and educator communities, learning the platform through roles such as patron, librarian, student, educator, group member, event organizer, meeting leader, conference attendee, tour leader, and virtual workplace builder. The interview phase included 12 in-world semi-structured interviews with seven librarians and five educators, conducted in Second Life workplaces or research facilities, along with visits to 50 virtual world workplaces. The analysis is framed through Goffman's concepts of presentation of self, face, role, setting, frame, and control over information and conversation.

The article's central finding is that librarians and educators had to establish professional identity, credibility, and trust for two different audiences: "newbies" and "residents." Newer users could be reassured by conservative, humanoid, friendly, professional avatar appearances and familiar workplace imagery such as books, bookshelves, computers, classrooms, and reference desks. Experienced Second Life residents, however, often read avatar quality as cultural competence. For them, a default or visibly novice avatar could reduce credibility, so librarians and educators sometimes purchased higher-quality skins, clothing, animations, and accessories to signal that they belonged in the virtual world.

Mon's findings are especially useful because they show that virtual-world library service is embodied and performative. Librarians and educators described wanting avatars that would be "taken seriously," and female avatars often struggled to find professional clothing in a marketplace oriented toward entertainment and display. Some participants changed avatar gender, ethnicity, or style depending on community expectations. Others used avatars as part of learning scenarios, historical reenactments, or case-study performances, making the avatar itself part of the informational environment rather than merely a pointer to a real-world professional.

The study also shows how virtual work unsettled traditional library space. In the 50 observed Second Life libraries, common features included photos and posters, books and bookshelves, computers, and reference desks. These cues helped visitors recognize scholarly purpose and trust that they were interacting with a "real" librarian, but participants also questioned whether virtual libraries should simply rebuild physical libraries. Some worked anywhere rather than at a stable desk, served international users across time zones and languages, used translation tools, placed reference desks outside buildings, or experimented with open areas and nontraditional environments. The key problem was not whether Second Life could imitate a library, but how visible setting and avatar cues could frame credible information interactions.

Alternative identity is another important finding. Five of the 12 participants maintained separate "alt" identities. Alts could be used for educational videos, role-playing characters, backup inventory, research, money management, or uninterrupted work when a main professional avatar was visible to contacts and likely to receive instant messages. This helps GBLS think about staff presence in online game worlds: persistent identity can build relationships, but it also creates interruption, privacy, labor, and boundary-management issues.

For the larger GBLS project, Mon contributes to the virtual-platform strand rather than to game programming narrowly. The article does not study a game night, game collection, or designed game-based learning program. Its relevance is that Second Life was widely perceived as a game or game-like environment, and librarians had to make professional service legible within that frame. Mon therefore gives the review vocabulary for avatar-mediated service, professional identity, credibility, trust, workplace staging, and platform-native expectations. These issues remain relevant for libraries considering services inside Minecraft, Roblox, social VR, Meta Horizon Worlds, Discord-linked game communities, or other persistent virtual environments where library staff appear through avatars, handles, or designed spaces.

The article's limits are also clear. It is based on a small interview sample from 2008 and a platform moment that has changed substantially. It does not measure patron outcomes, assess long-term service sustainability, or compare virtual-world library services with other online channels. Still, its ethnographic detail makes it one of the stronger sources for understanding the social mechanics of library work in game-like virtual environments, especially the tension between being recognizably professional and being fluent enough in the platform culture to be trusted by residents.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the Webber and Nahl (2011) paragraph and before Chow et al. (2012). Proposed text: "Mon (2012) adds a staff-facing account of avatar-mediated professionalism in Second Life. Through participant observation, 12 in-world interviews with librarians and educators, and visits to 50 virtual workplaces, Mon shows that virtual-world library service depended on professional avatar presentation, trust cues, and spatial staging: conservative humanoid avatars and familiar bookshelves or reference desks helped reassure newcomers, while polished avatar appearance signaled credibility to experienced residents. For GBLS, the study explains why services inside game-like worlds require platform fluency, identity management, and credible staff presence, not only a virtual room or collection."

# Jedlicka, C. M., & Eaton, M. E. (2024). Creating a homemade mobile augmented reality game in a community college library: An open source approach. *Journal of Web Librarianship*. https://doi.org/10.1080/19322909.2024.2391851

Unique identifier: `jedlickaCreatingHomemadeMobile2024`; Zotero item key `CQK5X98J`; attachment key `7KHWCHSP`.

Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) describe how librarians at Kingsborough Community College built a mobile augmented reality orientation game for new students using open-source tools and existing library infrastructure. The project began from a practical outreach problem: the library wanted a fun, welcoming activity that would orient students to library locations and services, reduce unease about the academic library, and provide a positive early experience. Instead of adopting a commercial AR platform, the librarians built a browser-based Web AR game using AR.js, A-Frame, JavaScript, and LibGuides.

The article is especially valuable because it documents the constraints behind the design. KCC is a public community college library with no budget for the activity, limited staff time, no large programming team, weak Wi-Fi, and a two-month launch window. Those constraints made a commercial app less attractive: students might need to download another app, storage and Wi-Fi could become barriers, vendors might change terms or discontinue platforms, and subscription costs might not fit library values or budgets. An open-source, browser-based approach let the game load once, run client-side without network calls during play, avoid app downloads, and remain shareable through openly available code.

The game itself was a self-guided AR tour. Students scanned a QR code, enabled their phone camera, and walked around the library looking for printed visual markers. Scanning each marker triggered a virtual shape and checked off a location in a drop-down progress list. After all eight markers were found, students saw a congratulations screen and returned to librarians for a small candy reward and praise. The locations were limited to the library's second floor, where service points and student-centered amenities such as the Reference Desk, Circulation, computers, printers, leisure books, and study rooms were located. A donated tablet and a paper checklist were available for students without compatible devices or for accessibility support.

The results are modest but concrete. In initial drop-in beta testing during two afternoon reference shifts, 12 students completed the activity and a similar number began but did not finish, often because they passed by with a class that lacked time. Slow Wi-Fi sometimes delayed the initial load, but once the game loaded it ran smoothly; 10 of 12 completers used their own phones and two used the library tablet. Students quickly adapted to the game, mostly ignored the paper checklist, and gave positive comments such as "fun," "cool," "pretty clear," and "pretty entertaining." One student said the activity helped him get to know the library and discover study rooms.

The second phase changed the service model. When librarians integrated the AR activity into introductory library instruction sessions, completion and engagement improved substantially: all 34 students across three sessions completed the activity. Student and faculty feedback remained positive, and students described the activity as engaging, fun, a good starter for new people, and useful even for someone who had been enrolled for years but did not know everything in the library. The authors conclude that the AR game worked better as a class-based activity than as an elective standalone game.

For the larger GBLS project, this source contributes practical implementation knowledge. It shows that AR orientation does not require a commercial platform or professional developer, but it does require a technically curious librarian with JavaScript skill, planning time, iterative testing, device contingencies, marker printing, staff facilitation, and a willingness to accept imperfect technology. It also shows how game-based orientation can be tied to actual library space rather than abstract service information. The strongest design lesson is to match the game format to the workflow: a self-guided drop-in activity may seem flexible, but classroom integration can produce better participation and completion.

The limitations are significant. Data collection was informal, based on observation and brief verbal feedback rather than formal surveys, learning tests, anxiety measures, or long-term use data. Accessibility remained a concern because AR is heavily visual and paper workarounds are not equivalent to the AR experience. Technical sustainability is also unresolved: JavaScript libraries change quickly, AR.js development has slowed, LibGuides analytics were limited, and future functionality would require more coding. Even so, the case is useful because it makes the hidden infrastructure of a small, open-source GBLS project visible.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Intawong, Puritat, Leenaraj, and Arayaphan (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) add a low-budget community-college AR orientation case: Kingsborough librarians built a browser-based scavenger-hunt-style tour with AR.js, A-Frame, JavaScript, and LibGuides rather than a commercial app. Students scanned markers around the library to reveal virtual shapes and check off service locations; informal feedback was positive, but the activity worked better when embedded in library instruction sessions than as a standalone drop-in game."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, after the Ponto and Waddle (2023) paragraph. Proposed text: "Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) show that immersive GBLS can be small, browser-based, and locally built. Their Web AR tour avoided app downloads, minimized Wi-Fi dependence after initial load, used existing LibGuides infrastructure, and published code openly, but it also required JavaScript skill, marker testing, device backup, accessibility workarounds, and ongoing attention to open-source maintainability."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services` > `## Match Game Form to Service Goal`, immediately after the heading. Proposed text: "Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) offer a concrete form-fit lesson: an AR scavenger hunt can orient students to physical locations, but participation depends on the surrounding service workflow. Their game was technically playable as a self-guided drop-in activity, yet completion and engagement improved when it became part of scheduled library instruction."

# **Bronack, S., Sanders, R., Cheney, A., Riedl, R., Tashner, J., & Matzen, N. (2008). Presence pedagogy: Teaching and learning in a 3D virtual immersive world. *International Journal of Teaching and Learning in Higher Education, 20*(1), 59-69.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `ZYRV8L4E`; Better BibTeX citation key `bronackPresencePedagogyTeaching`; attachment key `NQX8NNL3`.

Bronack, Sanders, Cheney, Riedl, Tashner, and Matzen describe Presence Pedagogy, or the P2 Model, through AET Zone, a 3D immersive virtual world used for more than seven years in Appalachian State University's Reich College of Education. The article is not about public libraries or game programming, but it is directly relevant to the GBLS strand on services inside game-like virtual worlds because it treats a persistent 3D environment as a social, instructional, and information-service space. Its opening example includes graduate students working in AET Zone, library science students posted at a virtual reference desk in front of a virtual library, and a higher education student being escorted by a reference librarian avatar into virtual stacks to locate resources.

The article argues that conventional distance education tools do not sufficiently support social presence, serendipitous interaction, informal learning, or communities of practice. AET Zone was built as an alternative: a persistent 3D environment where students, faculty, peers, experts, novices, tools, and resources are available across course, cohort, department, and program boundaries. The authors ground this model in social constructivist theory and describe learning as participation in a community of practice where knowledge is socially constructed, learners move from novice to expert with guidance, and shared tools, artifacts, dispositions, and repertoires accumulate over time.

Presence Pedagogy emphasizes that everyone in the virtual community can become a potential instructor, peer, expert, or novice. This matters for GBLS because many game-based or virtual-world services depend on co-presence rather than content delivery alone. In AET Zone, students can see others through avatars, approach them, use text and audio communication, ask questions, receive just-in-time support, and move through spaces designed to prompt conversation and discovery. The authors describe P2 as student powered and instructor navigated: learning emerges through feedback loops among learners, instructors, tools, tasks, spaces, and the community.

The article identifies ten core P2 principles: asking questions and correcting misperceptions; stimulating background knowledge and expertise; capitalizing on the presence of others; facilitating interactions and encouraging community; supporting distributed cognition; sharing tools and resources; encouraging exploration and discovery; delineating context and goals to act upon; fostering reflective practice; and using technology to achieve and disseminate results. These principles are connected to concrete virtual-world places, including the So What? Saloon, the Case Study Conference Center, the Commons, Information Gardens, the Training Shoppe, the Discussion Depot, Chit Chats, the Break Time Game House, Wiki World, blogs, wikis, databases, visual cues, and greeter bots.

The design details are especially useful for GBLS. The Commons brings participants into shared space at the start of each session, increasing the likelihood of cross-cohort and cross-program interaction. Information Gardens functions as a virtual library. Spaces such as coffee houses, saloons, depots, shops, newsstands, hypermazes, and game houses give abstract learning activities visible context and navigation. Resources persist in-world, so student work, case analyses, discussion artifacts, RSS feeds, blogs, wikis, journals, databases, and multimedia become part of a living curriculum. These are not game mechanics in the narrow sense, but they show how spatial metaphor, avatar presence, shared artifacts, and exploration can structure learning in ways that resemble game-world service design.

Bronack et al. also provide limited evidence of student response. In a spring 2007 survey of 121 current and former instructional technology students, 71.9% strongly agreed and 21.5% agreed that they felt part of an effective and supportive learning community; 81.8% strongly agreed and 11.6% agreed that the program promoted continuous, collaborative, and active learning. These survey results do not isolate the effect of the 3D world or compare it with other platforms, but they support the authors' claim that AET Zone helped create a durable learning community.

For the larger GBLS project, the article's value is conceptual and design-oriented. It shows why virtual game-like worlds should not be treated as decorative delivery channels. If a library builds services inside Minecraft, Roblox, a social VR world, a campus virtual world, or another persistent online environment, the service needs more than a static replica of a room. It needs visible community entry points, staff and peer presence, tools for synchronous and asynchronous interaction, places where resources can persist, activities that make use of the world, and opportunities for patrons to contribute artifacts back to the shared environment.

The limits are important. Bronack et al. study higher education, not public-library GBLS; the article does not analyze entertainment games, patron gaming programs, teen services, public access, collection development, or library outcomes. It also comes from an early 3D-world moment and describes an institutionally controlled platform rather than a public commercial game world. The source should therefore support the virtual-platform section as adjacent design evidence, not as direct evidence that libraries should run game-world programs.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Programming Formats and Service Models` > `## Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, immediately after the Mon (2012) paragraph and before Chow et al. (2012). Proposed text: "Bronack et al. (2008) broaden the virtual-platform discussion with AET Zone, a 3D immersive learning world that included a virtual library, Information Gardens, a virtual reference desk, and library science students serving as reference librarian avatars. Their Presence Pedagogy model treats persistent virtual worlds as communities of practice built through co-presence, shared spaces, just-in-time peer and expert support, reusable artifacts, visual metaphors, and exploration; for GBLS, the article is adjacent rather than library-program evidence, but it clarifies why game-like service worlds need social infrastructure and activity design rather than static room replicas."

# Hobart, E. (2024). Describing games for special collections libraries. *RBM: A Journal of Rare Books, Manuscripts, and Cultural Heritage, 25*(1), 50-64. https://doi.org/10.5860/rbm.25.1.50

Unique identifier: `hobartDescribingGamesSpecial2024`; Zotero item key `CG9SZAXC`; attachment key `Y5ZH3PRJ`.

Hobart (2024) describes the process of cataloging games in the Eberly Family Special Collections Library at Pennsylvania State University. The article begins from a cataloging problem that is central to GBLS infrastructure: games appear in special collections, but catalogers are often trained around printed formats, and no single standard fully addresses games as rare or special-collections objects. RDA provides general instructions, OLAC's object best practices gather guidance for three-dimensional objects, and the DCRM manuals support artifact-level description, but special-collections game cataloging often requires drawing from all of them.

The Penn State game collection included sixty-four cataloged items as of February 2024, chiefly from the nineteenth century, with some twentieth- and twenty-first-century materials. Formats included toys, card games, board games, puzzles, blocks, toy books, and construction games. Hobart explains why Penn State chose item-level MARC 21 cataloging rather than only archival or group-level description: the games did not share one provenance, country, genre, date range, or access point, and researchers needed detailed information about printers, techniques, materials, formats, provenance, and copy-specific condition.

The article's two examples, *Bal d'enfants: travesti* and *Musikalische Zauber-Karten*, show how detailed catalog records can make games legible as both play objects and research artifacts. Records included transcribed title and publication/manufacture information, supplied dates and places when necessary, physical descriptions that enumerated components, RDA content/media/carrier types, source-of-title notes, summaries of gameplay and physical construction, citations to external references, local notes for provenance or imperfections, subject headings, genre/form terms, and added entries for printers, publishers, lithographers, and former owners.

Several technical points are directly useful for the GBLS review. Penn State cataloged games using the visual materials workform in OCLC; coded MARC leader/006 as "r" for three-dimensional artifact or naturally occurring object; coded MARC 008/33 as "g" for game or "w" for toy; and added a local Blacklight facet labeled "Games/Toys." Hobart notes that other catalogs used varied and less user-facing format terms such as three-dimensional object, image, other, kit, realia, object, manuscript/archive, non-print, or unknown. The Penn State facet made the game collection more discoverable to users.

For the larger GBLS project, Hobart strengthens the argument that game collections require metadata labor beyond acquisition. Circulating and special-collections game services depend on description that can support discovery, identification, condition tracking, provenance research, component inventories, publisher/printer research, and genre access. This is especially important for older, unique, incomplete, or materially complex games that cannot be adequately represented by title and broad format alone.

The article also identifies infrastructure gaps. DCRMR currently addresses books, DCRM(G) is closest but defines graphic material around two-dimensional representation, OLAC is strong for objects but oriented toward general object cataloging, LCGFT is limited for games and toys, and detailed reference sources for the bibliographic history of games are uncommon. Hobart notes that cataloging from one specimen inevitably flattens some manifestation-level history, but also argues that getting games into catalogs and enhancing those records increases the likelihood that users will find them.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, after the Bianchini and Munini (2025) paragraph. Proposed text: "Hobart (2024) brings special-collections practice into the game-metadata discussion through Penn State's item-level cataloging of 64 toys and games. Combining RDA, OLAC object best practices, and DCRM guidance, the records used visual-materials coding, component inventories, summary notes, citations, provenance and imperfection notes, subject headings, genre/form terms, publisher and printer access points, and a local `Games/Toys` discovery facet so games could be identified as artifacts and found as research materials."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda` > `## Infrastructure and Metadata Gaps`, after the Bianchini and Munini (2025) paragraph. Proposed text: "Hobart (2024) adds a special-collections metadata gap: existing standards help but do not fully cover rare or unique games. RDA disperses three-dimensional-object guidance, OLAC focuses on object best practices rather than rare-materials needs, DCRM guidance is strongest for books and two-dimensional graphics, game genre/form vocabularies remain limited, and detailed bibliographic reference works for games are scarce, forcing local policy decisions for description, facets, provenance, components, and copy-specific notes."

# **Montola, M., & Stenros, J. (Eds.). (2008). *Playground worlds: Creating and evaluating experiences of role-playing games*. Ropecon ry.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `FR6RXCIL`; Better BibTeX citation key `montolaPlaygroundWorldsCreating2008`; attachment key `7E34TTR6`.

Montola and Stenros's edited volume *Playground Worlds* was published in conjunction with Solmukohta 2008 and belongs to the Nordic role-play/Knutepunkt book tradition. It is a large role-playing and larp design collection rather than library literature. The book gathers journalism, community reflection, design essays, production accounts, and research/theory pieces about tabletop role-playing, freeform, live-action role-playing, pervasive larp, parlor larp, game criticism, documentation, immersion, fantasy, medievalism, and role-playing pedagogy.

The editors frame the volume around "playground worlds": game worlds and ordinary-world play situations that can function as amusement parks, test laboratories, or efforts to expose and impose new perspectives on everyday life. The introduction emphasizes that characters do not exist without worlds and that role-playing games can create powerful experiences of special places and times. Examples include Dragonbane, Ground Zero, The White Road, Agabadan, A Nice Evening with the Family, Agerborn, Totem, and Sanningen om Marika. The editors also describe three design ideals in recent Nordic larp: powerful drama, the physically immersive 360-degree illusion, and pervasive larp, where play spills into streets and ordinary society.

The book is organized into three major sections. "Journalism & Community" includes accounts of the Role-Players' School at Osterskov Efterskole, role-playing game criticism, Dragonbane as ephemera and ruin, event production, work minimization, and British live-action role-play history. "Art & Design" turns to design process, high-resolution larping, hobo-drifter play, adventurous romanticism, jeepform, facade and family drama, narrativism, interaction design, indulgence, and parlor larp. "Research & Theory" includes essays on Ground Zero, adult pretend play, connections between fantasy play in children and adults, Allan Kaprow and happenings, Forge theory, broadcast culture and role-playing culture, larp and modernity, and fantasy/medievalism in role-playing games.

Several chapters are potentially useful as background for GBLS even though the volume is not directly citable as library evidence. Malik Hyltoft's chapter on Osterskov Efterskole describes a school that bases teaching on role-playing and other forms of game play, with narrative units, workshops, portfolio reflection, common ownership, and teachers designing learning materials around role-play structures. Design chapters discuss production labor, interaction design, parlor larp constraints, pervasive-game boundaries, and documentation problems. Theory chapters connect larp to adult pretend play, child fantasy play, performance art, immersion, character, and social worlds. These themes could inform librarians designing TTRPG/LARP programs, game design workshops, or reflective debriefs.

For the larger GBLS project, however, the volume's relevance is indirect. It can help a reviewer understand role-playing and larp as serious design cultures with traditions, vocabulary, production practices, documentation challenges, and theories of immersion and play. It also reminds GBLS work that role-playing services are not only about rules or entertainment; they involve worlds, roles, boundaries, labor, atmosphere, player culture, and post-event documentation. Those insights may be useful for program design, staff training, and comparison with library TTRPG literature.

The source should not be added to `review.md` because it does not discuss libraries as service providers, library collections, circulation, cataloging, outreach, youth services, public programming, information literacy, or patron outcomes. It is too broad to serve as evidence for what libraries do with games. A citation to this volume would shift the review toward general role-playing studies unless paired with a very specific argument about LARP design theory. The current review has more directly relevant TTRPG and LARP library sources for service models, access, facilitation, and community outcomes.

Suggested review placement: No direct addition to `review.md`. File in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` because this is a broad RPG/LARP design and theory volume, not a library-service source. Proposed review text: None.

# **Hassinger-Das, B., Zosh, J. M., Hansen, N., Talarowski, M., Zmich, K., Golinkoff, R. M., & Hirsh-Pasek, K. (2020). Play-and-learn spaces: Leveraging library spaces to promote caregiver and child interaction. *Library & Information Science Research, 42*(1), 101002. https://doi.org/10.1016/j.lisr.2020.101002**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `VKFJS5P8`; Better BibTeX citation key `hassinger-dasPlayandlearnSpacesLeveraging2020`; attachment key `PYXWDYJK`.

Hassinger-Das, Zosh, Hansen, Talarowski, Zmich, Golinkoff, and Hirsh-Pasek study how children's library spaces can be redesigned to promote playful learning, caregiver-child interaction, and discourse associated with language, literacy, and STEM development. The article is not about games in the narrow sense, but it is highly relevant to GBLS because it treats play as library infrastructure. It asks whether a public library's built environment can be redesigned so that families naturally engage in richer talk, movement, story creation, and shared attention during ordinary library visits.

The project responds to two linked problems. First, children spend most of their time outside formal school, so everyday spaces can help address language, reading-readiness, spatial-skill, and opportunity gaps. Second, public libraries are reimagining themselves as more than repositories for books, increasingly becoming community, activity, maker, health, and family-learning spaces. The Play-and-Learn Spaces project joined developmental science with this changing library role by embedding playful learning prompts directly into children's library areas.

The study took place in four Free Library of Philadelphia branches: three Play-and-Learn libraries and one control library. The design process included Studio Ludo, Smith Memorial Playground & Playhouse, the architecture firm DIGSAU, library staff, patrons, and community feedback sessions. Participants drew, built, painted, and discussed the kinds of play-and-learn spaces they wanted. Three major community themes emerged: more child-friendly physical spaces because children spent time after school doing homework; the library as a safe space while waiting for caregiver pickup; and more interactive activities for families with young children in the morning.

The resulting installations transformed children's areas into architectural prompts for play and learning. A climbing wall let children create words by climbing through different letters. Seating became large movable Tangram-like blocks that fit into reading nooks. A stage with magnetic play surfaces and letters invited children to create stories and engage in sociodramatic play. A perching tower offered space to climb, play, hide away, and read quietly. Each branch received a different combination of these elements.

Methodologically, the study used naturalistic observation rather than surveys or tests. Researchers compared Play-and-Learn and Non-Play-and-Learn conditions using data from pre-installation periods, a control library, and post-installation Play-and-Learn observations from December 2017 through August 2018. The sample included 72 observed groups in the Play-and-Learn condition and 83 groups in the Non-Play-and-Learn condition, with children roughly ages 1 to 10 and accompanying caregivers or child groups. Observers were trained, blind to hypotheses, and had to reach 85% agreement on interaction scoring.

The results are unusually useful for GBLS because they go beyond attendance. Children's programming attendance increased at all three Play-and-Learn branches after installation: 190% at Library A, 158% at Library B, and 201% at Library C, for an average increase of 189%, while the control library decreased during the same period. Groups also spent longer in Play-and-Learn spaces than in Non-Play-and-Learn spaces. Child interaction scores and combined caregiver/child interaction scores were significantly higher in the Play-and-Learn condition, though caregiver-only interaction scores did not reach significance.

The observed interaction quality also shifted. Children in Play-and-Learn spaces used technology less often, interacted physically with the space more frequently, and showed more laughter. Caregivers and children used more spatial-related and letter/sound-related language in the Play-and-Learn condition, while number and color language did not show the same pattern. The number of conversational turns did not increase, which the authors interpret as a change in the content and tone of conversations rather than simply more talk.

For the larger GBLS project, this source broadens the review's understanding of play. It shows that a library can design for playful learning without scheduling a formal game, buying a board game collection, or adding digital game mechanics. The service object is the space itself: movable objects, letters, climbing routes, stages, magnetic surfaces, reading nooks, and sightlines that invite children and caregivers to talk, move, laugh, imagine, and notice literacy or spatial concepts. This matters for GBLS because many game-based services depend on permissions that the room communicates before staff say anything. A children's area can tell families that play, noise, movement, and shared discovery belong in the library.

The limits are important. The study does not measure child learning outcomes directly, cannot isolate which installation elements produced which effects, does not know whether participants made repeat visits, and uses non-paired naturalistic observations rather than random assignment. Observers could see whether a Play-and-Learn space was present, even though they were blind to hypotheses. Still, the article is one of the stronger empirical sources for library play as spatial service design, especially because it links design, community input, attendance, interaction quality, language use, affect, physical engagement, and technology displacement.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service` > `## Games, Gamification, and Play`, immediately after the Wyatt and Leorke (2024) paragraph. Proposed text: "Hassinger-Das et al. (2020) provide empirical support for treating play as library spatial design rather than only as a program or collection. In three Free Library of Philadelphia branches, Play-and-Learn installations such as a letter climbing wall, movable Tangram-like seating, magnetic story surfaces, a stage, and a perching tower were associated with higher children's program attendance, longer visits, higher child and caregiver/child interaction scores, more spatial and letter/sound language, more laughter, less child technology use, and more physical interaction with the space; the study does not prove learning gains, but it shows that library environments can be intentionally designed to invite playful literacy, STEM talk, movement, and caregiver-child engagement."

# **Casucci, T., Locke, A. B., Henson, A., & Qeadan, F. (2020). A workplace well-being game intervention for health sciences librarians to address burnout. *Journal of the Medical Library Association, 108*(4), 605-617. https://doi.org/10.5195/jmla.2020.742**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `HYAVT29T`; Better BibTeX citation key `casucciWorkplaceWellbeingGame2020`; attachment key `TWUV8VD7`.

Casucci, Locke, Henson, and Qeadan study whether a serious game could improve personal and workplace well-being among employees at the University of Utah's Spencer S. Eccles Health Sciences Library. The article begins with burnout as a workplace syndrome involving emotional exhaustion, depersonalization or cynicism, and reduced personal accomplishment, and then connects burnout to the opposite condition of engagement, described through vigor, dedication, and absorption. The authors note that LIS has a burnout literature, but that health sciences librarians had received little specific attention and that LIS burnout-prevention interventions had not been created and assessed in the field. This makes the study unusual in the GBLS corpus: the game is not for patrons or students, but for library employees themselves.

The project used a modified American Medical Association Mini-Z burnout survey, administered to library faculty in 2016, faculty and staff in 2017, and again in abbreviated form in 2018. The survey included items on job satisfaction, burnout symptoms, job-related stress, teamwork, values alignment with leadership, work atmosphere, and workload control. Because 2017 results showed high burnout, the library and the University of Utah Resiliency Center developed a three-month team-based "Wellness Game" as an intervention. The game was designed to target personal resilience, build a culture of well-being, and give employees control over participation because control over work and environment had appeared as a concern in baseline results.

The game design combined wellness categories, social structure, visible progress, and recognition. Drawing on wellness dimensions, the team selected physical, mental, social, and appreciation as point categories. Appreciation was added because employees reported that they often heard complaints rather than thanks. Library employees were assigned to six cross-departmental teams with captains; participants earned points for any activity related to the categories, with bonus points for doing activities with colleagues. Each team used a large physical game board in the employee break room, and employees could nominate colleagues for serious or funny awards. The game ended with a potluck lunch, awards ceremony, and trophy. This low-tech design matters: the intervention relied on social nudges, shared visibility, team identity, and optional participation rather than on a digital platform.

The quantitative findings were sobering. Library faculty and staff reported substantial burnout and job-related stress, but the game did not significantly reduce burnout or improve job satisfaction. Faculty burnout symptoms were 41.67% in 2016 and 38.46% in 2018; staff burnout symptoms were 61.54% in 2017 and 42.86% in 2018, but the changes were not statistically significant. Several organizational indicators also moved in mixed directions, and the authors argue that broader factors such as workload, leadership, turnover, work atmosphere, and organizational culture likely shaped well-being in ways a single game could not address.

The qualitative findings are more positive, especially around collegiality. Thirty of 59 employees completed the post-game evaluation survey. Seventy percent said the game encouraged them to socialize with others, and more than half reported encouragement in appreciation, physical, and social categories. Free-text responses about what employees enjoyed most were coded into social, motivation, gameplay, and fun; the largest category was social, with comments about team building and increased socializing. Employees reported meeting new people, being invited into conversation in the staff lounge, joining walks or hikes, and eating lunch together. Most respondents said they would play the game again. At the same time, disliked elements included tedious point tracking, uneven team engagement, and excessive duration.

For the GBLS project, Casucci et al. provide a careful caution about wellness-oriented game services. The article supports the idea that games can create low-stakes social contact, recognition, and participation in wellness activities inside library workplaces. It also warns against overstating what a game can do. A playful intervention may build collegiality and make well-being visible, but it may not address root causes of burnout such as workload, leadership, turnover, control, and organizational culture. The study therefore belongs in the review's emerging wellness and care section and in its assessment lessons: success cannot be reduced to enjoyment, participation, or even social connection when the stated problem is structural burnout.

The study's limitations are important but useful. It took place in one health sciences library, used small samples, had uneven response rates, and could not isolate the game from other Resiliency Center and human-resources interventions occurring around the same time. Still, it is one of the clearer empirical examples of a game-based library intervention that reports mixed results rather than a simple success story. It helps GBLS distinguish between games as social and motivational infrastructure and games as insufficient substitutes for organizational change.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Emerging Area: Wellness, Mental Health, and Care`, after the Nance (2022) paragraph.

Suggested text: Casucci et al. (2020) add an employee-facing wellness case from a health sciences library, where a three-month team-based "Wellness Game" used physical, mental, social, and appreciation points to address burnout and workplace well-being. The game did not significantly reduce burnout or improve job satisfaction, but 70% of post-game respondents said it encouraged socializing, making the study a useful caution: playful wellness interventions can strengthen collegiality and recognition without resolving workload, leadership, turnover, and organizational-culture causes of burnout.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Maluck (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Casucci et al. (2020) similarly show why GBLS assessment must align outcomes with the problem being addressed. Their workplace well-being game produced valued social effects, including collegiality, appreciation, and willingness to play again, but it did not significantly change burnout or job satisfaction; for GBLS, that distinction helps separate participation, enjoyment, social connection, and structural impact.

# **Boyle, C. (2018). Play time: Why video games are essential to urban academic libraries. *Urban Library Journal, 24*(1), 1-11.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `NI839YJM`; Better BibTeX citation key `boylePlayTimeWhy2018`; attachment key `S5XQ7U9G`.

Boyle argues that video games are not merely advantageous for academic libraries but essential for urban academic libraries. The article is a practitioner-facing argumentative review rather than an empirical study, but it makes a focused GBLS contribution by connecting video game collections and programs to the specific service conditions of urban campuses: commuter students, diverse users, digital access gaps, limited campus attachment, and the need to make the library visible as an intellectual commons.

The article begins by challenging stereotypes about gamers. Drawing on Entertainment Software Association and Pew data, Boyle notes that the average player age is around 35, that the gender distribution of players is much less male-dominated than common assumptions suggest, and that many U.S. households include regular game players. This demographic framing matters because Boyle uses it to argue that games are already culturally relevant to a large portion of the student population. Urban academic libraries, in this view, should not treat games as a fringe recreational interest detached from their users' lives.

Boyle grounds the argument in ACRL's standards for academic libraries, especially the ideas that libraries are intellectual commons where users interact with ideas across physical and virtual environments, and that libraries support discovery of information in all formats. Video games are therefore framed as both community media and information objects. They can support recreation, collaboration, research, information seeking, and contact with library staff and services.

The first major claim is that video games act as community builders. Boyle synthesizes prior work by Nicholson, Brown and Kasper, Adams, and Cross, Mould, and Smith to argue that game collections, game labs, game clubs, and game events draw patrons into shared library space and can strengthen social connections. This is especially important for urban academic libraries because commuter students may have fewer informal reasons to linger on campus or identify the library as a gathering place. Group play gives students a specific, low-stakes reason to enter the library, meet others, collaborate, and interact with library staff.

The second claim is that video games can increase awareness and use of library sources and services. Boyle argues that game events can work as gateways to other library activity when librarians display related materials, connect games to research topics, advertise gaming-related reference services, or use game conversations to introduce broader information literacy. Examples include linking a game such as *Skyrim* to resources on feudalism, Nordic history, or Norse mythology; offering strategy guides or gaming research sources; and using game programs as occasions to discuss searching, source use, or library services.

The article also treats video games as information sources in their own right. Boyle notes that games are increasingly used as primary sources in game studies and multidisciplinary research, and that games can prompt players to seek books, historical information, strategy materials, forums, mods, and other supplemental resources. This supports the GBLS claim that games in libraries are not only entertainment items. They can connect to academic inquiry, curricular support, media literacy, and cross-format discovery.

For the larger GBLS project, Boyle is useful because it adds the urban academic-library layer to the literature. The article asks why an academic library serving commuter students and diverse urban users might need video games, not only why libraries in general might experiment with games. The answer is service-oriented: games can make the library a more inviting campus space, reduce distance between students and staff, support collaboration, provide equitable access to game media and hardware, and introduce users to services they might otherwise use only when required by a course.

The limits are clear. Boyle does not present new data, evaluate a local program, describe a collection budget or policy, report usage statistics, or test outcomes. The article relies on existing literature and broad demographic arguments. It should therefore be cited as a conceptual and advocacy source rather than as evidence of measured impact. Its strongest role in the review is to clarify why urban academic libraries may have distinctive reasons to collect and program with video games.

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Collections, Circulation, and Access`, immediately after the Buchanan and Vanden Elzen (2012) paragraph. Proposed text: "Boyle (2018) sharpens the access argument for urban academic libraries by treating video games as essential collection materials rather than optional recreation. Because commuter students and digitally divided users may have fewer reasons or resources to linger on campus, video game collections, labs, clubs, and events can provide equitable access to game media, support research and information-seeking across formats, and draw students into contact with library spaces, staff, sources, and services."

Suggested review placement: Add to `# Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service` > `## Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Monnier and Winters (2022) paragraph. Proposed text: "Boyle (2018) adds an urban academic-library version of the belonging claim: video games can give commuter students a low-stakes reason to enter and remain in the library, meet peers with shared interests, collaborate through group play, and interact with staff outside formal research-help encounters. The article is advocacy rather than assessment, but it names why urban campus libraries may need game collections and events as community infrastructure, not only curriculum support."

# **Sureephong, P., Chernbumroong, S., Niemsup, S., Homla, P., Intawong, K., & Puritat, K. (2024). Exploring the impact of the gamified metaverse on knowledge acquisition and library anxiety in academic libraries. *Information Technology and Libraries, 43*(1). https://doi.org/10.5860/ital.v43i1.16651**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `ENY4RRIW`; Better BibTeX citation key `sureephongExploringImpactGamified2024`; attachment key `UDSR6P99`.

Sureephong, Chernbumroong, Niemsup, Homla, Intawong, and Puritat examine whether a gamified metaverse platform can promote academic library services, increase first-year students' knowledge of library services, and reduce library anxiety. The article was published in *Information Technology and Libraries* with an editorial note dated October 21, 2024, stating that works cited in one endnote were fake and that the remaining citations had been verified. That note should temper how the literature review is used, but the study's reported design, intervention, and findings remain directly relevant to GBLS because they compare a metaverse-based library program with a traditional library program.

The project took place at Chiang Mai University and centered on an academic library in the Faculty of Humanities. The authors developed a digital twin of the library using photogrammetry, Meshroom, and the Unity game engine, then delivered it through Meta Quest 2 virtual reality devices. The virtual library represented the physical space and included interaction with books, computers, online resources, waypoints, virtual desks, and a user interface for navigation and search. Gamification elements included quests, challenges, points, levels, badges, and a leaderboard.

The metaverse intervention included four gamified services: Library Orientation, Research Support, Book Services, and Technology Services. In Library Orientation, students explored the virtual library, followed waypoints, and read signs or information. In Research Support and Technology Services, students interacted with virtual librarians and completed questions or tasks. In Book Services, students navigated shelves and used the Dewey Decimal Classification system to find books. The comparison group completed the same basic content in the physical library through a traditional tour, checkpoints, librarian explanations, questions, and assigned tasks.

Methodologically, the study used an explanatory sequential mixed-methods design with a randomized controlled trial. Eighty first-year students who had never used the university library were randomly assigned to a traditional library program group or a metaverse-based library program group, with 40 participants in each group. Both groups completed pre- and post-tests measuring knowledge acquisition and library anxiety. The knowledge questionnaire contained 30 questions adapted from an earlier gamified mobile orientation study. The anxiety measure was adapted from Kannegiser's modified version of Bostick's Library Anxiety Scale, covering barriers with library staff, affective barriers, comfort with the library, and knowledge of the library. The metaverse group also completed open-ended questions about the platform.

The knowledge findings are clear but modest. Both groups began with similar pre-test scores and improved substantially after the intervention. The traditional group moved from a mean of 2.37 to 17.50, and the metaverse group moved from 2.45 to 18.10. However, the difference in pre-post gains between the two groups was not statistically significant, and the effect size was small. In GBLS terms, the gamified metaverse worked as an orientation and library-services learning environment, but it did not outperform a traditional librarian-led program on knowledge acquisition.

The library anxiety findings are more cautionary. The traditional program produced significant improvements on four of five anxiety questions: comfort asking librarians for help, seeing libraries as welcoming places, feeling less overwhelmed, and knowing how to use services and resources. The metaverse program produced significant improvements only for feeling less overwhelmed and knowing how to use services and resources. It did not significantly improve comfort asking librarians for help, sense that libraries are welcoming, or perceptions that librarians are available for research and technology support. Qualitative comments help explain why: some students found the virtual librarian robotic, unapproachable, unable to communicate adequately, and lacking the personal touch and empathy of a real librarian.

Students nevertheless responded positively to gamification. Open-ended responses described the platform as interesting, engaging, fun, and useful for learning about library resources. Students appreciated quests, points, levels, leaderboards, competition, and the novelty of exploring the virtual library. Some also reported that the virtual environment helped them understand how the library worked without feeling lost or overwhelmed. At the same time, participants raised concerns about expensive VR equipment, accessibility, nausea, dizziness, and loss of balance.

For the GBLS project, this article is valuable because it separates several outcomes that are often collapsed in discussion of immersive library technology. A metaverse platform can be engaging, motivating, and effective for factual knowledge acquisition without being better than traditional instruction. It can reduce some spatial or procedural anxiety without reducing anxiety tied to human help-seeking and staff relationships. It also shows that game elements may improve perceived engagement even when the larger technology introduces cost, expertise, accessibility, and physical-comfort barriers. The source therefore supports a cautious service-design principle: match immersive and gamified forms to the parts of library learning they are good at, and preserve human facilitation where trust, empathy, and help-seeking matter.

The limitations are significant. The study is local to one university context, uses short-term post-tests rather than retention or later behavior, and does not fully explore prior VR experience, which could affect both engagement and side effects. The metaverse used NPC-style virtual librarians rather than human avatars or live chat, so its weak performance on librarian interaction should not be generalized to all social VR designs. Still, the article is a useful empirical companion to other GBLS sources on mobile orientation, AR tours, VR programming, and assessment beyond enjoyment.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Sureephong et al. (2024) extend immersive orientation evidence into a gamified metaverse at Chiang Mai University, comparing 40 first-year students in a Unity/Meta Quest 2 virtual-library program with 40 students in a traditional librarian-led program. Both groups completed the same orientation, research-support, book-service, and technology-service tasks and both improved substantially on library knowledge, but the metaverse group did not significantly outperform the traditional group, making the study a useful reminder that immersive novelty should be assessed against ordinary instruction.

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, after the Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Sureephong et al. (2024) also clarify what a library metaverse can and cannot do as a service model. Their digital twin of an academic library used photogrammetry, Unity, Meta Quest 2 headsets, waypoints, NPC-style virtual librarians, quests, points, levels, badges, and leaderboards; students found the platform engaging and motivating, but the authors caution that development expertise, headset cost, accessibility, nausea, dizziness, and lack of real librarian interaction make it better suited as a supplementary tool for specific audiences than as a replacement for in-person service.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Evaluate Outcomes Beyond Attendance`, after the Intawong et al. (2023) paragraph.

Suggested text: Sureephong et al. (2024) further demonstrate why GBLS assessment should separate knowledge, motivation, spatial comfort, help-seeking, and human connection. Their gamified metaverse produced knowledge gains comparable to traditional orientation and positive perceptions of quests, points, levels, and leaderboards, but it reduced fewer dimensions of library anxiety than the traditional program because an NPC librarian could not provide the personal touch, empathy, or responsive help associated with real staff.

# **Vanden Elzen, A. M., & Roush, J. (2013). Brawling in the library: Gaming programs for impactful outreach and instruction at an academic library. *Library Trends, 61*(4), 802-813.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `DEMPZ8YZ`; Better BibTeX citation key `elzenBrawlingLibraryGaming2013`; attachment key `YW7Y6A6P`.

Vanden Elzen and Roush describe how Lawrence University's Seeley G. Mudd Library used game programs to support outreach, instruction, and campus community. The article is framed around a practical academic-library problem: libraries need to teach students about resources, but they also need to reach students who might not independently seek out the library or ask librarians for help. Games are presented as a way to connect the library to a familiar part of undergraduate culture, bring students into the building for social reasons, and make library staff, collections, and spaces more visible.

The article reviews several academic-library uses of games, including orientation games, information-literacy games, alternate reality games, open gaming nights, LAN parties, console collections, gaming stations, and gaming events in learning commons. Its main contribution, however, is the Lawrence University case. Mudd Library had long used board games and puzzles, but beginning in 2009 it expanded into video game tournaments, open gaming, welcome-week programs, and collaborations with student gamers. Because Lawrence is a residential liberal arts campus, the library understood gaming programs as a way to strengthen the campus's sense of community while increasing library awareness.

The authors emphasize partnerships as a core planning principle. Library staff collaborated with student organizations, student workers, and knowledgeable players because game expertise matters. Serious participants care about rules, tournament structure, unlocked characters, equipment, timing, and game-specific norms. At Lawrence, a student worker helped staff avoid outdated choices such as *Rock Band* or *Guitar Hero* and identify *Super Smash Bros. Brawl* as a better tournament game for the local campus culture. Later tournaments were planned with the Lawrence University Gaming Club, and a Magic: The Gathering event was co-planned with the campus Magic Club using club-owned cards in a special-collections room whose atmosphere enhanced the event.

The planning advice is concrete. Libraries should consult the academic calendar so gaming does not collide with midterms or finals unless intentionally framed as a low-volume study break. They need adaptable spaces with enough room, light, power, and tolerance for noise, even if that means creating temporary setups with carts, folding tables, movable chairs, power strips, and borrowed televisions. They should think carefully about whether to buy equipment, borrow from other libraries, rely on partners, or use staff and student systems. The authors also warn that tournament play can exclude students who are less skilled or have less time, so open gaming, board games, retro systems, puzzles, and casual alternatives can make events more welcoming.

The article also treats marketing as part of service design. The authors found that library Facebook followers were mostly older than the traditional undergraduate audience, so social media alone was insufficient. Student workers helped staff learn that the university calendar was printed and posted in residence halls, making it an unexpectedly effective channel. Paper flyers also mattered, especially outside the library. The marketing lesson for GBLS is practical: game programs often aim to reach students who are not already library insiders, so promotion has to move through the channels those students actually notice.

The impact evidence is anecdotal and survey-based, but it is still useful. After the first tournament in 2009, 50 percent of respondents reported meeting new people with similar interests. In a 2012 survey of gaming-event participants and Gaming Club email-list members, the most frequently selected response was that gaming events had added to respondents' sense of belonging in the Lawrence University community, with a 76.9 percent selection rate; well over half said library gaming events were moderately, very, or extremely important to their overall Lawrence experience. The events also helped catalyze the Lawrence University Gaming Club, whose growth eventually included dedicated student housing for gaming.

For GBLS, this source is valuable because it connects academic-library game programming to outreach, belonging, collections, marketing, and partnerships rather than treating tournaments as isolated entertainment. Gaming events brought students with shared interests together, made the library more visible, helped students become familiar with staff and spaces, and created opportunities to display books on games, education, programming, new media, creative writing, and the art of video games. The library also developed a gaming LibGuide and added related books to the collection, showing how programs can feed collection development and advisory.

The limitations are straightforward. The article is a practitioner report, not a rigorous assessment study. Survey methods, response counts, and sampling are not fully reported, and the findings are local to a small residential campus with an active gaming culture. Still, the source offers a rich operational account of academic-library gaming programs, especially tournaments and open gaming, and it supplies language for the GBLS review's themes of community, outreach, facilitation, partnership, and sustainable program planning.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Tournaments and Esports`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Vanden Elzen and Roush (2013) provide a practical academic-library tournament model from Lawrence University's Seeley G. Mudd Library, where *Super Smash Bros. Brawl* tournaments, open gaming, retro systems, board games, and Magic: The Gathering events were planned with student workers and gaming clubs. Their advice foregrounds local game knowledge, standard rulesets, unlocked content, equipment, noise, timing around the academic calendar, casual alternatives for noncompetitive players, and marketing through the channels students actually use.

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Boyle (2018) paragraph.

Suggested text: Vanden Elzen and Roush (2013) also make the belonging claim concrete for a residential academic campus. In Mudd Library surveys, 50% of first-tournament respondents reported meeting new people with similar interests, and 76.9% of later respondents selected that gaming events added to their sense of belonging in the Lawrence University community; the events also helped catalyze a campus Gaming Club and made the library more visible as a welcoming second place.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Build Partnerships and Community Pipelines`, after the Monnier and Winters (2022) paragraph.

Suggested text: Vanden Elzen and Roush (2013) show that GBLS partnerships can begin with student expertise. Student workers and gaming clubs helped choose locally popular games, run tournaments, supply rules knowledge and equipment, organize Magic: The Gathering events, and turn library programs into a broader campus gaming pipeline, while the library offered visibility, space, supervision, and connection to collections.

# **Bishoff, C., Farrell, S. L., & Neeser, A. E. (2015). Outreach, collaboration, collegiality: Evolving approaches to library video game services. *Journal of Library Innovation, 6*(1), 92-109.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `4L7M87P4`; Better BibTeX citation key `bishoffOutreachCollaborationCollegiality2015`; attachment key `YJZTM36U`.

Bishoff, Farrell, and Neeser describe how the University of Minnesota - Twin Cities Libraries moved from a familiar academic-library video game service model toward a more relational, campus-specific strategy. Their starting point was the same set of services that appear repeatedly in the early academic gaming literature: a video game collection, undergraduate gaming events, and possible instruction modules. The authors were interested in games because video games were already being used, studied, and developed across many departments, including computer science, design, journalism, engineering, medicine, humanities, and the arts. Yet because the university had no single game studies department or natural selector home, a collection immediately raised questions about acquisition, preservation, replacement, cataloging, and long-term stewardship.

The Libraries chose a low-cost pilot instead of a collection. For less than $600, they bought a Nintendo Wii, extra controllers, and twenty multiplayer party games and hosted two finals-week game nights. The goals were modest: provide a study break, test whether students were receptive to gaming in the library, and gather feedback for future programming. The first event, held in a media viewing room, drew only three players, one of whom was a security guard who did not know the room existed. The second event moved to a computer lab adjacent to a busy study area, added snacks, Wii games, and retro computer games, and drew 33 students, but only 15 came to play. The authors emphasize that these results were disappointing not because no one on campus played games, but because the event form did not fit how students were using the library during finals week.

The failed pilots pushed the Libraries to assess demand rather than assume it. A first-year orientation survey produced 579 responses and found that only 54 percent of respondents said they would attend a video gaming event at the Libraries during finals week, and 56 percent said they would check out video games from the Libraries. Those numbers represented a small majority, not the strong evidence the team had expected. Informal event feedback also suggested that students wanted more than familiar Wii titles: some wanted access to newer systems such as the Wii U, Xbox One, PlayStation 4, or Oculus Rift, while others wanted LAN parties or graphically intensive PC gaming. The authors interpret this as a lesson about audience and environment. A large, urban, commuter campus is not the same service environment as a residential campus, a small college, or a public library youth program, even if all of those settings contain game players.

The article's central contribution is the pivot from stand-alone events and collections to partnerships, networking, game creation, education, and community outreach. The Libraries recognized that student groups were already doing game work on campus. Glitch hosted weekly gaming lounges and supported student developers; the Society for Physics Students borrowed the Libraries' Oculus Rift for an event that quickly became a collaborative learning environment; and the Video Game Development Club had built its own reference collection of game development, programming, and technology books. Rather than duplicating those activities, librarians began using their liaison relationships, spaces, technologies, collections, and convening power to support them.

Several later initiatives illustrate the new approach. The Libraries used an Xbox Kinect and Skanect software in a city-wide art festival to create 3D scans for 156 participants, showing how gaming hardware could be repurposed for creative public engagement. They partnered with Glitch on Co-Op Talk, a monthly discussion group in which a participant proposed a topic, Glitch identified a game, and a librarian selected related readings. They also collaborated on a campus event about gaming, gender, and identity that attracted more than 100 people. In another initiative, the Libraries hosted a panel of researchers using video games in different fields, drawing more than 40 attendees and prompting follow-up conversations among faculty, students, and researchers.

For the larger GBLS project, Bishoff et al. are especially useful because they resist a simple success-story template. The article shows that games are not automatically good outreach merely because students play games, and that a poorly matched program can underperform even when it is inexpensive, well promoted, and held in a busy library. It also shows that failure can be diagnostically productive when staff treat it as evidence about local needs. The UMN case supports a GBLS design principle: begin with the actual service ecology, not with a generic program type. On some campuses, a collection or game night may be the right starting point; on this campus, partnerships with existing student groups, researchers, makerspaces, and community organizations produced more meaningful service opportunities.

The article also broadens what counts as library video game service. Bishoff et al. are not arguing against collections or recreational events in general. They are arguing that academic libraries should develop services that can evolve with students' and researchers' work. In their conclusion, game services are most effective when they support networking, game creation, education, and community outreach. That makes the source a strong bridge between collection literature, outreach literature, and research-support literature. It should be cited when the review discusses matching game form to service goal, building partnerships, and evaluating outcomes beyond attendance.

The limitations are straightforward. This is a single institutional case, and the evidence includes event attendance, a brief informal survey, observations, and narrative accounts of subsequent collaborations rather than a controlled assessment. Still, its value lies precisely in the candid account of underperformance and redesign. GBLS needs such cases because they reveal the planning assumptions, environmental constraints, and partnership work that are often hidden behind successful program descriptions.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Start with Purpose, Audience, and Local Need`, after the Khademi Zare et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Bishoff, Farrell, and Neeser (2015) turn local fit into a cautionary academic-library case. At the University of Minnesota - Twin Cities, two well-promoted Wii-based finals-week game events drew little sustained participation, and a first-year survey showed only modest interest in gaming events or video game checkout; the authors therefore warn against inferring demand from general gamer demographics or from programs that worked at smaller, residential, or public-library sites.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Build Partnerships and Community Pipelines`, after the Monnier and Winters (2022) paragraph.

Suggested text: Bishoff et al. (2015) also show how partnership can become the service model rather than a supplement to a collection or event. After the weak pilot events, the University of Minnesota Libraries worked with student gaming groups, the Society for Physics Students, local community partners, Co-Op Talk, game-development students, and interdisciplinary researchers, using gaming technologies and librarian facilitation to support networking, game creation, education, and community outreach.

# **Keating, S. A. (2016). Organizing videogame metadata in CollectiveAccess. In *iConference 2016 Proceedings*. iSchools. https://doi.org/10.9776/16479**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `PFP4LUWL`; Better BibTeX citation key `keatingOrganizingVideogameMetadata2016`; attachment key `PPI57EKM`.

Keating examines whether CollectiveAccess, an open-source collection management system used by museums and cultural heritage organizations, can represent the complex metadata needs of video game collections. The paper is a short iConference poster, but it is directly relevant to the GBLS review because it turns the GAMER Group's Video Game Metadata Schema (VGMS) from a conceptual schema into an implementation problem. The core question is practical: can off-the-shelf digital collection software support specialized digital cultural heritage collections with complex attributes and relationships, or do game collections require more customized infrastructure?

The case study begins from the appeal of CollectiveAccess. CA was designed for special collections, has been praised by cultural heritage professionals, supports granular metadata, and lets administrators create and modify metadata schemas. Those features made it a plausible candidate for representing VGMS, which was created to describe video games and interactive media more fully than ordinary bibliographic systems. Keating evaluates CA through two sets of concerns: whether it can manage the special collection schema and relationships required by VGMS, and whether its design is usable enough for administrators and end users.

The implementation produced mixed results. CA made some metadata-management work easy. Changing element codes or labels was trivial, and the system propagated those changes throughout the site in an intuitive and visually understandable way. This matters for GBLS because game collections need flexible description: platforms, genres, versions, visual styles, relationships, hardware requirements, and distribution models often change or require local adaptation. A system that allows elements to be reordered, added, removed, and presented clearly is valuable for small institutions that cannot build custom software from scratch.

The major problem was relationship modeling. VGMS depends on relationships among games and related entities: series, franchises, universes, versions, ports, remakes, sequels, inspirations, downloadable content, and other connections that do not fit neatly into one hierarchy. Keating found that CollectiveAccess allowed some creation and contextualization of relationships, but top-level relationships were established by the system from the outset and could not be modified without code-level changes. Relationships were organized hierarchically, while the VGMS needed non-hierarchical and relationship-to-relationship linking. In Keating's conclusion, the inability to connect relationships to one another ultimately made CA insufficient for the GAMER Group's needs.

Keating also evaluates CA through modularity and reactivity. Drawing on design criteria from Norman and Bates, the paper argues that complex systems need modular interfaces and responsive user interaction. CA offered high-level modular features, but the VGMS implementation needed lower-level relationship functionality that was not exposed through the same easy interface. In the Pawtucket front end, search behavior was also frustrating because the system obscured how it performed searches; even known-item searches could be difficult. For end users, this weak reactivity would undermine discovery even if the metadata schema were theoretically rich.

For the larger GBLS project, Keating's contribution is infrastructural. Game-based library services depend not only on programs, collections, and events, but also on whether games and related cultural objects can be described, related, searched, and retrieved. A game collection that cannot distinguish versions, platforms, related works, relationship types, or complex series/franchise structures will be less useful for players, researchers, preservationists, educators, and creators. Keating therefore complements Lee, Clarke, Sacchi, and Jett's relationship metadata work and the later Keating et al. visual-style study: the former shows what relationships game metadata needs, the latter shows what descriptive facets users may require, and this poster shows how a real open-source platform strains when asked to implement those requirements.

The source also offers a useful caution for libraries considering open-source collection tools. Open source and schema flexibility are not enough. A platform must expose the right levels of control, model non-hierarchical relationships, make relationship creation understandable to administrators, and provide reactive search interfaces for end users. Otherwise, a system can appear customizable while still failing at the exact structures that make game metadata distinctive.

The limitations are significant. This is a poster paper, not a full implementation study or user evaluation. It does not report a large dataset, live public catalog, usability testing with patrons, or long-term maintenance outcomes. Still, it belongs in the review because it captures an early implementation test of video game metadata infrastructure and names a concrete barrier that matters for GBLS: game discovery is limited by the data models and interfaces available to local institutions.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the Lee, Clarke, Sacchi, and Jett (2014) paragraph.

Suggested text: Keating (2016) turns the GAMER Group's relationship metadata into an implementation test by attempting to represent VGMS in CollectiveAccess.
