The case shows that open-source collection systems may support flexible element labels and granular schemas while still failing at game-specific discovery when relationships are fixed, hierarchical, difficult to modify without code, or not linkable to other relationships; for GBLS, that makes platform data models and search reactivity part of collection access, not merely back-end technical detail.

# **Chen, C.-M., & Yang, Y.-C. (2024). A game-based augmented reality navigation system to support makerspace user education in a university library. *The Electronic Library, 42*(1), 78-101. https://doi.org/10.1108/EL-05-2023-0107**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `HRJCAEDX`; Better BibTeX citation key `chenGamebasedAugmentedReality2024`; attachment key `E3HU594P`.

Note on processing: the local PDF attachment extracted primarily as glyph-encoded text in this environment, so this summary relies on the article's available abstract and metadata records, supplemented only by reliably readable fragments from the attachment. The source should be revisited if clean full text becomes available.

Chen and Yang develop a game-based augmented reality navigation system (GARNS) for makerspace user education in a university library. The article begins from a service problem that is increasingly relevant to GBLS: library makerspaces can support creativity, interdisciplinary learning, practical skills, entrepreneurship, and hands-on collaboration, but their varied equipment and workflows can make user education labor-intensive for librarians. If every learner needs a full staff-guided tour or one-on-one explanation, makerspace services can become difficult to scale under ordinary staffing constraints.

The proposed GARNS combines augmented reality tools with game-based learning, the Octalysis gamification framework, and scaffolding theory. Its goal is to help learners navigate makerspace equipment, understand tasks, and build procedural knowledge through an immersive, autonomous learning environment. The study's framing is important for GBLS because the game layer is not simply decorative. It is meant to structure attention, sequence tasks, motivate exploration, and provide guidance inside a physical library makerspace where users need to find, evaluate, choose, and complete educational tasks.

The study used a true experimental research design with 24 grade 11 students from a high school in Keelung City, Taiwan. Ten students were randomly assigned to the GARNS experimental group, seven to a control group using a web navigation system, and seven to a control group using a narrative guided tour with a librarian. The comparison is useful because it sets the AR/game system against both a lower-tech self-guided tool and a familiar human-guided tour model. The article reports learning effectiveness and learning motivation outcomes, including value and expectation dimensions of motivation.

The reported findings are cautiously positive. Learners achieved significant learning effectiveness with all three approaches: GARNS, web navigation, and librarian-guided tour. There were no significant differences in learning effectiveness or motivation between GARNS and the librarian-guided tour, nor between the web navigation system and the librarian-guided tour. However, GARNS performed significantly better than the web navigation system on learning effectiveness and on value and expectation dimensions of learning motivation. The practical implication is not that AR should replace librarians, but that a carefully designed AR/game system may provide a scalable autonomous option that performs comparably to a guided tour and better than a flat web guide for some outcomes.

For the GBLS project, Chen and Yang extend the review's AR and orientation literature into makerspace user education. Their work differs from general library orientation because the learning object is not only where services are located but how to understand equipment, tasks, and procedural possibilities in a creative production environment. This makes the source relevant to library makerspaces, research/creative production, and emerging technology service models. It also supports the principle that game form should match service goal: AR navigation is most persuasive when users need situated, in-place guidance tied to physical objects and spaces.

The source also adds a staffing and sustainability argument. If makerspace services depend heavily on repeated tours or individualized explanations, staff workload can limit access. A GARNS-style system may help standardize introductory instruction, reduce repetitive explanation, and give users a more flexible way to revisit guidance. At the same time, the study's small sample and the lack of clean full-text extraction here require caution. The article should be used as promising evidence for AR-supported makerspace education, not as definitive proof that AR/game navigation is broadly superior to librarian instruction.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > VR, AR, and Immersive Technology`, after the Sureephong et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Chen and Yang (2024) extend AR service models from orientation to makerspace user education through a game-based augmented reality navigation system built on Octalysis gamification and scaffolding theory. In a small experiment with 24 grade 11 students, GARNS produced significant learning effectiveness, performed comparably to a librarian-guided tour, and outperformed a web navigation system on learning effectiveness and value/expectation dimensions of motivation, suggesting that AR is most useful when guidance is tied to physical equipment, spaces, and procedural tasks.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Match Game Form to Service Goal`, after the Jedlicka and Eaton (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Chen and Yang (2024) add a makerspace version of the form-fit lesson: AR navigation is well matched to user education when learners need situated prompts, task sequencing, and object-specific guidance in a physical production space. The system is less a replacement for librarians than a scalable way to standardize introductory instruction and reduce repetitive staff burden where equipment complexity would otherwise limit access.

# **Varsa, J. (2022). Oconee County Library puts on inaugural FanFest. *Georgia Library Quarterly, 59*(3), 1-2.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `JCT7DKA7`; Better BibTeX citation key `varsaOconeeCountyLibrary2022`; attachment key `MP63LALM`.

Varsa provides a short practitioner report on the first FanFest hosted by Oconee County Library, a branch of the Athens Regional Library System in Georgia. The event took place on April 2, 2022, at the Oconee County Civic Center in Watkinsville and was supported by the Oconee County Library Friends, staff, and volunteers. It was admission-free, lasted a full day, drew more than 350 participants, and was framed as a community celebration in the tradition of comic cons.

The article defines FanFest as an all-ages festival that brings together live music, guest authors and artists, a cosplay contest, gaming, vendors, food, and other activities. Its distinguishing feature is localism. Rather than building a program only around nationally recognizable franchises or commercial fandom culture, the library used the event to showcase local authors, musicians, gaming organizations, vendors, food trucks, student volunteers, and community partners. Varsa notes that many participating organizations were already library-system partners and represented the diversity of northeast Georgia.

The service model is spatially and organizationally broader than a single in-library program. Oconee County Library used a partner venue, the Oconee County Civic Center, from 9:30 a.m. to 5:30 p.m. The main hall held vendors who could display books and products while talking with attendees. The auditorium hosted three music acts, while the lawn supported food trucks and sword demonstrations from the Athens School of Arms. The program also included workshops, panel discussions, book signings, live music, and other activities.

Gaming appears as one part of a broader fandom and creative ecosystem. The University of Georgia Student Board Game Club ran a gaming table, while local high school Beta Club volunteers greeted attendees and distributed event schedules. The report does not describe the specific board games, facilitation methods, learning outcomes, or attendance at the gaming table, so it should not be treated as evidence for a game program's impact. Still, the presence of a university board game club is important for GBLS because it shows how libraries can use existing local gaming expertise inside a larger festival format rather than staffing every activity internally.

FanFest also functioned as a platform for local creative production. Featured guests included graphic novelists, musicians, filmmakers, a local actress leading acting workshops, comic creators, face painting and henna artists, and a podcast wrap party for Classic City Vibes, an Athens Regional Library System podcast about local musicians, visual artists, literary artists, and other creatives. A donated merchandise door prize was tied to electronic newsletter sign-ups, connecting event excitement back to ongoing library communication.

For the larger GBLS project, Varsa's value is as a compact example of game-adjacent public-library festival design. It complements Virello's larger Southington Library ComiCon case by showing a smaller, local-talent version of the same model. In both cases, games are not isolated from library service; they sit beside cosplay, comics, music, crafts, authors, vendors, food, displays, contests, and community organizations. This matters because many GBLS programs will not be pure game programs. A board game table, video game tournament, cosplay contest, or playful demonstration may be one node in a wider cultural event that advances library goals around access, belonging, local creativity, partner visibility, and community gathering.

The limitations are clear. The article is only two pages, does not include formal evaluation, and gives little detail about planning, budget, staffing, accessibility, safety policies, marketing, or post-event outcomes beyond the attendance figure. Its usefulness lies in documenting a replicable event frame: an admission-free, all-ages FanFest that uses a partner venue, local creative networks, student volunteers, a university board game club, food, music, workshops, and library communication channels to make fandom a public-library commons.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Fandom Festivals and Pop-Culture Events`, immediately after the Virello (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Varsa (2022) offers a smaller local-talent version of the same fandom-festival model. Oconee County Library's free all-ages FanFest used a partner civic-center venue, Friends support, local authors, artists, musicians, vendors, food trucks, cosplay, acting workshops, a library-system podcast tie-in, high-school volunteers, and a University of Georgia Student Board Game Club gaming table to draw more than 350 participants, showing how gaming can be one activity within a broader community creativity festival.

# **Storie, D. (2010). [Review of the book *Everyone plays at the library: Creating great gaming experiences for all ages*, by S. Nicholson]. *Journal of the Canadian Health Libraries Association / Journal de l'Association des bibliothèques de la santé du Canada, 31*, 123.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `VM87PPI8`; Better BibTeX citation key `nicholsonEveryonePlaysLibrary2010`; attachment key `NBZ5EDE5`.

Metadata note: the Zotero parent item is for Scott Nicholson's 2010 book *Everyone Plays at the Library*, but the attached PDF is Dale Storie's one-page book review. This entry summarizes the attached review rather than the full book.

Storie reviews Scott Nicholson's *Everyone Plays at the Library: Creating Great Gaming Experiences for All Ages*, positioning Nicholson as a leading figure in games and libraries and noting his roles at Syracuse University's School of Information Studies, the Library Game Lab of Syracuse, and ALA's Games and Gaming Member Initiatives Group. The review's central observation is that the book distinguishes itself by treating games as social experiences. Storie quotes Nicholson's emphasis that the important question is not the game itself but how games can be integrated into library programming and how different gaming experiences can meet different library goals.

The review describes the book as broadly relevant to many library types, with school and public libraries likely to benefit most. Public libraries are singled out because gaming programs can reach underserved patrons, provide recreational programs, and help the library function as a community hub. Storie also notes that creative librarians in other settings may find the book useful when designing classes or programs that use games. For GBLS, this matters because it frames game-based service around library goals and user experience rather than around a narrow format or technology.

Storie outlines the book's three-part structure. The first part introduces gaming and libraries and connects gaming programs to common library mission-statement goals. The second part functions like a readers' advisory tool for librarians looking to incorporate games into programs and services. It organizes game recommendations around five "gaming experience archetypes": knowledge, strategy, action, narrative, and social gaming. The third part covers logistical issues such as facilitation, marketing, and evaluation. Even though the review is brief, this structure is directly relevant to the GBLS review because it links selection, program design, service goals, facilitation, and assessment.

The review emphasizes inclusivity across game formats. Nicholson covers word games, board games, card games, live-action role-playing games, and "big games" such as murder mystery party games, rather than equating gaming with videogames. Storie praises Nicholson for pushing back against videogame marketing hype and for arguing that libraries do not need the newest or most heavily advertised games to run successful programs. Quality of play and enthusiastic staff are presented as more important than expensive technology. This point supports the GBLS project's recurring distinction between game form and service goal.

Storie also highlights the practical value of Nicholson's game reviews. Each review is categorized by gaming experience archetype and tied to common library goals. The reviews also include cost, number of players, complexity, recommended age group, time required, and skills used, such as strategic planning or teamwork. This is especially useful for GBLS because it describes the kind of advisory and program-planning metadata libraries need in order to select games responsibly for different audiences and settings.

For the larger GBLS project, this source should be used cautiously because it is a review of a book rather than the book itself. It does not provide independent empirical evidence, and it should not substitute for reading Nicholson's full framework if the book is later available. Still, it confirms that *Everyone Plays at the Library* was understood by contemporary reviewers as a practical, inclusive, mission-oriented guide to library gaming programs across formats and ages. It also reinforces several themes already present in the review: games as social experiences, games matched to library goals, broad analog/digital inclusion, the importance of facilitation, and the need for evaluation.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Game Modalities in Library Contexts`, after the Hill (2016) paragraph.

Suggested text: Storie's (2010) review of Nicholson's *Everyone Plays at the Library* characterizes Nicholson's practitioner framework as one organized around gaming experiences rather than formats alone: knowledge, strategy, action, narrative, and social gaming. The review is secondary rather than empirical, but it helps show why GBLS modality decisions should consider library goals, audience age, cost, player count, complexity, play time, facilitation, marketing, evaluation, and the skills a game invites.

# **Adams, M. J., Finch, M., & Gamble, B. (2023). More than a game: Scenario planning, imagination, and the public library's future. *Public Library Quarterly, 42*(6), 665-687. https://doi.org/10.1080/01616846.2022.2154068**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `EYQUEMRN`; Better BibTeX citation key `adamsMoreGameScenario2023`; attachment key `8NP9N39W`.

Adams, Finch, and Gamble examine scenario planning as a strategic-planning method for public libraries facing turbulence, uncertainty, novelty, and ambiguity. The article is centered on Reading Public Library in Pennsylvania and the process it used to plan amid COVID-19, U.S. political polarization, local economic and demographic change, and leadership transition. The authors argue that strategic planning should not focus only on present organizational goals; it should also imagine the contextual future world in which those goals might have to operate.

The paper defines scenarios as storytelling tools derived from current-day realities that present plausible glimpses into future contexts. In the Oxford Scenario Planning Approach used at Reading Public Library, scenarios are not predictions or preferred futures. They are imagined future contexts designed to contrast with current assumptions, stretch perception, expose blind spots, and help decision-makers ask what is happening, what it means, and what they can do. The article therefore treats scenario planning as a form of strategic reframing.

Reading Public Library's context matters to the case. The library serves Reading, Pennsylvania, a post-industrial city with a long library history, financial challenges, poverty, local political complexity, and pandemic disruptions. During the pandemic, RPL expanded digital services, streaming media, online programming, pickup services, and Go Packs distributed through school and recreation partnerships. The article positions these pressures as the setting for a planning process that could not simply extrapolate from stable past conditions.

The scenario process began with virtual workshops and a mapping exercise in which staff and trustees explored the library's business environment, flows of value, partner relationships, and uncertainties affecting the future. The team generated scenarios for 2040, eventually working with three future contexts: Life in the Clouds, The Wild Frontier, and Paris on the Schuylkill. These futures varied in their assumptions about digital life, cooperation, fragmentation, civic flourishing, arts, education, and community relationships. Staff and trustees then used the scenarios to look back at present choices, identifying signs that each future might already be emerging and considering what strategic actions would remain robust across possible futures.

The process produced four strategic tracks: Library Staff Reflecting the Community, Co-designed Programs, Digital Opportunities, and Collection Management. The language of tracks intentionally evoked Reading's railroad history. The article's first-person reflections emphasize that scenario planning helped staff look outward, talk about partners and community forces, imagine RPL serving residents in new ways, and avoid a conventional SWOT process that might have felt too narrow in a turbulent moment. The scenarios also shaped later decisions, including workspace planning, art display policy, digital access efforts, local history digitization, staffing, community art partnerships, and teen internship programs.

The article's title and discussion explicitly connect scenario planning to play. The authors draw on Leorke and Wyatt's idea of the library as playground, then argue that the play of mind in scenario work can extend beyond makerspaces, video gaming events, gamification, and live-action role-play. The scenario workshop becomes a playful heterotopia: a space separated from everyday expectations in which participants can suspend ordinary assumptions, play with future identities, and test strategic actions without real-world consequences. Strategy is described as a form of serious play and conceptual simulation.

For the GBLS project, this source is adjacent but not a direct literature-review addition. It is about using playful scenario methods for internal library strategy, not about delivering game-based library services to patrons, building game collections, designing game programs, teaching through games, or evaluating patron-facing play. Its most relevant insight is conceptual: games and play can shape library organizations not only through public programs but also through planning, imagination, and institutional identity work. However, including it in the main GBLS review would broaden the scope toward general strategic foresight and organizational play rather than the project's focus on game-based library services.

The source is therefore best kept as explicitly excluded literature with a note. It may become useful if the project later adds a section on staff-facing serious play, scenario planning, or gameful strategic design, but it should not currently be used as evidence for GBLS programming or services. Its limitations for the current review are not flaws in the article itself; they are scope limitations. The case is rich, reflective, and library-specific, but its audience and intervention are internal planning stakeholders rather than library patrons or learners.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. Keep in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` because the article addresses scenario planning and serious play as internal public-library strategy work rather than patron-facing game-based library services.

# **Rossi, S., Lee, J. H., & Clarke, R. I. (2014). Mood metadata for video games and interactive media. In *2014 IEEE/ACM Joint Conference on Digital Libraries* (pp. 475-476). IEEE. https://doi.org/10.1109/JCDL.2014.6970232**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `3TH7L7D9`; Better BibTeX citation key `rossiMoodMetadataVideo2014`; attachment key `HCJ7QQNH`.

Rossi, Lee, and Clarke present a short but useful poster on mood metadata for video games and interactive media. The paper begins from the broader GAMER Group argument that video games are becoming important digital library collection objects and cultural artifacts, so libraries need metadata schemas that support robust search, browse, collocation, and discovery. Mood is one element in the Video Game Metadata Schema, where it is defined as the pervading atmosphere or tone of a video game that evokes or recalls an emotion or state of mind.

The article's contribution is to make that mood element operational through a controlled vocabulary. The authors note that video game mood research was still in its infancy and that most prior mood-taxonomy work came from psychology and music information retrieval. They therefore drew on Russell's circumplex model, Thayer's work on mood and arousal, Watson and Tellegen's two-factor structure of affect, and music mood research. They also harvested mood terms from AllMusic.com, the Movie Genome Project, gamerDNA.com, and Amazon.com customer reviews, removing redundant and genre-related terms through iterative review.

The resulting controlled vocabulary contains 17 mood terms with definitions, equivalent terms, and game examples: Adventurous, Aggressive, Cute, Dark, Horror, Humorous, Imaginative, Intense, Light-hearted, Mysterious, Peaceful, Quirky, Romantic, Sad, Sarcastic, Sensual, and Solitary. The list is valuable because it treats mood as more than genre or subject. A game might be adventurous because it emphasizes exploration and risk-taking, dark because it has a grim atmosphere, imaginative because it foregrounds world-building or creative problem-solving, or solitary because the player feels isolated or alone. These descriptors could help users browse for experiential qualities that platform, genre, and title metadata do not capture.

To test applicability, the authors annotated a sample collection of 617 video game titles selected for variety in genre and release year. Adventurous, aggressive, and intense were the most frequently assigned mood terms, while sarcastic, sad, and sensual were least frequent. The annotation process also suggested possible correlations between genre and mood and possible chronological shifts, such as aggressive appearing more often after 1992. The poster does not present the full visualizations or detailed statistics, but it establishes that a mood vocabulary can be applied to a large sample and can reveal patterns across game collections.

The authors are careful about mood's subjectivity. Mood may vary by player, state of mind, prior experience, and interpretation of the game's prevailing atmosphere. One player may label *Bioshock* horror because of fear responses to Rapture and its residents, while another may reserve horror for games like *Silent Hill*. Rossi et al. therefore suggest allowing multiple mood annotations per game and possibly crowdsourcing mood annotation so user input can rank or weight terms. This caveat is important for GBLS because mood metadata is useful precisely where it is also unstable: it connects to user experience, affect, and advisory language rather than only to fixed bibliographic facts.

For the larger GBLS project, the source strengthens the cataloging, metadata, and discovery thread. Library game services need more than shelves, circulation, and events; they need ways for patrons, students, teachers, researchers, and staff to find games by the qualities that matter to play and learning. Mood metadata could support readers' advisory-style game advisory, thematic displays, curricular matching, wellness-oriented recommendations, collection browsing, and preservation description. It also complements related metadata sources in the review: Lee et al. (2013) establish the broader user-centered schema, Lee, Clarke, Sacchi, and Jett (2014) address relationships, Welhouse et al. (2015) address plot/narrative, and Keating et al. (2017) address visual style.

The limitations are those of a poster paper. It offers a proposed controlled vocabulary and a preliminary annotation exercise, not a patron study of mood-based searching or a deployed library catalog interface. The authors themselves call for future user studies asking players to annotate familiar games and describe how they would use mood in game-related information seeking. Even with those limits, the article belongs in the review because it gives GBLS a concrete example of how game metadata can move from generic description toward experiential discovery.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Cataloging, Metadata, and Discovery`, immediately after the Lee, Cho, Fox, and Perti (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Rossi, Lee, and Clarke (2014) make the mood/affect element of game metadata concrete through a 17-term controlled vocabulary and an annotation test on 617 video game titles. Terms such as Adventurous, Aggressive, Cute, Dark, Horror, Imaginative, Intense, Peaceful, Quirky, and Solitary show how discovery can describe the felt atmosphere of play, but the authors also caution that mood is subjective and may require multiple annotations or crowdsourced ranking to support advisory, browsing, and collocation responsibly.

# **Tanaka, C. (2022). Indigenous video games in libraries. *Pathfinder: A Canadian Journal for Information Science Students and Early Career Professionals, 3*(1), 14-29. https://doi.org/10.29173/pathfinder57**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `ZH8JITZ4`; Better BibTeX citation key `tanakaIndigenousVideoGames2022`; attachment key `AQ5YZH2W`.

Tanaka argues that Indigenous video games belong in library collections and spaces as part of decolonizing library practice, digital literacy, cultural media access, and support for Indigenous creative production. The article begins from Indigenous Futurisms, defined through Grace Dillon as Indigenous perspectives in the context of technology, and connects that movement to video games as a rapidly growing form of new media. Tanaka's central claim is that libraries often overlook video games when thinking about Indigenous representation, even though games can share Indigenous Knowledges and traditions, support literacy and creativity, and make Indigenous-created media more accessible to patrons.

The first major section critiques historical representation of Indigenous Peoples in games. Tanaka discusses *The Oregon Trail*, *Europa Universalis*, and *Civilization* as examples of settler-colonial mechanics in which land, animals, materials, and Indigenous presence are rendered through extraction, conquest, erasure, or the figure of the hostile or helpful Other. In contrast, Indigenous-created and collaboratively developed games such as Elizabeth LaPensee's *When Rivers Were Trails*, *Never Alone*, and Maize Longboat's *Terra Nova* are presented as different models of game design. These games center Indigenous characters, Indigenous languages, cultural practices, relationality, community, and alternate forms of placemaking rather than progress through exploitation and ownership.

Tanaka then frames Indigenous video games as educational media. Video games are interactive, strategic, and agency-based: players make choices, solve problems, move through systems, and learn from consequences. In *When Rivers Were Trails*, for example, players encounter Ojibwe words, Traditional foods and medicines, emotional and spiritual wellbeing, General Allotment Act history, and Indigenous Ways of Being. Tanaka also discusses workshops by Indigenous creators such as Skawennati Fragnito and the Aboriginal Territories in Cyberspace project, where game creation becomes a form of storytelling, language work, self-representation, and technical learning. The article therefore treats gaming not only as consumption but also as a pathway into coding, storyboarding, design, art, project management, and digital authorship.

The article's most direct GBLS contribution is its list of library service recommendations. Tanaka suggests that libraries increase accessibility by setting up computer or console stations with Indigenous games installed; pursue community funding and partnerships with software and gaming companies; invite Indigenous Elders or cultural teachers to contextualize the knowledges represented in games; use cooperative play and live walkthroughs as discussion-based programming; host Indigenous gaming nights with Indigenous creators and industry professionals; offer gaming workshops for Indigenous Peoples interested in game creation; provide physical and online space for Indigenous creators; and raise professional awareness of Indigenous video games through conferences, workshops, and staff learning.

For the larger GBLS project, Tanaka gives one of the clearest examples of why games should be treated as cultural media, not just recreation or instructional novelty. The article links collection development, access infrastructure, programming, creator support, advisory, and staff education to questions of Indigenous sovereignty, representation, decolonization, and whose histories are archived or preserved. It also widens the GBLS evidence base by reminding libraries that digital-download indie games may not fit ordinary circulation models, so access may require installed stations, Steam accounts, programming, local partnerships, and deliberate promotion rather than simple acquisition.

The source is primarily conceptual and practical rather than empirical. It does not measure learning outcomes or patron use, and Tanaka explicitly situates the article as a starting point for larger discussion rather than a comprehensive Indigenous-authored framework. Its value for the review is therefore normative, critical, and service-oriented: it helps define what inclusive game collection practice should attend to, especially when games involve Indigenous Knowledges, Indigenous creators, and culturally specific forms of representation.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Games as Cultural Media and Library Collections`, after the Noorda and Inman Berens (2021) paragraph.

Suggested text: Tanaka (2022) gives the cultural-media argument a decolonial and Indigenous Futurisms focus, arguing that Indigenous-created and collaboratively developed video games such as *When Rivers Were Trails*, *Never Alone*, and *Terra Nova* should be represented in library collections and spaces. The article treats Indigenous video games as media for sovereignty, language, relationality, storytelling, education, and creative production, while warning that digital-only indie distribution may require installed access stations, programming, partnerships, and active promotion rather than ordinary circulation alone.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Curate for Inclusion, Access, and Advisory`, after the Bayeck and Bayeck (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Tanaka (2022) further shows that inclusive game curation includes whose games are acquired, how they are made playable, and who contextualizes them. Her recommendations for Indigenous video games include access stations, partnerships with gaming companies and Indigenous communities, cultural teachings by Elders, cooperative play discussions, creator talks, game-making workshops, space for Indigenous creators, and staff learning so that decolonizing collection work extends into programming, advisory, and infrastructure.

# **Heron, M. J., Belford, P. H., Reid, H., & Crabb, M. (2018). Meeple centred design: A heuristic toolkit for evaluating the accessibility of tabletop games. *The Computer Games Journal, 7*(2), 97-114. https://doi.org/10.1007/s40869-018-0057-8**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `4TXA5CKJ`; Better BibTeX citation key `heronMeepleCentredDesign2018`; attachment key `AZDCWGJR`.

Heron, Belford, Reid, and Crabb develop the Meeple Centred Design heuristic toolkit for evaluating the accessibility of tabletop games. The article is not about libraries, but it is highly relevant to GBLS because tabletop collections, clubs, game nights, school-library lessons, and public-library programs can unintentionally exclude players when games are selected only for popularity, cost, theme, or age range. The authors argue that tabletop accessibility is more complicated than video game accessibility because board and card games vary widely in game systems, game mechanisms, interaction regimes, sensory demands, social rules, component design, and table logistics.

The article begins from the sociocultural importance of board games. Tabletop gaming is framed as a way to build social capital, participate in popular culture, and share cultural experiences with friends and family. This makes accessibility a cultural inclusion issue, not only a functional design issue. If people cannot participate in popular tabletop games because components, rules, time demands, communication systems, or economic conditions exclude them, they are also blocked from the social and conversational benefits of play.

The authors identify a wide range of accessibility barriers. Physical barriers include small components, spatially complex game states, cards held privately, tile flipping, cube movement, board size, fine motor demands, target zones, shuffling, dice rolling, and repeated physical manipulation. Visual barriers include color coding, poor contrast, small fonts, visually busy boards, component similarity, orientation-dependent symbols, and paper money. Cognitive barriers include complex rules, state-dependent exceptions, large decision spaces, memory burdens, hidden information, randomness, scoring complexity, and rule mutability. Communicative barriers include language demands, hearing requirements, secret communication, negotiation, bluffing, and body-language interpretation.

A key strength of the article is that it also treats emotional and socioeconomic accessibility as part of the design problem. Emotional barriers may arise from high randomness, betrayal, ganging up on a player, harsh loss conditions, take-that mechanics, player elimination, or situations where a game makes one person feel targeted or unable to recover. Socioeconomic barriers include cost, cultural familiarity, hobby knowledge, language, required expansions, and the assumptions games make about who has access to time, money, space, and a game-playing community. This broader framing is especially useful for libraries, which often use games to reduce access barriers but may reproduce them if collection and program decisions ignore who can actually play.

The Meeple Like Us project organizes the heuristic lens around six areas: visual impairments, cognitive impairments, physical impairments, communication impairments, socioeconomic impairments, and intersectional issues where combinations of impairments create new barriers. The authors emphasize that accessibility cannot be solved one impairment at a time. A symbol may work for some players with visual impairments but become difficult when visual and cognitive impairments intersect. A card hand may be manageable for someone with a physical impairment until visual inspection also requires movement or magnification. Dice may create both cognitive burden and physical interaction problems. Time limits may become unfair when compensatory regimes are needed.

The article is careful not to overclaim the heuristic. The authors state that the toolkit does not replace traditional user testing with impaired players. Direct playtesting produces richer feedback, but it also requires recruiting multiple players with varied impairments, teaching games, observing play, interpreting feedback, compensating participants, and adapting to schedule changes. The toolkit is therefore positioned as a preliminary evaluative pass by an informed analyst, helping designers, publishers, or evaluators identify common accessibility issues before deeper user testing. The Meeple Like Us project had applied the framework to more than one hundred games and published accessibility teardowns for the tabletop community.

For the larger GBLS project, Heron et al. offer a practical lens for inclusive tabletop service. Libraries that circulate board games, run tabletop clubs, host D&D or board-game cafes, teach through games, or advise families and educators need a way to talk about more than age recommendations and player counts. The heuristic can support collection development, catalog notes, advisory, program planning, facilitation, accessibility accommodations, staff training, and post-program reflection. It also reminds GBLS practitioners that accessible play may require flexible rules, multiple roles, component supports, alternative communication, larger tables, drop-in/drop-out options, session-length choices, and explicit attention to emotional safety.

The source should still be cited as adjacent rather than library-specific evidence. It does not assess library programs, patron outcomes, or library collection use. It is a game accessibility framework developed for tabletop games broadly. Its value for the review is translational: it gives libraries a vocabulary for evaluating whether tabletop game services are inclusive in practice. Because the GBLS review already includes adjacent audience and culture sources in the inclusion/advisory section, Heron et al. belongs there as the strongest accessibility-focused tabletop design source in this batch.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Curate for Inclusion, Access, and Advisory`, immediately after the Cross et al. (2023) paragraph.

Suggested text: Heron et al. (2018) provide an adjacent but practical accessibility lens for tabletop GBLS through the Meeple Centred Design heuristic toolkit. Their framework evaluates visual, cognitive, physical, communicative, emotional, socioeconomic, and intersectional barriers in tabletop games, including color and contrast, component size, board-state complexity, memory demands, hidden information, randomness, take-that mechanics, time limits, drop-in/drop-out capacity, and the need for compensatory regimes; for libraries, the toolkit can inform game selection, advisory, facilitation, catalog notes, and accessibility accommodations before and alongside direct user testing.

# **Manso Rodriguez, R. A. (2022). Gamificacion en bibliotecas para la alfabetizacion mediatica e informacional, tendencias, buenas practicas y recomendaciones. *Bibliotecas. Anales de Investigacion, 18*(3), 1-15.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `28AWBD5X`; Better BibTeX citation key `ramonalbertomansorodriguezGAMIFICATIONLIBRARIESMEDIA2022`; attachment key `GF6S36WS`.

Manso Rodriguez offers a descriptive literature review of gamification in library-based media and information literacy (MIL) programs. The article works from UNESCO's integrated idea of media and information literacy: the capacity to locate, retrieve, evaluate, use, produce, and communicate information across formats and media. Its purpose is not to report a new game or assessment, but to synthesize trends, good practices, and recommendations for libraries that want to design gamified MIL instruction.

Methodologically, the study uses documentary analysis supported by bibliographic review. Manso Rodriguez searched Scopus and Web of Science for "gamification" AND "media and information literacy" AND "library," focused on publications from 2011 through 2021, removed duplicates, screened according to inclusion and exclusion criteria, and then added additional sources through chain searching. The final corpus contained 49 relevant studies: 42 from database searching and 7 from citation chaining. The review categorizes the literature into theoretical studies that characterize gamification in library MIL and practical studies that report implementation.

Several trend findings matter for GBLS. The literature is heavily academic-library centered: 89.7 percent of the authors worked in academic libraries, compared with 6.2 percent in public libraries and 4.1 percent in school libraries. Keywords cluster around information literacy, library escape rooms, instructional design, academic libraries, library instruction, video games, games in libraries, media information literacy, and game design. The review identifies two main gamified MIL service forms: library orientation, where games introduce users to catalogs, electronic resources, library spaces, and general services; and MIL instruction, where games teach access, use, production, diffusion, evaluation, communication, citation, database searching, and critical information skills.

The article also gathers the game elements most often used in library MIL settings. These include role play, cooperation, movement, scenes and story, collection or selection, question-and-answer structures, rewards and penalties, feedback, points, levels, exploration, time pressure, competition, strategy, resource management, and capture or elimination mechanics. Manso Rodriguez is careful to note that gamification is not simply points and rewards. Drawing on the literature, he emphasizes that users must recognize the meaning and value of the content, be able to experiment without fear of failure, interact socially, and solve problems in a context where game mechanics support learning rather than merely decorate a tutorial.

The article's clearest practical contribution is a six-step implementation guide. Libraries should first define the target audience and its information and training needs; second, create a "tree of competencies" that identifies the MIL skills users need; third, evaluate other library projects and identify good practices; fourth, build a game prototype by choosing an MIL model, defining specific learning objectives, mapping game mechanics and learning mechanics, creating supporting instructional materials, designing the game, testing it with collaborating users, and adjusting it; fifth, implement the training program; and sixth, evaluate and gather systematic feedback so the program can be revised.

For GBLS, Manso Rodriguez strengthens the design and implementation layer of the review. The article connects gamification to lifelong learning, active learning, critical thinking, fake-news evaluation, collaborative learning, information-search tasks, citation work, database use, and the development of advanced communication and information skills. It also reinforces several cross-cutting cautions: games should be simple enough for users to understand, learning objectives should remain primary, failure should be part of the learning process, games should balance information seeking and information use, and librarians should treat gamified instruction as both teaching and assessment.

The main limitation is that this is a review and recommendation article rather than a tested intervention. Some claims about positive effects are synthesized from the reviewed literature rather than independently measured here, and the study's database strategy may miss non-indexed practitioner work. Still, its value for the larger GBLS project is substantial because it consolidates a decade of library gamification/MIL discourse and converts it into an actionable design sequence that can guide future GBLS program planning.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Horban and Skachenko (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Manso Rodriguez (2022) synthesizes 49 studies on gamification for library-based media and information literacy from 2011-2021, finding a literature dominated by academic libraries and organized around two main service forms: orientation to catalogs, electronic resources, spaces, and services, and MIL instruction in information access, evaluation, production, communication, citation, database searching, and critical information skills. The review is not a new assessment study, but it usefully gathers common mechanics such as role play, cooperation, narrative, collection, feedback, points, levels, exploration, time pressure, competition, strategy, and resource management.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Design for Reflection and Transfer`, after the Guo and Goh (2016) paragraph.

Suggested text: Manso Rodriguez (2022) translates gamified MIL literature into a practical design sequence: define the audience and its training needs, build a competency tree, review comparable library projects, select an MIL model, set learning objectives, map game mechanics to learning mechanics, create instructional materials, prototype and test with users, implement, and evaluate iteratively. For GBLS, this reinforces that gamification should be designed around competencies, problem solving, feedback, trial and error, and transfer rather than around points or rewards alone.

# **Danilovic, S., & de Voogt, A. (2021). Making sense of abstract board games: Toward a cross-ludic theory. *Games and Culture, 16*(5), 499-518. https://doi.org/10.1177/1555412020914722**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `YKZPV26J`; Better BibTeX citation key `danilovicMakingSenseAbstract2021`; attachment key `QR4Q2ENA`.

Danilovic and de Voogt offer a theoretical account of abstract board games, especially traditional, nonproprietary games such as checkers, senet, mancala games, morris games, and other board games whose formal rules and simple boards have traveled across long periods and wide geographic areas. The article is not about libraries, but it has conceptual value for GBLS because tabletop programs and collections often include abstract games whose rules, materials, and cultural meanings do not operate in the same way as heavily themed modern hobby games.

The central problem is that many game theories assume meaningful representation. Game studies often treats games as systems in which players interpret rules, materials, fiction, theme, and social context together. Abstract board games challenge that assumption because they often lack figurative or decorative markings and may use arrangements of squares, holes, lines, stones, seeds, or counters with little culturally specific iconography. Yet these games have moved across borders, persisted for centuries or millennia, and been appropriated by many play communities without always preserving knowledge of origin.

The authors distinguish between reliable and variable qualities. The reliable properties of abstract board games are their self-sufficient formal systems: rules, legal moves, procedural structures, and mathematical relationships that can be reproduced across contexts. The variable qualities are the culturally specific meanings, materials, rituals, interpretations, and social practices that players attach to those formal systems. Abstract board games can therefore function as placeholders or virtual machines: the same formal structure can be implemented with different materials and imbued with different local meanings without changing the identity of the game as a rule system.

Their case of checkers in the Marshall Islands illustrates this distinction. Checkers, known as jekab in Marshallese, uses a rule set found in several Pacific contexts, but those rules do not by themselves explain the game's cultural significance. The game has become part of cultural celebrations, social identity, intergenerational play, and Marshallese heritage, yet the specific formal rule set likely traveled through other historical pathways. The meaning of play is real, but it is not reducible to the intrinsic logic of the rules.

The article develops a modular ontology of abstract board games with three properties: procedural reliability, visual flexibility, and material nonessentiality. Procedural reliability means the formal rules can be transcribed and reproduced across cultural contexts. Visual flexibility means the layout can be rendered in different visual or symbolic ways without changing the game. Material nonessentiality means the board and tokens can be made from many local materials because those materials are not essential to the formal identity of the game. The authors also distinguish intrinsic game semantics, or logico-analytical interpretation of formal rules, from extrinsic game semantics, or culturally specific symbolic interpretation of the game and its materials.

For the larger GBLS project, this source should be used as conceptual grounding rather than evidence of library outcomes. It helps explain why some tabletop games may be especially portable in library programs: their rules can be taught with low-cost materials, different boards, found objects, or culturally local tokens. At the same time, the article warns against assuming that portability equals cultural neutrality. The formal system may travel, but libraries still need to ask how local communities understand, name, teach, display, and value the game.

This is useful alongside culturally situated sources such as Bayeck and Bayeck on African board games. Bayeck and Bayeck caution against flattening local games into generic mechanic labels; Danilovic and de Voogt explain why some abstract game systems can also be formally reproducible while remaining locally meaningful. Together, they support a nuanced GBLS position: libraries can use abstract tabletop games as accessible, low-material, cross-context play forms, but they should still treat names, histories, materials, rules variants, and community interpretation as part of facilitation and advisory.

The limitations are scope-based. The article does not discuss libraries, patrons, programming, collections, instruction, or evaluation. It is a game studies and archaeology theory article. It should not be cited as evidence that abstract board games succeed in library settings. Its value lies in helping the review's conceptual terrain distinguish between game mechanics, materials, themes, and meanings when libraries select and explain tabletop games.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Key Game Studies Debates Relevant to Libraries`, after the Wang and Huang (2023) paragraph and before `### Digital vs Analog Game Debate`.

Suggested text: Danilovic and de Voogt (2021) add a useful conceptual caution for abstract tabletop GBLS. Their cross-ludic theory distinguishes the reliable formal systems of abstract board games from the variable local meanings, materials, names, and cultural practices attached to them; for libraries, this means abstract games may be portable and low-material without being culturally blank, so facilitation and advisory should attend both to rules and to community-specific interpretation.

# **Pervolaraki, I.-E., Garoufallou, E., Siatri, R., Zafeiriou, G., & Virkus, S. (2015). How can video games facilitate information literacy? In S. Kurbanoğlu, J. Boustany, S. Špiranec, E. Grassian, D. Mizrachi, & L. Roy (Eds.), *Information literacy: Moving toward sustainability* (pp. 339-349). Springer. https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-28197-1_35**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `78GDIC6Y`; Better BibTeX citation key `pervolarakiHowCanVideo2015`; attachment key `TYK4FGGF`.

Processing note: the Zotero attachment is a full ECIL 2015 proceedings volume. This entry summarizes only the chapter "How Can Video Games Facilitate Information Literacy?" on pages 339-349.

Pervolaraki, Garoufallou, Siatri, Zafeiriou, and Virkus provide a systematic literature review on how video games have been adopted by educational institutions and libraries to support learning, literacy, and information literacy among adolescents and young adults. The chapter begins by acknowledging the long-standing concern that games may increase aggression or reduce prosocial behavior, but it shifts the review toward evidence that games can produce perceptual, cognitive, behavioral, affective, and motivational outcomes. Its central question for GBLS is how video games can become educational and library tools rather than simply entertainment media.

The authors use a systematic-review protocol adapted from Hemingway and Brereton: define review objectives, collect relevant literature, assess individual studies, combine results, and place findings in context. Searches were conducted from December 2014 to February 2015 across ERIC, JSTOR, ProQuest, PsycINFO, IEEE Xplore, ScienceDirect, Wiley Online Library, CiteSeerX, Emerald Insight, Google Scholar, dissertations, and conference proceedings, with citation chaining to reach saturation. The covered literature was published from 2004 to 2015, written in English, and focused on video games in public libraries, education, literacy, and information literacy.

The review is organized into five emerging themes. First, "video game literacy" treats games as cultural and social worlds in which players collaborate, solve problems, read, write, and participate in communities. The authors draw on Gee, Squire, Zimmerman, Steinkuehler, Shaffer, and others to argue that game literacy involves design, play, systems, social interaction, and situated expertise rather than only screen time. Second, "video games in education" summarizes evidence that games can support motivation, history learning, math achievement, computer science concepts, problem solving, and engagement, while also noting that educators still struggle to connect specific game tasks and skills to desired learning outcomes.

Third, the chapter discusses game design benefits. The reviewed studies suggest that adolescents who participate in making games can develop collaboration, engagement, critical thinking, hardware and software skills, and understanding of how technologies are produced rather than merely consumed. The authors note, however, that most research focuses on playing games rather than creating them. Fourth, the chapter reviews video games, reading, and writing. Studies of game manuals, forums, strategy sites, game reviews, MMOGs, and paratext creation suggest that adolescents may read and write extensively around games, sometimes performing above school-text reading levels when texts are tied to self-chosen game interests. Fifth, the public-library section gathers conceptual, practical, and limited empirical work on gaming programs, game collections, literacy, role-playing games, game selection, gaming policies, and library videogame events.

For the GBLS project, the chapter is valuable because it connects video games to information literacy without reducing the connection to a single library lesson. Information literacy appears in gameplay, game communities, out-of-game reading, collaborative information seeking, forum participation, wiki creation, strategy use, and the production of knowledge around games. This broadens the review's account of IL beyond orientation games and escape rooms. It also helps justify library videogame programs and collections as part of literacy ecology: they can attract adolescents and young adults, support social and collaborative learning, and provide a bridge between recreational interests and lifelong learning.

The chapter is also useful for its cautionary gaps. The authors argue that little is known about gaming and adolescents in library settings, that U.S. public libraries appear to have adopted videogame programs more readily than European libraries, that adult gaming in libraries is under-researched, and that studies of young people creating games remain limited compared with studies of game use. They also call for educator and librarian training so staff can move beyond unfamiliarity and fear of negative impacts. These gaps are directly relevant to the GBLS future research agenda, especially because later GBLS work still often relies on small cases, practitioner reports, and uneven evidence across library types.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Manso Rodriguez (2022) paragraph.

Suggested text: Pervolaraki et al. (2015) provide an earlier systematic review of video games, literacy, information literacy, education, and public libraries, organizing the literature around video game literacy, educational uses, game-design benefits, reading and writing, and public-library adoption. Their synthesis broadens IL beyond formal lessons: game-related information work can include in-game and out-of-game reading, forums, wikis, strategy sites, collaborative information seeking, paratext creation, problem solving, and game making.

Section and exact location: `Gaps in the Literature and Future Research Agenda > Evidence Gaps`, after the Khademi Zare et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Pervolaraki et al. (2015) identify durable video-game evidence gaps that remain relevant for GBLS: comparatively little library research on adolescents and gaming, limited evidence on adults and gaming in libraries, uneven public-library adoption between the United States and Europe, and a tendency to study game use more than young people's creation of games. Their review also shows why staff training matters, since unfamiliarity and fear of negative effects can prevent educators and librarians from evaluating game-based literacy work on its actual learning design.

# **Nelson, A. (2023). Review of the book *Let's roll: A guide to setting up tabletop role-playing games in your school or public library*, by L. Maxwell. *School Library Journal, 69*(5), 103.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `A7GUZTZH`; Better BibTeX citation key `nelsonMAXWELLLucasLets2023`; attachment key `3JJRHCXT`.

Nelson's short professional review evaluates Lucas Maxwell's *Let's Roll: A Guide to Setting up Tabletop Role-Playing Games in Your School or Public Library* for the May 2023 professional-reading section of *School Library Journal*. The review is not a research article, program case study, or direct account of a library TTRPG program. Its value for the GBLS project is instead bibliographic and field-facing: it records how a mainstream school-library review venue presented Maxwell's handbook to practitioners at the moment of publication.

The review frames Maxwell's book as a support text for library staff who are new to tabletop role-playing games and for staff who already understand gaming but feel uncertain about launching RPG programming. The named barriers are practical rather than theoretical: finding institutional support, marketing to patrons, and managing a chaotic program session. Nelson also identifies the book's supplemental materials, including a glossary of terms, a sample program proposal, case studies from school and public librarians, recommended reading lists, and brief reviews of simple gateway RPGs that require minimal preparation or expertise.

Nelson's assessment emphasizes tone and usability. The book is described as well organized, specific, gentle, and conversational. The review notes that Maxwell sometimes leans heavily on his own experience as a high school librarian, but it also highlights case study statements from librarians running TTRPGs in different library settings and with different age groups. The verdict recommends the title for school or public library professionals who feel trepidation about introducing an RPG program.

For the larger GBLS project, this review reinforces the practitioner-training problem around TTRPG services. It suggests that many librarians do not primarily need persuasion that TTRPGs exist; they need practical help translating the format into library workflows, administrative language, promotion, facilitation, and beginner-friendly game selection. The review also shows how professional literature positions TTRPG programming as relevant to both school and public libraries, and how anxiety about facilitation, institutional approval, and session management remains central to adoption.

At the same time, this item should not be used as substantive evidence in the literature review when Maxwell's handbook itself is available for direct processing. Nelson summarizes and recommends the book but does not add original data, independent program details, or a distinct theoretical argument. It is best retained as an adjacent professional-review record and a pointer to Maxwell (2023), not as a separate paragraph in `review.md`.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. Because this is a brief review of Maxwell (2023), and the reviewed book is available as a separate Zotero item for direct analysis, this source should be filed in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion rather than inserted into `review.md`.

# **Maxwell, L. (2023). *Let's roll: A guide to setting up tabletop role-playing games in your school or public library*. Facet Publishing.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `JCR8I7M9`; Better BibTeX citation key `maxwellLetsRollGuide2023`; attachment key `UR2GZ8HG`.

Processing note: the attached PDF contains the book's front matter, praise pages, table of contents, glossary, author note, acknowledgements, and introduction rather than the full text of all chapters. This entry summarizes the attached excerpt and uses the table of contents to identify the book's stated scope; the full book should be consulted before making detailed claims about its chapter-level recommendations.

Maxwell presents *Let's Roll* as a practical guide for school and public librarians who want to run tabletop role-playing game (TTRPG) programs, especially Dungeons & Dragons and related systems. The introductory framing is deliberately beginner-friendly: the guide is aimed at librarians, teachers, and other youth-serving professionals who may be intimidated by rules, Dungeon Master/Game Master responsibilities, preparation time, or the feeling that they must emulate expert online performers. Maxwell repeatedly stresses that the point is not rules mastery but creating a fun, welcoming, collaborative experience.

The excerpt establishes several practitioner principles that matter for GBLS. First, Maxwell treats TTRPGs as unusually sticky library programming. He contrasts D&D sessions with manga club, book club, film club, mock trial, makerspace, and other library clubs, noting that the same core students kept attending over a long period and rarely missed sessions. Second, he frames TTRPG play as connected to mental health and well-being, while carefully noting that he is not a psychologist. The introduction describes TTRPGs as personally therapeutic and as a cathartic space for youth, and the table of contents indicates that the first chapter argues for benefits to literacy, mental health and well-being, and autistic players.

The excerpt is also useful for its implementation details. Maxwell describes starting through student interest generated partly by *Stranger Things*, surveying students, beginning with a small budget, and initially allowing older, more experienced students to act as Game Masters when staff capacity was limited. During COVID lockdowns, he moved sessions online through Microsoft Teams and found that frequent virtual play helped sustain connection and reduce anxiety. These details reinforce a GBLS lesson already visible in other sources: successful play programs often begin with local enthusiasm, modest resources, flexible facilitation, and willingness to let patrons or students supply some expertise.

The glossary and table of contents make the guide's expected infrastructure visible. The book defines TTRPG terms such as Game Master, Session Zero, theatre of the mind, battle mat/map, one shot, non-player character, rule of cool, rule zero, sandbox, and total party kill. The chapter outline covers convincing decision-makers, case studies, program proposals, what materials are needed, running online sessions, Roll20, Zoom, promotion, zero-preparation games, Session Zero, before-session preparation, Game Master tips, teen Game Masters, a list of TTRPGs for libraries, online resources, recommended reading, and fiction for ages 9 and up. Even from the excerpt, TTRPG service work appears as facilitation, advocacy, safety-setting, collection development, and reader/player advisory rather than simply hosting a game.

For the GBLS project, Maxwell is best used as a practitioner guide rather than empirical evidence. It adds a concrete public/school-library TTRPG voice that emphasizes accessibility, beginner confidence, student leadership, low-cost startup, online adaptability, and session design. It also aligns TTRPGs with literacy, reading interests, youth belonging, social play, and well-being. Because the attachment is only an excerpt, the review should cite it cautiously and avoid detailed claims about the book's later recommendations until the full text is available.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > TTRPG and LARP Programs`, after the Watson et al. (2024) paragraph.

Suggested text: Maxwell's (2023) introductory material for *Let's Roll* positions TTRPGs as a practical school- and public-library program format built around fun, collaborative storytelling, student interest, and beginner-friendly facilitation. The attached excerpt is not the full guide, but it foregrounds several operational themes for GBLS: gaining decision-maker support, articulating literacy and well-being benefits, starting with modest resources, using experienced teens as Game Masters when appropriate, adapting to online platforms such as Teams, Roll20, or Zoom, and treating Session Zero as a way to set expectations before play.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Treat Facilitation as Core Infrastructure`, after the Capdarest-Arest et al. (2019) paragraph.

Suggested text: Maxwell (2023) also makes facilitation anxiety visible in TTRPG services. His advice to relax, use the "rule of cool," avoid unnecessary world-building, rely on pre-made or low-preparation games, and remember that the Game Master is also a player suggests that sustainable library TTRPG programs depend on staff confidence, shared play norms, teen leadership pathways, and manageable preparation as much as on game materials.

# **Anderson, K. (2022). Libraries & lizards: Playing D&D at public libraries program framework. *YA Hotline*, (116).**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `VITHPF47`; Better BibTeX citation key `kateandersonLibrariesLizardsPlaying2022`; attachment key `64EICZ9U`.

Anderson's "Libraries & Lizards" is a practical program framework for running Dungeons & Dragons with young adults in a public library, with possible adaptation for school libraries. The source is not an empirical study, but it is a detailed professional-planning document: it specifies audience, staffing, developmental objectives, materials, session timing, publicity, budget, remote delivery tools, and evaluation practices. Its main GBLS value is that it translates a popular tabletop role-playing game into a library program design that can be copied, adapted, and assessed locally.

The framework describes D&D as a tabletop role-playing game built from improvisation, storytelling, dice rolling, character action, and Dungeon Master facilitation. Anderson begins from an access problem: the game is increasingly popular, but young players may not know how to find a group or how to start. The library is presented as a familiar, safe, resourced space where teens can learn the hobby and find a supervised place to play. The design is intended for three to five players ages 15 to 18, run by one staff member acting as Dungeon Master, with possible expansion to multiple groups when more staff are available.

A notable feature is the explicit connection to the Search Institute's 40 Developmental Assets for Adolescents. Anderson maps the program to adult relationships, safety, positive peer influence, creative activities, youth programs, reading for pleasure, responsibility, planning and decision making, interpersonal competence, peaceful conflict resolution, personal power, and self-esteem. This gives the program a youth-development rationale rather than treating D&D as entertainment alone. The rationale is also operational: library staff supervise emotional and physical safety, participants cooperate in a shared story, and breaks can direct teens toward related fantasy books, role-playing resources, library cards, and displays.

The program timetable is unusually concrete. Session 1 is a four- to five-hour introduction and character-creation session with welcome, introductions, character creation, a break, rules instruction, a no-consequences combat encounter, and closing. Session 2 and later sessions use a similar four- to five-hour structure, moving more quickly into play, refreshing table etiquette, recapping the story, taking a break, continuing the game, and ending at a narratively appropriate moment or cliffhanger. Anderson also notes that the second-session template can be adapted for one-shots by using prewritten characters from the D&D Starter Set or Wizards of the Coast.

The materials and infrastructure sections make the program's hidden labor visible. Anderson lists campaign and rules books, the Player's Handbook, Dungeon Master's Guide, Monster Manual, Curse of Strahd, optional Starter Set, DM screen, character sheets, pencils, notebooks, dice, snacks, water, D&D Beyond, tablets, fantasy books, library-card forms, display cases, chairs, printer access, and remote tools such as Discord, Roll20, or other video-conferencing software. The budget ranges from a low-cost Starter Set model to a more complete setup, with a reminder that many purchases are reusable across months or years of play. The publicity plan includes posters, handouts, conversations, local game shops or cafes, school librarians, the library website, newsletters, Instagram, and TikTok.

For the larger GBLS project, Anderson is useful because it shows how TTRPG programming can be treated as a replicable service model. It connects play to developmental assets, reading, social connection, safety, and decision making, but it also attends to program logistics: staff expertise, group size, recurring scheduling, snacks, physical/digital tools, remote participation, starter materials, partnerships, and post-session debriefing. It also reinforces that evaluation for social play may be informal and formative: staff should check whether players are having fun, debrief after emotional or stressful encounters, ask what players enjoyed or disliked, and improve the experience session by session.

The limitation is evidentiary. Anderson provides a framework, not measured outcomes from an implemented program. It should therefore be used in the review as a practitioner model and service-design example rather than as proof of developmental impact. Its strongest contribution is to show what public-library D&D planning looks like when staff connect youth-development goals to concrete table practice.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > TTRPG and LARP Programs`, after the Maxwell (2023) paragraph.

Suggested text: Anderson (2022) turns public-library D&D into a replicable teen program framework: a one-staff, three- to five-player model for ages 15-18 with an introductory character-creation session, recurring four- to five-hour play sessions, physical and remote tool lists, publicity channels, budget ranges, and informal debriefing. Its developmental-assets rationale links TTRPG play to adult relationships, safety, peer influence, creative activity, reading for pleasure, planning, interpersonal competence, conflict resolution, personal power, and self-esteem, making the format legible as youth-development programming as well as recreation.

# **Simpson, K., & Midgley, K. (2023). Learning through fun: Board games cafes for undergraduate nursing students. *British Journal of Nursing, 32*(8), 384-389.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `RTWHJ98M`; Better BibTeX citation key `simpsonLearningFunBoard2023`; attachment key `GT5EN7ZJ`.

Simpson and Midgley describe the development, implementation, and evaluation of clinical board-game cafes for first-year undergraduate nursing students at the University of Nottingham. The article is not about libraries, but it is relevant background for game-based learning, tabletop play, and low-tech social learning environments. The authors begin from the popularity of contemporary board-game cafes and from educational literature suggesting that games can support motivation, participation, experimentation, critical thinking, creativity, collaboration, communication, decision making, and safe practice.

The model was developed after curriculum changes increased Year 1 students' simulated clinical learning. The authors wanted a low-cost, cafe-style classroom environment where students could rehearse clinical concepts in a more relaxed and engaging way. The initiative was piloted with final-year volunteer students so that games could be adjusted for first-year difficulty. Three cafes took place before COVID-19 closures, and the model later resumed with the same delivery format and some added games.

Each two-hour cafe served 24 students across adult, child, mental health, and learning disability nursing fields. At least three staff were present to serve drinks, answer questions, and join games. The cafe atmosphere included hot drinks, biscuits, background music, and tables arranged for groups of no more than eight students. Games were aligned with topics already covered in the curriculum, including hydration, the drug round, infection control, anatomy, and physiology. Textbooks and the British National Formulary were available so students could look up uncertain answers, and staff used the sessions to ask questions and facilitate discussion.

Student feedback produced several themes. Students valued the cafe as a different and safe way to learn, especially because it offered repetition, hands-on engagement, and a relaxed environment away from lecture halls or seminars. Some students connected the activities to placement confidence, medication calculations, and clinical knowledge. Students also valued the social opportunity: the cafe helped them meet peers across nursing fields, participate in a timetabled social event despite caring responsibilities or commuting pressures, and build belonging. Staff observed peer support, encouragement of quieter students, and a light-hearted atmosphere with laughter and conversation.

The discussion connects the cafes to Kolb's experiential learning theory and Bandura's social cognitive theory. Students were responsible for their own learning while observing and sharing knowledge with peers. The authors argue that board-game cafes can support teamworking, communication, interpersonal skills, social integration, staff-student relationships, and psychologically safe learning. They also acknowledge practical cautions: games require space and cost, competition may feel threatening for some learners, and noisy environments may challenge neurodiverse students.

For GBLS, the article is useful as adjacent evidence for why low-tech tabletop play can make professional learning more social, safe, and memorable. It also names a possible library connection: future plans included storing games in the library so students could borrow them for self-directed learning. However, the intervention itself was designed and delivered by nursing faculty in a university teaching context, not by a library. It should therefore not be inserted into the main GBLS review as library evidence. It may be useful later as background if the project develops an appendix on health education, academic-library partnerships, or board-game cafes as a transferable format.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. Because this is a nursing education intervention rather than a library service, collection, or program, it should be filed in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion.

# **Huber, S. E., Kiili, K., Nebel, S., Ryan, R. M., Sailer, M., & Ninaus, M. (2024). Leveraging the potential of large language models in education through playful and game-based learning. *Educational Psychology Review, 36*(1), Article 25. https://doi.org/10.1007/s10648-024-09868-z**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `KY2K8TWH`; Better BibTeX citation key `huberLeveragingPotentialLarge2024`; attachment key `Y72FNTPV`.
