Huber, Kiili, Nebel, Ryan, Sailer, and Ninaus write a perspective article about large language models in education and the possibility that playful and game-based learning can help address the risks those systems introduce. The article is not about libraries, information services, collections, youth programming, or public access to games. It is nevertheless an adjacent education/game-based-learning source because it connects generative AI, playful experimentation, prompt engineering, game-based learning, and expertise development.

The problem the authors identify is over-reliance. LLMs can help teachers and learners generate teaching units, curricula, study plans, assessments, explanations, feedback, writing support, literature reviews, and personalized learning materials. Yet the authors emphasize that present LLMs generate text through stochastic processes, so they can produce fabricated information, bias, false citations, misleading answers, and authoritative-sounding errors. Responsible use therefore requires domain expertise, critical evaluation, and human judgment. The educational danger is that learners may outsource the very practice through which expertise is developed.

The first proposed response is a playful approach to LLM use. Drawing on Papert's constructionist use of Logo, Piaget, Vygotsky, and self-determination theory, the article argues that new technology can be treated as a tool for meaningful, exploratory activity rather than as a shortcut for task completion. Prompt engineering becomes a form of playful interaction with LLMs when users iteratively adjust prompts, test outputs, use role or persona prompting, request alternative approaches, and critically revise results. In that framing, the value lies not only in the generated text but in the user's repeated practice of judgment, domain knowledge, and reflective control.

The second response is game-based learning. Huber et al. argue that well-designed games can foster persistence, autonomy, competence, relatedness, safe failure, double-loop reflection, and sustained practice. Their account draws on self-determination theory, the problem-based gaming model, and sociocultural accounts of game communities, affinity groups, theorycrafting, modding, peer apprenticeship, and spontaneous expert roles. Games matter because they can make practice intrinsically engaging, reducing the temptation to delegate cognitive effort to AI when the activity itself is meaningful.

The article then connects LLMs to two game-based-learning scenarios. First, students can learn by generating games: generative AI may lower technical barriers so learners can design games, reformulate academic content, prototype, playtest, share, reflect, and iterate. The authors call for a "prompting pedagogy" that sequences AI-supported game-making through idea generation, core design, prototyping, assessment, peer testing, and reflective discussion. Second, teachers can use LLMs and other generative AI tools to gamify learning materials or create learning-game prototypes by generating artwork, code, levels, mechanics, scenarios, and instructional-design options. In both cases, the authors stress that prompting and critical evaluation remain central.

For the GBLS project, this source is useful mainly as a horizon marker. It suggests possible future library-adjacent directions: AI-supported game design workshops, patron-created learning games, staff-created playful learning materials, and digital-literacy programs about evaluating LLM outputs through playful experimentation. It also reinforces a design caution that applies to GBLS: games should not merely decorate instruction; they should create meaningful practice, reflection, and motivation. However, the article provides no library evidence and no direct service model for public, school, or academic libraries.

This source should therefore remain excluded from the main review text. It can be retained as background for future-thinking notes about AI-assisted game design or playful AI literacy, but it should not be cited as evidence that libraries are implementing or evaluating such programs. Its proper use is conceptual adjacency, not GBLS literature review support.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. This is an education/AI/game-based-learning perspective article with no library setting, library staff, patrons, collections, programming, or service evaluation. File in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion.

# **Elkins, A. J. (2015). Lets play! Why school librarians should embrace gaming in the library. *Knowledge Quest, 43*(5), 58-63.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `PQJ96YIY`; Better BibTeX citation key `elkinsLetsPlayWhy2015`; attachment key `276NTI2N`.

Elkins makes a practitioner-facing case that school librarians should play, collect, recommend, and program around games because games can support 21st-century literacies and deepen relationships with students. The article begins by pushing against the stereotype that games are only for young males, noting that mobile and social games have widened participation and that even relatively casual games may influence behavior. The central argument is that games are not only entertainment; they can be information-rich informal learning environments where students practice critical thinking, problem solving, collaboration, media literacy, and information literacy.

The article situates games within a long library history of games, puzzles, and toys, citing Nicholson's history of play in North American libraries. Elkins emphasizes that learners develop skills in both formal and informal environments, and that digital games provide engaging spaces where players interact with information objects and learning supports. Because failure in games is usually low-cost, students can take risks, experiment, and build understanding through active participation rather than passive reception.

Elkins gives particular attention to online and multiplayer gaming as literacy work. Players collaborate in-game, but they also extend collaboration outside the game by creating modifications, tools, strategies, gameplay videos, wiki pages, mathematical models, strategy guides, creative writing, and storytelling. This framing is useful for GBLS because it treats gaming literacy as production, analysis, communication, and information work around games, not merely consumption of games. It also connects directly to school librarians' existing concerns with reading, learning, and equitable access.

The access argument is one of the article's strongest GBLS contributions. Elkins argues that if school libraries provide print materials to foster traditional literacy, they should also provide access to digital media and mentoring so students can fully engage with 21st-century literacies. This is especially important for socioeconomically disadvantaged students who may not otherwise have access to games, platforms, or knowledgeable adults who can help them engage productively with gaming cultures.

The article also gives a modest starter list for librarians who are new to gaming. Elkins recommends accessible, relatively inexpensive games such as *The Cave*, *Cogs*, *Monster Loves You!*, *Spaceteam*, and *World of Goo*, tying them to critical thinking, problem solving, spatial reasoning, collaboration, moral choice, replay, and accessibility. The recommendations are dated in platform terms, but the selection logic remains useful: begin with games that do not require expensive consoles, are approachable for novice staff, and can be connected to particular literacy or thinking goals.

Finally, Elkins turns from rationale to implementation questions. School libraries considering games need to decide what materials to collect, including games, consoles, controllers, and strategy guides; how to circulate these materials, including loan periods, transportation, parent pickup, liability, damage, and loss; and what programming they can support, from meeting space to discussion groups and tournaments. He points readers to Gallaway, Neiburger, and Nicholson for further practical guidance. For the larger GBLS review, this source is best used as professional advocacy and early-stage implementation guidance rather than as outcome evidence.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Collections, Circulation, and Access`, after the Boyle (2018) paragraph.

Suggested text: Elkins (2015) adds a school-library access argument for digital games: if libraries provide print materials to foster traditional literacy, they can also provide games, platforms, strategy guides, and mentoring so students develop 21st-century literacies through media-rich informal learning. His practitioner guidance names early collection questions around consoles, controllers, games, strategy guides, loan periods, transportation, parent pickup, liability, damage, and loss.

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Instruction, Orientation, and Information Literacy`, after the Pervolaraki et al. (2015) paragraph.

Suggested text: Elkins (2015) translates game-literacy scholarship into school-library practice by arguing that gameplay can develop critical thinking, problem solving, collaboration, information literacy, and media literacy, especially when students also create mods, tools, strategies, videos, wiki pages, mathematical models, creative writing, and strategy guides around games. The article is advocacy rather than assessment evidence, but it helps connect school-library gaming to equitable access and informal literacy learning.

# **Mattson, J. L. (2021). Review of the book *Games and gamification in academic libraries*, edited by S. Crowe and E. Sclippa. *Communications in Information Literacy, 15*(1), 158-163.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `XRRW28N5`; Better BibTeX citation key `mattsonGamesGamificationAcademic2021`; attachment key `XANPMQBZ`.

Mattson reviews Stephanie Crowe and Eva Sclippa's edited ACRL volume *Games and Gamification in Academic Libraries*. The review is not an original research study, but it provides a useful map of how a contemporary professional book organized academic-library gaming practice across collection development, information literacy instruction, programming, and outreach. Mattson frames the book as responding to the tabletop gaming renaissance and to higher education's interest in active and experiential learning.

The reviewed book is organized into four sections. The first section, "An Overview of Games and Gamification," includes case studies of game nights, campus partnerships, game-based student-worker training, reward systems such as "library flair," team-building escape rooms, accessibility, ADA compliance, and diversity, equity, and inclusion considerations. Mattson singles out the accessibility chapter as essential for anyone planning gaming in instruction or outreach.

The second section focuses on adding and maintaining circulating game collections. Mattson notes chapters on growing electronic and tabletop collections through strategic planning, accessibility questions, circulation policies, maintenance procedures, custom arcades for game-based curricula, staff training for game recommendations, BoardGameGeek-informed collection development, and collection assessment through surveys and circulation data. For GBLS, this portion reinforces that game collections require policy, accessibility, maintenance, recommendation knowledge, and assessment infrastructure.

The third section covers games and gamification in information literacy instruction. Mattson describes chapters on information games for high-volume first-year one-shots, gaming and digital badging in business first-year experience programming, semester-long gamification using *Pandemic Legacy* to teach disinformation, and escape rooms as experiential approaches to hands-on information literacy lessons. She notes that stronger chapters connect game design to the ACRL Framework for Information Literacy for Higher Education.

The fourth section covers programming and outreach. Examples include transforming a library services fair into a mystery game, tabletop programs for casual gamers and international students, a Center for Games & Learning supported by an IMLS grant, game jams, smaller campus gaming events, gaming as a way to reduce library anxiety and foster student-faculty relationships, escape rooms built from scratch, and a special-collections orientation modeled on *Clue*. Mattson's evaluation is positive but not uncritical: she notes repetition around escape rooms and resources such as BreakoutEDU and BoardGameGeek, inconsistent chapter structure, and the absence of a concluding chapter.

For the larger GBLS project, Mattson's review is most useful as a source-base marker. It indicates that by 2021, academic-library gaming literature had consolidated into a professional development volume spanning collections, instruction, outreach, accessibility, staff training, game nights, escape rooms, game jams, badging, and partnerships. Because the item is a review of an edited book, it should not be used as evidence for any single program's outcomes unless the underlying chapter is consulted. Its value is synthetic and bibliographic: it helps identify the scope of professional discourse and points to specific implementation areas worth tracking.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Scope, Definition, and Review Approach > Source Base`, after the heading.

Suggested text: Mattson's (2021) review of Crowe and Sclippa's edited volume *Games and Gamification in Academic Libraries* helps characterize the professional source base for academic-library GBLS. The reviewed volume spans game nights, collection development, circulating electronic and tabletop games, information-literacy games, badging, escape rooms, outreach fairs, game jams, accessibility, staff training, student-faculty relationship building, and special-collections orientations; because Mattson is reviewing an edited book rather than reporting a new study, it is best used as a map of professional discourse and as a pointer to underlying chapters rather than as outcome evidence.

# **Banihashem, S. K., Dehghanzadeh, H., Clark, D., Noroozi, O., & Biemans, H. J. A. (2024). Learning analytics for online game-based learning: A systematic literature review. *Behaviour & Information Technology, 43*(12), 2689-2716. https://doi.org/10.1080/0144929X.2023.2255301**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `WNZ7L4A2`; Better BibTeX citation key `banihashemLearningAnalyticsOnline2024`; attachment key `U9RCHYA2`.

Banihashem, Dehghanzadeh, Clark, Noroozi, and Biemans review how learning analytics is used in online game-based learning in higher education. The article is not about libraries, but it is adjacent to GBLS because many library games, escape rooms, online orientations, digital breakouts, and virtual-world programs struggle with the same assessment question: what data should be collected, how should it be analyzed, why is it being collected, and who is supposed to use it?

The review is organized around four core elements of learning analytics: what, how, why, and who. "What" refers to the data collected; "how" refers to analytic methods and levels; "why" refers to the objectives of analytics; and "who" refers to the stakeholders who receive or use the analytic outputs. The authors searched Web of Science, Scopus, ERIC, IEEE, and Learning Analytics and Knowledge conference proceedings using PRISMA-informed procedures, screened 484 records, and included 20 empirical studies published between 2011 and 2021 that addressed online game-based learning in higher education.

For the "what" question, gameplay data dominated. All 20 studies collected internal gameplay data such as time spent, badges, learner-game interactions, points, attempts, or activity traces; some also collected external gameplay data such as eye tracking, emotional reactions, cognitive reactions, or webcam/video data. Assessment data appeared in most studies and included quizzes, tests, interviews, open-ended questions, surveys, and scales. Player data, such as demographics, academic background, profiles, emotions, cognition, and behavior, appeared less often. Game metadata was also part of the overall framework.

For the "how" question, the review found data mining, visualization, text mining, and social network analysis. Data mining was the most coded method category and included classification, regression, clustering, prediction, sequential pattern mining, relationship mining, and related techniques. Visualization appeared in most studies through dashboards, graphs, tables, charts, network diagrams, plots, and other representations. Text mining was used for written interaction data, and social network analysis was used to examine relationships and interaction patterns. The authors also connect methods to descriptive, diagnostic, predictive, and prescriptive analytics levels.

For the "why" question, the review distinguishes anticipated objectives from additional objectives. Anticipated learning-analytics objectives included assessment, monitoring, reflection, recommendation, adaptation, and prediction. Additional objectives included engagement, motivation and emotion, learning achievement, performance, decision making, interaction, satisfaction, stress detection, problem solving, language learning, and self-regulation. For the "who" question, learners were the primary stakeholders, followed by educators and system designers. Learners used analytics for reflection, awareness, recommendations, and adaptive support; educators used analytics for assessment, monitoring, feedback, and interventions; designers used analytics to evaluate the game system itself.

The discussion cautions against technological determinism. Learning analytics should not be bolted onto games just because data are available; the authors argue that analytic design should be guided by learning theory, the game-based-learning model, and the intended beneficiary and objective. Their conceptual framework begins with identifying the stakeholder and purpose, then selecting data types, analytics levels, and methods. They also name future-research needs: broader database coverage, inclusion of non-empirical work, deeper analysis of the relationship between game elements and analytics elements, implementation barriers, and more prescriptive models.

For the larger GBLS project, this source is useful as an assessment-adjacent vocabulary. It could help libraries think more carefully about online orientation games, digital breakouts, virtual-world programs, or game-design workshops if they decide to collect data beyond attendance and satisfaction: gameplay traces, assessment data, player data, dashboards, reflection prompts, recommendations, and stakeholder-specific feedback. It also reinforces that assessment design should start with service goals and user needs, not with whatever data the platform happens to log.

However, the article should remain excluded from the main review. Its evidence base is higher education online game-based learning, not library service. It does not study librarians, patrons, library collections, public/school/academic library programs, or GBLS outcomes. It is best retained as background for future assessment design, especially if the project later develops evaluation guidance for digital GBLS formats.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. This is a higher-education online game-based-learning analytics review rather than library literature, so it should remain in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion.

# **Bremer, P. (2017). Games people play. *The Reference Librarian, 58*(3), 202-205. https://doi.org/10.1080/02763877.2017.1320699**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `NHFEUHCI`; Better BibTeX citation key `bremerGamesPeoplePlay2017`; attachment key `KE2LT7BN`.

Bremer describes a tabletop Game Day created through collaboration between Rodney A. Briggs Library at the University of Minnesota Morris and the Morris Public Library. The academic library already hosted monthly game nights for students, but this event deliberately moved beyond campus to reach the wider community. The public library director wanted to attract underserved segments of the population, while the academic library wanted to use partnership to expand audience, bridge the town/gown divide, and foster future collaboration.

The event design centered inclusion. Bremer and the public library director wanted families, young adults, children, college students, and adults to feel comfortable, while recognizing that teenagers would be the hardest group to attract. They focused on board and card games because they lacked video-game resources and because tabletop games were experiencing a renaissance. The game list mixed familiar titles and hobby games, including *Risk*, *Monopoly*, checkers, chess, *Clue*, *Cranium*, *Forbidden Island*, *Dominion*, *Exploding Kittens*, *Apples to Apples*, *Bananagrams*, and *Welcome to the Dungeon*. Adult-oriented games such as *Cards Against Humanity* were deliberately excluded to keep the event family friendly.

Several operational choices are useful for GBLS. The organizers chose the public library rather than the university library because it had a large meeting room, a central location, and less perceived separation from community members. They advertised the partnership prominently, mentioned free pizza, designed colorful posters, checked the community calendar, and promoted the event through local newspaper, social media, both library websites, radio, campus channels, and town postings. The Friends of the Morris Public Library and Briggs Library Associates funded pizza, snacks, soda, cups, plates, and napkins because neither library had a gaming budget.

The event was successful enough to fill the community room. Bremer reports that college students, university and community adults, and younger children attended, while teenagers remained underrepresented. The facilitation work was active: organizers helped participants choose games, explained rules, connected people with suitable games, joined tables when a game needed another player, and stayed flexible when games ran past closing. The event also revealed collection and planning gaps. More games for younger elementary children were needed, because the academic library's collection leaned toward teen/adult play. The organizers also planned to rethink teen publicity and consider a teen-focused event.

For the larger GBLS project, Bremer is a practical community-partnership case. It shows that tabletop programming can bridge academic and public library missions, use existing academic-library game collections for community outreach, and make the library feel welcoming through food, simple games, visible partnership, and staff facilitation. It also reinforces several recurring implementation lessons: choose the venue that reduces access barriers, keep games short and approachable, match games to actual attendees, plan for noise and closing time, budget for refreshments, and evaluate who did not show up as carefully as who did.

The article is not an assessment study and offers no formal survey or outcome data. Its evidence is narrative and reflective. Even so, it is valuable because it describes a complete small-scale GBLS service model from idea to planning, delivery, and revision. It belongs in the review as a practitioner example of tabletop games as community outreach and inter-library collaboration.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Community, Belonging, and the Public Commons`, after the Vanden Elzen and Roush (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Bremer (2017) gives a small academic-public library partnership example of tabletop games as community bridge. By moving a University of Minnesota Morris game program to the Morris Public Library, the partners used a more accessible civic venue, shared game collections and staff expertise, food, broad publicity, and simple family-friendly board and card games to bring together college students, adults, families, and children, while also identifying teens as a still-unreached audience.

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Build Partnerships and Community Pipelines`, after the Vanden Elzen and Roush (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Bremer (2017) also shows that GBLS partnerships can reduce place-based barriers. The academic library had games and staff experience, while the public library had a central community location and local audience; together they could host a tabletop Game Day neither library could have delivered as effectively alone, using Friends-group support, shared publicity, rule facilitation, and post-event reflection to plan the next iteration.

# **Beth and Angel Make Games. (2024). *LSHM: Were You Paying Attention?!* Google Docs.**

Unique identifier: Zotero item key `NCPXFTAT`; Better BibTeX citation key `LSHMWereYou2024`; attachment key `9GU3XWS2`.

This short LSHM entry is a resource/advisory document for *Were You Paying Attention?!*, a compact game by Beth and Angel Make Games. It describes the game as being about attention, focus, and the cost of overwhelming listeners with too much information. The game premise is competitive and intentionally paradoxical: players learn about attention by trying to overwhelm opponents with information. The document also links to a Twitch VOD of a related session.

The entry notes that *Were You Paying Attention?!* was included in the TTRPGs for Trans Rights in Florida bundle on itch.io, a fundraising bundle supporting Zebra Youth and Transinclusive Group. This gives the item a useful cultural and community context: it is part of independent tabletop role-playing game culture, charity bundles, and queer/trans mutual-aid-oriented game publishing rather than a library program or research study.

Most of the document is advisory rather than rules text. It lists play-alikes across TTRPGs and board games, including *Fiasco*, *Epitaph*, *Stealing Stories*, *Cameleon*, *Codenames*, *Decrypto*, *A Fake Artist Goes to New York*, *Hanabi*, *The Moth Presents: A Game of Storytelling*, *Sounds Fishy*, *Telestrations*, *Wavelength*, and *Word Slam*. It also points to BoardGameGeek mechanism terms such as communication limits, memory, and storytelling. The read-alikes include storytelling craft books such as *How to Tell a Story*, *Long Story Short*, *Out on the Wire*, and *The Science of Storytelling*.

The entry also gathers website articles and safety tools. The article links include improvisation and storytelling resources and a piece on cognitive load theory in roleplaying games. The safety-tool list includes *Script Change*, *The Door Is Open*, and the TTRPG Safety Toolkit. The final section reproduces a GameRT Twitch policy emphasizing kindness, respect, no hate speech, moderator removal rights, harassment reporting, and private contact options.

For the larger GBLS project, the item is useful only as an example of game advisory metadata and community-contextual description. It shows how a library-adjacent game resource can connect one game to play-alikes, read-alikes, mechanisms, storytelling resources, safety tools, streaming policies, and social justice bundle context. These are all relevant to reader/player advisory and inclusive facilitation, but the item itself is not scholarship and does not report a library service.

This source should therefore remain outside the main review. It may be useful as a collection-development or advisory example if the project later builds a practical appendix of tabletop game records, read-alike/play-alike language, or safety-tool references. It should not be cited as evidence for GBLS outcomes, implementation, or patron learning.

# **Hedreen, R. C., Johnson, J. L., Lundy, M. A., Burnette, P., Perryman, C., Van Den Brekel, G., Jacobson, J. J., Gullett, M., & Czarnecki, K. (2008). Exploring virtual librarianship: Second Life Library 2.0. *Internet Reference Services Quarterly, 13*(2-3), 167-195. https://doi.org/10.1080/10875300802103833**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `CWH727S6`; Better BibTeX citation key `hedreenExploringVirtualLibrarianship2008`; attachment key `U7FWSVIZ`.

Hedreen and colleagues provide a detailed practitioner account of Second Life Library 2.0, one of the clearest early examples of librarians treating a game-adjacent virtual world as a service environment rather than as a novelty website. The article describes a project begun in April 2006 by the Alliance Library System and Online Programming for All Libraries, with Info Island and later Teen Second Life spaces serving as experimental library sites. Second Life is framed as a non-game descendant of massively multiplayer online games: it has no winning condition or quest structure, but it shares avatar presence, spatial navigation, user-created objects, social economies, and immersive worldbuilding with game worlds. The central question is therefore not whether Second Life is a library in the conventional sense, but what library services become when patrons, staff, collections, and programs meet inside a virtual third place.

The article is especially useful because it catalogues concrete services. SLL2 offered ready reference through in-world reference desks, instant-message "buzzers," librarian avatar labels, and an OCLC QuestionPoint trial. It offered instruction in basic Second Life skills, information literacy, and in-world content creation. It built collections through linked "books," note cards, audio books, government information objects, specialty areas such as anime and manga, and educational models such as a rotating Euglena. These examples show that virtual collections were not simply digital shelves. Any in-world object could become an information object: a poster could link to a bibliography, a portrait to a biography, a model to an assignment, or a plant in a medicinal herb garden to quality health information.

Several branch examples extend the service model. Mystery Manor used a gothic genre space for reader's advisory, horror and mystery fiction, interactive guides, book discussions, readings, and literature-based role-play. The Medical Library and Consumer Health Information Library experimented with health reference, PubMed instruction, current-awareness searches, support-group outreach, RSS feeds, self-guided tours, and confidential patron interaction across language and health-literacy differences. The Caledon Branch Library served a steampunk and nineteenth-century role-play community through special collections, local authors, archives, and reference tied to the community's own emerging identity. The Teen Grid work moved youth services into a secure teen-only environment with adult background checks, a teen advisory board, peer mentoring, homework help, machinima, building and scripting classes, book release parties, and partnerships with youth-serving organizations.

For the GBLS project, Hedreen et al. are important because they make visible the operational complexity behind library service in game-like worlds. The project required international volunteers, partner organizations, island spaces, branch identities, training, events, communication channels, background checks for teen work, and constant adaptation as the platform changed. It also raised durable questions for GBLS: which library services translate into a virtual world, which services need to be redesigned around the affordances of avatars and objects, how trust and confidentiality operate when staff and patrons meet through avatars, and how libraries remain relevant when social, educational, and cultural activity moves into networked play spaces.

The article is not an outcome study and does not measure learning, attendance effects, or long-term sustainability. It is also historically situated in the first-wave enthusiasm around Second Life. Even so, its value is substantial. It shows that early virtual-world librarianship linked reference, collections, reader's advisory, health information, youth services, professional collaboration, literary culture, and community identity in one experimental service ecology. For a GBLS review, it helps connect later VR/metaverse work to an earlier library tradition of building services inside persistent, social, game-adjacent worlds.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Programs Inside Game Worlds and Virtual Platforms`, after the Bronack et al. (2008) paragraph.

Suggested text: Hedreen et al. (2008) give the detailed practitioner account behind Second Life Library 2.0, showing how a game-adjacent virtual world became a multi-branch library service ecology rather than a single virtual room. Info Island and related spaces combined avatar reference, linked information objects, in-world books and note cards, information-literacy instruction, anime and manga collections, Mystery Manor reader's advisory, health-information outreach, Caledon special collections for a steampunk role-play community, Teen Grid services, partner islands, tours, events, and volunteer coordination; for GBLS, the case shows that virtual-platform services require translation of library values into platform-specific forms of presence, trust, collections, facilitation, youth safety, and community identity.

# **Minchin, P. (2018). In praise of tabletop games: Part 1. *Access, 32*(1), 41-45.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `P8EW3DI4`; Better BibTeX citation key `minchinPraiseTabletopGames2018`; attachment key `8CKIPZK9`.

Minchin's short professional article argues that tabletop games belong in school libraries because play is a form of self-directed learning and shared culture. The piece begins from a critique of older didactic models of education and the persistent assumption that play is trivial, childish, or opposed to seriousness. Minchin accepts that explicitly educational games can teach particular facts or systems, but he argues that libraries and educators miss much of the value of games when they treat them only as delivery mechanisms for preselected curriculum content. Like quality fiction, games can teach important habits, interpretive abilities, and dispositions even when they are not designed around conventional subject matter.

The article's most useful contribution is its vocabulary of multiple literacies. Minchin defines psychological literacy as understanding minds, emotions, vulnerability, self-knowledge, and other people as independent agents. He defines social literacy as awareness of power, influence, group dynamics, healthy and unhealthy patterns, and the ways emergent group forces shape individual choices. Systems literacy names the ability to understand rule-governed interactions, causes, effects, economies, ecologies, and other complex systems. Minchin then proposes "procedural literacy" as a distinct literacy: the ability to understand, observe, interpret, and use procedures, from queues and turn-taking to meeting protocols, electoral rules, and legal codes.

Tabletop games, in Minchin's account, are especially well suited to these literacies. Games that reward reading opponents' intentions foster psychological literacy. Multiplayer games develop social literacy because every game has an implicit or explicit social contract. Rule sets and simulations cultivate systems literacy because players repeatedly encounter causes, effects, constraints, and interactions. Tabletop games are also particularly strong procedural-literacy environments because they are rule systems governing human interaction. The familiar figure of the "rules lawyer" becomes evidence that games can train close attention to procedure, interpretation, enforcement, exception, and advantage.

The second major argument is that play itself should be understood as learning. Minchin draws on Huizinga's "magic circle," biological accounts of play as training for the unexpected, Diane Ackerman's claim that play is a favored way of learning, and Raph Koster's theory that fun is bound up with learning. The point is not that every game automatically produces desirable learning, but that play creates structured experimentation, feedback, resilience, pattern recognition, and meaning-making. For libraries, this means that games should be curated thoughtfully in the same way libraries curate literature, film, sequential art, and other cultural forms.

For the GBLS project, Minchin is valuable as a conceptual and advocacy source rather than as an assessment study. It gives language for connecting tabletop services to learning outcomes that are often hard to name: reading people, negotiating groups, understanding systems, interpreting rules, and practicing shared decision procedures. It also makes a library-specific fit claim. Libraries support self-directed learning, and genuine play also needs self-direction; libraries share culture, and tabletop games are cultural works that must be shared in order to be experienced. This helps justify tabletop collections and programs without reducing them to curricular instruments.

The limitations are clear. This is a professional essay, not empirical research, and it does not report a library implementation, participant data, or outcome measures. It also gestures to research and theory rather than systematically reviewing them. Still, it is useful in the review because it clarifies the learning theory behind tabletop GBLS and helps explain why a library might collect and facilitate games for intrinsic literacies, community learning, and cultural participation.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Conceptual Terrain: Games, Play, and Library Service > Games, Gamification, and Play`, after the Seay "G Word" paragraph.

Suggested text: Minchin (2018) adds a tabletop-specific learning rationale by arguing that games need not be explicitly didactic to teach. He frames tabletop play as practice in psychological, social, systems, and procedural literacies: reading minds and motives, navigating group dynamics, understanding rule-governed systems, and interpreting procedures from turn-taking to formal protocols. For GBLS, the article is advocacy rather than assessment evidence, but it usefully connects tabletop collections and programs to libraries' commitments to self-directed learning and shared culture.

# **Okada, T. (2024). *LSHM: TTRPG To The Bitter End*. Google Docs.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `VVGXLYAL`; Better BibTeX citation key `LSHMTTRPGBitter2024`; attachment key `4C9U8GTS`.

This short Low Stress High Mayhem resource document supports a streamed session of Takuma Okada's *To the Bitter End!* It identifies the game, links to the Twitch VOD, points to Okada's social and itch.io pages, and notes that the game appears in *The Ultimate Micro-RPG Book*, edited by James D'Amato. The handout then gathers play-alikes, safety tools, GameRT links, a Twitch chat policy, and a brief stream outline.

These elements could inform practical guidance for libraries designing low-preparation TTRPG streams or online game demonstrations.

However, this source should remain outside the main literature review. It is not scholarship, does not report a study, and does not describe a library program in enough detail to support claims about GBLS outcomes or implementation. Its best use is as an excluded primary artifact that may later support an appendix on advisory metadata, safety tooling, or streamed game-program planning.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. This is a short primary game/advisory artifact rather than library literature; file in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion.

# **Lewallen, K. (2019). Curating breakout boxes. *Knowledge Quest, 48*(2), 42-46.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `FWRDR5NU`; Better BibTeX citation key `lewallenCuratingBreakoutBoxes2019`; attachment key `86Q52IZJ`.

Lewallen offers a practitioner introduction to breakout boxes as school-library resources for inquiry, collaboration, curriculum support, and student-centered play. The article is grounded in the AASL Standards Framework for Learners, especially the belief that learners should be prepared for college, career, and life and the Shared Foundations of Inquire and Collaborate. Lewallen argues that soft skills such as communication, collaboration, resilience, perseverance, critical thinking, and problem solving can be difficult to teach through ordinary classroom routines, but breakout boxes make them visible through playful, time-bound challenges.

The article defines breakout boxes as portable cousins of escape rooms. Instead of escaping from a room, learners work together to solve puzzles, open locks, and complete a scenario before time runs out. A typical kit can include a large box, small box, hasp, invisible-ink pen, UV flashlight, red lens viewers, reflection cards, and several reprogrammable locks. Because the locks can be reset, one kit can support many games, difficulty levels, subjects, and grade bands. Lewallen notes that games can be used to introduce a unit, review content, test comprehension at the end of a unit, support team building, or bring physical movement into instruction.

The school librarian's role is important but deliberately restrained. Lewallen describes the librarian as a "guide on the side" who offers hints as needed while letting students control gameplay. Students must search the room, share clues, communicate what they have found, connect evidence, recognize peers' different talents, and apply prior knowledge in a new context. Reflection questions included with games can help students examine their experience, peer feedback, and future strategies. In AASL terms, breakout boxes give learners opportunities to build new knowledge, work toward shared goals, think critically, and persist when they fail to solve a puzzle or complete the whole game.

The collection-development argument is also useful for GBLS. Lewallen treats breakout boxes as curatable school-library resources rather than one-off classroom activities. The school library can purchase or build kits, lend them to teachers, help match games to curriculum, create games for library orientation, host lunch or after-school breakout programs, run breakout clubs, and support students in designing their own games. Lewallen's own school used four kits in classrooms, sixth- and ninth-grade library orientations, informal library programs, and a weeklong summer breakout camp in which students both played and created games.

The article includes pragmatic implementation advice. BreakoutEDU supplies ready-made physical and digital games, including user-created games searchable by subject, grade, and format; some require subscriptions, while many are free. Teachers Pay Teachers, Pinterest, Facebook groups, librarian communities, and digital Google Sites breakouts provide additional resources. Lewallen recommends starting with an easy game, playing before designing, joining a professional community, and recognizing that older students may need time to learn this style of thinking.

For the GBLS project, Lewallen's value is practical and service-oriented. The article shows how breakout boxes can live at the intersection of collection development, instruction, teacher collaboration, orientation, programming, and student creation. It also clarifies why breakout boxes are attractive for school libraries: they are reusable, adaptable, collaborative, budget-scalable, and aligned with library inquiry goals. The limitations are equally clear. This is a professional guidance article, not a research study, and it does not report formal assessment data. It should be used as evidence of practitioner rationale and implementation patterns, not as proof of learning outcomes.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Escape Rooms and Breakout Boxes`, immediately after the heading.

Suggested text: Lewallen (2019) gives the school-library breakout-box service model in practitioner form: reusable kits with reprogrammable locks can be lent to teachers, adapted across subjects and grade levels, used for library orientation, offered during lunch or after school, extended into breakout clubs, or used in summer camps where students design their own games. The article is not assessment evidence, but it connects breakout boxes to AASL inquiry and collaboration goals and shows how librarians facilitate by offering hints, curating games, supporting reflection, and letting students control the play.

# **Alvarez, V. G. (2017). Engaging students in the library through tabletop gaming. *Knowledge Quest, 45*(4), 40-48.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `NZ5ZLFV2`; Better BibTeX citation key `alvarezEngagingStudentsLibrary2017`; attachment key `LH9P8MRB`.

Alvarez argues for modern tabletop games as analog learning experiences that can support student agency, interpersonal engagement, comprehension, strategic thinking, and college-and-career readiness. The article is written from a public-library media center that functions as a drop-in out-of-school learning environment and works with nearby middle and high schools that lack school libraries. Alvarez frames tabletop gaming as a deliberate response to young people's sometimes isolated or low-effort digital play habits: students may sit side by side with online games while remaining socially disengaged, or rely on trial and error rather than close reading, strategic planning, and sustained attention.

The article's core concept is student agency, defined through Toshalis and Nakkula as students' initiative and capacity to construct possibilities for themselves and act toward desired goals. Alvarez argues that modern tabletop games can help students practice agency because they require players to interpret rules, make choices, test strategies, adapt to failure, and see how their own knowledge and decisions influence outcomes. This is contrasted with older luck-heavy games such as Monopoly and Risk and with digital games whose mechanics may remain nearly unchanged beneath different themes. Modern games are described as more likely to involve player agency, reduced downtime, multiple paths to victory, cooperation, decision-making, reading, and strategic reassessment.

Alvarez connects tabletop play to soft skills and academic readiness. Drawing on Wagner's seven skills, the article names critical thinking, problem solving, collaboration, leadership by influence, agility, adaptability, initiative, communication, information analysis, curiosity, and imagination as skills practiced through games. Agricola is used as a detailed example: players have limited turns, limited workers, competing priorities, food pressures, family growth choices, farm development decisions, and end-game scoring. These mechanics require players to assess priorities, plan under constraints, interpret rules together, and reflect afterward on whether their strategy worked. Alvarez sees a direct analogy to school tasks that require students to manage time, plan across constraints, and revise approaches.

The program examples are concrete. Games used successfully in the library included Agricola, Descent: Journeys in the Dark, Dominion, Mansions of Madness, Mysterium, Pandemic, Pathfinder, Splendor, Star Realms, and Zombicide. Alvarez compares games by skill reliance, luck reliance, reading frequency, cooperation, character cards, and dice. Cooperative games were especially useful for students who preferred not to compete directly and for building camaraderie, leadership, and storytelling. The article also describes an intentional progression: after students found Magic: The Gathering unfamiliar and complicated, staff and a teen advisory committee introduced less reading-intensive games first, then moved from Splendor to Dominion and Star Realms, then to cooperative and more complex games such as Zombicide, Descent, Mysterium, Pandemic, Pathfinder, Agricola, and Mansions of Madness.

For GBLS, Alvarez is important because it treats game selection and facilitation as developmental scaffolding. Students initially resisted reading rules or struggled with comprehension, but with staff knowledge, welcoming facilitation, teen advisory input, and carefully sequenced games, they became more willing to engage with complexity and to act as authors of their own outcomes. The educator's role is to know the rules, animate the game, include students with different skill sets, identify leadership skills, and gradually help students become moderators themselves. This makes the article relevant not only to tabletop clubs but also to advisory, collection development, youth leadership, and facilitation infrastructure.

The article is practitioner advocacy rather than formal assessment. Its evidence is observational and local, and it sometimes overgeneralizes digital games as encouraging shallow trial and error. Even so, it is valuable because it offers a detailed rationale for why modern tabletop games, especially skill-based and cooperative games, may support agency, reading comprehension, social interaction, and strategic self-confidence in library youth services. It also shows that analog and digital games need not be opposed; the stated aim is to help students notice the habits formed by both and to develop stamina, reflection, and agency through shared play.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Clubs and Recurring Play Groups`, after the Maluck (2013) paragraph.

Suggested text: Alvarez (2017) adds a youth-agency rationale for recurring tabletop play in a public-library media center serving students whose schools lacked libraries. Her practitioner account describes modern tabletop games such as Splendor, Dominion, Star Realms, Zombicide, Descent, Mysterium, Pandemic, Pathfinder, Agricola, and Mansions of Madness as sequenced tools for building rule reading, strategic reassessment, cooperation, leadership, resilience, and student moderation; the evidence is observational, but it shows why game selection and facilitation should scaffold participants from approachable games toward more complex, skill-based play.

# **Blummer, B., & Kenton, J. M. (2019). Academic libraries' outreach efforts: Identifying themes in the literature. *Public Services Quarterly, 15*(3), 179-204. https://doi.org/10.1080/15228959.2019.1592058**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `9UCTXT7P`; Better BibTeX citation key `blummerAcademicLibrariesOutreach2019`; attachment key `TIY68CVZ`.

Blummer and Kenton review 174 peer-reviewed papers on academic-library outreach published from 2008 through January 2019. The article is not primarily about games, but it is useful for GBLS because it places gaming within the broader modernization of academic-library outreach. The authors define outreach through instruction, cooperation or community-based work, and special programming, then code the literature through qualitative comparison and simple counts. Their five main themes are outreach to targeted groups, specialized library outreach programs, collaboration, management of outreach efforts, and social media outreach.

The targeted-groups section shows how broad academic-library outreach had become during the decade. Libraries reached community organizations and area residents, middle and high school students, students by class standing, international students, veterans, athletes, college staff, adult learners, transfer students, distance students, diverse student bodies, faculty, and academic majors. For GBLS, this matters because game-based services often appear as outreach to particular audiences rather than as standalone "gaming" services. The review reinforces that outreach design should begin with the audience and local institutional context.

The specialized-programs section is the clearest point of contact with GBLS. Blummer and Kenton identify 47 papers describing outreach programs, with subthemes including instruction and information, roaming services, orientations and other activities, gaming, exhibits, makerspaces, and collections. Gaming appears in six papers. The authors summarize this small gaming subset as including video-game events used to promote library space and resources, low-cost and popular programming, planning and marketing concerns, assessment, and other games used for information-literacy instruction or library/resource orientation. The cited gaming literature includes Bishoff, Farrell, and Neeser (2015), Vanden Elzen and Roush (2013), Courtier and DeLooper (2017), Martin and Martin (2015), Miller (2014), and Spencer (2015).

The article's discussion identifies four cross-cutting outreach qualities that are useful for GBLS: relevance, collaboration, cultural artifacts, and flexibility. Relevance means designing outreach around user groups and needs. Collaboration includes work with faculty, departments, campus groups, community organizations, public libraries, schools, cultural groups, businesses, and state organizations. Cultural artifacts include social media, video games, makerspaces, films, and graphic novels as attention- and interest-building materials. Flexibility includes meeting users where they are, moving services into popular campus and community spaces, and adapting programs to local interests.

Blummer and Kenton are also useful for their caution about assessment. Although many authors described outreach and often reported positive responses, only a handful detailed evaluation. Stronger assessment used both qualitative and quantitative data to improve future programs, while weaker accounts relied on usage statistics, comments, or anecdotal evidence. This echoes a recurring GBLS problem: outreach success is often inferred from popularity, attendance, or enthusiasm, but durable claims require clearer measures and reporting.

For the GBLS project, this source should be used as adjacent context. It supports the claim that gaming was already visible in academic-library outreach discourse by the late 2010s, but it does not evaluate game programs directly. Its main contribution is framing: games are one kind of cultural artifact and special program within larger outreach systems that also depend on audience relevance, collaboration, management, marketing, flexibility, and assessment. The article is therefore best cited in the source-base or review-approach discussion rather than in a section making claims about game-based learning outcomes.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Scope, Definition, and Review Approach > Source Base`, after the Mattson (2021) paragraph.

Suggested text: Blummer and Kenton (2019) provide adjacent source-base context by reviewing 174 papers on academic-library outreach from 2008 to January 2019. Gaming appears as one small specialized-program subtheme, alongside instruction, roaming services, orientations, exhibits, makerspaces, and collections; the review is too broad to evidence GBLS outcomes, but it shows that video-game events, information-literacy games, and library-orientation games were being treated as academic-library outreach tools within wider patterns of relevance, collaboration, cultural artifacts, flexibility, marketing, management, and uneven assessment.

# **Werner, K. (2013). Bringing them in: Developing a gaming program for the library. *Library Trends, 61*(4), 790-801.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `EBRLUTKR`; Better BibTeX citation key `wernerBringingThemDeveloping2013`; attachment key `9NK563TG`.

Werner offers a practitioner guide to starting and sustaining a youth gaming program, grounded in the Benton Harbor Public Library's experience. The program began when a parent asked whether her son could use the library as a place to play *Yu-Gi-Oh!* after a local gaming store closed. Staff opened the auditorium once a month for a two-hour supervised gaming afternoon with light refreshments, in-house flyers, and bookmarks. Initial attendance was about fifteen children and teens; after the library added game equipment and collections, attendance doubled within a year. The program eventually split into a children's gaming afternoon for ages twelve and younger and a teen program for ages thirteen and older, with steady attendance over several years.

The article is useful because it traces a complete service arc from one patron request to a durable youth-services program. Werner describes adding a library-owned *Yu-Gi-Oh!* deck for patrons without cards, consulting teen patrons and a local GameStop manager when purchasing systems and games, and connecting gaming afternoons to other library structures. Teen Advisory Board meetings were scheduled before teen gaming, children's book group meetings before children's gaming, and Summer Reading Program rewards were redesigned to include program attendance. Teens who first came for gaming became Teen Advisory Board and Teen Book Club participants, children's-program volunteers, and regular library users. Teen summer reading participation rose from about fifty participants five years earlier to over one hundred in 2011.

Werner's format guidance covers card games, board games, role-playing games, video games, and tournaments. Traditional card games are easy to teach, while collectible card games such as *Yu-Gi-Oh!* and *Magic: The Gathering* can draw dedicated players who need meeting space. Board games should be quick enough for library sessions and staff should play them first so they can teach patrons. Role-playing games require more time, continuity, and a knowledgeable facilitator. Video games require decisions about consoles, controllers, Internet access, content ratings, multiplayer modes, and whether online play should be muted or avoided. Recommended core purchases include approachable multiplayer games, sports games, racing games, dance games, and fighting games that avoid Mature-rated content.

The tournament discussion is especially concrete. Werner recommends agreeing on rules before play begins, using library-owned games and systems to keep the playing field even, choosing games with clear winners, and using brackets, random pairings, byes, double elimination, or points depending on the game. Prizes can be small, and local gaming stores can help with flyers, prizes, advice, and visibility. Staff must be ready to act as referees and adjudicators because competition can heighten conflict if rules feel unclear or shifting.

Operational challenges are treated honestly. Gaming programs can be loud and need appropriate rooms, lock-ins, or quieter tabletop formats. Theft affected games and personal cards, leading the library to check bags, cancel programs temporarily after thefts, store unused games behind locked doors, and consider locking equipment or RFID tags. Downloadable content and online codes made the future of used-game purchasing uncertain. Parent challenges were possible, especially around violent games, so Werner recommends a clear game-selection policy, age-rating restrictions, reviews, and alignment with existing policies for R-rated movies.

For GBLS, Werner is valuable as an implementation manual for youth game programming. It foregrounds flexibility: the most successful programs change with the patrons who attend them. It also shows that gaming can operate as a pipeline into broader library relationships, including advisory boards, book clubs, volunteering, reading programs, collections, and regular use. The evidence is local and mostly descriptive, but the article gives concrete service-design details that remain useful: start from community demand, involve the target audience, consult local expertise, select short multiplayer games, build policies before challenges arise, plan for noise and theft, assess circulation/library-card/program data, and let patrons help shape the program over time.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Programming Formats and Service Models > Clubs and Recurring Play Groups`, after the Alvarez (2017) paragraph.

Suggested text: Werner (2013) gives a public-library youth-services implementation guide rooted in Benton Harbor Public Library's gaming afternoons, which grew from a patron request for *Yu-Gi-Oh!* space into separate children's and teen programs with card games, board games, RPGs, video games, tournaments, Teen Advisory Board links, book-group links, volunteering, and summer-reading participation. The article is descriptive rather than evaluative, but it makes recurring gaming visible as a flexible service pipeline: start with local demand, involve teens in selection, consult local game expertise, plan for noise and theft, create game-selection policies, and let the program change with its participants.

# **Dionne, K., & The Arcane Library. (2024). *LSHM: Shadowdark RPG Series*. Google Docs.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `7VH8M8XR`; Better BibTeX citation key `LSHMShadowdarkRPG2024`; attachment key `YNYXX8TS`.

This Low Stress High Mayhem handout supports a multi-session GameRT stream series for *Shadowdark RPG* by Kelsey Dionne and The Arcane Library. The document frames *Shadowdark* as an intuitive bridge between fifth-edition Dungeons & Dragons players and the Old School Renaissance, emphasizing dungeon exploration, torchlight, old-school risk, familiar fantasy adventure, and modernized quality-of-life features. It links to VODs for three streamed sessions and a Q&A.

The resource list is organized as a broad discovery and preparation packet. It identifies *Shadowdark RPG*, links to the Kickstarter campaign, and notes the published adventure used for the stream, *Puzzle Dungeon: The Seers' Sanctum*. It also lists live-play examples, an interview with Kelsey Dionne, articles on moving D&D players to new systems and understanding OSR play, and play-alikes across tabletop and video games. The play-alikes include *Knave*, *Mausritter*, *Rouge Second Edition*, *Ten Candles*, *Troika!*, *Trophy*, *Vast in the Dark*, *Dungeon Solitaire*, *A Plague Tale*, *Elden Ring*, and *Darkest Dungeon*.

The handout also extends advisory work across media. Read-alikes and view-alikes include fantasy novels, classic adventures, manga, anime, and animated fantasy series, while the helpful-links section gathers resources on session zero, character creation, and game mastering for different kinds of players. This makes the item more than a game record: it is a preparation scaffold for a streamed tabletop campaign, helping viewers and potential facilitators understand genre, system expectations, tone, adjacent media, and practical tools.

The facilitation and safety infrastructure is prominent. The document links to GameRT session-zero materials, guides for running session zero, D&D Beyond and Sly Flourish resources, James D'Amato character guides, Johnn Four roleplaying tips, game-master guides, Adventuring Academy interviews, *Script Change*, lines and veils, *The Door Is Open*, the TTRPG Safety Toolkit, and *Consent in Gaming*. It also includes GameRT Twitch policy language about respectful conduct, no hate speech, moderator support, harassment assistance, and private contact.

For the larger GBLS project, this source is useful as a practitioner artifact showing how a library-adjacent gaming group can scaffold a longer streamed TTRPG series. It demonstrates how advisory, collection-like pathways, staff preparation, player onboarding, session-zero norms, safety tools, and cross-media recommendations can be packaged around one program. It is especially relevant to practical planning for recurring online tabletop programs or system-introduction series.

This source should remain outside the main literature review. It is not scholarship, not an evaluation, and not a detailed case study of library implementation. It may be valuable for a practical appendix on TTRPG program packets, OSR/D&D advisory language, streamed series design, or safety and onboarding resources, but it should not be cited as evidence for GBLS outcomes.

No direct addition recommended. This is a primary game/advisory/program resource rather than library literature; file in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion.

# **Farrell, S. L., Neeser, A. E., & Bishoff, C. (2017). Academic uses of video games: A qualitative assessment of research and teaching needs at a large research university. *College & Research Libraries, 78*(5), 675-700. https://doi.org/10.5860/crl.78.5.675**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `8XX2IH2G`; Better BibTeX citation key `farrellAcademicUsesVideo2017`; attachment key `ZY8L8XKX`.

Farrell, Neeser, and Bishoff provide one of the strongest empirical needs assessments for academic-library video game services. Working at the University of Minnesota Twin Cities, a large doctoral research university with no dedicated game design department, the authors asked how scholars across disciplines used video games in research and teaching and what information and technology needs the library might support. They identified 92 faculty, staff, and graduate students who appeared to use video games or game technology academically, then conducted 30 semistructured interviews. The interview sample included arts and humanities, social sciences, STEM, and health sciences.

The study found that video games were distributed across the university rather than concentrated in one department. Twenty-eight of 30 interviewees used games in research, and 23 used games in teaching; 21 used games in both. Research uses fell into five categories: development of games or game technology, games as instrumentation, games as objects of study, games as influences on people or society, and games as educational technology. Teaching uses included designing games for classes, teaching game design, using games as course material, and discussing games, game mechanics, or the instructor's game-related research in class. Every disciplinary group used games as course material or as discussion material.

The article is especially valuable because it complicates assumptions about what an academic game collection should contain. Existing library game collections often emphasize console games and commercially successful titles, but the interview data showed that PC games were nearly as important as all other game types combined and that PCs, software, peripherals, programming languages, web applications, servers, and game design tools also mattered. Arts and humanities and social sciences researchers had stronger needs for games as primary sources or course materials; STEM and health sciences participants reported stronger software and peripheral needs. Health sciences uses could involve Wii balance boards or other peripherals as low-cost instrumentation rather than play in the usual sense.

Information needs were also broader than standard library resources. Interviewees used journals, Google Scholar, web sources, colleagues, books, datasets, interviews, game manuals, trade magazines, streaming video, and other nontraditional sources. Colleagues were the single most common information source, particularly in STEM and health sciences, and many interviewees collaborated across departments. The authors argue that libraries can act as connectors across disciplinary boundaries through events, experimental technology spaces, shared collections, guides, and liaison coordination.

Access and equity are central to the findings. Graduate students often relied on personal collections, borrowed technology, free tools, or out-of-pocket purchases, while faculty and staff more often used grants, departmental funds, startup funds, or other institutional resources. Arts and humanities and social sciences graduate students were especially likely to pay personally for games or related materials needed for research. The authors therefore argue that library access to games and game technology could reduce disciplinary funding barriers and support graduate students whose work treats games as primary research materials analogous to texts.

For GBLS, this article shifts the academic-library game conversation from "Should libraries have games?" to "Which local scholarly practices should game services support?" Its recommendations are needs-based and institutional: conduct local assessment, recognize cross-disciplinary networks, build collections and services around actual research and teaching uses, collect PC games as well as console games, consider peripherals and software, support students in classes that require games, and help graduate students locate funding. The study is limited to one campus and does not include students taking game-related courses, but it provides a strong model for needs assessment and evidence-based academic game collection development.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Research, Scholarship, and Creative Production`, after the Wood and Carter (2018) paragraph.

Suggested text: Farrell, Neeser, and Bishoff (2017) add a needs-assessment layer to academic game services through 30 semistructured interviews with University of Minnesota faculty, staff, and graduate students who used video games in research or teaching. Their findings show that games supported development, instrumentation, textual/cultural analysis, social-impact research, educational technology, course materials, game design, and classroom discussion across arts and humanities, social sciences, STEM, and health sciences; they also caution that collections should reflect local disciplinary needs, including PC games, peripherals, software, game manuals, trade magazines, collaborative networks, and graduate-student funding barriers rather than defaulting to console collections alone.
