# **Farmer, L. S. J. (2011). Are girls game? *Knowledge Quest, 40*(1), 14-17.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `5N2HI87D`; Better BibTeX citation key `farmerAreGirlsGame2011`; attachment key `CSDD4ZPJ`.

Farmer argues that school librarians who add electronic gaming to library programs need to address gender equity deliberately. The article begins from the premise that gaming has become part of school-library resources and services because youth are interested in games and because games can support information and digital literacies. Farmer distinguishes long-standing board-game participation from newer e-gaming, including video, console, and computer games, and notes that e-games have historically appeared to engage boys more than girls. The core question is how school librarians can make e-gaming positive and accessible for all students.

The article summarizes gendered patterns in youth e-gaming. Farmer notes that girls and women make up a substantial share of the gaming population, especially because of Wii and casual cell-phone games, but boys tend to play more often and for longer periods. Teenage girls' gaming activity may decline because of gendered perceptions of gaming, discomfort with mechanical or violent game styles, difficulty identifying with male, nonhuman, or sexualized default characters, and negative first experiences with complex games. Farmer emphasizes that gender differences may fade with experience and age, which makes early access and supportive design especially important.

The equity argument is linked to technology futures. If girls generalize negative e-gaming experiences into avoidance of technology courses, professional development, or technology-rich learning environments, school-library gaming can unintentionally contribute to broader opportunity gaps. Farmer warns that technology avoidance may narrow college, career, and professional options. Conversely, thoughtful e-gaming can introduce technology through motivating activity, improve problem solving and collaboration, enhance spatial skills, and increase self-efficacy.

Farmer's learning rationale is built around activity theory and intentional instructional design. E-games may support learning when they provide fixed and equitable rules, meaningful contexts, clear goals, identity exploration, cognitive and affective engagement, timely feedback, control, and personal investment. But games do not guarantee learning by themselves. Farmer stresses that e-games need academic context, conceptual purpose, skill-building design, and a rationale. Merely assigning a game without context may alienate girls in particular.

For GBLS, Farmer is useful as an early school-library equity lens for digital gaming. The source supports the idea that game-based library services should not assume that a game collection or event is automatically inclusive because it is available to everyone. Selection, characters, genres, difficulty curves, first experiences, social context, instructional framing, and staff support all shape who feels invited. The article also helps connect game services to digital-literacy equity: inclusive gaming may support technology confidence, while poorly designed gaming may reinforce exclusion.

The limitations are clear. This is a short professional article that synthesizes prior research and offers guidance rather than reporting new data or a specific library program. Some statistics and market conditions are historically situated in 2011. Even so, it gives GBLS a useful gender-equity caution and vocabulary for thinking about e-gaming as technology access, not just entertainment.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Cross-Cutting Lessons for Game-Based Library Services > Curate for Inclusion, Access, and Advisory`, after Tanaka (2022).

Suggested text: Farmer (2011) adds a gender-equity caution for school-library e-gaming. Because girls' participation may be shaped by stereotyped characters, violent or mechanical genre cues, negative first experiences, confidence with complex games, and broader associations between gaming and technology, inclusive GBLS requires more than offering games equally; librarians need gender-sensitive selection, purposeful instructional context, supportive onboarding, and attention to whether gaming builds or undermines technology self-efficacy.

# **Chen, K.-F., Hwang, G.-J., & Chen, M.-R. A. (2024). Knowledge check-based concept mapping in digital games: Impacts on students' learning performance and behaviors. *Educational Technology Research and Development, 72*(4), 2297-2324. https://doi.org/10.1007/s11423-024-10378-6**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `RERILY87`; Better BibTeX citation key `chenKnowledgeCheckbasedConcept2024`; attachment key `F32ZWKJP`.

Chen, Hwang, and Chen study how a knowledge check-based concept map-guided gaming strategy affects secondary students' chemistry learning. The article begins from a common problem in digital game-based learning: students may play through tasks, guess answers, or follow game prompts without connecting game activity to formal disciplinary concepts. Concept maps can help learners organize relationships among concepts, but concept maps alone may not ensure conceptual understanding. The authors therefore propose knowledge check-based concept map-guided gaming, or KC-CMG, as a scaffold that combines concept maps with two-tier knowledge checks inside a digital game.

The study was conducted in a chemistry unit on the classification and separation of substances. The researchers built a role-playing digital game in RPG Maker, aligned with a middle-school chemistry curriculum. Learners moved through game levels, received concept maps about mixtures, pure substances, compounds, elements, and principles of classification, and then completed in-game tasks. The experimental group used KC-CMG: after learners encountered a concept map, they answered a first-tier factual question and then a second-tier conceptual-understanding question. Incorrect answers led students back toward the material or concept map before they could continue. The control group used the same game and concept maps but completed only first-tier factual questions before moving on.

The quasi-experiment included 48 seventh-grade students, 24 in the experimental group and 24 in the control group. Both groups used the same game, teacher, learning content, class time, pretests, posttests, and questionnaires. Measures included chemistry learning achievement, problem-solving awareness, critical thinking tendency, germane cognitive load, game behavior logs, and interviews. ANCOVA and t-test results favored the KC-CMG group: students using the two-tier knowledge check strategy had significantly higher learning achievement, problem-solving awareness, critical thinking tendency, and germane cognitive load than students using ordinary concept map-guided gaming.

The learning-behavior analysis explains why the strategy mattered. When experimental-group learners missed second-tier conceptual questions, they often returned to read the concept map again. Their behavior suggested double-loop learning: instead of only repeating a task or guessing, they reconsidered the concept relationships behind the task. The experimental group also showed a stronger path from completing learning objectives to challenge success, while the control group did not show the same pattern. Interviews reinforced this interpretation. Experimental-group students said that two-tier questions helped them correct concepts, think about why a concept map was drawn a certain way, and look back at the concept map more carefully; control-group students reported that the game and concept maps made learning more engaging and efficient but were less reflective.

For the larger GBLS project, this article is useful as adjacent instructional-design evidence. It suggests that game-based learning services should not rely on play, points, narrative, or maps alone when the goal is conceptual understanding. Learners may need embedded checks, prompts to explain reasoning, feedback loops, and opportunities to revisit conceptual materials. This is potentially relevant to library escape rooms, information-literacy games, digital tutorials, and game-based workshops where the library wants transfer rather than simple task completion.

Even so, the source should remain outside the main GBLS review. It is a school chemistry study, not library literature, and its digital game was a researcher-developed curriculum tool rather than a library service. The sample was small, the intervention was short, and the authors acknowledge that developing such games is time-consuming and not easily generalizable across subjects. The article can inform design principles for future GBLS instruction, especially scaffolding and assessment inside games, but it should not be cited as evidence about library programs, collections, outreach, or patron outcomes.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. This is adjacent educational-technology evidence without a library service context and is already best handled as excluded literature.

# **Nyitray, K. J. (2019). Game on to game after: Sources for video game history. *Reference & User Services Quarterly, 59*(1), 7-11.**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `JPG7LU78`; Better BibTeX citation key `nyitrayGameGameSources2019`; attachment key `VRMSBYLM`.

Nyitray's Alert Collector column is a curated guide to sources for video game history. It is not a study of a library program, but it is directly relevant to academic and special-collections GBLS because it explains why video games require research support, preservation attention, and historically informed collecting. Nyitray writes from experience founding the William A. Higinbotham Game Studies Collection and frames video games as dominant entertainment, complex cultural artifacts, and increasingly legitimate objects of historical and critical inquiry.

The opening argument is definitional and historiographic. Nyitray notes that even defining "video game" can involve technical and philosophical debate, using *Tennis for Two*, *Computer Space*, and *Pong* to distinguish analog computer games, commercial arcade video games, and broader interactive screen-based game experiences. She argues that early writing on games often presented chronological facts for younger audiences, while recent work has moved toward critical video game historiography. Video games bring together computer science, arts, humanities, social sciences, design, industry, and popular culture, making them transdisciplinary objects rather than simple entertainment products.

The article emphasizes material and archival complexity. Video games can be studied through source code, ROM cartridges, platforms, controllers, circuits, corporate records, developers' papers, box art, manuals, and other ephemera. Arcade machines, consoles, joysticks, cartridges, and packaging provide tangible evidence of shared play experiences, while online games, app-based games, emulation, and digital distribution complicate preservation and access. Nyitray connects this to the growth of archives and documentation initiatives at institutions such as The Strong National Museum of Play, the Library of Congress, the Smithsonian, the Museum of the Moving Image, Stanford, the University of Texas, the University of Illinois, and the University of Michigan.

Most of the column is an annotated bibliography organized by reference works, visual histories, histories, culture and critical studies, industry, art, music, and digital collections. It highlights works such as *The Video Games Guide*, *Phoenix IV*, Brett Weiss's classic home-video-game guides, *The Game Console*, *Supercade*, *History of Video Games in 64 Objects*, *Replay*, *The Ultimate History of Video Games*, *Debugging Game History*, *Game After*, *Coin-Operated Americans*, *Racing the Beam*, *Console Wars*, *Masters of Doom*, *Blood, Sweat, and Pixels*, *Art of Atari*, *The Art of Video Games*, and *Music Video Games*. It also points to the Learning Games Initiative Research Archive and the Internet Archive Console Living Room.

For GBLS, Nyitray is useful as a research-support and collection-development source. It gives librarians vocabulary for explaining why game collections are not only recreational collections: games are primary sources, technological artifacts, art objects, industrial products, music/media forms, and cultural records. It also supports reference work for students and researchers entering game history, game studies, media archaeology, preservation, industry history, platform studies, and critical studies. In a GBLS review, it helps connect game services to special collections, archives, bibliographic instruction, and scholarly discovery.

The source's limitation is that it is a curated professional bibliography, not empirical evidence. It does not assess use of these sources, evaluate a game collection, or report patron outcomes. Its value lies in mapping the scholarly and reference terrain for video game history and in showing why academic and research libraries need robust source pathways, preservation partnerships, and descriptive practices for games and game-related ephemera.

## **Suggested additions to `review.md`**

Section and exact location: `Major Areas of Game-Based Library Service > Research, Scholarship, and Creative Production`, after the Farrell, Neeser, and Bishoff (2017) paragraph.

Suggested text: Nyitray (2019) adds a reference and special-collections perspective to game research support. Her curated guide to video game history treats games as complex artifacts made of source code, ROM cartridges, platforms, controllers, circuits, corporate records, developer papers, box art, manuals, trade materials, emulated experiences, and digital collections; for GBLS, the column is not outcome evidence, but it shows why game collections support historical research, platform studies, preservation, media archaeology, art and music studies, industry history, and critical game historiography.

# **Janik, J. (2021). Intra-acting bio-object: A posthuman approach to the player-game relation. *Journal of Gaming & Virtual Worlds, 13*(1), 21-39. https://doi.org/10.1386/jgvw_00026_1**

Unique identifier: Zotero parent item key `KUH3ZRH3`; Better BibTeX citation key `janikIntraactingBioobjectPosthuman2021`; attachment key `NKHUJNMT`.

Janik develops a theoretical framework for understanding the relation between a video game and a player as a posthuman, meaning-making process. The article argues that game studies has often centered the human player through concepts such as avatar, agency, immersion, implied player, and player experience. Even when scholars recognize that player freedom is constrained by rules and systems, analysis still often begins from the player. Janik instead asks what happens if the game object is treated as an equal partner in play rather than as a secondary container for human agency.

The conceptual framework joins Tadeusz Kantor's theatrical idea of the bio-object with Karen Barad's new-materialist concept of intra-action. Kantor's bio-object describes an indivisible but tense stage relation between actor and object: the actor animates the object, but the object also frames, limits, and shapes the actor's movement and meaning. Barad's intra-action helps Janik describe how entities do not simply preexist their relations; their boundaries, properties, and meanings emerge through relational material-discursive practices. Janik applies these ideas to gameplay, arguing that player and game form a temporary bio-object during play.

The article uses examples from video game play, glitches, subversive player practices, and games that resist player control to show how meaning emerges through tension. The player animates the game by playing, interpreting, exploring, modifying, or exploiting it, but the game also structures action through software, hardware, audiovisual form, rules, limits, saved states, anti-piracy mechanisms, glitches, and unexpected behaviors. When a player encounters a glitch, a resistant system, or an apparently autonomous game object, the game can become visible as more than a tool. The player and game then co-produce meanings that belong to neither one alone.

Janik's larger claim is that agency and meaning-making should be understood as distributed across the player-game relation. The player does not simply impose meaning on the game, and the game does not simply deliver designed meaning to the player. The player and game intra-actively shape each other: the player becomes a specific player through the encounter, and the game becomes a specific played object through the player's actions, data, interpretations, and traces. The bio-object remains tense because the game and player stay linked but distinct; this difference is what allows meaning to emerge.

For the GBLS project, this article is only indirectly useful. It offers a vocabulary for thinking about games as active cultural and technological objects rather than neutral containers for library programming. That could matter when discussing why game-based services should attend to rules, systems, interfaces, glitches, platform constraints, and player interpretation. It may also help explain why patrons' experiences of the same game can diverge depending on their bodies, histories, skills, expectations, and interactions with the game object.

However, this source should remain outside the main literature review. It is not library literature, not an empirical study of learning or service, and not a discussion of collections, programming, outreach, access, or advisory. Its theoretical vocabulary is more specialized than the review currently needs. It can be retained as excluded adjacent game-studies theory, useful if the project later develops a deeper conceptual section on player-game ontology, but it should not be used as evidence for GBLS practice or outcomes.

## **Suggested addition to `review.md`**

No direct addition recommended. This is adjacent game-studies theory without a library service context; file in `Explicitly Excluded Literature` after summary completion.
